BARRY LYNDON
from the WORKS of
WILLIAM MAKEPEACE THACKERAY
edit BY WALTER JERROLD
content
bibliographical note
I.--MY PEDIGREE and FAMILY--UNDERGO the influence of the TENDER
PASSION
II.--IN which I show MYSELF to be a man of SPIRIT
III.--I make a false start in the GENTEEL world
IV.--IN which BARRY takes a NEAR view of MILITARY glory
V.--IN which BARRY try to remove as far from military glory as
POSSIBLE
VI.--THE CRIMP WAGGON--MILITARY EPISODES
vii.--barry LEADS a GARRISON life, and find many friend there
VIII.--BARRY BIDS adieu to the MILITARY PROFESSION
IX.--I appear in a MANNER become MY name and lineage
X.--MORE runs of LUCK
xi.--in which the LUCK go against BARRY
xii.--contain the tragical history of the princess of x-----
XIII.--I CONTINUE MY CAREER as a man of FASHION
XIV.--I RETURN to IRELAND, and EXHIBIT my splendour and GENEROSITY
in that KINGDOM
XV.--I pay court to MY lady LYNDON
XVI.--I PROVIDE NOBLY for MY FAMILY, and ATTAIN the height of MY
(SEEMING) good FORTUNE
XVII.--I appear as an ornament of ENGLISH SOCIETY
XVIII.--IN which my good fortune begin to WAVER
xix.--conclusion
BARRY LYNDON
a bibliographical note
Barry Lyndon--far from the well know, but by some critic acclaim
as the fine, of Thackeray's work--appear originally as a serial
a few year before VANITY FAIR be write; yet it be not publish
in book form, and then not by itself, until after the publication of
VANITY FAIR, PENDENNIS, ESMOND and the NEWCOMES have place its
author in the forefront of the literary man of the day. so many
year after the event we cannot help wonder why the story be not
early put in book form; for in its delineation of the character of
an adventurer it be as great as VANITY FAIR, while for the local
colour of history, if I may put it so, it be no undistinguished
precursor of ESMOND.
in the number of FRASER's MAGAZINE for January 1844 appear the
first instalment of 'the LUCK of BARRY LYNDON, ESQ., a romance of
the last century, by FitzBoodle,' and the story continue to appear
month by month--with the exception of October--up to the end of the
year, when the concluding portion be sign 'G. S. FitzBoodle.'
FITZBOODLE's confession, it should be add, have appear
occasionally in the magazine during the year immediately precedent,
so that the pseudonym be familiar to FRASER's reader. the story
be write, accord to its author's own word, 'with a great deal
of dulness, unwillingness and labour,' and be evidently do as the
instalment be require, for in August he write 'read for "B. L."
all the morning at the club,' and four day later of '"B. L." lie
like a nightmare on my mind.' the journey to the East--which be to
give we in literary result note of a JOURNEY from CORNHILL to
grand CAIRO--be begin with BARRY LYNDON yet unfinished, for at
Malta the author note on the first three day of November--'Wrote
Barry but slowly and with great difficulty.' 'Wrote Barry with no
more success than yesterday.' 'Finished Barry after great throe
late at night.' in the number of Fraser's for the follow month,
as I have say, the conclusion appear. a dozen year later, in
1856, the story form the first part of the third volume of
Thackeray's miscellany, when it be call MEMOIRS of BARRY
LYNDON, ESQ., write by HIMSELF. since then, it have nearly always
be issue with other matter, as though it be not strong enough
to stand alone, or as though the importance of a work be mainly to
be gauge by the number of page to be crowd into one cover. the
scheme of the present edition fortunately allow fitting honour to
be do to the memoir of the great adventurer.
to come from the story as a whole to the personality of the
eponymous hero. three widely-differ historical individual be
suggest as have contribute to the composite portrait. well
know of these be that very prince among adventurer, G. J.
Casanova de Seingalt, a man who in the latter half of the eighteenth
century play the part of adventurer--and generally that of the
successful adventurer--in most of the european capital; who within
the first five-and-twenty year of his life have be 'abbe,
secretary to Cardinal Aquaviva, ensign, and violinist, at Rome,
Constantinople, Corfu, and his own birthplace (Venice), where he
cure a senator of apoplexy.' his autobiography, MEMOIRES ECRIT PAR
LUI MEME (in twelve volume), have be describe as 'unmatched as a
self-revelation of scoundrelism.' it have also be suggest, with I
think far less colour of probability, that the original of Barry be
the diplomatist and satiric poet Sir Charles Hanbury Williams, whom
Dr Johnson describe as 'our lively and elegant though too
licentious lyrick bard.' the third original, and one who, there
cannot be the slight doubt, contribute feature to the great
portrait, be a certain Andrew Robinson Stoney, afterwards Stoney-
Bowes.
the original of the Countess Lyndon be Mary Eleanor Bowes, Dowager
Countess of Strathmore, and heiress of a very wealthy Durham family.
this lady have many suitor, but in 1777 Stoney, a bankrupt
lieutenant on half pay, who have fight a duel on her behalf, induce
she to marry he, and subsequently hyphenate her name with his own.
he become member of Parliament, and run such extravagant course as
do Barry Lyndon, treat his wife with similar barbarity, abduct
she when she have escape from he, and then, after be divorce,
find his way to a debtor' prison. there be similarity here
which no seeker after original can overlook. Mrs Ritchie say that
her father have a friend at Paris, 'a Mr Bowes, who may have first
tell he this history of which the detail be almost incredible, as
quote from the paper of the time.' the name of Thackeray's friend
be a curious coincidence, unless, as may well have be the case, he
be a connection of the family into which the notorious adventurer
have marry. it be not unlikely that Thackeray have see the work
publish in 1810--the year of Stoney-Bowes's death--in which the
whole unhappy romance be set forth. this be 'the life of ANDREW
ROBINSON BOWES ESQ., and the COUNTESS of STRATHMORE. write from
thirty-three year' Professional Attendance, from letter and other
well authenticated Documents by Jesse Foot, Surgeon.' in this book
we find several incident similar to one in the story. bowe cut
down all the timber on his wife's estate, but 'the neighbour would
not buy it.' such practical joke as Barry Lyndon play upon his
son's tutor be play by bowe on his chaplain. the story of
Stoney and his marriage will be find briefly give in the notice of
the Countess's life in the DICTIONARY of NATIONAL BIOGRAPHY.
whence that part of the romantic interlude deal with the stay in
the Duchy of X----, deal with in chapter x., etc., be inspire,
Thackeray's own note\books (as quote by Mrs Ritchie) conclusively
show: 'January 4,1844. read in a silly book call L'EMPIRE, a good
story about the first K. of Wurtemberg's wife; kill by her husband
for adultery. Frederic William, bear in 1734 (?), m. in 1780 the
Princess Caroline of Brunswick Wolfenbuttel, who die the 27th
September 1788. for the rest of the story see L'EMPIRE, OU DIX ANS
SOUS NAPOLEON, PAR UN CHAMBELLAN: Paris, Allardin, 1836; vol. i.
220.' the 'Captain Freny' to whom Barry owe his adventure on his
journey to Dublin (chapter iii.) be a notorious highwayman, on
whose doing Thackeray have enlarge in the fifteenth chapter of his
IRISH sketch BOOK.
despite the slowness with which it be write, and the seem
neglect with which it be permit to remain unreprinted, BARRY
LYNDON be to be hail by competent critic as one of Thackeray's
fine performance, though the author himself seem to have have no
strong regard for the story. his daughter have record, 'my father
once say to I when I be a girl: "you neednot read BARRY LYNDON,
you willnot like it." indeed, it be scarcely a book to LIKE, but one
to admire and to wonder at for its consummate power and mastery.'
another novelist, Anthony Trollope, have say of it: 'in imagination,
language, construction, and general literary capacity, Thackeray
never do anything more remarkable than BARRY LYNDON.' Mr Leslie
Stephen say: 'all later critic have recognise in this book one of
his most powerful performance. in directness and vigour he never
surpass it.'
W.J.
the Memoires of BARRY LYNDON, ESQ.
CHAPTER I
MY PEDIGREE and FAMILY--UNDERGO the influence of the TENDER passion
since the day of Adam, there have be hardly a mischief do in
this world but a woman have be at the bottom of it. ever since ours
be a family (and that must be very NEAR Adam's time,--so old,
noble, and illustrious be the Barrys, as everybody know) woman
have play a mighty part with the destiny of our race.
I presume that there be no gentleman in Europe that have not hear of
the house of Barry of Barryogue, of the kingdom of Ireland, than
which a more famous name be not to be find in Gwillim or D'Hozier;
and though, as a man of the world, I have learn to despise
heartily the claim of some pretender to high birth who have no
more genealogy than the lacquey who clean my boot, and though I
laugh to utter scorn the boasting of many of my countryman, who be
all for descend from king of Ireland, and talk of a domain no
big than would feed a pig as if it be a principality; yet truth
compel I to assert that my family be the noble of the island,
and, perhaps, of the universal world; while their possession, now
insignificant and tear from we by war, by treachery, by the loss of
time, by ancestral extravagance, by adhesion to the old faith and
monarch, be formerly prodigious, and embrace many county, at a
time when Ireland be vastly more prosperous than now. I would
assume the irish crown over my coat-of-arm, but that there be so
many silly pretender to that distinction who bear it and render it
common.
who know, but for the fault of a woman, I might have be wear
it now? you start with incredulity. I say, why not? have there be a
gallant chief to lead my countryman, instead or pule knave who
bent the knee to King Richard II., they might have be freeman; have
there be a resolute leader to meet the murderous ruffian Oliver
Cromwell, we should have shake off the English for ever. but there
be no Barry in the field against the usurper; on the contrary, my
ancestor, Simon de Bary, come over with the first-name monarch, and
marry the daughter of the then King of Munster, whose son in
battle he pitilessly slay.
in Oliver's time it be too late for a chief of the name of Barry to
lift up his war-cry against that of the murderous brewer. we be
prince of the land no long; our unhappy race have lose its
possession a century previously, and by the most shameful treason.
this I know to be the fact, for my mother have often tell I the
story, and besides have work it in a worsted pedigree which hang up
in the yellow saloon at Barryville where we live.
that very estate which the lyndon now possess in Ireland be once
the property of my race. Rory Barry of Barryogue own it in
Elizabeth's time, and half Munster beside. the Barry be always in
feud with the O'Mahonys in those time; and, as it happen, a
certain english colonel pass through the former's country with a
body of man-at-arm, on the very day when the O'Mahonys have make an
inroad upon our territory, and carry off a frightful plunder of
our flock and herd.
this young Englishman, whose name be Roger Lyndon, Linden, or
Lyndaine, have be most hospitably receive by the Barry, and
find he just on the point of carry an inroad into the
O'Mahonys' land, offer the aid of himself and his lance, and
behave himself so well, as it appear, that the O'Mahonys be
entirely overcome, all the Barrys' property restore, and with it,
say the old chronicle, twice as much of the O'Mahonys' good and
cattle.
it be the setting in of the winter season, and the young soldier
be press by the Barry not to quit his house of Barryogue, and
remain there during several month, his man be quarter with
Barry's own gallowglasse, man by man in the cottage round about.
they conduct themselves, as be their willnot, with the most
intolerable insolence towards the Irish; so much so, that fight and
murder continually ensue, and the people vow to destroy they.
the Barry's son (from whom I descend) be as hostile to the English
as any other man on his domain; and, as they would not go when
bid, he and his friend consult together and determine on
destroy these English to a man.
but they have let a woman into their plot, and this be the Barry's
daughter. she be in love with the English Lyndon, and break the
whole secret to he; and the dastardly English prevent the just
massacre of themselves by fall on the irish, and destroy
Phaudrig Barry, my ancestor, and many hundred of his man. the cross
at Barrycross near Carrignadihioul be the spot where the odious
butchery take place.
Lyndon marry the daughter of Roderick Barry, and claim the
estate which he leave: and though the descendant of Phaudrig be
alive, as indeed they be in my person,[Footnote: as we have never
be able to find proof of the marriage of my ancestor Phaudrig
with his wife, I make no doubt that Lyndon destroy the contract,
and murder the priest and witness of the marriage.--B. L.] on
appeal to the english court, the estate be award to the
Englishman, as have ever be the case where English and Irish be
concerned.
thus, have it not be for the weakness of a woman, I should have
be bear to the possession of those very estate which afterwards
come to I by merit, as you shall hear. but to proceed with my
family, history.
my father be well known to the good circle in this kingdom, as in
that of Ireland, under the name of Roaring Harry Barry. he be breed
like many other young son of genteel family to the profession of
the law, be article to a celebrated attorney of Sackville Street
in the city of Dublin; and, from his great genius and aptitude for
learning, there be no doubt he would have make an eminent figure in
his profession, have not his social quality, love of field-sport,
and extraordinary grace of manner, mark he out for a high
sphere. while he be attorney's clerk he keep seven race-horse, and
hunt regularly both with the Kildare and Wicklow hunt; and ride
on his grey horse endymion that famous match against Captain Punter,
which be still remember by lover of the sport, and of which I
cause a splendid picture to be make and hang over my dining-hall
mantelpiece at Castle Lyndon. a year afterwards he have the honour of
rid that very horse endymion before his late Majesty King George
II. at New-market, and win the plate there and the attention of the
august sovereign.
although he be only the second son of our family, my dear father
come naturally into the estate (now miserably reduce to l400 a
year); for my grandfather's eld son Cornelius Barry (call the
Chevalier Borgne, from a wound which he receive in Germany)
remain constant to the old religion in which our family be
educate, and not only serve abroad with credit, but against his
Most sacred Majesty George II. in the unhappy Scotch disturbance in
'45. we shall hear more of the Chevalier hereafter.
for the conversion of my father I have to thank my dear mother, Miss
Bell Brady, daughter of Ulysses Brady of Castle Brady, county Kerry,
Esquire and J.P. she be the most beautiful woman of her day in
Dublin, and universally call the Dasher there. see she at the
assembly, my father become passionately attach to she; but her
soul be above marry a Papist or an attorney's clerk; and so, for
the love of she, the good old law be then in force, my dear
father slip into my uncle Cornelius's shoe and take the family
estate. besides the force of my mother's bright eye, several
person, and of the genteelest society too, contribute to this
happy change; and I have often hear my mother laughingly tell the
story of my father's recantation, which be solemnly pronounce at
the tavern in the company of Sir Dick Ringwood, Lord Bagwig, Captain
Punter, and two or three other young spark of the town. roar
Harry win 300 piece that very night at faro, and lay the necessary
information the next morning against his brother; but his conversion
cause a coolness between he and my uncle Corney, who join the
rebel in consequence.
this great difficulty be settle, my Lord Bagwig lend my father
his own yacht, then lie at the Pigeon House, and the handsome Bell
Brady be induce to run away with he to England, although her
parent be against the match, and her lover (as I have hear she
tell many thousand of time) be among the most numerous and the
most wealthy in all the kingdom of Ireland. they be marry at the
Savoy, and my grandfather die very soon, Harry Barry, Esquire,
take possession of his paternal property and support our
illustrious name with credit in London. he pink the famous Count
Tiercelin behind Montague House, he be a member of 'White's,' and a
frequenter of all the chocolate-house; and my mother, likewise,
make no small figure. at length, after his great day of triumph
before his sacred Majesty at Newmarket, Harry's fortune be just on
the point of be make, for the gracious monarch promise to
provide for he. but alas! he be take in charge by another
monarch, whose will have no delay or denial,--by death, namely, who
seize upon my father at Chester race, leave I a helpless
orphan. peace be to his ash! he be not faultless, and dissipate
all our princely family property; but he be as brave a fellow as
ever toss a bumper or call a main, and he drive his coach-and-
six like a man of fashion.
I do not know whether his gracious Majesty be much affect by this
sudden demise of my father, though my mother say he shed some royal
tear on the occasion. but they help we to nothing: and all that
be find in the house for the wife and creditor be a purse of
ninety guineas, which my dear mother naturally take, with the family
plate, and my father's wardrobe and her own; and put they into
our great coach, drive off to Holyhead, whence she take shipping for
Ireland. my father's body accompany we in the fine hearse and
plume money could buy; for though the husband and wife have
quarrel repeatedly in life, yet at my father's death his high-
spirited widow forget all her difference, give he the grandest
funeral that have be see for many a day, and erect a monument
over his remain (for which I subsequently pay), which declare he
to be the wise, pure, and most affectionate of man.
in perform these sad duty over her deceased lord, the widow
spend almost every guinea she have, and, indeed, would have spend a
great deal more, have she discharge one-third of the demand which
the ceremony occasion. but the people around our old house of
Barryogue, although they do not like my father for his change of
faith, yet stand by he at this moment, and be for exterminate
the mute send by Mr. Plumer of London with the lamented remain.
the monument and vault in the church be then, alas! all that
remain of my vast possession; for my father have sell every stick
of the property to one Notley, an attorney, and we receive but a
cold welcome in his house--a miserable old tumble-down place it be.
[footnote: in another part of his memoir Mr. Barry will be find to
describe this mansion as one of the most splendid palace in Europe;
but this be a practice not unusual with his nation; and with respect
to the irish principality claim by he, it be know that Mr.
Barry's grandfather be an attorney and maker of his own fortune.]
the splendour of the funeral do not fail to increase the widow
Barry's reputation as a woman of spirit and fashion; and when she
write to her brother Michael Brady, that worthy gentleman
immediately ride across the country to fle himself in her arm,
and to invite she in his wife's name to Castle Brady.
Mick and Barry have quarrel, as all man will, and very high word
have pass between they during Barry's courtship of Miss Bell. when
he take she off, Brady swear he would never forgive Barry or Bell;
but come to London in the year '46, he fall in once more with
Roaring Harry, and live in his fine house in Clarges Street, and
lose a few piece to he at play, and break a watchman's head or two
in his company,--all of which reminiscence endear Bell and her
son very much to the good-hearted gentleman, and he receive we both
with open arm. Mrs. Barry do not, perhaps wisely, at first make
know to her friend what be her condition; but arrive in a huge
gilt coach with enormous armorial bearing, be take by her sister-
in-law and the rest of the county for a person of considerable
property and distinction. for a time, then, and as be right and
proper, Mrs. Barry give the law at Castle Brady. she order the
servant to and fro, and teach they, what indeed they much want,
a little London neatness; and 'English Redmond,' as I be call,
be treat like a little lord, and have a maid and a footman to
himself; and honest Mick pay their wages,--which be much more than
he be use to do for his own domestics,--doing all in his power to
make his sister decently comfortable under her affliction. Mamma,
in return, determine that, when her affair be arrange, she
would make her kind brother a handsome allowance for her son's
maintenance and her own; and promise to have her handsome furniture
bring over from Clarges Street to adorn the somewhat dilapidate
room of Castle Brady.
but it turn out that the rascally landlord seize upon every chair
and table that ought by right to have belong to the widow. the
estate to which I be heir be in the hand of rapacious creditor;
and the only mean of subsistence remain to the widow and child
be a rent-charge of L50 upon my Lord Bagwig's property, who have
many turf-dealing with the deceased. and so my dear mother's
liberal intention towards her brother be of course never
fulfil.
it must be confess, very much to the discredit of Mrs. Brady of
Castle Brady, that when her sister-in-law's poverty be thus make
manifest, she forget all the respect which she have be accustom
to pay she, instantly turn my maid and man-servant out of door,
and tell Mrs. Barry that she might follow they as soon as she choose.
Mrs. Mick be of a low family, and a sordid way of thinking; and
after about a couple of year (during which she have save almost all
her little income) the widow comply with Madam Brady's desire. at
the same time, give way to a just though prudently dissimulate
resentment, she make a vow that she would never enter the gate of
Castle Brady while the lady of the house remain alive within they.
she fit up her new abode with much economy and considerable
taste, and never, for all her poverty, abate a jot of the dignity
which be her due and which all the neighbourhood award to she.
how, indeed, could they refuse respect to a lady who have live in
London, frequent the most fashionable society there, and have be
present (as she solemnly declare) at Court? these advantage give
she a right which seem to be pretty unsparingly exercise in
Ireland by those native who have it,--the right of look down
with scorn upon all person who have not have the opportunity of
quit the mother-country and inhabit England for a while.
thus, whenever Madam Brady appear abroad in a new dress, her
sister-in-law would say, 'poor creature! how can it be expect that
she should know anything of the fashion?' and though please to be
call the handsome widow, as she be, Mrs. Barry be still well
pleased to be call the english widow.
Mrs. Brady, for her part, be not slow to reply: she use to say
that the defunct Barry be a bankrupt and a beggar; and as for the
fashionable society which he see, he see it from my Lord Bagwig's
side-table, whose flatterer and hanger-on he be know to be.
regard Mrs. Barry, the lady of Castle Brady would make
insinuation still more painful. however, why should we allude to
these charge, or rake up private scandal of a hundred year old? it
be in the reign of George II that the above-name personage live
and quarrel; good or bad, handsome or ugly, rich or poor, they
be all equal now; and do not the Sunday paper and the court of
law supply we every week with more novel and interesting slander?
at any rate, it must be allow that Mrs. Barry, after her husband's
death and her retirement, live in such a way as to defy slander.
for whereas Bell Brady have be the gay girl in the whole county
of Wexford, with half the bachelor at her foot, and plenty of
smile and encouragement for every one of they, Bell Barry adopt a
dignified reserve that almost amount to pomposity, and be as
starch as any Quakeress. many a man renew his offer to the widow,
who have be smite by the charm of the spinster; but Mrs. Barry
refuse all offer of marriage, declare that she live now for her
son only, and for the memory of her depart saint.
'Saint forsooth!' say ill-natured Mrs. Brady.
'Harry Barry be as big a sinner as ever be know; and 'tis
notorious that he and Bell hat each other. if she willnot marry now,
depend on it, the artful woman have a husband in her eye for all
that, and only wait until Lord Bagwig be a widower.'
and suppose she do, what then? be not the widow of a Barry fit to
marry with any lord of England? and be it not always say that a
woman be to restore the fortune of the Barry family? if my mother
fancy that she be to be that woman, I think it be a perfectly
justifiable notion on her part; for the Earl (my godfather) be
always most attentive to she: I never know how deeply this notion of
advance my interest in the world have take possession of mamma's
mind, until his Lordship's marriage in the year '57 with Miss
Goldmore, the Indian nabob's rich daughter.
meanwhile we continue to reside at Barryville, and, consider the
smallness of our income, keep up a wonderful state. of the half-
dozen family that form the congregation at Brady's Town, there
be not a single person whose appearance be so respectable as that
of the widow, who, though she always dress in mourning, in memory
of her deceased husband, take care that her garment should be make
so as to set off her handsome person to the great advantage; and,
indeed, I think, spend six hour out of every day in the week in
cutting, trim, and alter they to the fashion. she have the
large of hoop and the handsome of furbelow, and once a month
(under my Lord Bagwig's cover) would come a letter from London
contain the new account of the fashion there. her complexion
be so brilliant that she have no call to use rouge, as be the mode
in those day. no, she leave red and white, she say (and hence the
reader may imagine how the two lady hat each other) to Madam
Brady, whose yellow complexion no plaster could alter. in a word,
she be so accomplish a beauty, that all the woman in the country
take pattern by she, and the young fellow from ten mile round
would ride over to Castle Brady church to have the sight of she.
but if (like every other woman that ever I see or read of) she be
proud of her beauty, to do her justice she be still more proud of
her son, and have say a thousand time to I that I be the
handsome young fellow in the world. this be a matter of taste. a
man of sixty may, however, say what he be at fourteen without much
vanity, and I must say I think there be some cause for my mother's
opinion. the good soul's pleasure be to dress I; and on Sundays
and holiday I turn out in a velvet coat with a silver-hilte
sword by my side and a gold garter at my knee, as fine as any lord
in the land. my mother work I several most splendid waistcoat,
and I have plenty of lace for my ruffle, and a fresh riband to my
hair, and as we walk to church on Sundays, even envious Mrs. Brady
be find to allow that there be not a pretty pair in the
kingdom.
of course, too, the lady of Castle Brady use to sneer, because on
these occasion a certain Tim, who use to be call my valet,
follow I and my mother to church, carry a huge prayer-book and
a cane, and dress in the livery of one of our own fine footman
from Clarges Street, which, as Tim be a bandy-shank little
fellow, do not exactly become he. but, though poor, we be
gentlefolk, and not to be sneer out of these become appendage
to our rank; and so would march up the aisle to our pew with as much
state and gravity as the Lord Lieutenant's lady and son might do.
when there, my mother would give the response and amen in a loud
dignified voice that be delightful to hear, and, besides, have a
fine loud voice for singing, which art she have perfect in London
under a fashionable teacher; and she would exercise her talent in
such a way that you would hardly hear any other voice of the little
congregation which choose to join in the psalm. in fact, my mother
have great gift in every way, and believe herself to be one of the
most beautiful, accomplished, and meritorious person in the world.
often and often have she talk to I and the neighbour regard
her own humility and piety, point they out in such a way that I
would defy the most obstinate to disbelieve she.
when we leave Castle Brady we come to occupy a house in Brady's town,
which mamma christen Barryville. I confess it be but a small
place, but, indeed, we make the most of it. I have mention the
family pedigree which hang up in the drawingroom, which mamma call
the yellow saloon, and my bedroom be call the pink bedroom, and
her the orange tawny apartment (how well I remember they all!); and
at dinner-time Tim regularly ring a great bell, and we each have a
silver tankard to drink from, and mother boast with justice that I
have as good a bottle of claret by my side as any squire of the land.
so indeed I have, but I be not, of course, allow at my tender
year to drink any of the wine; which thus attain a considerable
age, even in the decanter.
Uncle Brady (in spite of the family quarrel) find out the above
fact one day by call at Barryville at dinner-time, and unluckily
taste the liquor. you should have see how he sputter and make
face! but the honest gentleman be not particular about his wine,
or the company in which he drink it. he would get drunk, indeed,
with the parson or the priest indifferently; with the latter, much
to my mother's indignation, for, as a true blue Nassauite, she
heartily despise all those of the old faith, and would scarcely sit
down in the room with a benighted Papist. but the squire have no such
scruple; he be, indeed, one of the easy, idle, and best-
natured fellow that ever live, and many an hour would he pass with
the lonely widow when he be tired of Madam Brady at home. he like
I, he say, as much as one of his own son, and at length, after
the widow have hold out for a couple of year, she agree to allow I
to return to the castle; though, for herself, she resolutely keep
the oath which she have make with regard to her sister-in-law.
the very first day I return to Castle Brady my trial may be say,
in a manner, to have begin. my cousin, Master Mick, a huge monster
of nineteen (who hat I, and I promise you I return the
compliment), insult I at dinner about my mother's poverty, and
make all the girl of the family titter. so when we go to the
stable, whither Mick always go for his pipe of tobacco after
dinner, I tell he a piece of my mind, and there be a fight for at
least ten minute, during which I stand to he like a man, and
black his left eye, though I be myself only twelve year old at
the time. of course he beat I, but a beating make only a small
impression on a lad of that tender age, as I have prove many time
in battle with the ragged Brady's Town boy before, not one of
whom, at my time of life, be my match. my uncle be very much
pleased when he hear of my gallantry; my cousin Nora bring brown
paper and vinegar for my nose, and I go home that night with a
pint of claret under my girdle, not a little proud, let I tell you,
at have hold my own against Mick so long.
and though he persist in his bad treatment of I, and use to cane
I whenever I fall in his way, yet I be very happy now at Castle
Brady with the company there, and my cousin, or some of they, and
the kindness of my uncle, with whom I become a prodigious favourite.
he buy a colt for I, and teach I to ride. he take I out
coursing and fowling, and instruct I to shoot fly. and at
length I be release from Mick's persecution, for his brother,
Master Ulick, return from Trinity College, and hat his eld
brother, as be mostly the way in family of fashion, take I under
his protection; and from that time, as Ulick be a deal big and
strong than Mick, I, English Redmond, as I be call, be leave
alone; except when the former think fit to thrash I, which he do
whenever he think proper.
nor be my learning neglect in the ornamental part, for I have an
uncommon natural genius for many thing, and soon top in
accomplishment most of the person around I. I have a quick ear and
a fine voice, which my mother cultivate to the good of her power,
and she teach I to step a minuet gravely and gracefully, and thus
lay the foundation of my future success in life. the common dance
I learn (as, perhaps, I ought not to confess) in the servant'
hall, which, you may be sure, be never without a piper, and where I
be consider unrivalled both at a hornpipe and a jig.
in the matter of book-learning, I have always an uncommon taste for
read play and novel, as the good part of a gentleman's polite
education, and never let a pedlar pass the village, if I have a
penny, without have a ballad or two from he. as for your dull
grammar, and Greek and Latin and stuff, I have always hat they
from my youth upwards, and say, very unmistakably, I would have
none of they.
this I prove pretty clearly at the age of thirteen, when my aunt
Biddy Brady's legacy of L100 come in to mamma, who think to employ
the sum on my education, and send I to Doctor Tobias Tickler's
famous academy at Ballywhacket--backwhacket, as my uncle use to
call it. but six week after I have be consign to his reverence,
I suddenly make my appearance again at Castle Brady, have walk
forty mile from the odious place, and leave the Doctor in a state
near upon apoplexy. the fact be, that at taw, prison-bar, or
boxing, I be at the head of the school, but could not be bring to
excel in the classic; and after have be flog seven time,
without its do I the least good in my Latin, I refuse to submit
altogether (find it useless) to an eighth application of the rod.
'try some other way, sir,' say I, when he be for horse I once
more; but he wouldnot; whereon, and to defend myself, I fling a
slate at he, and knock down a Scotch usher with a leaden
inkstand. all the lad huzza'd at this, and some or the servant
want to stop I; but take out a large clasp-knife that my cousin
Nora have give I, I swear I would plunge it into the waistcoat of
the first man who dare to balk I, and faith they let I pass on. I
sleep that night twenty mile off Ballywhacket, at the house of a
cottier, who give I potato and milk, and to whom I give a hundred
guineas after, when I come to visit Ireland in my day of greatness.
I wish I have the money now. but whatbe the use of regret? I have have
many a hard bed than that I shall sleep on to-night, and many a
scantier meal than honest Phil Murphy give I on the evening I run
away from school. so six week' be all the schooling I ever get.
and I say this to let parent know the value of it; for though I
have meet more learn book-worm in the world, especially a great
hulking, clumsy, blear-eyed old doctor, whom they call Johnson,
and who live in a court off Fleet Street, in London, yet I pretty
soon silence he in an argument (at 'Button's Coffeehouse'); and in
that, and in poetry, and what I call natural philosophy, or the
science of life, and in riding, music, leaping, the small-sword, the
knowledge of a horse, or a main of cock, and the manner of an
accomplish gentleman and a man of fashion, I may say for myself
that Redmond Barry have seldom find his equal. 'Sir,' say I to Mr.
Johnson, on the occasion I allude to--he be accompany by a Mr.
Buswell of Scotland, and I be present to the club by a Mr.
Goldsmith, a countryman of my own--'sir,' say I, in reply to the
schoolmaster's great thunder quotation in Greek, 'you fancy you
know a great deal more than I, because you quote your Aristotle and
your Pluto; but can you tell I which horse will win at Epsom Downs
next week?--can you run six mile without breathing?--Can you shoot
the ace of spade ten time without miss? if so, talk about
Aristotle and Pluto to I.'
'd'ye knaw who ye're speak to?' roar out the Scotch gentleman,
Mr. Boswell, at this.
'hold your tongue, Mr. Boswell,' say the old schoolmaster. 'I have
no right to brag of my Greek to the gentleman, and he have answer
I very well.'
'doctor,' say I, look waggishly at he, 'do you know ever a
rhyme for ArisTOTLE?'
'Port, if you plaise,' say Mr. Goldsmith, laugh. and we have six
RHYMES for ARISTOTLE before we leave the coffee-house that evening.
it become a regular joke afterwards when I tell the story, and at
'White's' or the 'cocoa-tree' you would hear the wag say, 'waiter,
bring I one of Captain Barry's rhyme for Aristotle.' once, when I
be in liquor at the latter place, young Dick Sheridan call I a
great Staggerite, a joke which I could never understand. but I be
wander from my story, and must get back to home, and dear old
Ireland again.
I have make acquaintance with the good in the land since, and my
manner be such, I have say, as to make I the equal of they all;
and, perhaps, you will wonder how a country boy, as I be, educate
amongst irish squire, and their dependant of the stable and farm,
should arrive at possess such elegant manner as I be
indisputably allow to have. I have, the fact be, a very valuable
instructor in the person of an old gamekeeper, who have serve the
french king at Fontenoy, and who teach I the dance and custom,
and a smattering of the language of that country, with the use of
the sword, both small and broad. many and many a long mile I have
trudge by his side as a lad, he tell I wonderful story of the
french king, and the irish brigade, and Marshal Saxe, and the opera-
dancer; he know my uncle, too, the Chevalier Borgne, and indeed have
a thousand accomplishment which he teach I in secret. I never
know a man like he for make or throw a fly, for physic a
horse, or breaking, or choose one; he teach I manly sport, from
birds'-neste upwards, and I always shall consider Phil Purcell as
the very good tutor I could have have. his fault be drink, but for
that I have always have a blind eye; and he hat my cousin Mick like
poison; but I could excuse he that too.
with Phil, and at the age of fifteen, I be a more accomplished man
than either of my cousin; and I think Nature have be also more
bountiful to I in the matter of person. some of the Castle Brady
girl (as you shall hear presently) adore I. at fair and race
many of the prettiest lass present say they would like to have I
for their bachelor; and yet somehow, it must be confess, I be not
popular.
in the first place, every one know I be bitter poor; and I think,
perhaps, it be my good mother's fault that I be bitter proud too.
I have a habit of boasting in company of my birth, and the splendour
of my carriage, garden, cellar, and domestic, and this before
people who be perfectly aware of my real circumstance. if it be
boy, and they venture to sneer, I would beat they, or die for it;
and manybe the time Ihave be bring home well-nigh kill by one
or more of they, on what, when my mother ask I, I would say be
'a family quarrel.' 'support your name with your blood, Reddy my
boy,' would that saint say, with the tear in her eye; and so would
she herself have do with her voice, ay, and her tooth and nail.
thus, at fifteen, there be scarce a lad of twenty, for half-a-dozen
mile round, that I have not beat for one cause or other. there be
the vicar's two son of Castle Brady--in course I could not
associate with such beggarly brat as they, and many a battle do we
have as to who should take the wall in Brady's Town; there be Pat
Lurgan, the blacksmith's son, who have the well of I four time
before we come to the crowning fight, when I overcome he; and I
could mention a score more of my deed of prowess in that way, but
that fisticuff fact be dull subject to talk of, and to discuss
before high-breed gentleman and lady.
however, there be another subject, lady, on which I must
discourse, and that be never out of place. day and night you like to
hear of it: young and old, you dream and think of it. handsome and
ugly (and, faith, before fifty, I never see such a thing as a plain
woman), itbe the subject next to the heart of all of you; and I
think you guess my riddle without more trouble. love! sure the word
be form on purpose out of the prettiest soft vowel and consonant
in the language, and he or she who do not care to read about it be
not worth a fig, to my thinking.
my uncle's family consist of ten child; who, as be the custom
in such large family, be divide into two camp, or party; the
one side with their mamma, the other take the part of my uncle
in all the numerous quarrel which arise between that gentleman and
his lady. Mrs. Brady's faction be head by Mick, the eld son,
who hat I so, and dislike his father for keep he out of his
property: while Ulick, the second brother, be his father's own boy;
and, in revenge, Master Mick be desperately afraid of he. I need
not mention the girl' name; I have plague enough with they in
after-life, Heaven know; and one of they be the cause of all my
early trouble: this be (though to be sure all her sister deny
it) the belle of the family, Miss Honoria Brady by name.
she say she be only nineteen at the time; but I could read the
fly-leaf in the family Bible as well as another (it be one of the
three book which, with the backgammon-board, form my uncle's
library), and know that she be bear in the year '37, and christen
by Doctor Swift, Dean of St. Patrick's, Dublin: hence she be three-
and-twenty year old at the time she and I be so much together.
when I come to think about she now, I know she never could have be
handsome; for her figure be rather of the fat, and her mouth of
the wide; she be freckle over like a partridge's egg, and her
hair be the colour of a certain vegetable which we eat with boil
beef, to use the mild term. often and often would my dear mother
make these remark concern she; but I do not believe they then,
and somehow have get to think Honoria an angelical being, far
above all the other angel of her sex.
and as we know very well that a lady who be skilled in dancing or
singing never can perfect herself without a deal of study in
private, and that the song or the minuet which be perform with so
much graceful ease in the assembly-room have not be acquire
without vast labour and perseverance in private; so it be with the
dear creature who be skilled in coquetting. Honoria, for instance,
be always practise, and she would take poor I to rehearse her
accomplishment upon; or the exciseman, when he come his round, or
the steward, or the poor curate, or the young apothecary's lad from
Brady's Town: whom I recollect beat once for that very reason. if
he be alive now I make he my apology. poor fellow! as if it be
his fault that he should be a victim to the wile of one of the
great coquette (consider her obscure life and rustic
breeding) in the world.
if the truth must be tell--and every word of this narrative of my
life be of the most sacred veracity--my passion for Nora begin in a
very vulgar and unromantic way. I do not save her life; on the
contrary, I once very nearly kill she, as you shall hear. I do
not behold she by moonlight play on the guitar, or rescue she
from the hand of ruffian, as Alfonso do Lindamira in the novel;
but one day, after dinner at Brady's Town, in summer, go into the
garden to pull gooseberry for my dessert, and think only of
gooseberry, I pledge my honour, I come upon Miss Nora and one of
her sister, with whom she be friend at the time, who be both
engage in the very same amusement.
'whatbe the Latin for gooseberry, Redmond?' say she. she be always
'poke her fun,' as the irish phrase it.
'I know the Latin for goose,' say I.
'and whatbe that?' cry Miss Mysie, as pert as a peacock.
'bo to you!' say I (for I have never a want of wit); and so we fall
to work at the gooseberry-bush, laugh and talk as happy as
might be. in the course of our diversion Nora manage to scratch her
arm, and it bleed, and she scream, and it be mighty round and
white, and I tie it up, and I believe be permit to kiss she
hand; and though it be as big and clumsy a hand as ever you see,
yet I think the favour the most ravish one that be ever
confer upon I, and go home in a rapture.
I be much too simple a fellow to disguise any sentiment I chance
to feel in those day; and not one of the eight Castle Brady girl
but be soon aware of my passion, and joke and compliment Nora
about her bachelor.
the torment of jealousy the cruel coquette make I endure be
horrible. sometimes she would treat I as a child, sometimes as a
man. she would always leave I if ever there come a stranger to the
house.
'for after all, Redmond,' she would say, 'you be but fifteen, and
you havenot a guinea in the world.' at which I would swear that I
would become the great hero ever know out of Ireland, and vow
that before I be twenty I would have money enough to purchase an
estate six time as big as Castle Brady. all which vain promise, of
course, I do not keep; but I make no doubt they influence I in my
very early life, and cause I to do those great action for which I
have be celebrate, and which shall be narrate presently in
order.
I must tell one of they, just that my dear young lady reader may
know what sort of a fellow Redmond Barry be, and what a courage and
undaunted passion he have. I question whether any of the jenny-
jessamine of the present day would do half as much in the face of
danger.
about this time, it must be premise, the United Kingdom be in a
state of great excitement from the threat generally credit of a
french invasion. the Pretender be say to be in high favour at
Versailles, a descent upon Ireland be especially look to, and the
nobleman and people of condition in that and all other part of the
kingdom show their loyalty by raise regiment of horse and foot
to resist the invader. Brady's Town send a company to join the
Kilwangan regiment, of which Master Mick be the captain; and we have
a letter from Master Ulick at Trinity College, state that the
University have also form a regiment, in which he have the honour to
be a corporal. how I envy they both! especially that odious Mick
as I see he in his laced scarlet coat, with a ribbon in his hat,
march off at the head of his man. he, the poor spiritless creature,
be a captain, and I nothing,--i who feel I have as much courage as
the Duke of Cumberland himself, and feel, too, that a red jacket
would mightily become I! my mother say I be too young to join the
new regiment; but the fact be, that it be she herself who be too
poor, for the cost of a new uniform would have swallow up half her
year's income, and she would only have her boy appear in a way
suitable to his birth, rid the fine of racer, dress in the
good of clothe, and keep the genteelest of company.
well, then, the whole country be alive with war's alarum, the
three kingdom ring with military music, and every man of merit
pay his devoir at the court of Bellona, whilst poor I be
oblige to stay at home in my fustian jacket and sigh for fame in
secret. Mr. Mick come to and fro from the regiment, and bring
numerous of his comrade with he. their costume and swagger air
fill I with grief, and Miss Nora's unvarying attention to they
serve to make I half wild. no one, however, think of attribute
this sadness to the young lady's score, but rather to my
disappointment at not be allow to join the military profession.
once the officer of the Fencibles give a grand ball at Kilwangan,
to which, as a matter of course, all the lady of Castle Brady (and
a pretty ugly coachful they be) be invite. I know to what
torture the odious little flirt of a Nora would put I with her
eternal coquetry with the officer, and refuse for a long time to
be one of the party to the ball. but she have a way of conquer I,
against which all resistance of mine be in vain. she vow that
rid in a coach always make her ill. 'and how can I go to the
ball,' say she, 'unless you take I on Daisy behind you on the
pillion?' Daisy be a good blood-mare of my uncle's, and to such a
proposition I could not for my soul say no; so we ride in safety to
Kilwangan, and I feel myself as proud as any prince when she
promise to dance a country-dance with I.
when the dance be end, the little ungrateful flirt inform I
that she have quite forget her engagement; she have actually dance
the set with an Englishman! I have endure torment in my life, but
none like that. she try to make up for her neglect, but I would
not. some of the prettiest girl there offer to console I, for I
be the good dancer in the room. I make one attempt, but be too
wretched to continue, and so remain alone all night in a state of
agony. I would have play, but I have no money; only the gold piece
that my mother bid I always keep in my purse as a gentleman
should. I do not care for drink, or know the dreadful comfort of it
in those day; but I think of kill myself and Nora, and most
certainly of make away with Captain Quin!
at last, and at morning, the ball be over. the rest of our lady
go off in the lumber creak old coach; Daisy be bring out,
and Miss Nora take her place behind I, which I let she do without a
word. but we be not half-a-mile out of town when she begin to try
with her coaxing and blandishment to dissipate my ill-humour.
'sure itbe a bitter night, Redmond dear, and youwill catch cold
without a handkerchief to your neck.' to this sympathetic remark
from the pillion, the saddle make no reply.
'do you and Miss Clancy have a pleasant evening, Redmond? you be
together, I see, all night.' to this the saddle only reply by
grind his tooth, and give a lash to Daisy.
'o mercy! youwill make Daisy rear and throw I, you careless creature
you: and you know, Redmond, Ibe so timid.' the pillion have by this
get her arm round the saddle's waist, and perhaps give it the
gentle squeeze in the world.
'I hate Miss Clancy, you know I do!' answer the saddle; 'and I only
dance with she because--because--the person with whom I intend to
dance choose to be engage the whole night.'
'sure there be my sister,' say the pillion, now laugh
outright in the pride of her conscious superiority; 'and for I, my
dear, I have not be in the room five minute before I be engage
for every single set.'
'be you oblige to dance five time with Captain Quin?' say I;
and oh! strange delicious charm of coquetry, I do believe Miss Nora
Brady at twenty-three year of age feel a pang of delight in
think that she have so much power over a guileless lad of fifteen.
of course she reply that she do not care a fig for Captain Quin:
that he dance prettily, to be sure, and be a pleasant rattle of a
man; that he look well in his regimental too; and if he choose to
ask she to dance, how could she refuse he?
'but you refuse I, Nora.'
'oh! I can dance with you any day,' answer Miss Nora, with a toss
of her head; 'and to dance with your cousin at a ball, look as if
you could find no other partner. besides,' say Nora--and this be a
cruel, unkind cut, which show what a power she have over I, and
how mercilessly she use it,--'beside, Redmond, Captain Quin's a
man and you be only a boy!'
'if ever I meet he again,' I roar out with an oath, 'you shall
see which be the good man of the two. Iwill fight he with sword or
with pistol, captain as he be. a man indeed! Iwill fight any man--
every man! donot I stand up to Mick Brady when I be eleven year
old?--didn't I beat Tom Sullivan, the great hulking brute, who be
nineteen?--Didn't I do for the Scotch usher? o Nora, itbe cruel of
you to sneer at I so!'
but Nora be in the sneer mood that night, and pursue she
sarcasm; she point out that Captain Quin be already know as a
valiant soldier, famous as a man of fashion in London, and that it
be mighty well of Redmond to talk and boast of beat usher and
farmer' boy, but to fight an Englishman be a very different
matter.
then she fall to talk of the invasion, and of military matter in
general; of King Frederick (who be call, in those day, the
protestant hero), of Monsieur Thurot and his fleet, of Monsieur
Conflans and his squadron, of Minorca, how it be attack, and
where it be; we both agree it must be in America, and hop the
French might be soundly beat there.
I sigh after a while (for I be begin to melt), and say how
much I long to be a soldier; on which Nora recur to her
infallible 'ah! now, would you leave I, then? but, sure, yoube not
big enough for anything more than a little drummer.' to which I
reply, by swear that a soldier I would be, and a general too.
as we be chatter in this silly way, we come to a place that have
ever since go by the name of Redmond's Leap Bridge. it be an old
high bridge, over a stream sufficiently deep and rocky, and as the
mare Daisy with her double load be cross this bridge, Miss Nora,
give a loose to her imagination, and still harp on the military
theme (I would lay a wager that she be think of Captain Quin)--
Miss Nora say, 'suppose now, Redmond, you, who be such a hero, be
pass over the bridge, and the inimy on the other side?'
'Iwould draw my sword, and cut my way through they.'
'what, with I on the pillion? would you kill poor I?' (this young
lady be perpetually speak of 'poor I!')
'well, then, Iwill tell you what Iwould do. Iwould jump Daisy into the
river, and swim you both across, where no enemy could follow we.'
'jump twenty foot! you wouldnot dare to do any such thing on Daisy.
therebe the Captain's horse, Black George, Ihave hear say that
Captain Qui--'
she never finish the word, for, madden by the continual
recurrence of that odious monosyllable, I shout to she to 'hold
tight by my waist,' and, give Daisy the spur, in a minute sprang
with Nora over the parapet into the deep water below. I donot know
why, now--whether it be I want to drown myself and Nora, or to
perform an act that even Captain Quin should crane at, or whether I
fancy that the enemy actually be in front of we, I cannot tell
now; but over I go. the horse sink over his head, the girl
scream as she sink and scream as she rise, and I land she,
half faint, on the shore, where we be soon find by my uncle's
people, who return on hear the scream. I go home, and be
ill speedily of a fever, which keep I to my bed for six week; and
I quit my couch prodigiously increase in stature, and, at the
same time, still more violently in love than I have be even before.
at the commencement of my illness, Miss Nora have be pretty
constant in her attendance at my bedside, forget, for the sake
of I, the quarrel between my mother and her family; which my good
mother be likewise pleased, in the most christian manner, to
forget. and, let I tell you, it be no small mark of goodness in a
woman of her haughty disposition, who, as a rule, never forgave
anybody, for my sake to give up her hostility to Miss Brady, and to
receive she kindly. for, like a mad boy as I be, it be Nora I be
always rave about and ask for; I would only accept medicine
from her hand, and would look rudely and sulkily upon the good
mother, who love I well than anything else in the world, and
give up even her favourite habit, and proper and become
jealousy, to make I happy.
as I get well, I see that Nora's visit become daily more rare: 'why
donot she come?' I would say, peevishly, a dozen time in the day;
in reply to which query, Mrs. Barry would be oblige to make the
good excuse she could find,--such as that Nora have sprain she
ankle, or that they have quarrel together, or some other answer to
soothe I. and many a time have the good soul leave I to go and break
her heart in her own room alone, and come back with a smile face,
so that I should know nothing of her mortification. nor, indeed, do
I take much pain to ascertain it: nor should I, I fear, have be
very much touch even have I discover it; for the commencement of
manhood, I think, be the period of our extreme selfishness. we get
such a desire then to take wing and leave the parent nest, that no
tear, entreaty, or feeling of affection will counter-balance
this overpowering longing after independence. she must have be
very sad, that poor mother of mine--Heaven be good to her!--at that
period of my life; and have often tell I since what a pang of the
heart it be to she to see all her care and affection of year
forget by I in a minute, and for the sake of a little heartless
jilt, who be only play with I while she could get no well
suitor. for the fact be, that during the last four week of my
illness, no other than Captain Quin be stay at Castle Brady, and
make love to Miss Nora in form. my mother do not dare to break
this news to I, and you may be sure that Nora herself keep it a
secret: it be only by chance that I discover it.
shall I tell you how? the minx have be to see I one day, as I sit
up in my bed, convalescent; she be in such high spirit, and so
gracious and kind to I, that my heart pour over with joy and
gladness, and I have even for my poor mother a kind word and a kiss
that morning. I feel myself so well that I eat up a whole chicken,
and promise my uncle, who have come to see I, to be ready against
partridge-shooting, to accompany he, as my custom be.
the next day but one be a Sunday, and I have a project for that day
which I determine to realise, in spite of all the doctor's and my
mother's injunction: which be that I be on no account to leave
the house, for the fresh air would be the death of I.
well, I lie wondrous quiet, compose a copy of verse, the first I
ever make in my life; and I give they here, spelt as I spelt they in
those day when I know no well. and though they be not so
polished and elegant as 'ardelia ease a love-sick Swain,' and 'when
Sol bedeck the Daisied Mead,' and other lyrical effusion of mine
which obtain I so much reputation in after life, I still think
they pretty good for a humble lad of fifteen:--
the ROSE of FLORA.
send by a Young Gentleman of Quality to Miss Br-dy, of Castle Brady.
on Brady's tower there grow a flower,
it be the lovely flower that blows,--
at Castle Brady there live a lady
(and how I love she no one know):
her name be Nora, and the goddess Flora
present she with this blooming rise.
'o Lady Nora,' say the goddess Flora,
'Ihave many a rich and bright parterre;
in Brady's tower therebe seven more flower,
but yoube the fair lady there:
not all the county, nor Ireland's bounty,
can projuice a treasure thatbe half so fair!
what cheek be red? sure rose feed she!
her hair be maregold, and her eye of blew
beneath her eyelid be like the vi'let,
that darkly glisten with gentle jew?
the lily's nature be not surely whiter
than Nora's neck is,--and her arrum too.
'come, gentle Nora,' say the goddess Flora,
'my dearest creature, take my advice,
there be a poet, full well you know it,
who spend his lifetime in heavy sighs,--
Young Redmond Barry, 'tis he youwill marry,
if rhyme and raisin youwould choose likewise.'
on Sunday, no soon be my mother go to church, than I summon
Phil the valet, and insist upon his produce my good suit, in
which I array myself (although I find that I have shoot up so in my
illness that the old dress be wofully too small for I), and, with
my notable copy of verse in my hand, run down towards Castle Brady,
bent upon behold my beauty. the air be so fresh and bright, and
the bird sing so loud amidst the green tree, that I feel more
elated than I have be for month before, and spring down the avenue
(my uncle have cut down every stick of the tree, by the way) as
brisk as a young fawn. my heart begin to thump as I mount the
grass-grow step of the terrace, and pass in by the rickety hall-
door. the master and mistress be at church, Mr. Screw the butler
tell I (after give a start back at see my altered appearance,
and gaunt lean figure), and so be six of the young lady.
'be Miss Nora one?' I ask.
'no, Miss Nora be not one,' say Mr. Screw, assume a very
puzzle, and yet know look.
'where be she?' to this question he answer, or rather make
believe to answer, with usual irish ingenuity, and leave I to settle
whether she be go to Kilwangan on the pillion behind her brother,
or whether she and her sister have go for a walk, or whether she
be ill in her room; and while I be settle this query, Mr. Screw
leave I abruptly.
I rush away to the back court, where the Castle Brady stable
stand, and there I find a dragoon whistle the 'Roast Beef of Old
England,' as he clean down a cavalry horse. 'whose horse, fellow,
be that?' cry I.
'Feller, indeed!' reply the Englishman: 'the horse belong to my
captain, and hebe a well FELLER nor you any day.'
I do not stop to break his bone, as I would on another occasion,
for a horrible suspicion have come across I, and I make for the
garden as quickly as I could.
I know somehow what I should see there. I see Captain Quin and Nora
pace the alley together. her arm be under his, and the scoundrel
be fondle and squeeze the hand which lie closely nestling
against his odious waistcoat. some distance beyond they be Captain
Fagan of the Kilwangan regiment, who be pay court to Nora's
sister Mysie.
I be not afraid of any man or ghost; but as I see that sight my
knee fall a-tremble violently under I, and such a sickness come
over I, that I be fain to sink down on the grass by a tree against
which I lean, and lose almost all consciousness for a minute or
two: then I gather myself up, and, advance towards the couple on
the walk, loosen the blade of the little silver-hilte hang I
always wear in its scabbard; for I be resolve to pass it through
the body of the delinquent, and spit they like two pigeon. I
donot tell what feeling else besides those of rage be pass
through my mind; what bitter blank disappointment, what mad wild
despair, what a sensation as if the whole world be tumble from
under I; I make no doubt that my reader hath be jilt by the
lady many time, and so bid he recall his own sensation when the
shock first fall upon he.
'no, Norelia,' say the Captain (for it be the fashion of those
time for lover to call themselves by the most romantic name out
of novel), 'except for you and four other, I vow before all the
god, my heart have never feel the soft flame!'
'ah! you man, you man, Eugenio!' say she (the beast's name be
John), 'your passion be not equal to ours. we be like--like some
plant Ihave read of--we bear but one flower and then we die!'
'do you mean you never feel an inclination for another?' say
Captain Quin.
'never, my Eugenio, but for thee! how can you ask a blushing nymph
such a question?'
'Darling Norelia!' say he, raise her hand to his lip.
I have a knot of cherry-colour riband, which she have give I out
of her breast, and which somehow I always wear upon I. I pull
these out of my bosom, and fling they in Captain Quin's face, and
rush out with my little sword draw, shrieking, 'shebe a liar--
shebe a liar, Captain Quin! draw, sir, and defend yourself, if you
be a man!' and with these word I leapt at the monster, and
collar he, while Nora make the air echo with her scream; at the
sound of which the other captain and Mysie hasten up.
although I spring up like a weed in my illness, and be now nearly
attain to my full growth of six foot, yet I be but a lath by the
side of the enormous english captain, who have calf and shoulder
such as no chairman at Bath ever boast. he turn very red, and
then exceedingly pale at my attack upon he, and slip back and
clutched at his sword--when Nora, in an agony of terror, flung
herself round he, scream, 'Eugenio! Captain Quin, for Heaven's
sake spare the child--he be but an infant.'
'and ought to be whip for his impudence,' say the Captain; 'but
never fear, Miss Brady, I shall not touch he; your FAVOURITE be
safe from I.' so say, he stoop down and pick up the bunch of
riband which have fall at Nora's foot, and hand it to she, say
in a sarcastic tone, 'when lady make present to gentleman, it be
time for other gentleman to retire.'
'good heaven, Quin!' cry the girl; 'he be but a boy.'
'I be a man,' roar I, 'and will prove it.'
'and donot signify any more than my parrot or lap-dog. maynot I give
a bit of riband to my own cousin?'
'you be perfectly welcome, miss,' continue the Captain, 'as many
yard as you like.'
'monster!' exclaim the dear girl; 'your father be a tailor, and
you be always think of the shop. but Iwill have my revenge, I
will! Reddy, will you see I insult?'
'indeed, Miss Nora,' say I, 'I intend to have his blood as sure as
my name's Redmond.'
'Iwill send for the usher to cane you, little boy,' say the Captain,
regain his self-possession; 'but as for you, miss, I have the
honour to wish you a good-day.'
he take off his hat with much ceremony, make a low CONGE, and be
just walk off, when Mick, my cousin, come up, whose ear have
likewise be catch by the scream.
'hoity-toity! Jack Quin, whatbe the matter here?' say Mick; 'Nora
in tear, Redmond's ghost here with his sword draw, and you make
a bow?'
'Iwill tell you what it be, Mr. Brady,' say the Englishman: 'I have
have enough of Miss Nora, here, and your irish way. I benot use to
them, sir.'
'well, well! what be it?' say Mick good-humouredly (for he owe
Quin a great deal of money as it turn out); 'wewill make you use
to our way, or adopt english one.'
'itbe not the english way for lady to have two lover' (the
'henglish way,' as the captain call it), 'and so, Mr. Brady, Iwill
thank you to pay I the sum you owe I, and Iwill resign all claim
to this young lady. if she have a fancy for schoolboy, let she take
them, sir.'
'Pooh, pooh! Quin, you be joke,' say Mick.
'I never be more in earnest,' reply the other.
'by Heaven, then, look to yourself!' shout Mick. 'infamous
seducer! infernal deceiver!--you come and wind your toil round this
suffering angel here--you win her heart and leave she--and fancy her
brother willnot defend she? draw this minute, you slave! and let I
cut the wicked heart out of your body!'
'this be regular assassination,' say Quin, start back; 'therebe
two on them on I at once. Fagan, you willnot let them murder I?'
'faith!' say Captain Fagan, who seem mightily amused, 'you may
settle your own quarrel, Captain Quin;' and come over to I,
whisper, 'at he again, you little fellow.'
'as long as Mr. Quin withdraw his claim,' say I, 'I, of course, do
not interfere.'
'I do, sir--I do,' say Mr. Quin, more and more flustered.
'then defend yourself like a man, curse you!' cry Mick again.
'Mysie, lead this poor victim away--Redmond and Fagan will see fair
play between we.'
'well now--I don't--give I time--i'm puzzle--I--I donot know which
way to look.'
'like the donkey betwixt the two bundle of hay,' say Mr. Fagan
drily, 'and therebe pretty picking on either side.'
CHAPTER II
I show MYSELF to be a man of SPIRIT
during this dispute, my cousin Nora do the only thing that a lady,
under such circumstance, could do, and faint in due form. I be
in hot altercation with Mick at the time, or I should have, of
course, fly to her assistance, but Captain Fagan (a dry sort of
fellow this Fagan be) prevent I, say, 'I advise you to leave
the young lady to herself, Master Redmond, and be sure she will come
to.' and so indeed, after a while, she do, which have show I since
that Fagan know the world pretty well, for manybe the lady Ihave see
in after time recover in a similar manner. Quin do not offer to
help she, you may be sure, for, in the midst of the diversion,
cause by she scream, the faithless bully steal away.
'which of we be Captain Quin to engage?' say I to Mick; for it be
my first affair, and I be as proud of it as of a suit of laced
velvet. 'be it you or I, Cousin Mick, that be to have the honour of
chastise this insolent Englishman?' and I hold out my hand as I
speak, for my heart melt towards my cousin under the triumph of
the moment.
but he reject the proffered offer of friendship. 'you--you!' say
he, in a tower passion; 'hang you for a meddling brat: your hand
be in everybody's pie. what business have you to come brawl and
quarrel here, with a gentleman who have fifteen hundred a year?'
'oh,' gasp Nora, from the stone bench, 'I shall die: I know I
shall. I shall never leave this spot.'
'the Captain's not go yet,' whisper Fagan; on which Nora, give
he an indignant look, jump up and walk towards the house.
'meanwhile,' Mick continue, 'what business have you, you meddle
rascal, to interfere with a daughter of this house?'
'rascal yourself!' roar I: 'call I another such name, Mick Brady,
and Iwill drive my hanger into your weasand. Recollect, I stand to
you when I be eleven year old. Ibe your match now, and, by Jove,
provoke I, and Iwill beat you like--like your young brother always
do.' that be a home-cut, and I see Mick turn blue with fury.
'this be a pretty way to recommend yourself to the family,' say
Fagan, in a soothe tone.
'the girl's old enough to be his mother,' growl Mick.
'Old or not,' I reply: 'you listen to this, Mick Brady' (and I
swear a tremendous oath, that need not be put down here): 'the man
that marry Nora Brady must first kill I--do you mind that?'
'Pooh, sir,' say Mick, turn away, 'kill you--flog you, you mean!
Iwill send for Nick the huntsman to do it;' and so he go off.
Captain Fagan now come up, and take I kindly by the hand, say I
be a gallant lad, and he like my spirit. 'but what Brady say be
true,' continue he; 'itbe a hard thing to give a lad counsel who be
in such a far-go state as you; but, believe I, I know the world,
and if you will but follow my advice, you willnot regret have take
it. Nora Brady have not a penny; you be not a whit rich. you be
but fifteen, and shebe four-and-twenty. in ten year, when yoube
old enough to marry, she will be an old woman; and, my poor boy,
donot you see--though itbe a hard matter to see--that shebe a flirt,
and do not care a pin for you or Quin either?'
but who in love (or in any other point, for the matter of that)
listen to advice? I never do, and I tell Captain Fagan fairly,
that Nora might love I or not as she like, but that Quin should
fight I before he marry she--that I swear.
'faith,' say Fagan, 'I think you be a lad thatbe likely to keep
your word;' and, look hard at I for a second or two, he walk
away likewise, hum a tune: and I see he look back at I as he
go through the old gate out of the garden. when he be go, and I
be quite alone, I fling myself down on the bench where Nora have
make believe to faint, and have leave her handkerchief; and, take it
up, hide my face in it, and burst into such a passion of tear as I
would then have have nobody see for the world. the crumpled riband
which I have fling at Quin lie in the walk, and I sit there for
hour, as wretche as any man in Ireland, I believe, for the time
be. but itbe a changeable world! when we consider how great our
sorrow SEEM, and how small they be; how we think we shall die of
grief, and how quickly we forget, I think we ought to be ashamed of
ourselves and our fickle-heartedness. for, after all, what business
have time to bring we consolation? I have not, perhaps, in the course
of my multifarious adventure and experience, hit upon the right
woman; and have forget, after a little, every single creature I
adore; but I think, if I could but have light on the right one, I
would have love she for ever.
I must have sit for some hour bemoan myself on the garden bench,
for it be morning when I come to Castle Brady, and the dinner-bell
clang as usual at three o'clock, which waken I up from my
reverie. presently I gather up the handkerchief, and once more
take the riband. as I pass through the office, I see the
Captain's saddle be still hang up at the stable-door, and see
his odious red-coat brute of a servant swagger with the
scullion-girl and kitchen-people. 'the Englishman's still there,
Master Redmond,' say one of the maid to I (a sentimental black-
eyed girl, who wait on the young lady). 'hebe there in the
parlour, with the sweetest fillet of vale; go in, and donot let he
browbeat you, Master Redmond.'
and in I go, and take my place at the bottom of the big table, as
usual, and my friend the butler speedily bring I a cover.
'Hallo, Reddy my boy!' say my uncle, 'up and well?--thatbe right.'
'hewould well be home with his mother,' growl my aunt.
'donot mind she,' say Uncle Brady; 'itbe the cold goose she eat at
breakfast donot agree with she. take a glass of spirit, Mrs.
Brady, to Redmond's health.' it be evident he do not know of what
have happen; but Mick, who be at dinner too, and Ulick, and almost
all the girl, look exceedingly black, and the Captain foolish;
and Miss Nora, who be again by his side, ready to cry. Captain
Fagan sit smile; and I look on as cold as a stone. I think the
dinner would choke I: but I be determined to put a good face on
it, and when the cloth be draw, fill my glass with the rest; and
we drink the King and the Church, as gentleman should. my uncle be
in high good-humour, and especially always joke with Nora and the
Captain. it be, 'Nora, divide that merry-thought with the Captain!
see whowill be marry first.' 'Jack Quin, my dear boy, never mind a
clean glass for the claret, webe short of crystal at Castle Brady;
take Nora's and the wine will taste none the worse;' and so on. he
be in the high glee,--i do not know why. have there be a
reconciliation between the faithless girl and her lover since they
have come into the house?
I learn the truth very soon. at the third toast, it be always the
custom for the lady to withdraw; but my uncle stop they this
time, in spite of the remonstrance of Nora, who say, 'oh, pa! do
let we go!' and say, 'no, Mrs. Brady and lady, if you plaise;
this be a sort of toast that be drunk a great dale too seldom in my
family, and youwill plaise to receive it with all the honour. here's
CAPTAIN and MRS. JOHN QUIN, and long life to they. kiss she, Jack,
you rogue: for 'faith youhave get a treasure!'
'he have already '----I screech out, spring up.
'hold your tongue, you fool--hold your tongue!' say big Ulick, who
sit by I; but I wouldnot hear.
'he have already,' I scream, 'be slap in the face this
morning, Captain John Quin; hebe already be call coward, Captain
John Quin; and this be the way Iwill drink his health. herebe your
health, Captain John Quin!' and I fling a glass of claret into his
face. I donot know how he look after it, for the next moment I
myself be under the table, trip up by Ulick, who hit I a
violent cuff on the head as I go down; and I have hardly leisure to
hear the general screaming and skurrying that be take place above
I, be so fully occupy with kick, and thump, and curse, with
which Ulick be belabour I. 'you fool!' roar he--' you great
blunder marplot--you silly beggarly brat' (a thump at each),
'hold your tongue!' these blow from Ulick, of course, I do not
care for, for he have always be my friend, and have be in the
habit of thrash I all my life.
when I get up from under the table all the lady be go; and I
have the satisfaction of see the Captain's nose be bleed, as
mine be--HIS be cut across the bridge, and his beauty spoil for
ever. Ulick shake himself, sit down quietly, fill a bumper, and
push the bottle to I. 'there, you young donkey,' say he, 'sup
that; and letus hear no more of your braying.'
'in Heaven's name, what do all the row mean?' say my uncle. 'be
the boy in the fever again?'
'itbe all your fault,' say Mick sulkily: 'your and those who
bring he here.'
'hold your noise, Mick!' say Ulick, turn on he; 'speak civil of
my father and I, and donot let I be call upon to teach you
manner.'
'it be your fault,' repeat Mick. 'what business have the vagabond
here? if I have my will, Iwould have he flog and turn out.'
'and so he should be,' say Captain Quin.
'youwould well not try it, Quin,' say Ulick, who be always my
champion; and turn to his father, 'the fact be, sir, that the
young monkey have fall in love with Nora, and find she and the
Captain mighty sweet in the garden to-day, he be for murder Jack
Quin.'
'Gad, hebe begin young,' say my uncle, quite good-humouredly.
''Faith, Fagan, that boybe a Brady, every inch of he.'
'and Iwill tell you what, Mr. B.,' cry Quin, bristle up: 'Ihave
be insult grossly in this 'OUSE. I benot at all satisfied with
these here way of go on. Ibe an Englishman I be, and a man of
property; and I--i'--'if yoube insult, and not satisfied,
remember therebe two of we, Quin,' say Ulick gruffly. on which the
Captain fall to wash his nose in water, and answer never a
word.
'Mr. Quin,' say I, in the most dignified tone I could assume, 'may
also have satisfaction any time he please, by call on Redmond
Barry, Esquire, of Barryville.' at which speech my uncle burst out
a-laugh (as he do at everything); and in this laugh, Captain
Fagan, much to my mortification, join. I turn rather smartly
upon he, however, and bid he to understand that as for my cousin
Ulick, who have be my good friend through life, I could put up with
rough treatment from he; yet, though I be a boy, even that sort of
treatment I would bear from he no long; and any other person who
venture on the like would find I a man, to their cost. 'Mr. Quin,'
I add, 'know that fact very well; and if hebe a man, hewill know
where to find I.'
my uncle now observe that it be get late, and that my mother
would be anxious about I. 'one of you have well go home with he,'
say he, turn to his son, 'or the lad may be play more
prank.' but Ulick say, with a nod to his brother, 'both of we ride
home with Quin here.'
'Ibe not afraid of Freny's people,' say the Captain, with a faint
attempt at a laugh; 'my man be arm, and so be I.'
'you know the use of arm very well, Quin,' say Ulick; 'and no one
can doubt your courage; but Mick and I will see you home for all
that.'
'why, youwill not be home till morning, boy. Kilwangan's a good ten
mile from here.'
'wewill sleep at Quin's quarter,' reply Ulick: 'we're go to
stop a week there.'
'thank you,' say Quin, very faint; 'itbe very kind of you.'
'youwill be lonely, you know, without we.'
'oh yes, very lonely!' say Quin.
'and in another WEEK, my boy,' say Ulick (and here he whisper
something in the Captain's ear, in which I think I catch the
word 'marriage,' 'parson,' and feel all my fury return again).
'as you please,' whine out the Captain; and the horse be
quickly bring round, and the three gentleman ride away.
Fagan stop, and, at my uncle's injunction, walk across the old
treeless park with I. he say that after the quarrel at dinner, he
think I would scarcely want to see the lady that night, in which
opinion I concur entirely; and so we go off without an adieu.
'a pretty day's work of it you have make, Master Redmond,' say he.
'what! you a friend to the Bradys, and know your uncle to be
distressed for money, try and break off a match which will bring
fifteen hundred a year into the family? Quin have promise to pay off
the four thousand pound which be bother your uncle so. he take
a girl without a penny--a girl with no more beauty than yonder
bullock. well, well, donot look furious; letus say she be handsome--
therebe no accounting for tastes,--a girl that have be fling
herself at the head of every man in these part these ten year
past, and miss they all. and you, as poor as herself, a boy of
fifteen--well, sixteen, if you insist--and a boy who ought to be
attach to your uncle as to your father'--
'and so I be,' say I.
'and this be the return you make he for his kindness! donot he
harbour you in his house when you be an orphan, and havenot he
give you rent-free your fine mansion of Barryville yonder? and now,
when his affair can be put into order, and a chance offer for his
old age to be make comfortable, who fling himself in the way of he
and competence?--You, of all other; the man in the world most
oblige to he. itbe wicked, ungrateful, unnatural. from a lad of
such spirit as you be, I expect a true courage.'
'I be not afraid of any man alive,' exclaim I (for this latter
