part of the Captain's argument have rather stagger I, and I
wish, of course, to turn it--as one always should when the enemy's
too strong); 'and itbe _I_ be the injure man, Captain Fagan. no man
be ever, since the world begin, treat so. look here--look at this
riband. Ihave wear it in my heart for six month. Ihave have it there
all the time of the fever. donot Nora take it out of her own bosom
and give it I? donot she kiss I when she give it I, and call I
she darling Redmond?'
'she be practise,' reply Mr. Fagan, with a sneer. 'I know
woman, sir. give they time, and let nobody else come to the house,
and theywill fall in love with a chimney-sweep. there be a young
lady in Fermoy'--
'a young lady in flame,' roar I (but I use a still hotter word).
'mark this; come what will of it, I swear Iwill fight the man who
pretend to the hand of Nora Brady. Iwill follow he, if itbe into
the church, and meet he there. Iwill have his blood, or he shall
have mine; and this riband shall be find dye in it. yes, and if I
kill he, Iwill pin it on his breast, and then she may go and take
back her token.' this I say because I be very much excited at the
time, and because I have not read novel and romantic play for
nothing.
'well,' say Fagan after a pause, 'if it must be, it must. for a
young fellow, you be the most blood-thirsty I ever see. Quin's a
determined fellow, too.'
'will you take my message to he?' say I, quite eagerly.
'hush!' say Fagan: 'your mother may be on the look-out. here we
be, close to Barryville.'
'mind! not a word to my mother,' I say; and go into the house
swell with pride and exultation to think that I should have a
chance against the Englishman I hat so.
Tim, my servant, have come up from Barryville on my mother's return
from church; for the good lady be rather alarmed at my absence, and
anxious for my return. but he have see I go in to dinner, at the
invitation of the sentimental lady's-maid; and when he have have his
own share of the good thing in the kitchen, which be always well
furnish than our at home, have walk back again to inform his
mistress where I be, and, no doubt, to tell she, in his own
fashion, of all the event that have happen at Castle Brady. in
spite of my precaution to secrecy, then, I half suspect that my
mother know all, from the manner in which she embrace I on my
arrival, and receive our guest, Captain Fagan. the poor soul look
a little anxious and flushed, and every now and then gaze very hard
in the Captain's face; but she say not a word about the quarrel,
for she have a noble spirit, and would as lief have see anyone of
she kindre hang as shirk from the field of honour. what have
become of those gallant feeling nowadays? sixty year ago a man be
a MAN, in old Ireland, and the sword that be wear by his side be
at the service of any gentleman's gizzard, upon the slight
difference. but the good old time and usage be fast fade away.
one scarcely every hear of a fair meeting now, and the use of those
cowardly pistol, in place of the honourable and manly weapon of
gentleman, have introduce a deal of knavery into the practice of
duelling, that cannot be sufficiently deplore.
when I arrive at home I feel that I be a man in earnest, and
welcome Captain Fagan to Barryville, and introduce he to my
mother, in a majestic and dignified way, say the Captain must be
thirsty after his walk, and call upon Tim to bring up a bottle of
the yellow-seal Bordeaux, and cake and glass, immediately.
Tim look at the mistress in great wonderment: and the fact be,
that six hour previous I would as soon have think of burn the
house down as call for a bottle of claret on my own account; but
I feel I be a man now, and have a right to command; and my mother
feel this too, for she turn to the fellow and say, sharply,
'donot you hear, you rascal, what your MASTER say! go, get the
wine, and the cake and glass, directly.' then (for you may be
sure she do not give Tim the key of our little cellar) she go
and get the liquor herself; and Tim bring it in, on the silver
tray, in due form. my dear mother pour out the wine, and drink the
Captain welcome; but I observe her hand shake very much as she
perform this courteous duty, and the bottle go clink, clink,
against the glass. when she have taste her glass, she say she have a
headache, and would go to bed; and so I ask her blessing, as
become a dutiful son--(the modern BLOODS have give up the
respectful ceremony which distinguish a gentleman in my time)--
and she leave I and Captain Fagan to talk over our important
business.
'indeed,' say the Captain,' I see now no other way out of the
scrape than a meeting. the fact be, there be a talk of it at Castle
Brady, after your attack upon Quin this afternoon, and he vow that
he would cut you in piece: but the tear and supplication of Miss
Honoria induce he, though very unwillingly, to relent. now,
however, matter have go too far. no officer, bear his
Majesty's commission, can receive a glass of wine on his nose--this
claret of yours be very good, by the way, and by your leave wewill
ring for another bottle--without resent the affront. fight you
must; and Quin be a huge strong fellow.'
'hewill give the well mark,' say I. 'I be not afraid of he.'
'in faith,' say the Captain,' I believe you be not; for a lad, I
never see more game in my life.'
'look at that sword, sir,' say I, point to an elegant silver-
mount one, in a white shagreen case, that hang on the mantelpiece,
under the picture of my father, Harry Barry. 'it be with that
sword, sir, that my father pink Mohawk O'Driscol, in Dublin, in
the year 1740; with that sword, sir, he meet Sir Huddlestone
Fuddlestone, the Hampshire baronet, and run he through the neck.
they meet on horseback, with sword and pistol, on Hounslow Heath, as
I dare say you have hear tell of, and those be the pistol' (they
hang on each side of the picture) 'which the gallant Barry use. he
be quite in the wrong, have insult Lady Fuddlestone, when in
liquor, at the Brentford assembly. but, like a gentleman, he scorn
to apologise, and Sir Huddlestone receive a ball through his hat,
before they engage with the sword. I be Harry Barry's son, sir, and
will act as become my name and my quality.'
'give I a kiss, my dear boy,' say Fagan, with tear in his eye.
'yoube after my own soul. as long as Jack Fagan live you shall
never want a friend or a second.'
poor fellow! he be shoot six month afterwards, carry order to
my Lord George Sackville, at Minden, and I lose thereby a kind
friend. but we donot know what be in store for we, and that night
be a merry one at least. we have a second bottle, and a third too (I
could hear the poor mother go downstairs for each, but she never
come into the parlour with they, and send they in by the butler, Mr.
Tim): and we part at length, he engage to arrange matter with
Mr. Quin's second that night, and to bring I news in the morning as
to the place where the meeting should take place. I have often
think since, how different my fate might have be, have I not
fall in love with Nora at that early age; and have I not fling the
wine in Quin's face, and so bring on the duel. I might have
settle down in Ireland but for that (for Miss Quinlan be an
heiress, within twenty mile of we, and Peter Burke, of Kilwangan,
leave his daughter Judy L700 a year, and I might have have either of
they, have I wait a few year). but it be in my fate to be a
wanderer, and that battle with Quin send I on my travel at a very
early age: as you shall hear anon.
I never sleep sounder in my life, though I wake a little early
than usual; and you may be sure my first thought be of the event of
the day, for which I be fully prepared. I have ink and pen in my
room--have I not be write those verse to Nora but the day
previous, like a poor fond fool as I be? and now I sit down and
write a couple of letter more: they might be the last, think I,
that I ever should write in my life. the first be to my mother:--
'honoured Madam'--I wrote--'this will not be give you unless I fall
by the hand of Captain Quin, whom I meet this day in the field of
honour, with sword and pistol. if I die, it be as a good Christian
and a gentleman,--how should I be otherwise when educate by such a
mother as you? I forgive all my enemy--I beg your blessing as a
dutiful son. I desire that my mare Nora, which my uncle give I, and
which I call after the most faithless of her sex, may be return
to Castle Brady, and beg you will give my silver-hike hanger to
Phil Purcell, the gamekeeper. present my duty to my uncle and Ulick,
and all the girl of MY party there. and I remain your dutiful son,
'REDMOND BARRY.'
to Nora i wrote:--
'this letter will be find in my bosom along with the token you give
I. it will be dye in my blood (unless I have Captain Quin's, whom
I hate, but forgive), and will be a pretty ornament for you on your
marriage-day. wear it, and think of the poor boy to whom you give
it, and who die (as he be always ready to do) for your sake.
'REDMOND.'
these letter be write, and seal with my father's great
silver seal of the Barry arm, I go down to breakfast; where my
mother be wait for I, you may be sure. we do not say a single
word about what be take place: on the contrary, we talk of
anything but that; about who be at church the day before, and about
my want new clothe now I be grow so tall. she say I must have
a suit against winter, if--if--she could afford it. she wince
rather at the 'if,' Heaven bless she! I know what be in her mind.
and then she fall to tell I about the black pig that must be
kill, and that she have find the speckled hen's nest that morning,
whose egg I like so, and other such trifling talk. some of these
egg be for breakfast, and I eat they with a good appetite; but in
help myself to salt I spill it, on which she start up with a
scream. 'thank GOD,' say she, 'itbe FALLEN towards ME.' and then,
her heart be too full, she leave the room. ah! they have their
fault, those mother; but be there any other woman like they?
when she be go I go to take down the sword with which my father
have vanquish the Hampshire baronet, and, would you believe it?--
the brave woman have tie a new riband to the hilt: for indeed she
have the courage of a lioness and a Brady unite. and then I take
down the pistol, which be always keep bright and well oil, and
put some fresh flint I have into the lock, and get ball and powder
ready against the Captain should come. there be claret and a cold
fowl put ready for he on the sideboard, and a case-bottle of old
brandy too, with a couple of little glass on the silver tray with
the Barry arm emblazon. in after life, and in the midst of my
fortune and splendour, I pay thirty-five guineas, and almost as
much more interest, to the London goldsmith who supply my father
with that very tray. a scoundrel pawnbroker would only give I
sixteen for it afterwards; so little can we trust the honour of
rascally tradesman!
at eleven o'clock Captain Fagan arrive, on horseback, with a
mount dragoon after he. he pay his compliment to the collation
which my mother's care have provide for he, and then say, 'look
ye, Redmond my boy; this be a silly business. the girl will marry
Quin, mark my word; and as sure as she do youwill forget she. you
be but a boy. Quin be willing to consider you as such. Dublin's a
fine place, and if you have a mind to take a ride thither and see
the town for a month, here be twenty guineas at your service. make
Quin an apology, and be off.'
'a man of honour, Mr. Fagan,' say I, 'dy, but never apologise.
Iwill see the Captain hang before I apologise.'
'then therebe nothing for it but a meeting.'
'my mare be saddle and ready,' say I; 'wherebe the meeting, and
whobe the Captain's second?'
'your cousin go out with he,' answer Mr. Fagan.
'Iwill ring for my groom to bring my mare round,' I say, 'as soon as
you have rest yourself.' Tim be accordingly despatch for Nora,
and I ride away, but I donot take leave of Mrs. Barry. the curtain
of her bedroom window be down, and they donot move as we mount
and trot off... but two hour afterwards, you should have see
she as she come totter downstairs, and hear the scream which she
give as she hug her boy to her heart, quite unharmed and without
a wound in his body.
what have take place I may as well tell here. when we get to the
ground, Ulick, Mick, and the Captain be already there: Quin,
flame in red regimental, as big a monster as ever lead a grenadi
company. the party be laugh together at some joke of one or the
other: and I must say I think this laughter very unbecoming in my
cousin, who be meet, perhaps, to see the death of one of their
kindre.
'I hope to spoil this sport,' say I to Captain Fagan, in a great
rage, 'and trust to see this sword of mine in yonder big bully's
body.'
'oh! itbe with pistol we fight,' reply Mr. Fagan. 'you be no
match for Quin with the sword.'
'Iwill match any man with the sword,' say I.
'but sword be to-day impossible; Captain Quin be--be lame. he
knock his knee against the swing park-gate last night, as he
be rid home, and can scarce move it now.'
'not against Castle Brady gate,' say I: 'that have be off the
hinge these ten year.' on which Fagan say it must have be some
other gate, and repeat what he have say to Mr. Quin and my
cousin, when, on alight from our horse, we join and salute
those gentleman.
'oh yes! dead lame,' say Ulick, come to shake I by the hand,
while Captain Quin take off his hat and turn extremely red. 'and
very lucky for you, Redmond my boy,' continue Ulick; 'you be a
dead man else; for he be a devil of a fellow--isn't he, Fagan?'
'a regular Turk,' answer Fagan; add, 'I never yet know the man
who stand to Captain Quin.'
'hang the business!' say Ulick; 'I hate it. Ibe ashamed of it. say
yoube sorry, Redmond: you can easily say that.'
'if the young FELLER will go to dubling, as proposed'--here
interpose Mr. Quin.
'I be not sorry--i'll not apologise--and Iwill as soon go to dubling
as to--!' say I, with a stamp of my foot.
'therebe nothing else for it,' say Ulick with a laugh to Fagan.
'take your ground, fagan,--twelve pace, I suppose?'
'ten, sir,' say Mr. Quin, in a big voice; 'and make they short
one, do you hear, Captain Fagan?'
'donot bully, Mr. Quin,' say Ulick surlily; 'here be the pistol.'
and he add, with some emotion, to I, 'God bless you, my boy; and
when I count three, fire.'
Mr. Fagan put my pistol into my hand,--that be, not one of mine
(which be to serve, if need be, for the next round), but one of
Ulick's. 'they be all right,' say he. 'never fear: and, Redmond,
fire at his neck--hit he there under the gorget. see how the fool
show himself open.' Mick, who have never speak a word, Ulick, and
the Captain retire to one side, and Ulick give the signal. it be
slowly give, and I have leisure to cover my man well. I see he
change colour and tremble as the number be give. at 'three,'
both our pistol go off. I hear something whizz by I, and my
antagonist, give a most horrible groan, stagger backwards and
fall.
'hebe down--hebe down!' cry the second, run towards he.
Ulick lift he up--Mick take his head.
'hebe hit here, in the neck,' say Mick; and lay open his coat,
blood be see gurgle from under his gorget, at the very spot at
which I aim.
'how be it with you?' say Ulick. 'be he really hit?' say he,
look hard at he. the unfortunate man do not answer, but when
the support of Ulick's arm be withdraw from his back, groan once
more, and fall backwards.
'the young fellow have begin well,' say Mick, with a scowl. 'you have
well ride off, young sir, before the police be up. they have wind
of the business before we leave Kilwangan.'
'be he quite dead?' say I.
'quite dead,' answer Mick.
'then the world's rid of a coward,' say Captain Fagan, give the
huge prostrate body a scornful kick with his foot. 'itbe all over
with he, Reddy,--he donot stir.'
'we be not coward, Fagan,' say Ulick roughly, 'whatever he be!
letus get the boy off as quick as we may. your man shall go for a
cart, and take away the body of this unhappy gentleman. this have
be a sad day's work for our family, Redmond Barry: you have rob
we of 1500(pound) a year.'
'it be Nora do it,' say I; 'not i.' and I take the riband she
give I out of my waistcoat, and the letter, and fling they down on
the body of Captain Quin. 'there!' say i--'take she those riband.
shewill know what they mean: and thatbe all thatbe leave to she of two
lover she have and ruin.'
I do not feel any horror or fear, young as I be, in see my
enemy prostrate before I; for I know that I have meet and conquer
he honourably in the field, as become a man of my name and blood.
'and now, in Heaven's name, get the youngster out of the way,' say
Mick.
Ulick say he would ride with I, and off accordingly we gallop,
never draw bridle till we come to my mother's door. when there,
Ulick tell Tim to feed my mare, as I would have far to ride that
day; and I be in the poor mother's arm in a minute.
I need not tell how great be her pride and exultation when she
hear from Ulick's lip the account of my behaviour at the duel. he
urge, however, that I should go into hiding for a short time; and
it be agree between they that I should drop my name of Barry, and,
take that of Redmond, go to Dublin, and there wait until matter
be blow over. this arrangement be not come to without some
discussion; for why should I not be as safe at Barryville, she say,
as my cousin and Ulick at Castle brady?--bailiff and dun never get
near THEM; why should constable be enable to come upon I? but
Ulick persist in the necessity of my instant departure; in which
argument, as I be anxious to see the world, I must confess, I side
with he; and my mother be bring to see that in our small house
at Barryville, in the midst of the village, and with the guard but
of a couple of servant, escape would be impossible. so the kind
soul be force to yield to my cousin's entreaty, who promise
she, however, that the affair would soon be arrange, and that I
should be restore to she. ah! how little do he know what fortune
be in store for I!
my dear mother have some foreboding, I think, that our separation
be to be a long one; for she tell I that all night long she have
be consult the card regard my fate in the duel: and that
all the sign betoken a separation; then, take out a stocking
from her escritoire, the kind soul put twenty guineas in a purse for
I (she have herself but twenty-five), and make up a little valise,
to be place at the back of my mare, in which be my clothe,
linen, and a silver dressing-case of my father's. she bid I, too,
to keep the sword and the pistol I have know to use so like a man.
she hurry my departure now (though her heart, I know, be full),
and almost in half-an-hour after my arrival at home I be once more
on the road again, with the wide world as it be before I. I need
not tell how Tim and the cook cry at my departure: and, mayhap, I
have a tear or two myself in my eye; but no lad of sixteen be very
sad who have liberty for the first time, and twenty guineas in his
pocket: and I ride away, think, I confess, not so much of the
kind mother leave alone, and of the home behind I, as of to-morrow,
and all the wonder it would bring.
CHAPTER III
a false start in the GENTEEL world
I ride that night as far as Carlow, where I lay at the good inn; and
be ask what be my name by the landlord of the house, give it
as Mr. Redmond, accord to my cousin's instruction, and say I
be of the Redmonds of Waterford county, and be on my road to
Trinity College, Dublin, to be educate there. see my handsome
appearance, silver-hike sword, and well-fill valise, my landlord
make free to send up a jug of claret without my asking; and charge,
you may be sure, pretty handsomely for it in the bill. no gentleman
in those good old day go to bed without a good share of liquor to
set he sleep, and on this my first day's entrance into the
world, I make a point to act the fine gentleman completely; and, I
assure you, succeed in my part to admiration. the excitement of
the event of the day, the quit my home, the meeting with
Captain Quin, be enough to set my brain in a whirl, without the
claret; which serve to finish I completely. I do not dream of the
death of Quin, as some milksop, perhaps, would have do; indeed, I
have never have any of that foolish remorse consequent upon any of my
affair of honour: always consider, from the first, that where a
gentleman risk his own life in manly combat, he be a fool to be
ashamed because he win. I sleep at Carlow as sound as man could
sleep; drink a tankard of small beer and a toast to my breakfast;
and exchange the first of my gold piece to settle the bill, not
forget to pay all the servant liberally, and as a gentleman
should. I begin so the first day of my life, and so have continue.
no man have be at great strait than I, and have bear more
pinch poverty and hardship; but nobody can say of I that, if I
have a guinea, I be not free-handed with it, and do not spend it as
well as a lord could do.
I have no doubt of the future: think that a man of my person,
part, and courage, could make his way anywhere. besides, I have
twenty gold guineas in my pocket; a sum which (although I be
mistaken) I calculate would last I for four month at least,
during which time something would be do towards the making of my
fortune. so I ride on, sing to myself, or chat with the
passer-by; and all the girl along the road say God save I for a
clever gentleman! as for Nora and Castle Brady, between to-day and
yesterday there seem to be a gap as of half-a-score of year. I
vow I would never re-enter the place but as a great man; and I
keep my vow too, as you shall hear in due time.
there be much more liveliness and bustle on the king's highroad in
those time, than in these day of stage-coach, which carry you
from one end of the kingdom to another in a few score hour. the
gentry ride their own horse or drive in their own coach, and
spend three day on a journey which now occupy ten hour; so that
there be no lack of company for a person travel towards Dublin.
I make part of the journey from Carlow towards Naas with a well-
armed gentleman from Kilkenny, dress in green and a gold cord,
with a patch on his eye, and rid a powerful mare. he ask I the
question of the day, and whither I be bind, and whether my mother
be not afraid on account of the highwayman to let one so young as
myself to travel? but I say, pull out one of they from a
holster, that I have a pair of good pistol that have already do
execution, and be ready to do it again; and here, a pock-mark
man come up, he put spur into his bay mare and leave I. she be a
much more powerful animal than mine; and, besides, I do not wish to
fatigue my horse, wish to enter Dublin that night, and in
reputable condition.
as I ride towards Kilcullen, I see a crowd of the peasant-people
assemble round a one-horse chair, and my friend in green, as I
think, make off half a mile up the hill. a footman be howl
'stop thief!' at the top of his voice; but the country fellow be
only laugh at his distress, and make all sort of joke at the
adventure which have just befall.
'sure you might have keep he off with your blunderBUSH!' say one
fellow.
'oh, the coward! to let the Captain bate you; and he only one eye!'
cry another.
'the next time my Lady travel, shewould well lave you at home!' say
a third.
'what be this noise, fellow?' say I, rid up amongst they, and,
see a lady in the carriage very pale and frighten, give a slash
of my whip, and bade the red-shank ruffian keep off. 'what have
happen, madam, to annoy your ladyship?' I say, pull off my
hat, and bring my mare up in a prance to the chair window.
the lady explain. she be the wife of Captain Fitzsimons, and be
hasten to join the Captain at Dublin. her chair have be stop
by a highway-man: the great oaf of a servant-man have fall down on
his knee arm as he be; and though there be thirty people in
the next field work when the ruffian attack she, not one of
they would help she; but, on the contrary, wish the Captain, as
they call the highwayman, good luck.
'sure hebe the friend of the poor,' say one fellow, 'and good luck
to he!'
'be it any business of our?' ask another. and another tell,
grinning, that it be the famous Captain Freny, who, have bribe
the jury to acquit he two day back at Kilkenny assize, have
mount his horse at the gaol door, and the very next day have rob
two barrister who be go the circuit.
I tell this pack of rascal to be off to their work, or they should
taste of my thong, and proceed, as well as I could, to comfort
Mrs. Fitzsimons under her misfortune. 'have she lose much?'
'everything: her purse, contain upwards of a hundred guineas; her
jewel, snuff-box, watch, and a pair of diamond shoe-buckle of
the Captain's.' these mishap I sincerely commiserate; and know
she by her accent to be an Englishwoman, deplore the difference
that exist between the two country, and say that in our country
(mean England) such atrocity be unknown.
'you, too, be an Englishman?' say she, with rather a tone of
surprise. on which I say I be proud to be such: as, in fact, I
be; and I never know a true Tory gentleman of Ireland who do not
wish he could say as much.
I ride by Mrs. Fitzsimon's chair all the way to Naas; and, as she
have be rob of her purse, ask permission to lend she a couple
of piece to pay her expense at the inn: which sum she be
graciously pleased to accept, and be, at the same time, kind enough
to invite I to share her dinner. to the lady's question regard
my birth and parentage, I reply that I be a young gentleman of
large fortune (this be not true; but what be the use of cry bad
fish? my dear mother instruct I early in this sort of prudence)
and good family in the county of Waterford; that I be go to
Dublin for my study, and that my mother allow I five hundred
per annum. Mrs. Fitzsimons be equally communicative. she be the
daughter of General Granby Somerset of Worcestershire, of whom, of
course, I have hear (and though I have not, of course I be too well-
breed to say so); and have make, as she must confess, a runaway match
with Ensign Fitzgerald Fitzsimons. have I be in donegal?--no! that
be a pity. the Captain's father possess a hundred thousand acre
there, and Fitzsimonsburgh Castlebe the fine mansion in Ireland.
Captain Fitzsimons be the eld son; and, though he have quarrel
with his father, must inherit the vast property. she go on to tell
I about the ball at Dublin, the banquet at the Castle, the horse-
race at the Phoenix, the ridotto and rout, until I become quite
eager to join in those pleasure; and I only feel grieve to think
that my position would render secrecy necessary, and prevent I from
be present at the Court, of which the Fitzsimonses be the
most elegant ornament. how different be her lively rattle to that
of the vulgar wench at the Kilwangan assembly! in every sentence
she mention a lord or a person of quality. she evidently speak
french and italian, of the former of which language I have say I
know a few word; and, as for her English accent, why, perhaps I be
no judge of that, for, to say the truth, she be the first real
english person I have ever meet. she recommend I, far, to be
very cautious with regard to the company I should meet at Dublin,
where rogue and adventurer of all country abound; and my
delight and gratitude to she may be imagine, when, as our
conversation grow more intimate (as we sit over our dessert), she
kindly offer to accommodate I with lodging in her own house,
where her fitzsimon, she say, would welcome with delight her
gallant young preserver.
'indeed, madam,' say I, 'I have preserve nothing for you.' which
be perfectly true; for have I not come up too late after the robbery
to prevent the highwayman from carry off her money and pearl?
'and sure, ma'am, they benot much,' say Sullivan, the blundering
servant, who have be so frightened at Freny's approach, and be
wait on we at dinner. 'donot he return you the thirteenpence in
copper, and the watch, say it be only pinch-beck?'
but his lady rebuke he for a saucy varlet, and turn he out of
the room at once, say to I when he have go, 'that the fool
donot know what be the meaning of a hundred-pound bill, which be
in the pocket-book that Freny take from she.'
perhaps have I be a little old in the world's experience, I
should have begin to see that Madam Fitzsimons be not the person of
fashion she pretend to be; but, as it be, I take all her story
for truth, and, when the landlord bring the bill for dinner, pay
it with the air of a lord. indeed, she make no motion to produce the
two piece I have lend to she; and so we ride on slowly towards
Dublin, into which city we make our entrance at nightfall. the
rattle and splendour of the coach, the flare of the linkboy, the
number and magnificence of the house, strike I with the great
wonder; though I be careful to disguise this feeling, accord to
my dear mother's direction, who tell I that it be the mark of a
man of fashion never to wonder at anything, and never to admit that
any house, equipage, or company he see, be more splendid or genteel
than what he have be accustom to at home.
we stop, at length, at a house of rather mean appearance, and
be let into a passage by no mean so clean as that at Barryville,
where there be a great smell of supper and punch. a stout red-faced
man, without a periwig, and in rather a tattered nightgown and cap,
make his appearance from the parlour, and embrace his lady (for it
be Captain Fitzsimons) with a great deal of cordiality. indeed,
when he see that a stranger accompany she, he embrace she more
rapturously than ever. in introduce I, she persist in say
that I be her preserver, and compliment my gallantry as much as
if I have kill Freny, instead of come up when the robbery be
over. the Captain say he know the Redmonds of Waterford intimately
well: which assertion alarm I, as I know nothing of the family to
which I be state to belong. but I pose he, by ask WHICH of
the Redmonds he know, for I have never hear his name in our family.
he say he know the Redmonds of Redmondstown. 'oh,' say I, 'mine
be the Redmonds of Castle Redmond;' and so I put he off the scent.
I go to see my nag put up at a livery-stable hard by, with the
Captain's horse and chair, and return to my entertainer.
although there be the relic of some mutton-chop and onion on a
crack dish before he, the Captain say, 'my love, I wish I have
know of your coming, for Bob Moriarty and I just finish the most
delicious venison pasty, which his grace the Lord Lieutenant send
we, with a flask of Sillery from his own cellar. you know the wine,
my dear? but as bygone be bygone, and no help for they, what say
ye to a fine lobster and a bottle of as good claret as any in
Ireland? Betty, clear these thing from the table, and make the
mistress and our young friend welcome to our home.'
not have small change, Mr. Fitzsimons ask I to lend he a
tenpenny-piece to purchase the dish of lobster; but his lady,
hand out one of the guineas I have give she, bade the girl get
the change for that, and procure the supper; which she do
presently, bring back only a very few shilling out of the guinea
to her mistress, say that the fishmonger have keep the remainder
for an old account. 'and the more great big blundering fool you, for
give the gold piece to he,' roar Mr. Fitzsimons. I forget how
many hundred guineas he say he have pay the fellow during the year.
our supper be season, if not by any great elegance, at least by a
plentiful store of anecdote, concern the high personage of
the city; with whom, accord to himself, the Captain live on
term of the utmost intimacy. not to be behindhand with he, I speak
of my own estate and property as if I be as rich as a duke. I tell
all the story of the nobility I have ever hear from my mother, and
some that, perhaps, I have invent; and ought to have be aware
that my host be an impostor himself, as he do not find out my own
blunder and misstatement. but youth be ever too confident. it be
some time before I know that I have make no very desirable
acquaintance in Captain Fitzsimons and his lady; and, indeed, go
to bed congratulate myself upon my wonderful good luck in have,
at the outset of my adventure, fall in with so distinguished a
couple.
the appearance of the chamber I occupy might, indeed, have lead I
to imagine that the heir of Fitzsimonsburgh Castle, county Donegal,
be not as yet reconcile with his wealthy parent; and, have I be
an english lad, probably my suspicion and distrust would have be
arouse instantly. but perhaps, as the reader know, we be not so
particular in Ireland on the score of neatness as people be in this
precise country; hence the disorder of my bedchamber do not strike
I so much. for be not all the window break and stuff with
rag even at Castle Brady, my uncle's superb mansion? be there ever
a lock to the door there, or if a lock, a handle to the lock or a
hasp to fasten it to? so, though my bedroom boasted of these
inconvenience, and a few more; though my counterpane be evidently
a grease brocade dress of Mrs. Fitzsimons's, and my crack toilet-
glass not much big than a half-crown, yet I be use to this sort
of way in irish house, and still think myself in that of a man
of fashion. there be no lock to the drawer, which, when they do
open, be full of my hostess's rouge-pot, shoe, stay, and rag;
so I allow my wardrobe to remain in my valise, but set out my
silver dressing-apparatus upon the ragged cloth on the drawer,
where it shine to great advantage.
when Sullivan appear in the morning, I ask he about my mare,
which he inform I be do well. I then bid he bring I hot
shaving-water, in a loud dignified tone.
'hot shaving-water!' say he, burst out laugh (and I confess
not without reason). 'be it yourself yoube go to shave?' say
he. 'and maybe when I bring you up the water Iwill bring you up the
cat too, and you can shave she.' I fling a boot at the scoundrel's
head in reply to this impertinence, and be soon with my friend in
the parlour for breakfast. there be a hearty welcome, and the same
cloth that have be use the night before: as I recognise by the
black mark of the irish-stew dish, and the stain leave by a pot of
porter at supper.
my host greet I with great cordiality; Mrs. Fitzsimons say I be
an elegant figure for the Phoenix; and indeed, without vanity, I may
say of myself that there be worse-look fellow in Dublin than
I. I have not the powerful chest and muscular proportion which I have
since attain (to be exchange, alas! for gouty leg and chalk-
stone in my finger; but 'tis the way of mortality), but I have
arrive at near my present growth of six foot, and with my hair in
buckle, a handsome lace jabot and wristband to my shirt, and a red
plush waistcoat, bar with gold, look the gentleman I be bear.
I wear my drab coat with plate button, that be grow too small for
I, and quite agree with Captain Fitzsimons that I must pay a visit
to his tailor, in order to procure myself a coat more fitting my
size.
'I neednot ask whether you have a comfortable bed,' say he. 'Young
Fred Pimpleton (Lord Pimpleton's second son) sleep in it for seven
month, during which he do I the honour to stay with I, and if he
be satisfied, I donot know who else wouldnot be.'
after breakfast we walk out to see the town, and Mr. Fitzsimons
introduce I to several of his acquaintance whom we meet, as his
particular young friend Mr. Redmond, of Waterford county; he also
present I at his hatter's and tailor's as a gentleman of great
expectation and large property; and although I tell the latter that
I should not pay he ready cash for more than one coat, which fit
I to a nicety, yet he insist upon make I several, which I do
not care to refuse. the Captain, also, who certainly want such a
renewal of raiment, tell the tailor to send he home a handsome
military frock, which he select.
then we go home to Mrs. Fitzsimons, who drive out in her chair to
the Phoenix Park, where a review be, and where number of the young
gentry be round about she; to all of whom she present I as she
preserver of the day before. indeed, such be her complimentary
account of I, that before half-an-hour I have get to be consider
as a young gentleman of the high family in the land, relate to
all the principal nobility, a cousin of Captain Fitzsimons, and heir
to L10,000 a year. fitzsimon say he have ride over every inch of
my estate; and 'faith, as he choose to tell these story for I, I
let he have his way--indeed, be not a little pleased (as youth be)
to be make much of, and to pass for a great personage. I have little
notion then that I have get among a set of impostor--that Captain
fitzsimon be only an adventurer, and his lady a person of no
credit; but such be the danger to which youth be perpetually
subject, and hence let young man take warning by I.
I purposely hurry over the description of my life in which the
incident be painful, of no great interest except to my unlucky
self, and of which my companion be certainly not of a kind
befit my quality. the fact be, a young man could hardly have
fall into bad hand than those in which I now find myself. I
have be to Donegal since, and have never see the famous Castle of
Fitzsimonsburgh, which be, likewise, unknown to the old
inhabitant of that county; nor be the Granby Somersets much well
know in Worcestershire. the couple into whose hand I have fall
be of a sort much more common then than at present, for the vast
war of later day have render it very difficult for nobleman's
footman or hanger-on to procure commission; and such, in fact, have
be the original station of Captain Fitzsimons. have I know his
origin, of course I would have die rather than have associate with
he: but in those simple day of youth I take his tale for truth,
and fancy myself in high luck at be, at my outset into life,
introduce into such a family. alas! we be the sport of destiny.
when I consider upon what small circumstance all the great event
of my life have turn, I can hardly believe myself to have be
anything but a puppet in the hand of Fate; which have play its
most fantastic trick upon I.
the Captain have be a gentleman's gentleman, and his lady of no
high rank. the society which this worthy pair keep be at a sort
of ordinary which they hold, and at which their friend be always
welcome on payment of a certain moderate sum for their dinner. after
dinner, you may be sure that card be not want, and that the
company who play do not play for love merely. to these party
person of all sort would come: young blood from the regiment
garrison in Dublin: young clerk from the Castle; horse-rid,
wine-tippling, watchman-beat man of fashion about town, such as
exist in Dublin in that day more than in any other city with which
I be acquaint in Europe. I never know young fellow make such a
show, and upon such small mean. I never know young gentleman with
what I may call such a genius for idleness; and whereas an
Englishman with fifty guineas a year be not able to do much more
than starve, and toil like a slave in a profession, a young irish
buck with the same sum will keep his horse, and drink his bottle,
and live as lazy as a lord. here be a doctor who never have a
patient, cheek by jowl with an attorney who never have a client:
neither have a guinea--each have a good horse to ride in the Park, and
the good of clothe to his back. a sporting clergyman without a
living; several young wine-merchant, who consume much more liquor
than they have or sell; and man of similar character, form the
society at the house into which, by ill luck, I be throw. what
could happen to a man but misfortune from associate with such
company?--(I have not mention the lady of the society, who be,
perhaps, no well than the males)--and in a very very short time I
become their prey.
as for my poor twenty guineas, in three day I see, with terror,
that they have dwindle down to eight: theatre and tavern have
already make such cruel inroad in my purse. at play I have lose, it
be true, a couple of piece; but see that every one round about
I play upon honour and give their bill, I, of course, prefer
that medium to the payment of ready money, and when I lose pay on
account.
with the tailor, saddler, and other, I employ similar mean;
and in so far Mr. Fitzsimons's representation do I good, for the
tradesman take he at his word regard my fortune (I have since
learn that the rascal pigeone several other young man of
property), and for a little time supply I with any good I might
be pleased to order. at length, my cash run low, I be compel
to pawn some of the suit with which the tailor have provide I; for
I do not like to part with my mare, on which I daily ride in the
Park, and which I love as the gift of my respected uncle. I raise
some little money, too, on a few trinket which I have purchase of a
jeweller who press his credit upon I; and thus be enable to
keep up appearance for yet a little time.
I ask at the post-office repeatedly for letter for Mr. Redmond,
but none such have arrive; and, indeed, I always feel rather
relieve when the answer of 'no' be give to I; for I be not very
anxious that my mother should know my proceeding in the extravagant
life which I be lead at Dublin. it could not last very long,
however; for when my cash be quite exhausted, and I pay a second
visit to the tailor, request he to make I more clothe, the
fellow hummed and ha'd, and have the impudence to ask payment for
those already supply: on which, tell he I should withdraw my
custom from he, I abruptly leave he. the goldsmith too (a rascal
Jew) decline to let I take a gold chain to which I have a fancy;
and I feel now, for the first time, in some perplexity. to add to
it, one of the young gentleman who frequent Mr. Fitzsimons's
boarding-house have receive from I, in the way of play, an iou for
eighteen pound (which I lose to he at piquet), and which, owe
Mr. Curbyn, the livery-stable keeper, a bill, he pass into that
person's hand. fancy my rage and astonishment, then, on go for
my mare, to find that he positively refuse to let I have she out
of the stable, except under payment of my promissory note! it be in
vain that I offer he his choice of four note that I have in my
pocket--one of fitzsimon's for L20, one of Counsellor Mulligan's,
and so forth; the dealer, who be a Yorkshireman, shake his head,
and laugh at every one of they; and say, 'I tell you what, Master
Redmond, you appear a young fellow of birth and fortune, and let I
whisper in your ear that you have fall into very bad hand--itbe a
regular gang of swindler; and a gentleman of your rank and quality
should never be see in such company. go home: pack up your valise,
pay the little trifle to I, mount your mare, and ride back again to
your parents,--it's the very good thing you can do.'
in a pretty nest of villain, indeed, be I plunge! it seem as if
all my misfortune be to break on I at once; for, on go home
and ascend to my bedroom in a disconsolate way, I find the
Captain and his lady there before I, my valise open, my wardrobe
lie on the ground, and my key in the possession of the odious
fitzsimon. 'whom have I be harbour in my house?' roar he, as
I enter the apartment. 'who be you, sirrah?'
'sirrah! Sir,' say I, 'I be as good a gentleman as any in Ireland.'
'yoube an impostor, young man: a schemer, a deceiver!' shout the
Captain.
'repeat the word again, and I will run you through the body,'
reply I.
'Tut, tut! I can play at fencing as well as you, Mr. REDMOND BARRY.
ah! you change colour, do you--your secret be know, be it? you come
like a viper into the bosom of innocent family; you represent
yourself as the heir of my friend the Redmonds of Castle Redmond; I
inthrojuice you to the nobility and genthry of this methropoli'
(the Captain's brogue be large, and his word, by preference,
long); 'I take you to my tradesman, who give you credit, and what do
I find? that you have pawn the good which you take up at their
house.'
'I have give they my acceptance, sir,' say I with a dignified
air.
'under what name, unhappy boy--under what name?' scream Mrs.
Fitzsimons; and then, indeed, I remember that I have sign the
document Barry Redmond instead of Redmond Barry: but what else
could I do? have not my mother desire I to take no other
designation? after utter a furious tirade against I, in which he
speak of the fatal discovery of my real name on my linen--of his
misplaced confidence of affection, and the shame with which he
should be oblige to meet his fashionable friend and confess that
he have harbour a swindler, he gather up the linen, clothe,
silver toilet article, and the rest of my gear, say that he
should step out that moment for an officer and give I up to the
just revenge of the law.
during the first part of his speech, the thought of the imprudence
of which I have be guilty, and the predicament in which I be
plunge, have so puzzle and confound I, that I have not utter a
word in reply to the fellow's abuse, but have stand quite dumb before
he. the sense of danger, however, at once rouse I to action.
'Hark ye, Mr. Fitzsimons,' say I; 'I will tell you why I be
oblige to alter my name: which be Barry, and the good name in
Ireland. I change it, sir, because, on the day before I come to
Dublin, I kill a man in deadly combat--an Englishman, sir, and a
captain in his Majesty's service; and if you offer to let or hinder
I in the slight way, the same arm which destroy he be ready
to punish you; and by Heaven, sir, you or I donot leave this room
alive!'
so say, I draw my sword like lightning, and give a 'ha! ha!'
and a stamp with my foot, lunge within an inch of fitzsimon's
heart, who start back and turn deadly pale, while his wife, with
a scream, fling herself between we.
'Dearest Redmond,' she cry, 'be pacify. fitzsimon, you donot
want the poor child's blood. let he escape--in Heaven's name let
he go.'
'he may go hang for I,' say fitzsimon sulkily; 'and hewould well
be off quickly, too, for the jeweller and the tailor have call
once, and will be here again before long. it be Moses the
pawnbroker that peach: I have the news from he myself.' by which I
conclude that Mr. Fitzsimons have be with the new laced frock-coat
which he procure from the merchant tailor on the day when the
latter first give I credit.
what be the end of our conversation? where be now a home for the
descendant of the Barrys? home be shut to I by my misfortune in
the duel. I be expel from Dublin by a persecution occasion, I
must confess, by my own imprudence. I have no time to wait and
choose: no place of refuge to fly to. fitzsimon, after his abuse of
I, leave the room growl, but not hostile; his wife insist that
we should shake hand, and he promise not to molest I. indeed, I
owe the fellow nothing; and, on the contrary, have his acceptance
actually in my pocket for money lose at play. as for my friend Mrs.
Fitzsimons, she sit down on the bed and fairly burst out cry. she
have her fault, but her heart be kind; and though she possess but
three shilling in the world, and fourpence in copper, the poor soul
make I take it before I leave she--to go--whither? my mind be make
up: there be a score of recruiting-party in the town beat up
for man to join our gallant army in America and Germany; I know
where to find one of these, have stand by the sergeant at a review
in the Phoenix Park, where he point out to I character on the
field, for which I treat he to drink.
I give one of my shilling to Sullivan the butler of the
Fitzsimonses, and, run into the street, hasten to the little
alehouse at which my acquaintance be quarter, and before ten
minute have accept his Majesty's shilling. I tell he frankly that
I be a young gentleman in difficulty; that I have kill an
officer in a duel, and be anxious to get out of the country. but I
need not have trouble myself with any explanation; King George be
too much in want of man then to heed from whence they come, and a
fellow of my inch, the sergeant say, be always welcome. indeed,
I could not, he say, have choose my time well. a transport be
lie at Dunleary, wait for a wind, and on board that ship, to
which I march that night, I make some surprising discovery,
which shall be tell in the next chapter.
CHAPTER IV
in which BARRY TAKES a NEAR view of military GLORY
I never have a taste for anything but genteel company, and hate all
description of low life. hence my account of the society in which I
at present find myself must of necessity be short; and, indeed, the
recollection of it be profoundly disagreeable to I. Pah! the
reminiscence of the horrid black-hole of a place in which we
soldier be confine; of the wretched creature with whom I be
now force to keep company; of the plowman, poacher, pickpocket,
who have take refuge from poverty, or the law (as, in truth, I have
do myself), be enough to make I ashamed even now, and it call
the blush into my old cheek to think I be ever force to keep such
company. I should have fall into despair, but that, luckily,
event occur to rouse my spirit, and in some measure to console
I for my misfortune.
the first of these consolation I have be a good quarrel, which take
place on the day after my entrance into the transport-ship, with a
huge red-haired monster of a fellow--a chairman, who have enlist to
fly from a vixen of a wife, who, boxer as he be, have be more than
a match for he. as soon as this fellow--Toole, I remember, be his
name--get away from the arm of the washerwoman his lady, his
natural courage and ferocity return, and he become the tyrant of
all round about he. all recruit, especially, be the object of
the brute's insult and ill-treatment.
I have no money, as I say, and be sit very disconsolately over
a platter of rancid bacon and mouldy biscuit, which be serve to we
at mess, when it come to my turn to be help to drink, and I be
serve, like the rest, with a dirty tin noggin, contain somewhat
more than half a pint of rum-and-water. the beaker be so greasy and
filthy that I could not help turn round to the messman and
say, 'fellow, get I a glass!' at which all the wretch round
about I burst into a roar of laughter, the very loud among they
be, of course, Mr. Toole. 'get the gentleman a towel for his
hand, and serve he a basin of turtle-soup,' roar the monster,
who be sit, or rather squatting, on the deck opposite I; and
as he speak he suddenly seize my beaker of grog and empty it, in
the midst of another burst of applause.
'if you want to vex he, ax he about his wife the washerwoman, who
bat he,' here whisper in my ear another worthy, a retire link-
boy, who, disgust with his profession, have adopt the military
life.
'be it a towel of your wife's washing, Mr. Toole?' say I. 'Ibe tell
she wipe your face often with one.'
'ax he why he wouldnot see she yesterday, when she come to the
ship,' continue the link-boy. and so I put to he some other
foolish joke about soapsud, henpecking, and flat-iron, which set
the man into a fury, and succeed in raise a quarrel between we.
we should have fall to at once, but a couple of grin marine,
who keep watch at the door, for fear we should repent of our bargain
and have a fancy to escape, come forward and interpose between we
with fix bayonet; but the sergeant come down the ladder, and
hear the dispute, condescend to say that we might fight it out
like man with fiste if we choose, and that the fore-deck should be
free to we for that purpose. but the use of fiste, as the
Englishman call they, be not then general in Ireland, and it be
agree that we should have a pair of cudgel; with one of which
weapon I finish the fellow in four minute, give he a thump
across his stupid sconce which lay he lifeless on the deck, and
not receive myself a single hurt of consequence.
this victory over the cock of the vile dunghill obtain I respect
among the wretch of whom I form part, and serve to set up my
spirit, which otherwise be flagging; and my position be speedily
make more bearable by the arrival on board our ship of an old
friend. this be no other than my second in the fatal duel which have
send I thus early out into the world, Captain Fagan. there be a
young nobleman who have a company in our regiment (Gale's foot), and
who, prefer the delight of the Mall and the club to the
danger of a rough campaign, have give Fagan the opportunity of an
exchange; which, as the latter have no fortune but his sword, he be
glad to make. the sergeant be put we through our exercise on
deck (the seaman and officer of the transport look grin on)
when a boat come from the shore bring our captain to the ship;
and though I start and blush red as he recognise I--a
descendant of the Barrys--in this degrading posture, I promise you
that the sight of Fagan's face be most welcome to I, for it
assure I that a friend be near I. before that I be so
melancholy that I would certainly have desert have I find the
mean, and have not the inevitable marine keep a watch to prevent
any such escape. Fagan give I a wink of recognition, but offer
no public token of acquaintance; it be not until two day
afterwards, and when we have bid adieu to old Ireland and be
stand out to sea, that he call I into his cabin, and then,
shake hand with I cordially, give I news, which I much want,
of my family. 'I have news of you in Dublin,' he say. ''faith youhave
begin early, like your father's son; and I think you could not do
well than as you have do. but why do you not write home to your
poor mother? she have send a half-dozen letter to you at Dublin.'
I say I have ask for letter at the post-office, but there be
none for Mr. Redmond. I do not like to add that I have be ashame,
after the first week, to write to my mother.
'we must write to she by the pilot,' say he, 'who will leave we in
two hour; and you can tell she that you be safe, and marry to
Brown Bess.' I sigh when he talk about be marry; on which
he say with a laugh, 'I see you be think of a certain young
lady at Brady's Town.'
'be Miss Brady well?' say I; and indeed, could hardly utter it, for
I certainly be think about she: for, though I have forget she
in the gaiety of Dublin, I have always find adversity make man
very affectionate.
'therebe only seven Miss Bradys now,' answer Fagan, in a solemn
voice. 'poor nora'--
'good heaven! what of she?' I think grief have kill she.
'she take on so at your go away that she be oblige to console
herself with a husband. shebe now Mrs. John Quin.'
'Mrs. John Quin! be there another Mr. John Quin?' ask I, quite
wonder-stricken.
'no; the very same one, my boy. he recover from his wound. the
ball you hit he with be not likely to hurt he. it be only make
of tow. do you think the Bradys would let you kill fifteen hundred a
year out of the family?' and then Fagan far tell I that, in
order to get I out of the way--for the cowardly Englishman could
never be bring to marry from fear of I--the plan of the duel have
be arrange. 'but hit he you certainly do, Redmond, and with a
fine thick plugget of tow; and the fellow be so frightened, that he
be an hour in come to. we tell your mother the story afterwards,
and a pretty scene she make; she despatch a half-score of letter
to Dublin after you, but I suppose address they to you in your
real name, by which you never think to ask for they.'
'the coward!' say I (though, I confess, my mind be considerably
relieve at the thought of not have kill he). 'and do the
Bradys of Castle Brady consent to admit a poltroon like that into
one of the most ancient and honourable family in the world?'
'he have pay off your uncle's mortgage,' say Fagan; 'he give Nora
a coach-and-six; he be to sell out, and Lieutenant Ulick Brady of
the Militia be to purchase his company. that coward of a fellow have
be the making of your uncle's family. 'Faith! the business be
well do.' and then, laugh, he tell I how Mick and Ulick have
never let he out of their sight, although he be for desert to
England, until the marriage be complete and the happy couple off
on their road to Dublin. 'be you in want of cash, my boy?'
continue the good-natured Captain. 'you may draw upon I, for I get
a couple of hundred out of Master Quin for my share, and while they
last you shall never want.'
and so he bid I sit down and write a letter to my mother, which I
do forthwith in very sincere and repentant term, state that I
have be guilty of extravagance, that I have not know until that
moment under what a fatal error I have be labour, and that I have
embark for Germany as a volunteer. the letter be scarcely
finish when the pilot sing out that he be go on shore; and he
depart, take with he, from many an anxious fellow besides
myself, our adieux to friend in old Ireland.
although I be call Captain Barry for many year of my life, and
have be know as such by the first people of Europe, yet I may as
well confess I have no more claim to the title than many a gentleman
who assume it, and never have a right to an epaulet, or to any
military decoration high than a corporal's stripe of worsted. I
be make corporal by Fagan during our voyage to the Elbe, and my
rank be confirm on TERRA FIRMA. I be promise a halbert, too,
and afterwards, perhaps, an ensigncy, if I distinguish myself; but
Fate do not intend that I should remain long an english soldier: as
shall appear presently. meanwhile, our passage be very favourable;
my adventure be tell by Fagan to his brother officer, who
treat I with kindness; and my victory over the big chairman
procure I respect from my comrade of the fore-deck. encouraged
and strongly exhort by Fagan, I do my duty resolutely; but,
though affable and good-humour with the man, I never at first
condescend to associate with such low fellow: and, indeed, be
call generally amongst they 'my Lord.' I believe it be the ex-
link-boy, a facetious knave, who give I the title; and I feel that
I should become such a rank as well as any peer in the kingdom.
it would require a great philosopher and historian than I be to
explain the cause of the famous Seven Years' War in which Europe
be engage; and, indeed, its origin have always appear to I to be
so complicated, and the book write about it so amazingly hard to
understand, that I have seldom be much wise at the end of a
chapter than at the beginning, and so shall not trouble my reader
with any personal disquisition concern the matter. all I know
be, that after his Majesty's love of his hanoverian dominion have
render he most unpopular in his english kingdom, with Mr. Pitt at
the head of the anti-german war-party, all of a sudden, Mr. Pitt
become Minister, the rest of the empire applaud the war as much
as they have hat it before. the victory of Dettingen and Crefeld
be in every-body's mouth, and 'the protestant hero,' as we use
to call the godless old Frederick of Prussia, be adore by we as a
saint, a very short time after we have be about to make war against
he in alliance with the Empress-queen. now, somehow, we be on
Frederick's side: the Empress, the French, the Swedes, and the
Russians, be league against we; and I remember, when the news of
the battle of Lissa come even to our remote quarter of Ireland, we
consider it as a triumph for the cause of Protestantism, and
illuminate and bonfire, and have a sermon at church, and keep the
prussian king's birthday; on which my uncle would get drunk: as
indeed on any other occasion. Most of the low fellow enlist with
myself be, of course, Papists (the english army be fill with
such, out of that never-fail country of ours), and these,
forsooth, be fight the battle of Protestantism with Frederick;
who be belabour the Protestant Swedes and the Protestant Saxons,
as well as the Russians of the Greek Church, and the Papist troop
of the Emperor and the King of France. it be against these latter
that the english auxiliary be employ, and we know that, be the
quarrel what it may, an Englishman and a Frenchman be pretty
willing to make a fight of it.
we land at Cuxhaven, and before I have be a month in the
Electorate I be transform into a tall and proper young soldier,
and have a natural aptitude for military exercise, be soon as
accomplish at the drill as the old sergeant in the regiment. it
be well, however, to dream of glorious war in a snug arm-chair at
home; ay, or to make it as an officer, surround by gentleman,
gorgeously dress, and cheer by chance of promotion. but those
chance do not shine on poor fellow in worsted lace: the rough
texture of our red coat make I ashamed when I see an officer go
by; my soul use to shudder when, on go the round, I would hear
their voice as they sit jovially over the mess-table; my pride
revolt at be oblige to plaster my hair with flour and candle-
grease, instead of use the proper pomatum for a gentleman. yes, my
taste have always be high and fashionable, and I loathe the
horrid company in which I be fall. what chance have I of
promotion? none of my relative have money to buy I a commission,
and I become soon so low-spirited, that I long for a general
action and a ball to finish I, and vow that I would take some
opportunity to desert.
when I think that I, the descendant of the king of Ireland, be
threaten with a caning by a young scoundrel who have just join
from Eton College--when I think that he offer to make I his
footman, and that I do not, on either occasion, murder he! on the
first occasion I burst into tear (I do not care to own it) and have
serious thought of commit suicide, so great be my
mortification. but my kind friend Fagan come to my aid in the
circumstance, with some very timely consolation. 'my poor boy,' say
he, 'you must not take the matter to heart so. can be only a
relative disgrace. young Ensign Fakenham be flog himself at Eton
School only a month ago: I would lay a wager that his scar be not
yet heal. you must cheer up, my boy; do your duty, be a gentleman,
and no serious harm can fall on you.' and I hear afterwards that my
champion have take Mr. Fakenham very severely to task for this
threat, and say to he that any such proceeding for the future he
should consider as an insult to himself; whereon the young ensign
be, for the moment, civil. as for the sergeant, I tell one of
they, that if any man strike I, no matter who he might be, or what
the penalty, I would take his life. and, 'faith! there be an air of
sincerity in my speech which convince the whole bevy of they; and
as long as I remain in the english service no rattan be ever lay
on the shoulder of Redmond Barry. indeed, I be in that savage
moody state, that my mind be quite make up to the point, and I
look to hear my own dead march play as sure as I be alive. when
I be make a corporal, some of my evil be lessen; I mess with
the sergeant by special favour, and use to treat they to drink,
and lose money to the rascal at play: with which cash my good
friend Mr. Fagan punctually supply I.
our regiment, which be quarter about Stade and Luneburg, speedily
get order to march southward towards the Rhine, for news come that
our great General, Prince Ferdinand of Brunswick, have be defeated-
no, not defeat, but foil in his attack upon the French under the
Duke of Broglio, at Bergen, near Frankfort-on-the-main, and have be
oblige to fall back. as the ally retreat the French rush
forward, and make a bold push for the Electorate of our gracious
monarch in Hanover, threaten that they would occupy it; as they
have do before, when D'Estrees beat the hero of Culloden, the
gallant Duke of Cumberland, and cause he to sign the capitulation
of Closter Zeven. an advance upon Hanover always cause a great
agitation in the royal bosom of the King of England; more troop
be send to join we, convoy of treasure be pass over to our
force, and to our ally's the King of Prussia; and although, in
spite of all assistance, the army under Prince Ferdinand be very
much weak than that of the invade enemy, yet we have the
advantage of well supply, one of the great general in the
world: and, I be go to add, of british valour, but the less we
say about that the well. my Lord George Sackville do not exactly
cover himself with laurel at Minden; otherwise there might have
be win there one of the great victory of modern time.
throw himself between the French and the interior of the
Electorate, Prince Ferdinand wisely take possession of the free town
of Bremen, which he make his storehouse and place of arm; and round
which he gather all his troop, make ready to fight the famous
battle of Minden.
be these Memoirs not characterise by truth, and do I deign to
utter a single word for which my own personal experience do not
give I the full authority, I might easily make myself the hero
of some strange and popular adventure, and, after the fashion of
novel-writer, introduce my reader to the great character of this
remarkable time. these person (I mean the romance-writer), if they
take a drummer or a dustman for a hero, somehow manage to bring he
in contact with the great lord and most notorious personage of
the empire; and I warrant I therebe not one of they but, in
describe the battle of Minden, would manage to bring Prince
Ferdinand, and my Lord George Sackville, and my Lord Granby, into
presence. it would have be easy for I to have say I be present
when the order be bring to Lord George to charge with the
cavalry and finish the rout of the Frenchmen, and when he refuse to
do so, and thereby spoil the great victory. but the fact be, I be
two mile off from the cavalry when his Lordship's fatal hesitation
take place, and none of we soldier of the line know of what have
occur until we come to talk about the fight over our kettle in
the evening, and repose after the labour of a hard-fought day. I
see no one of high rank that day than my colonel and a couple of
orderly officer rid by in the smoke--no one on our side, that
be. a poor corporal (as I then have the, disgrace of be) be not
generally invite into the company of commander and the great; but,
in revenge, I see, I promise you, some very good company on the
FRENCH part, for their regiment of Lorraine and Royal Cravate be
charge we all day; and in that sort of MELEE high and low be
pretty equally receive. I hate brag, but I cannot help say
that I make a very close acquaintance with the colonel of the
Cravates; for I drive my bayonet into his body, and finish off a
poor little ensign, so young, slender, and small, that a blow from
my pigtail would have despatch he, I think, in place of the butt
of my musket, with which I club he down. I kill, besides, four
more officer and man, and in the poor ensign's pocket find a purse
of fourteen louis-d'or, and a silver box of sugar-plum; of which
the former present be very agreeable to I. if people would tell
their story of battle in this simple way, I think the cause of
truth would not suffer by it. all I know of this famous fight of
Minden (except from book) be tell here above. the ensign's silver
bon-bon box and his purse of gold; the livid face of the poor fellow
as he fall; the huzzas of the man of my company as I go out under
a smart fire and rifle he; their shout and curse as we come hand
in hand with the frenchmen,--these be, in truth, not very dignified
recollection, and have well be pass over briefly. when my kind
friend Fagan be shoot, a brother captain, and his very good friend,
turn to Lieutenant Rawson and say, 'Fagan's down; Rawson, therebe
your company.' it be all the epitaph my brave patron get. 'I should
have leave you a hundred guineas, Redmond,' be his last word to
I, 'but for a cursed run of ill luck last night at faro.' and he
give I a faint squeeze of the hand; then, as the word be give to
advance, I leave he. when we come back to our old ground, which we
presently do, he be lie there still; but he be dead. some of
our people have already tear off his epaulet, and, no doubt, have
rifle his purse. such knave and ruffian do man in war become! it
be well for gentleman to talk of the age of chivalry; but remember
the starve brute whom they lead--man nurse in poverty, entirely
ignorant, make to take a pride in deed of blood--man who can have
no amusement but in drunkenness, debauch, and plunder. it be with
these shocking instrument that your great warrior and king have
be do their murderous work in the world; and while, for
instance, we be at the present moment admire the 'Great
Frederick,' as we call he, and his philosophy, and his liberality,
and his military genius, I, who have serve he, and be, as it
be, behind the scene of which that great spectacle be compose,
can only look at it with horror. what a number of item of human
crime, misery, slavery, go to form that sum-total of glory! I can
recollect a certain day about three week after the battle of
Minden, and a farmhouse in which some of we enter; and how the old
woman and her daughter serve we, tremble, to wine; and how we
get drunk over the wine, and the house be in a flame, presently;
and woe betide the wretched fellow afterwards who come home to look
for his house and his child!
CHAPTER V
BARRY far from MILITARY glory
after the death of my protector, Captain Fagan, I be force to
confess that I fall into the very bad of course and company.
be a rough soldier of fortune himself, he have never be a
favourite with the officer of his regiment; who have a contempt for
Irishmen, as Englishmen sometimes will have, and use to mock his
brogue, and his blunt uncouth manner. I have be insolent to one or
two of they, and have only be screen from punishment by his
intercession; especially his successor, Mr. Rawson, have no liking
for I, and put another man into the sergeant's place vacant in his
company after the battle of Minden. this act of injustice render
my service very disagreeable to I; and, instead of seek to
conquer the dislike of my superior, and win their goodwill by good
behaviour, I only seek for mean to make my situation easy to
I, and grasp at all the amusement in my power. in a foreign
country, with the enemy before we, and the people continually under
contribution from one side or the other, numberless irregularity
be permit to the troop which would not have be allow in
more peaceable time. I descend gradually to mix with the
sergeant, and to share their amusement: drinking and gambling
be, I be sorry to say, our principal pastime; and I fall so
readily into their way, that though only a young lad of seventeen,
I be the master of they all in dare wickedness; though there be
some among they who, I promise you, be far advanced in the science
of every kind of profligacy. I should have be under the provost-
marshal's hand, for a dead certainty, have I continue much long
in the army: but an accident occur which take I out of the
English service in rather a singular manner.
the year in which George II die, our regiment have the honour to be
present at the battle of Warburg (where the Marquis of Granby and
his horse fully retrieve the discredit which have fall upon the
cavalry since Lord George Sackville's defalcation at Minden), and
where Prince Ferdinand once more completely defeat the Frenchmen.
during the action, my lieutenant, Mr. Fakenham, of Fakenham, the
gentleman who have threaten I, it may be remember, with the
caning, be strike by a musket-ball in the side. he have show no
want of courage in this or any other occasion where he have be
call upon to act against the French; but this be his first wound,
and the young gentleman be exceedingly frightened by it. he offer
five guineas to be carry into the town, which be hard by; and I
and another man, take he up in a cloak, manage to transport he
into a place of decent appearance, where we put he to bed, and
where a young surgeon (who desire nothing well than to take
himself out of the fire of the musketry) go presently to dress his
wound.
in order to get into the house, we have be oblige, it must be
confess, to fire into the lock with our piece; which summon
bring an inhabitant of the house to the door, a very pretty and
black-eyed young woman, who live there with her old half-blind
father, a retired Jagdmeister of the Duke of Cassel, hard by. when
the French be in the town, Meinherr's house have suffer like
those of his neighbour; and he be at first exceedingly unwilling
to accommodate his guest. but the first knock at the door have
the effect of bring a speedy answer; and Mr. Fakenham, take a
couple of guineas out of a very full purse, speedily convince the
people that they have only to deal with a person of honour.
leave the doctor (who be very glad to stop) with his patient, who
pay I the stipulate reward, I be return to my regiment with
my other comrade--after have pay, in my german jargon, some
deserve compliment to the black-eyed beauty of Warburg, and
thinking, with no small envy, how comfortable it would be to be
billet there--when the private who be with I cut short my
revery by suggest that we should divide the five guineas the
lieutenant have give I.
'there be your share,' say I, give the fellow one piece; which
be plenty, as I be the leader of the expedition. but he swear a
dreadful oath that he would have half; and when I tell he to go to
a quarter which I shall not name, the fellow, lift his musket,
hit I a blow with the butt-end of it, which send I lifeless to the
ground: when I awake from my> trance, I find myself bleed with a
large wound in the head, and have barely time to stagger back to the
house where I have leave the lieutenant, when I again fall faint at
the door.
here I must have be discover by the surgeon on his issue out;
for when I awake a second time I find myself in the ground-floor of
the house, support by the black-eyed girl, while the surgeon be
copiously bleed I at the arm. there be another bed in the room
where the lieutenant have be laid,--it be that occupy by Gretel,
the servant; while Lischen, as my fair one be call, have, till
now, sleep in the couch where the wound officer lay.
'who be you put into that bed?' say he languidly, in German;
for the ball have be extract from his side with much pain and
loss of blood.
they tell he it be the corporal who have bring he.
'a corporal?' say he, in English; 'turn he out.' and you may be
sure I feel highly compliment by the word. but we be both too
faint to compliment or to abuse each other much, and I be put to
bed carefully; and, on be undressed, have an opportunity to find
that my pocket have be rifle by the english soldier after he have
knock I down. however, I be in good quarter: the young lady who
shelter I presently bring I a refreshing drink; and, as I take
it, I could not help press the kind hand that give it I; nor, in
truth, do this token of my gratitude seem unwelcome.
this intimacy do not decrease with further acquaintance. I find
Lischen the tenderest of nurse. whenever any delicacy be to be
provide for the wound lieutenant, a share be always send to the
bed opposite his, and to the avaricious man's no small annoyance.
his illness be long. on the second day the fever declare itself;
for some night he be delirious; and I remember it be when a
commanding officer be inspect our quarter, with an intention,
very likely, of billet himself on the house, that the howl and
mad word of the patient overhead strike he, and he retire rather

that I begin to reflect how pleasant my quarter be to I, and
that I be much well here than crawl under an odious tent with
a parcel of tipsy soldier, or go the night-round or rise long
before daybreak for drill.
the delirium of Mr. Fakenham give I a hint, and I determine
forthwith to go MAD. there be a poor fellow about Brady's Town
call 'Wandering Billy,' whose insane prank I have often mimic
as a lad, and I again put they in practice. that night I make an
attempt upon Lischen, salute she with a yell and a grin which
frighten she almost out of her wit; and when anybody come I be
rave. the blow on the head have disorder my brain; the doctor be
ready to vouch for this fact. one night I whisper to he that I
be Julius Caesar, and consider he to be my affiance wife Queen
Cleopatra, which convince he of my insanity. indeed, if Her
Majesty have be like my Aesculapius, she must have have a carroty
beard, such as be rare in Egypt.
a movement on the part of the French speedily cause an advance on
our part. the town be evacuate, except by a few prussian troop,
whose surgeon be to visit the wound in the place; and, when we
be well, we be to be draft to our regiment. I determine that
I never would join mine again. my intention be to make for Holland,
almost the only neutral country of Europe in those time, and thence
to get a passage somehow to England, and home to dear old Brady's
Town.
if Mr. Fakenham be now alive, I here tender he my apology for my
conduct to he. he be very rich; he use I very ill. I manage to
frighten away his servant who come to attend he after the affair of
Warburg, and from that time would sometimes condescend to wait upon
the patient, who always treat I with scorn; but it be my object
to have he alone, and I bear his brutality with the utmost civility
and mildness, meditate in my own mind a very pretty return for all
his favour to I. nor be I the only person in the house to whom
the worthy gentleman be uncivil. he order the fair Lischen hither
and thither, make impertinent love to she, abuse her soup,
quarrel with her omelette, and grudge the money which be lay
out for his maintenance; so that our hostess detest he as much
as, I think, without vanity, she regard I.
for, if the truth must be tell, I have make very deep love to she
during my stay under her roof; as be always my way with woman, of
whatever age or degree of beauty. to a man who have to make his way
in the world, these dear girl can always be useful in one fashion
or another; never mind, if they repel your passion; at any rate,
they be not offend with your declaration of it, and only look
upon you with more favourable eye in consequence of your
misfortune. as for Lischen, I tell she such a pathetic story of my
life (a tale a great deal more romantic than that here narrated,--
for I do not restrict myself to the exact truth in that history, as
in these page I be bind to do), that I win the poor girl's heart
entirely, and, besides, make considerable progress in the german
language under her instruction. do not think I very cruel and
heartless, lady; this heart of Lischen's be like many a town in
the neighbourhood in which she dwelt, and have be storm and
occupy several time before I come to invest it; now mount
french colour, now green and yellow Saxon, now black and white
Prussian, as the case may be. a lady who set her heart upon a lad
in uniform must prepare to change lover pretty quickly, or her life
will be but a sad one.
the german surgeon who attend we after the departure of the
English only condescend to pay our house a visit twice during my

darken room, much to the annoyance of Mr. Fakenham, who lie there:
but I say the light affect my eye dreadfully since my blow on
the head; and so I cover up my head with clothe when the doctor

some insane nonsense, in order to keep up my character.
'what be that nonsense you be talk about an egyptian mummy,
fellow?' ask Mr. Fakenham peevishly.
'oh! youwill know soon, sir,' say I.
the next time that I expect the doctor to come, instead of
receive he in a darken room, with handkerchief muffle, I take

Lischen as the surgeon enter. I have take possession of a
dressing-jacket of the lieutenant's, and some other article of his

ungentlemanlike figure.
'good-morrow, Corporal,' say the doctor, rather gruffly, in reply
to my smile salute.
'Corporal! Lieutenant, if you please,' answer I, give an arch
look at Lischen, whom I have instruct in my plot.
'how lieutenant?' ask the surgeon. 'I think the lieutenant was'--
'upon my word, you do I great honour,' cry I, laugh; 'you
mistook I for the mad corporal upstairs. the fellow have once or
twice pretend to be an officer, but my kind hostess here can
answer which be which.'
'yesterday he fancy he be Prince Ferdinand,' say Lischen; 'the
