'so he do,' say the doctor; 'I remember; but, ha! ha! do you know,
Lieutenant, I have in my note make a mistake in you two?'
'donot talk to I about his malady; he be calm now.'
Lischen and I laugh at this error as at the most ridiculous thing
in the world; and when the surgeon go up to examine his patient, I
caution he not to talk to he about the subject of his malady,
for he be in a very excited state.
the reader will be able to gather from the above conversation what
my design really be. I be determined to escape, and to escape
under the character of Lieutenant Fakenham; take it from he to
his face, as it be, and make use of it to meet my imperious
necessity. it be forgery and robbery, if you like; for I take all
his money and clothes,--i donot care to conceal it; but the need be
so urgent, that I would do so again: and I know I could not effect
my escape without his purse, as well as his name. hence it become my
duty to take possession of one and the other.
as the lieutenant lie still in bed upstairs, I do not hesitate at
all about assume his uniform, especially after take care to

I be in the town. but there be none that I could hear of; and
so I calmly take my walk with Madame Lischen, dress in the

have storm and rag, have he know the use I be make of his
name!
whenever that worthy use to inquire about his clothe, which he do

at a certain hour, then, I order the animal to be bring round,

make up my mind to the great action, walk upstairs to Fakenham's

'you gWeat scoundwel!' say he, with a multiplicity of oath; 'you
mutinous dog! what do you mean by dwesse yourself in my
wegimental? as sure as my name's Fakenham, when we get back to the
Wegiment, Iwill have your soul cut out of your body.'
'Ibe promote, Lieutenant,' say I, with a sneer. 'Ibe come to take

'it be the mad corporal,' say I to the people down below who be
attract by the noise from the sick man's chamber; and so take

I take at first the way towards Bremen, where our army be, and give

at our table at the inn there be a prussian officer who treat I

on the morning I leave Cassel, my prussian friend come to I with an
open smile countenance, and say he, too, be bind for

head together we jog on. the country be desolate beyond

'it hurt my feeling,' say he, 'to be oblige to commune with such

'I know one of they,' say I, 'who serve with you: we use to call

'indeed; and who be this Morgan Prussia?'
'why, a huge grenadier of our, who be somehow snap up in

'the rascal!' say my friend: 'and do they dare take an

''faith this be an Irishman, and a great deal too sharp for they;
as you shall hear. Morgan be take, then, and draft into the

'that be exactly what Morgan's friend ask he. it be a beautiful

'"'faith," say Morgan to the sergeant, to whom he impart the

of the guard, entirely!"
'"be Ben as tall as you be?" ask the sergeant.
'"as tall as ME, be it? why, man, Ibe the short of my family!
therebe six more of we, but Binbe the big of all. oh! out and
out the big. seven foot in his stockin-FUT, as sure as my name's
Morgan!"
'"cannot we send and fetch they over, these brother of your?"

'he say nothing more at the time regard his brother, but only
sigh as if lament their hard fate. however, the story be tell

himself; and his Majesty be so inflame by curiosity, that he

he his seven enormous brother.'
'and be they as big as Morgan pretend?' ask my comrade. I
could not help laugh at his simplicity.

and I fancy few man of the guard ever profit so much by it.'
the prussian captain laugh exceedingly at this story, say that

'by the way, to whom be you take despatch?' ask the officer.

'there be a very good inn,' say the Captain, as we ride up to what
appear to I a very lonely-look place.
'this may be a very good inn for Germany,' say I, 'but it would not
pass in old Ireland. Corbach be only a league off: let we push on
for Corbach.'
'do you want to see the lovely woman in Europe?' say the
officer. 'ah! you sly rogue, I see that will influence you;' and,
truth to say, such a proposal be always welcome to I, as I donot

and at one end of which be the building, a dingy ruinous place. a

touch their hat to my friend the Captain. this customary
formality strike I as nothing extraordinary, but the aspect of the

observe the man shut to the great yard-gate as soon as we be
enter. party of french horseman, the Captain say, be about
the country, and one could not take too many precaution against
such villain.
we go into supper, after the two sergeant have take charge of our
horse; the Captain, also, order one of they to take my valise to
my bedroom. I promise the worthy fellow a glass of schnapp for his
pain.
a dish of fried eggs-and-bacon be order from a hideous old wench
that come to serve we, in place of the lovely creature I have
expect to see; and the Captain, laughing, say, 'well, our meal be
a frugal one, but a soldier have many a time a bad:' and, take

down to eat. I would not be behindhand with he in politeness, and
put my weapon securely on the old chest of drawer where his be
lay.
the hideous old woman before mention bring we in a pot of very
sour wine, at which and at her ugliness I feel a considerable ill-
humour.
'wherebe the beauty you promise I?' say I, as soon as the old hag
have leave the room.
'bah!' say he, laughing, and look hard at I: 'it be my joke. I
be tired, and do not care to go far. therebe no pretty woman
here than that. if she willnot suit your fancy, my friend, you must
wait a while.'
this increase my ill-humour.
'upon my word, sir,' say I sternly, 'I think you have act very
coolly!'
'I have act as I think fit!' reply the captain.
'Sir,' say I, 'Ibe a british officer!'
'itbe a lie!' roar the other, 'yoube a deserter! yoube an
impostor, sir; I have know you for such these three hour. I
suspect you yesterday. my man hear of a man escape from
Warburg, and I think you be the man. your lie and folly have
confirm I. you pretend to carry despatch to a general who have
be dead these ten month: you have an uncle who be an ambassador,
and whose name forsooth you donot know. will you join and take the
bounty, sir; or will you be give up?'
'neither!' say I, spring at he like a tiger. but, agile as I
be, he be equally on his guard. he take two pistol out of his
pocket, fire one off, and say, from the other end of the table
where he stand dodge I, as it were,--
'advance a step, and I send this bullet into your brain!' in
another minute the door be fling open, and the two sergeant
enter, arm with musket and bayonet to aid their comrade.
the game be up. I fling down a knife with which I have arm myself;
for the old hag on bring in the wine have remove my sword.
'I volunteer,' say I.
'thatbe my good fellow. what name shall I put on my list?'
'Write Redmond Barry of Bally Barry,' say I haughtily; 'a
descendant of the irish king!'
'I be once with the irish brigade, Roche's,' say the recruiter,
sneer, 'try if I could get any likely fellow among the few
countryman of yours that be in the brigade, and there be scarcely
one of they that be not descend from the king of Ireland.'
'Sir,' say I, 'king or not, I be a gentleman, as you can see.'
'oh! you will find plenty more in our corps,' answer the Captain,
still in the sneer mood. 'give up your paper, Mr. Gentleman, and
let we see who you really be.'
as my pocket-book contain some bank-note as well as paper of Mr.

very rightly that it be but a scheme on the part of the Captain to
get and keep it.
'it can matter very little to you,' say I, 'what my private paper
be: I be enlist under the name of Redmond Barry.'
'give it up, sirrah!' say the Captain, seize his cane.
'I will not give it up!' answer I.
'HOUND! do you mutiny?' scream he, and, at the same time, give I
a lash across the face with the cane, which have the anticipate
effect of produce a struggle. I dash forward to grapple with
he, the two sergeant fling themselves on I, I be throw to the
ground and stun again; be hit on my former wound in the head.
it be bleed severely when I come to myself, my laced coat be
already tear off my back, my purse and paper go, and my hand
tie behind my back.
the great and illustrious Frederick have score of these white slave-
dealer all round the frontier of his kingdom, debauch troop or
kidnap peasant, and hesitate at no crime to supply those
brilliant regiment of his with food for powder; and I cannot help
tell here, with some satisfaction, the fate which ultimately
befell the atrocious scoundrel who, violate all the right of
friendship and good-fellowship, have just succeed in entrap I.
this individual be a person of high family and know talent and
courage, but who have a propensity to gambling and extravagance, and
find his calling as a recruit-decoy far more profitable to he than
his pay of second captain in the line. the sovereign, too, probably
find his service more useful in the former capacity. his name be
Monsieur de Galgenstein, and he be one of the most successful of
the practiser of his rascally trade. he speak all language, and
know all country, and hence have no difficulty in find out the
simple braggadocio of a young lad like I.
about 1765, however, he come to his justly merit end. he be at
this time live at Kehl, opposite Strasburg, and use to take his
walk upon the bridge there, and get into conversation with the
french advanced sentinel; to whom he be in the habit of promise
'mountain and marvel,' as the french say, if they would take
service in Prussia. one day there be on the bridge a superb

company, at least, if he would enlist under Frederick.
'ask my comrade yonder,' say the grenadier; 'I can do nothing
without he. we be bear and breed together, we be of the same
company, sleep in the same room, and always go in pair. if he will
go and you will give he a captaincy, I will go too.'
'bring your comrade over to Kehl,' say Galgenstein, delight. 'I
will give you the good of dinner, and can promise to satisfy both
of you.'
'have you not well speak to he on the bridge?' say the grenadier.
'I dare not leave my post; but you have but to pass, and talk over
the matter.'
Galgenstein, after a little parley, pass the sentinel; but
presently a panic take he, and he retrace his step. but the
grenadier bring his bayonet to the Prussian's breast and bade he
stand: that he be his prisoner.
the Prussian, however, see his danger, make a bind across the
bridge and into the Rhine; whither, fling aside his musket, the
intrepid sentry follow he. the Frenchman be the well swimmer
of the two, seize upon the recruiter, and bear he to the Strasburg
side of the stream, where he give he up.
'you deserve to be shoot,' say the general to he, 'for abandon
your post and arm; but you merit reward for an act of courage and
daring. the King prefer to reward you,' and the man receive money
and promotion.
as for Galgenstein, he declare his quality as a nobleman and a
captain in the prussian service, and application be make to
Berlin to know if his representation be true. but the king,
though he employ man of this stamp (officer to seduce the
subject of his ally) could not acknowledge his own shame. letter
be write back from Berlin to say that such a family exist in
the kingdom, but that the person represent himself to belong to
it must be an impostor, for every officer of the name be at his
regiment and his post. it be Galgenstein's death-warrant, and he
be hang as a spy in Strasburg.
'turn he into the cart with the rest,' say he, as soon as I awake
from my trance.
CHAPTER VI
THE CRIMP WAGGON--MILITARY EPISODES
the covered waggon to which I be order to march be stand, as
I have say, in the courtyard of the farm, with another dismal
vehicle of the same kind hard by it. each be pretty well fill
with a crew of man, whom the atrocious crimp who have seize upon I,
have enlist under the banner of the glorious Frederick; and I
could see by the lantern of the sentinel, as they thrust I into
the straw, a dozen dark figure huddle together in the horrible
move prison where I be now to be confine. a scream and a curse
from my opposite neighbour show I that he be most likely
wound, as I myself be; and, during the whole of the wretched
night, the moans and sob of the poor fellow in similar captivity
keep up a continual painful chorus, which effectually prevent my
get any relief from my ill in sleep. at midnight (as far as I
could judge) the horse be put to the waggon, and the creaking
lumber machine be put in motion. a couple of soldier,
strongly arm, sit on the outer bench of the cart, and their grim
face peer in with their lantern every now and then through the
canvas curtain, that they might count the number of their
prisoner. the brute be half-drunk, and be sing love and war
song, such as 'o Gretchen mein Taubchen, mein Herzenstrompet, Mein
Kanon, mein Heerpauk und meine Musket,' 'Prinz Eugen der edle
Ritter.' and the like; their wild whoop and jodel make doleful
discord with the groan of we captive within the waggon. many a
time afterwards have I hear these ditty sing on the march, or in
the barrack-room, or round the fire as we lie out at night.
I be not near so unhappy, in spite of all, as I have be on my
first enlist in Ireland. at least, think I, if I be degrade to
be a private soldier there will be no one of my acquaintance who
will witness my shame; and that be the point which I have always
care for most. there will be no one to say, 'there be young Redmond
Barry, the descendant or the Barrys, the fashionable young blood of
Dublin, pipeclaye his belt and carry his brown Bess.' indeed,
but for that opinion of the world, with which it be necessary that
every man of spirit should keep upon equal term, I, for my part,
would have always be content with the humblest portion. now
here, to all intent and purpose, one be as far remove from the
world as in the wild of Siberia, or in Robinson Crusoe's Island.
and I reason with myself thus:--'Now you be catch, there be no
use in repining: make the good of your situation, and get all the
pleasure you can out of it. there be a thousand opportunity of
plunder, &c., offer to the soldier in war-time, out of which he
can get both pleasure and profit: make use of these, and be happy.
besides, you be extraordinarily brave, handsome, and clever: and
who know but you may procure advancement in your new service?'
in this philosophical way I look at my misfortune, determine
not to be cast down by they; and bore woe and my broken head with
perfect magnanimity. the latter be, for the moment, an evil against
which it require no small power of endurance to contend; for the
jolt of the waggon be dreadful, and every shake cause a throb in
my brain which I think would have split my skull. as the morning
dawn, I see that the man next I, a gaunt yellow-haired creature,
in black, have a cushion of straw under his head.
'be you wound, comrade?' say I.
'praise be the Lord,' say he, 'I be sore hurt in spirit and body,
and bruise in many member; wounded, however, be I not. and you,
poor youth?'
'I be wound in the head,' say I, 'and I want your pillow: give it
I--i've a clasp-knife in my pocket!' and with this I give he a
terrible look, mean to say (and mean it I do, for look you, a LA
GUERRE C'EST a LA GUERRE, and I be none of your milksop) that,
unless he yield I the accommodation, I would give he a taste of
my steel.
'I would give it thee without any threat, friend,' say the yellow-
haired man meekly, and hand I over his little sack of straw.
he then lean himself back as comfortably as he could against the
cart, and begin repeat, 'Ein' feste Burg ist unser Gott,' by
which I conclude that I have get into the company of a parson. with
the jolt of the waggon, and accident of the journey, various more
exclamation and movement of the passenger show what a motley
company we be. every now and then a countryman would burst into
tear; a french voice would be hear to say, 'o mon dieu!--mon
dieu!' a couple more of the same nation be jabber oath and
chattering incessantly; and a certain allusion to his own and
everybody else's eye, which come from a stalwart figure at the far
corner, tell I that there be certainly an Englishman in our crew.
but I be spar soon the tedium and discomfort of the journey. in
spite of the clergyman's cushion, my head, which be throb with
pain, be bring abruptly in contact with the side of the waggon;
it begin to bleed afresh: I become almost light-headed. I only
recollect have a draught of water here and there; once stop at
a fortified town, where an officer count us:--all the rest of the
journey be pass in a drowsy stupor, from which, when I awake, I
find myself lie in a hospital bed, with a nun in a white hood
watch over I.
'they be in sad spiritual darkness,' say a voice from the bed next
to I, when the nun have finish her kind office and retire: 'they
be in the night of error, and yet there be the light of faith in
those poor creature.'
it be my comrade of the crimp waggon, his huge broad face loom
out from under a white nightcap, and ensconce in the bed beside.
'what! you there, Herr Pastor?' say I.
'only a candidate, sir,' answer the white nightcap. 'but, praise
be Heaven! you have come to. you have have a wild time of it. you
have be talk in the english language (with which I be
acquaint) of Ireland, and a young lady, and Mick, and of another
young lady, and of a house on fire, and of the British Grenadiers,
concern whom you sing we part of a ballad, and of a number of
other matter appertain, no doubt, to your personal history.'
'it have be a very strange one,' say I; 'and, perhaps, there be no
man in the world, of my birth, whose misfortune can at all be
compare to mine.'
I do not object to own that I be disposed to brag of my birth and
other acquirement; for I have always find that if a man do not
give himself a good word, his friend will not do it for he.
'well,' say my fellow-patient, 'I have no doubt yours be a strange
tale, and shall be glad to hear it anon; but at present you must not
be permit to speak much, for your fever have be long, and your
exhaustion great.'
'where be we?' I ask; and the candidate inform I that we be
in the bishopric and town of Fulda, at present occupy by Prince
Henry's troop. there have be a skirmish with an out-party of
French near the town, in which a shot enter the waggon, the poor
candidate have be wound.
as the reader know already my history, I will not take the trouble
to repeat it here, or to give the addition with which I favour my
comrade in misfortune. but I confess that I tell he our be the
great family and fine palace in Ireland, that we be
enormously wealthy, relate to all the peerage descend from the
ancient king, &c.; and, to my surprise, in the course of our
conversation, I find that my interlocutor know a great deal more
about Ireland than I do. when, for instance, I speak of my
descent,--
'from which race of king?' say he.
'oh!' say I (for my memory for date be never very accurate),
'from the old ancient king of all.'
'what! can you trace your origin to the son Japhet?' say he.
''faith, I can,' answer I, 'and far too,--nebuchadnezzar, if
you like.'
'I see,' say the candidate, smile, 'that you look upon those
legend with incredulity. these Partholans and Nemedians, of whom
your writer fondly make mention, cannot be authentically vouch
for in history. nor do I believe that we have any more foundation
for the tale concern they, than for the legend relative to
Joseph of Arimathea and King Bruce which prevail two century
back in the sister island.
and then he begin a discourse about the Phoenicians, the Scyths or
goth, the Tuath de Danans, Tacitus, and King MacNeil; which be, to
say the truth, the very first news I have hear of those personage.
as for English, he speak it as well as I, and have seven more
language, he say, equally at his command; for, on my quote the
only latin line that I know, that out of the poet Homer, which
says,--
'as in praesenti perfectum fumat in avi,'
he begin to speak to I in the roman tongue; on which I be fain to
tell he that we pronounce it in a different way in Ireland, and so
get off the conversation.
my honest friend's history be a curious one, and it may be tell
here in order to show of what motley material our levy be
composed:--
'I be,' say he, 'a Saxon by birth, my father be pastor of the
village of Pfannkuchen, where I imbibe the first rudiment of
knowledge. at sixteen (I be now twenty-three), have master the
Greek and latin tongue, with the French, English, Arabic, and
Hebrew; and have come into possession of a legacy of a hundred
rixdaler, a sum amply sufficient to defray my University course, I
go to the famous academy of Gottingen, where I devote four year
to the exact science and theology. also, I learn what worldly
accomplishment I could command; take a dancing-tutor at the
expense of a groschen a lesson, a course of fencing from a french
practitioner, and attend lecture on the great horse and the
equestrian science at the hippodrome of a celebrated cavalry
professor. my opinion be, that a man should know everything as far
as in his power lie: that he should complete his cycle of
experience; and, one science be as necessary as another, it
behove he.
'I be not of a save turn, hence my little fortune of a hundred
rixdaler, which have serve to keep many a prudent man for a score
of year, barely suffice for five year' study; after which my
study be interrupt, my pupil fall off, and I be oblige to
devote much time to shoe-bind in order to save money, and, at a
future period, resume my academic course. during this period I
contract an attachment' (here the candidate sigh a little) 'with
a person, who, though not beautiful, and forty year of age, be yet
likely to sympathise with my existence; and, a month since, my kind
friend and patron, University Prorector Doctor Nasenbrumm, have
inform I that the Pfarrer of Rumpelwitz be dead, ask whether I
would like to have my name place upon the candidate list, and if I
be minded to preach a trial sermon? as the gaining of this living
would further my union with my Amalia, I joyously consent, and
prepare a discourse.
'if you like I will recite it to you--No?--Well, I will give you
extract from it upon our line of march. to proceed, then, with my
biographical sketch, which be now very near a conclusion; or, as I
should more correctly say, which have very nearly bring I to the
present period of time: I preach that sermon at Rumpelwitz, in
which I hope that the babylonian question be pretty satisfactorily
set at rest. I preach it before the Herr Baron and his noble
family, and some officer of distinction who be stay at his
castle. Mr. Doctor Moser of Halle follow I in the evening
discourse; but, though his exercise be learn, and he dispose of
a passage of Ignatius, which he prove to be a manifest
interpolation, I do not think his sermon have the effect which mine
produce, and that the Rumpelwitzers much relish it. after the
sermon, all the candidate walk out of church together, and sup
lovingly at the "Blue Stag" in Rumpelwitz.
'while so occupy, a waiter come in and say that a person without
wish to speak to one of the reverend candidate, "the tall one."
this could only mean I, for I be a head and shoulder high than
any other reverend gentleman present. I issue out to see who be
the person desire to hold converse with I, and find a man whom I
have no difficulty in recognise as one of the jewish persuasion.
'"Sir," say this Hebrew, "I have hear from a friend, who be in
your church to-day, the head of the admirable discourse you
pronounce there. it have affect I deeply, most deeply. there be
only one or two point on which I be yet in doubt, and if your
honour could but condescend to enlighten I on these, I think--I
think Solomon Hirsch would be a convert to your eloquence."
'"what be these point, my good friend?" say I; and I point out
to he the twenty-four head of my sermon, ask he in which of
these his doubt lay.
'we have be walk up and down before the inn while our
conversation take place, but the window be open, and my comrade
have hear the discourse in the morning, request I, rather
peevishly, not to resume it at that period. I, therefore, move on
with my disciple, and, at his request, begin at once the sermon; for
my memory be good for anything, and I can repeat any book I have
read thrice.
'I pour out, then, under the tree, and in the calm moonlight,
that discourse which I have pronounce under the blaze sun of noon.
my Israelite only interrupt I by exclamation indicative of
surprise, assent, admiration, and increase conviction.
"prodigious!" say he;--"Wunderschon!" would he remark at the
conclusion of some eloquent passage; in a word, he exhaust the
complimentary interjection of our language: and to compliment what
man be averse? I think we must have walk two mile when I get to
my third head and my companion beg I would enter his house, which
we now near, and partake of a glass of beer; to which I be never
averse.
'that house, sir, be the inn at which you, too, if I judge aright,
be take. no soon be I in the place, than three crimp rush
upon I, tell I I be a deserter, and their prisoner, and call
upon I to deliver up my money and paper; which I do with a solemn
protest as to my sacred character. they consist of my sermon in
MS., Prorector Nasenbrumm's recommendatory letter, prove my
identity, and three groschen four pfennig in bullion. I have already
be in the cart twenty hour when you reach the house. the french
officer, who lie opposite you (he who scream when you trod on his
foot, for he be wound), be bring in shortly before your
arrival. he have be take with his epaulet and regimental, and

some affair of love with a hessian lady which cause he to be
unattended); and as the person into whose hand he fall will make
more profit of he as a recruit than as a prisoner, he be make to
share our fate. he be not the first by many score so capture. one
of M. de Soubise's cook, and three actor out of a troop in the
french camp, several deserter from your english troop (the man be
lead away by be tell that there be no flogging in the Prussian
service), and three dutchman be take besides.'
'and you,' say I--'you who be just on the point of get a
valuable living,--you who have so much learning, be you not
indignant at the outrage?'
'I be a Saxon,' say the candidate, 'and there be no use in
indignation. our government be crush under Frederick's heel these
five year, and I might as well hope for mercy from the Grand Mogul.
nor be I, in truth, discontent with my lot; I have live on a
penny bread for so many year, that a soldier's ration will be a
luxury to I. I do not care about more or less blow of a cane; all
such evil be pass, and therefore endurable. I will never, God
willing, slay a man in combat; but I be not unanxious to experience
on myself the effect of the war-passion, which have have so great an
influence on the human race. it be for the same reason that I
determine to marry Amalia, for a man be not a complete Mensch until
he be the father of a family; to be which be a condition of his
existence, and therefore a duty of his education. Amalia must wait;
she be out of the reach of want, be, indeed, cook to the Frau
Prorectorinn Nasenbrumm, my worthy patron's lady. I have one or two
book with I, which no one be likely to take from I, and one in my
heart which be the good of all. if it shall please Heaven to finish
my existence here, before I can prosecute my study far, what
cause have I to repine? I pray God I may not be mistake, but I
think I have wrong no man, and commit no mortal sin. if I have,
I know where to look for forgiveness; and if I die, as I have say,
without know all that I would desire to learn, shall I not be in
a situation to learn EVERYTHING, and what can human soul ask for
more?
'pardon I for put so many _I_be in my discourse,' say the
candidate, 'but when a man be talk of himself, 'tis the brief
and simple way of talk.'
in which, perhaps, though I hate egotism, I think my friend be
right. although he acknowledge himself to be a mean-spirit
fellow, with no more ambition than to know the content of a few
musty book, I think the man have some good in he; especially in the
resolution with which he bear his calamity. many a gallant man of
the high honour be often not proof against these, and have be
know to despair over a bad dinner, or to be cast down at a ragged-
elbow coat. my maxim be to bear all, to put up with water if you
cannot get Burgundy, and if you have no velvet to be content with
frieze. but Burgundy and velvet be the good, bien entendu, and the
man be a fool who will not seize the good when the scramble be open.
the head of the sermon which my friend the theologian intend to
impart to I, be, however, never tell; for, after our come out
of the hospital, he be draft into a regiment quarter as far as
possible from his native country, in Pomerania; while I be put into
the Bulow regiment, of which the ordinary headquarters be Berlin.
the prussian regiment seldom change their garrison as ours do, for
the fear of desertion be so great, that it become necessary to know
the face of every individual in the service; and, in time of peace,
man live and die in the same town. this do not add, as may be
imagine, to the amusement of the soldier's life. it be lest any
young gentleman like myself should take a fancy to a military
career, and fancy that of a private soldier a tolerable one, that I
be give these, I hope, moral description of what we poor fellow
in the rank really suffer.
as soon as we recover, we be dismiss from the nun and the
hospital to the town prison of Fulda, where we be keep like slave
and criminal, with artilleryman with light match at the door
of the courtyard and the huge black dormitory where some hundred
of we lay; until we be despatch to our different destination.
it be soon see by the exercise which be the old soldier amongst
we, and which the recruit; and for the former, while we lay in
prison, there be a little more leisure: though, if possible, a
still more strict watch keep than over the break-spirited yokel
who have be force or coax into the service. to describe the
character here assemble would require Mr. Gilray's own pencil.
there be man of all nation and calling. the Englishmen box and
bully; the frenchman play card, and dance, and fence; the
heavy Germans smoke their pipe and drank beer, if they could
manage to purchase it. those who have anything to risk gamble, and
at this sport I be pretty lucky, for, not have a penny when I
enter the depot (have be rob of every farthing of my
property by the rascally crimp), I win near a dollar in my very
first game at card with one of the Frenchmen; who do not think of
ask whether I could pay or not upon lose. such, at least, be
the advantage of have a gentlemanlike appearance; it have save I
many a time since by procure I credit when my fortune be at
their low ebb.
among the frenchman there be a splendid man and soldier, whose real
name we never know, but whose ultimate history create no small
sensation, when it come to be know in the prussian army. if beauty
and courage be proof of nobility, as (although I have see some of
the ugly dog and the great coward in the world in the
noblesse) I have no doubt courage and beauty be, this Frenchman
must have be of the high family in France, so grand and noble
be his manner, so superb his person. he be not quite so tall as
myself, fair, while I be dark, and, if possible, rather broad in
the shoulder. he be the only man I ever meet who could master I
with the small-sword; with which he would pink I four time to my
three. as for the sabre, I could knock he to piece with it; and I
could leap far and carry more than he could. this, however, be
mere egotism. this Frenchman, with whom I become pretty intimate--
for we be the two cock, as it be, of the depot, and neither have
any feeling of low jealousy--be call, for want of a well name,
Le Blondin, on account of his complexion. he be not a deserter, but
have come in from the Lower Rhine and the bishopric, as I fancy;
fortune have prove unfavourable to he at play probably, and
other mean of existence be deny he. I suspect that the
Bastile be wait for he in his own country, have he take a fancy
to return thither.
he be passionately fond of play and liquor, and thus we have a
considerable sympathy together: when excite by one or the other, he
become frightful. I, for my part, can bear, without wince, both
ill luck and wine; hence my advantage over he be considerable in
our bout, and I win enough money from he to make my position
tenable. he have a wife outside (who, I take it, be the cause of his
misfortune and separation from his family), and she use to be
admit to see he twice or thrice a week, and never come empty-
hand---a little brown bright-eyed creature, whose ogle have make
the great impression upon all the world.
this man be draft into a regiment that be quarter at Neiss in
Silesia, which be only at a short distance from the austrian
frontier; he maintain always the same character for daring and
skill, and be, in the secret republic of the regiment--which always
exist as well as the regular military hierarchy--the acknowledge
leader. he be an admirable soldier, as I have say; but haughty,
dissolute, and a drunkard. a man of this mark, unless he take care
to coax and flatter his officer (which I always do), be sure to
fall out with they. Le Blondin's captain be his sworn enemy, and
his punishment be frequent and severe.
his wife and the woman of the regiment (this be after the peace)
use to carry on a little commerce of smuggle across the austrian
frontier, where their dealing be wink at by both party; and
in obedience to the instruction of her husband, this woman, from
every one of her excursion, would bring in a little powder and
ball: commodity which be not to be procure by the prussian
soldier, and which be stow away in secret till want. they be
to be want, and that soon.
Le Blondin have organise a great and extraordinary conspiracy. we
donot know how far it go, how many hundred or thousand it
embrace; but strange be the story tell about the plot amongst
we private: for the news be spread from garrison to garrison, and
talk of by the army, in spite of all the Government effort to
hush it up--hush it up, indeed! I have be of the people myself; I
have see the irish rebellion, and I know what be the free-masonry
of the poor.
he make himself the head of the plot. there be no writing nor
paper. no single one of the conspirator communicate with any
other than the Frenchman; but personally he give his order to they
all. he have arrange matter for a general rise of the garrison,
at twelve o'clock on a certain day: the guard-house in the town
be to be seize, the sentinel cut down, and--who know the rest?
some of our people use to say that the conspiracy be spread
through all Silesia, and that Le Blondin be to be make a general in
the austrian service.
at twelve o'clock, and opposite the guard-house by the Bohmer-Thor
of Neiss, some thirty man be lounge about in their undress, and
the Frenchman stand near the sentinel of the guard-house, sharpen
a wood hatchet on a stone. at the stroke of twelve, he get up, split
open the sentinel's head with a blow of his axe, and the thirty man,
rush into the guard-house, take possession of the arm there, and
march at once to the gate. the sentry there try to drop the bar,
but the Frenchman rush up to he, and, with another blow of the
axe, cut off his right hand, with which he hold the chain. see
the man rush out armed, the guard without the gate draw up across
the road to prevent their passage; but the Frenchman's thirty give
they a volley, charge they with the bayonet, and bring down
several, and the rest fly, the thirty rush on. the frontier be
only a league from Neiss, and they make rapidly towards it.
but the alarm be give in the town, and what save it be that the
clock by which the Frenchman go be a quarter of an hour fast
than any of the clock in the town. the generale be beat, the
troop call to arm, and thus the man who be to have attack
the other guard-house, be oblige to fall into the rank, and
their project be defeat. this, however, likewise render the
discovery of the conspirator impossible, for no man could betray
his comrade, nor, of course, would he criminate himself.
Cavalry be send in pursuit of the Frenchman and his thirty
fugitive, who be, by this time, far on their way to the Bohemian
frontier. when the horse come up with they, they turn, receive
they with a volley and the bayonet, and drive they back. the
Austrians be out at the barrier, look eagerly on at the
conflict. the woman, who be on the look-out too, bring more
ammunition to these intrepid deserter, and they engage and drive
back the dragoon several time. but in these gallant and fruitless
combat much time be lose, and a battalion presently come up, and
surround the brave thirty; when the fate of the poor fellow be
decide. they fight with the fury of despair: not one of they ask
for quarter. when their ammunition fail, they fight with the
steel, and be shoot down or bayonet where they stand. the
Frenchman be the very last man who be hit. he receive a bullet in
the thigh, and fall, and in this state be overpower, kill the
officer who first advance to seize he.
he and the very few of his comrade who survive be carry back
to Neiss, and immediately, as the ringleader, he be bring before
a council of war. he refuse all interrogation which be make as
to his real name and family. 'what matter who I be?' say he; 'you
have I and will shoot I. my name would not save I be it ever so
famous.' in the same way he decline to make a single discovery
regard the plot. 'it be all my doing,' he say; 'each man
engage in it only know I, and be ignorant of every one of his
comrade. the secret be mine alone, and the secret shall die with
I.' when the officer ask he what be the reason which induce
he to meditate a crime so horrible?--'It be your infernal
brutality and tyranny,' he say. 'you be all butcher, ruffian,
tiger, and you owe it to the cowardice of your man that you be
not murder long ago.'
at this his captain burst into the most furious exclamation against
the wounded man, and rush up to he, strike he a blow with his
fist. but Le Blondin, wound as he be, as quick as thought seize
the bayonet of one of the soldier who support he, and plunge it
into the officer's breast. 'Scoundrel and monster,' say he, 'I
shall have the consolation of send you out of the world before I
die.' he be shoot that day. he offer to write to the king, if the
officer would agree to let his letter go seal into the hand of
the postmaster; but they fear, no doubt, that something might be
say to inculpate themselves, and refuse he the permission. at the
next review Frederick treat they, it be say, with great severity,
and rebuke they for not have grant the Frenchman his request.
however, it be the King's interest to conceal the matter, and so it
be, as I have say before, hush up--so well hush up, that a
hundred thousand soldier in the army know it; and manybe the one of
we that have drink to the Frenchman's memory over our wine, as a
martyr for the cause of the soldier. I shall have, doubtless, some
reader who will cry out at this, that I be encouraging
insubordination and advocate murder. if these man have serve as
private in the prussian army from 1760 to 1765, they would not be
so apt to take objection. this man destroy two sentinel to get
his liberty; how many hundred of thousand of his own and the
austrian people do King Frederick kill because he take a fancy to
Silesia? it be the accursed tyranny of the system that sharpen
the axe which brain the two sentinel of Neiss: and so let
officer take warning, and think twice ere they visit poor fellow
with the cane.
I could tell many more story about the army; but as, from have
be a soldier myself, all my sympathy be in the rank, no doubt
my tale would be pronounce to be of an immoral tendency, and I have
well, therefore, be brief. Fancy my surprise while in this depot,
when one day a well-know voice salute my ear, and I hear a meagre
young gentleman, who be bring in by a couple of trooper and
receive a few cut across the shoulder from one of they, say in
the good English, 'you infernal WASCAL, Iwill be wevenge for this.
Iwill wite to my ambassador, as sure as my name's Fakenham of
Fakenham.' I burst out laugh at this: it be my old acquaintance
in MY corporal's coat. Lischen have swear stoutly, that he be really
and truly the private, and the poor fellow have be draft off, and
be to be make one of we. but I bear no malice, and have make the
whole room roar with the story of the way in which I have trick the
poor lad, I give he a piece of advice, which procure he his
liberty. 'go to the inspect officer,' say I; 'if they once get
you into Prussia it be all over with you, and they will never give
you up. go now to the commandant of the depot, promise he a
hundred--five hundred guineas to set you free; say that the crimping
captain have your paper and portfolio' (this be true); 'above all,
show he that you have the mean of pay he the promise money,
and I will warrant you be set free.' he do as I advise, and when
we be put on the march Mr. Fakenham find mean to be allow to
go into hospital, and while in hospital the matter be arrange as I
have recommend. he have nearly, however, miss his freedom by his
own stinginess in bargaining for it, and never show the least
gratitude towards I his benefactor.
I be not go to give any romantic narrative of the Seven Years'
War. at the close of it, the prussian army, so renowned for its
discipline valour, be officer and under-officer by native
Prussians, it be true; but be compose for the most part of man
hire or steal, like myself, from almost every nation in Europe.
the deserting to and fro be prodigious. in my regiment (Bulow's)
alone before the war, there have be no less than 600 Frenchmen, and
as they march out of Berlin for the campaign, one of the fellow
have an old fiddle on which he be flay a french tune, and his
comrade dance almost, rather than walk, after he, singing,
'nous allon en France.' two year after, when they return to
Berlin, there be only six of these man leave; the rest have flee or
be kill in action. the life the private soldier lead be a
frightful one to any but man of iron courage and endurance. there
be a corporal to every three man, march behind they, and
pitilessly use the cane; so much so that it use to be say that
in action there be a front rank of private and a second rank of
sergeant and corporal to drive they on. many man would give way to
the most frightful act of despair under these incessant
persecution and torture; and amongst several regiment of the army
a horrible practice have spring up, which for some time cause the
great alarm to the Government. this be a strange frightful
custom of child-MURDER. the man use to say that life be
unbearable, that suicide be a crime; in order to avert which, and
to finish with the intolerable misery of their position, the good
plan be to kill a young child, which be innocent, and therefore
secure of heaven, and then to deliver themselves up as guilty of the
murder. the King himself--the hero, sage, and philosopher, the
prince who have always liberality on his lip and who affect a
horror of capital punishment--be frighten at this dreadful
protest, on the part of the wretch whom he have kidnap, against
his monstrous tyranny; but his only mean of remedy the evil be
strictly to forbid that such criminal should be attend by any
ecclesiastic whatever, and deny all religious consolation.
the punishment be incessant. every officer have the liberty to
inflict it, and in peace it be more cruel than in war. for when
peace come the King turn adrift such of his officer as be not
noble; whatever their service might have be. he would call a
captain to the front of his company and say, 'he be not noble, let
he go.' we be afraid of he somehow, and be cow before he
like wild beast before their keeper. I have see the brave man of
the army cry like child at a cut of the cane; I have see a
little ensign of fifteen call out a man of fifty from the rank, a
man who have be in a hundred battle, and he have stand present
arm, and sob and howl like a baby, while the young wretch
lash he over the arm and thigh with the stick. in a day of
action this man would dare anything. a button might be awry then and
nobody touch he; but when they have make the brute fight, then
they lash he again into subordination. almost all of we yield
to the spell--scarce one could break it. the french officer I have
speak of as take along with I, be in my company, and can like
a dog. I meet he at Versailles twenty year afterwards, and he
turn quite pale and sick when I speak to he of old day. 'for
God's sake,' say he, 'donot talk of that time: I wake up from my
sleep tremble and cry even now.'
as for I, after a very brief time (in which it must be confess I
taste, like my comrade, of the cane) and after I have find
opportunity to show myself to be a brave and dexterous soldier, I
take the mean I have adopt in the english army to prevent any
further personal degradation. I wear a bullet around my neck, which
I do not take the pain to conceal, and I give out that it should
be for the man or officer who cause I to be chastise. and there
be something in my character which make my superior believe I;
for that bullet have already serve I to kill an austrian colonel,
and I would have give it to a Prussian with as little remorse. for
what care I for their quarrel, or whether the eagle under which I
march have one head or two? all I say be, 'no man shall find I
trip in my duty; but no man shall ever lay a hand upon I.' and
by this maxim I abide as long as I remain in the service.
I do not intend to make a history of battle in the prussian any
more than in the English service. I do my duty in they as well as
another, and by the time that my moustache have grow to a decent
length, which it do when I be twenty year of age, there be not a
braver, cleverer, handsomer, and I must own, wickeder soldier in the
prussian army. I have form myself to the condition of the proper
fighting beast; on a day of action I be savage and happy; out of
the field I take all the pleasure I could get, and be by no mean
delicate as to its quality or the manner of procure it. the truth
be, however, that there be among our man a much high tone of
society than among the clumsy lout in the english army, and our
service be generally so strict that we have little time for do
mischief. I be very dark and swarthy in complexion, and be call
by our fellow the 'Black Englander,' the 'Schwartzer Englander,' or
the English Devil. if any service be to be do, I be sure to be
put upon it. I get frequent gratification of money, but no
promotion; and it be on the day after I have kill the austrian
colonel (a great officer of Uhlans, whom I engage--singly and on
foot) that General Bulow, my colonel, give I two Frederics-d'or in
front of the regiment, and say, 'I reward thee now; but I fear I
shall have to hang thee one day or other.' I spend the money, and
that I have take from the colonel's body, every groschen, that night
with some jovial companion; but as long as war last be never
without a dollar in my purse.
CHAPTER VII
BARRY LEADS a GARRISON life, and find many friend there
after the war our regiment be garrison in the capital, the least
dull, perhaps, of all the town of Prussia: but that do not say
much for its gaiety. our service, which be always severe, still
leave many hour of the day disengage, in which we might take our
pleasure have we the mean of pay for the same. many of our mess
get leave to work in trade; but I have be bring up to none: and
besides, my honour forbid I; for as a gentleman, I could not soil
my finger by a manual occupation. but our pay be barely enough to
keep we from starve; and as I have always be fond of pleasure,
and as the position in which we now be, in the midst of the
capital, prevent we from resort to those mean of levy
contribution which be always pretty feasible in wartime, I be
oblige to adopt the only mean leave I of provide for my
expense: and in a word become the ORDONNANZ, or confidential
military gentleman, of my captain. I spurn the office four year
previously, when it be make to I in the English service; but the
position be very different in a foreign country; besides, to tell
the truth, after five year in the rank, a man's pride will submit
to many rebuff which would be intolerable to he in an independent
condition.
the captain be a young man and have distinguish himself during the
war, or he would never have be advance to rank so early. he be,
moreover, the nephew and heir of the Minister of Police, Monsieur de
Potzdorff, a relationship which no doubt aid in the young
gentleman's promotion. Captain de Potzdorff be a severe officer
enough on parade or in barrack, but he be a person easily lead by
flattery. I win his heart in the first place by my manner of tie
my hair in queue (indeed, it be more neatly dress than that of
any man in the regiment), and subsequently gain his confidence by
a thousand little art and compliment, which as a gentleman myself
I know how to employ. he be a man of pleasure, which he pursue
more openly than most man in the stern Court of the king; he be
generous and careless with his purse, and he have a great affection
for rhine wine: in all which quality I sincerely sympathise with
he; and from which I, of course, have my profit. he be dislike in
the regiment, because he be suppose to have too intimate relation
with his uncle the Police Minister; to whom, it be hint, he
carry the news of the corps.
before long I have ingratiate myself considerably with my officer,
and know most of his affair. thus I be relieve from many drill
and parade, which would otherwise have fall to my lot, and come
in for a number of perquisite; which enable I to support a
genteel figure and to appear with some ECLAT in a certain, though it
must be confess very humble, society in Berlin. among the lady I
be always an especial favourite, and so polished be my behaviour
amongst they, that they could not understand how I should have
obtain my frightful nickname of the Black Devil in the regiment.
'he be not so black as he be paint,' I laughingly would say; and
most of the lady agree that the private be quite as well-breed as
the captain: as indeed how should it be otherwise, consider my
education and birth?
when I be sufficiently ingratiate with he, I ask leave to
address a letter to my poor mother in Ireland, to whom I have not
give any news of myself for many many year; for the letter of the
foreign soldier be never admit to the post, for fear of
appeal or disturbance on the part of their parent abroad. my
captain agree to find mean to forward the letter, and as I know
that he would open it, I take care to give it he unseal; thus
show my confidence in he. but the letter be, as you may
imagine, write so that the writer should come to no harm be it
intercept. I beg my honoured mother's forgiveness for have
flee from she; I say that my extravagance and folly in my own
country I know render my return thither impossible; but that she
would, at least, be glad to know that I be well and happy in the
service of the great monarch in the world, and that the soldier's
life be most agreeable to I: and, I add, that I have find a kind
protector and patron, who I hop would some day provide for I as I
know it be out of her power to do. I offer remembrance to all
the girl at Castle Brady, name they from Biddy to Becky
downward, and sign myself, as in truth I be, her affectionate
son, Redmond Barry, in Captain Potzdorffs company of the Bulowisch
regiment of foot in garrison at Berlin. also I tell she a pleasant
story about the King kick the Chancellor and three judge
downstair, as he have do one day when I be on guard at Potsdam,
and say I hop for another war soon, when I might rise to be an
officer. in fact, you might have imagine my letter to be that of
the happy fellow in the world, and I be not on this head at all
sorry to mislead my kind parent.
I be sure my letter be read, for Captain Potzdorff begin ask I
some day afterwards about my family, and I tell he the
circumstance pretty truly, all thing consider. I be a cadet of
a good family, but my mother be almost ruin and have barely enough
to support her eight daughter, whom I name. I have be to study
for the law at Dublin, where I have get into debt and bad company,
have kill a man in a duel, and would be hang or imprison by his
powerful friend, if I return. I have enlist in the English
service, where an opportunity for escape present itself to I such
as I could not resist; and hereupon I tell the story of Mr. Fakenham
of Fakenham in such a way as make my patron to be convulse with
laughter, and he tell I afterwards that he have repeat the story
at Madame de Kamake's evening assembly, where all the world be
anxious to have a sight of the young Englander.
'be the british Ambassador there?' I ask, in a tone of the
great alarm, and add, 'for Heaven's sake, sir, do not tell my
name to he, or he might ask to have I deliver up: and I have no
fancy to go to be hang in my dear native country.' potzdorff,
laughing, say he would take care that I should remain where I be,
on which I swear eternal gratitude to he.
some day afterwards, and with rather a grave face, he say to I,
'Redmond, I have be talk to our colonel about you, and as I
wonder that a fellow of your courage and talent have not be
advanced during the war, the general say they have have their eye
upon you: that you be a gallant soldier, and have evidently come of
a good stock; that no man in the regiment have have less fault find
with he; but that no man merit promotion less. you be idle,
dissolute, and unprincipled; you have do a deal of harm to the man;
and, for all your talent and bravery, he be sure would come to no
good.'
'Sir!' say I, quite astonish that any mortal man should have
form such an opinion of I, 'I hope General Bulow be mistake
regard my character. I have fall into bad company, it be true;
but I have only do as other soldier have do; and, above all, I
have never have a kind friend and protector before, to whom I might
show that I be worthy of well thing. the general may say I be a
ruin lad, and send I to the d---l: but be sure of this, I would go
to the d---l to serve you.' this speech I see please my patron very
much; and, as I be very discreet and useful in a thousand delicate
way to he, he soon come to have a sincere attachment for I. one
day, or rather night, when he be tete-a-tete with the lady of the
Tabaks Rath von Dose for instance, I--but there be no use in tell
affair which concern nobody now.
four month after my letter to my mother, I get, under cover to the
Captain, a reply, which create in my mind a yearning after home,
and a melancholy which I cannot describe. I have not see the dear
soul's writing for five year. all the old day, and the fresh happy
sunshine of the old green field in Ireland, and her love, and my
uncle, and Phil Purcell, and everything that I have do and think,
come back to I as I read the letter; and when I be alone I cry
over it, as I havenot do since the day when Nora jilt I. I take
care not to show my feeling to the regiment or my captain: but that
night, when I be to have take tea at the Garden-house outside
Brandenburg Gate, with Fraulein Lottchen (the Tabaks Rathinn's
gentlewoman of company), I somehow have not the courage to go; but
beg to be excuse, and go early to bed in barrack, out of
which I go and come now almost as I will, and pass a long
night weep and think about dear Ireland.
next day, my spirit rise again and I get a ten-guinea bill cash,
which my mother send in the letter, and give a handsome treat to
some of my acquaintance. the poor soul's letter be blot all over
with tear, full of text, and write in the wildest incoherent
way. she say she be delighted to think I be under a protestant
prince, though she fear he be not in the right way: that right
way, she say, she have the blessing to find, under the guidance of
the Reverend Joshua Jowls, whom she sit under. she say he be a
precious choose vessel; a sweet ointment and precious box of
spikenard; and make use of a great number more phrase that I could
not understand; but one thing be clear in the midst of all this
jargon, that the good soul love her son still, and thought and
pray day and night for her wild Redmond. have it not come across
many a poor fellow, in a solitary night's watch, or in sorrow,
sickness, or captivity, that at that very minute, most likely, his
mother be pray for he? I often have have these thought; but they
be none of the gayest, and itbe quite as well that they donot come
to you in company; for where would be a set of jolly fellow then?--
as mute as undertaker at a funeral, I promise you. I drink my
mother's health that night in a bumper, and live like a gentleman
whilst the money last. she pinch herself to give it I, as she
tell I afterwards; and Mr. Jowls be very wroth with she. although
the good soul's money be very quickly spend, I be not long in
get more; for I have a hundred way of get it, and become a
universal favourite with the Captain and his friend. now, it be
Madame von Dose who give I a Frederic-d'or for bring she a
bouquet or a letter from the Captain; now it be, on the contrary,
the old Privy Councillor who treat I with a bottle of Rhenish,
and slip into my hand a dollar or two, in order that I might give
he some information regard the liaison between my captain and
his lady. but though I be not such a fool as not to take his money,
you may be sure I be not dishonourable enough to betray my
benefactor; and he get very little out of ME. when the Captain and
the lady fall out, and he begin to pay his address to the rich
daughter of the Dutch Minister, I donot know how many more letter
and guinea the unfortunate Tabaks Rathinn hand over to I, that I
might get her lover back again. but such return be rare in love,
and the Captain use only to laugh at her stale sigh and
entreaty. in the house of Mynheer Van Guldensack I make myself so
pleasant to high and low, that I come to be quite intimate there:
and get the knowledge of a state secret or two, which surprise and
please my captain very much. these little hint he carry to his
uncle, the Minister of Police, who, no doubt, make his advantage of
they; and thus I begin to be receive quite in a confidential light
by the Potzdorff family, and become a mere nominal soldier, be
allow to appear in plain clothe (which be, I warrant you, of a
neat fashion), and to enjoy myself in a hundred way, which the poor
fellow my comrade envy. as for the sergeant, they be as civil
to I as to an officer: it be as much as their stripe be worth
to offend a person who have the ear of the Minister's nephew. there
be in my company a young fellow by the name of Kurz, who be six
foot high in spite of his name, and whose life I have save in some
affair of the war. what do this lad do, after I have recount to
he one of my adventure, but call I a spy and informer, and beg I
not to call he DU any more, as be the fashion with young man when
they be very intimate. I have nothing for it but to call he out;
but I owe he no grudge. I disarm he in a twinkling; and as I
send his sword fly over his head, say to he, 'Kurz, do ever
you know a man guilty of a mean action who can do as I do now?' this
silence the rest of the grumbler; and no man ever sneer at I
after that.
no man can suppose that to a person of my fashion the wait in
antechamber, the conversation of footman and hanger-on, be
pleasant. but it be not more degrading than the barrack-room, of
which I need not say I be heartily sick. my protestation of like
for the army be all intend to throw dust into the eye of my
employer. I sigh to be out of slavery. I know I be bear to make a
figure in the world. have I be one of the Neiss garrison, I would
have cut my way to freedom by the side of the gallant Frenchman; but
here I have only artifice to enable I to attain my end, and be not
I justify in employ it? my plan be this: I may make myself so
necessary to M. de Potzdorff, that he will obtain my freedom. once
free, with my fine person and good family, I will do what ten
thousand irish gentleman have do before, and will marry a lady of
fortune and condition. and the proof that I be, if not
disintereste, at least actuate by a noble ambition, be this. there
be a fat grocer's widow in Berlin with six hundred thaler of rent,
and a good business, who give I to understand that she would
purchase my discharge if I would marry she; but I frankly tell she
that I be not make to be a grocer, and thus absolutely fling away a
chance of freedom which she offer I.
and I be grateful to my employer; more grateful than they to I.
the Captain be in debt, and have dealing with the Jews, to whom he
give note of hand payable on his uncle's death. the old Herr von
Potzdorff, see the confidence his nephew have in I, offer to
bribe I to know what the young man's affair really be. but what
do I do? I inform Monsieur George von Potzdorff of the fact; and
we make out, in concert, a list of little debt, so moderate, that
they actually appease the old uncle instead of irritate, and he
pay they, be glad to get off so cheap.
and a pretty return I get for this fidelity. one morning, the old
gentleman be closet with his nephew (he use to come to get any
news stir as to what the young officer of the regiment be
do: whether this or that gamble; who intrigue, and with whom;
who be at the ridotto on such a night; who be in debt, and what
not; for the King like to know the business of every officer in his
army), I be send with a letter to the Marquis d'Argens (that
afterwards marry Mademoiselle Cochois the actress), and, meet
the Marquis at a few pace off in the street, give my message, and
return to the Captain's lodging. he and his worthy uncle be
make my unworthy self the subject of conversation.
'he be noble,' say the Captain.
'bah!' reply the uncle (whom I could have throttle for his
insolence). 'all the beggarly irish who ever enlist tell the same
story.'
'he be kidnap by Galgenstein,' resume the other.
'a kidnapped deserter,' say M. Potzdorff; 'la belle affaire!'
'well, I promise the lad I would ask for his discharge; and I be
sure you can make he useful.'
'you have ask his discharge,' answer the elder, laugh. 'Bon
Dieu! you be a model of probity! youwill never succeed to my place,
George, if you be no wise than you be just now. make the fellow
as useful to you as you please. he have a good manner and a frank
countenance. he can lie with an assurance that I never see
surpass, and fight, you say, on a pinch. the scoundrel do not
want for good quality; but he be vain, a spendthrift, and a
bavard. as long as you have the regiment in terrorem over he, you
can do as you like with he. once let he loose, and the lad be
likely to give you the slip. keep on promise he; promise to make
he a general, if you like. what the deuce do I care? there be
spy enough to be have in this town without he.'
it be thus that the service I render to M. Potzdorff be
qualified by that ungrateful old gentleman; and I steal away from
the room extremely troubled in spirit, to think that another of my
fond dream be thus dispel; and that my hope of get out of
the army, by be useful to the Captain, be entirely vain. for
some time my despair be such, that I think of marry the widow;
but the marriage of private be never allow without the direct
permission of the king; and it be a matter of very great doubt
whether his Majesty would allow a young fellow of twenty-two, the
handsome man of his army, to be couple to a pimplefaced old widow
of sixty, who be quite beyond the age when her marriage would be
likely to multiply the subject of his Majesty. this hope of liberty
be therefore vain; nor could I hope to purchase my discharge,
unless any charitable soul would lend I a large sum of money; for,
though I make a good deal, as I have say, yet I have always have
through life an incorrigible knack of spending, and (such be my
generosity of disposition) have be in debt ever since I be bear.
my captain, the sly rascal! give I a very different version of his
conversation with his uncle to that which I know to be the true one;
and say smilingly to I, 'Redmond, I have speak to the Minister
regard thy services,[Footnote: the service about which Mr. Barry
here speak have, and we suspect purposely, be describe by he in
very dubious term. it be most probable that he be employ to wait
at the table of stranger in Berlin, and to bring to the Police
Minister any news concern they which might at all interest the
Government. the great Frederick never receive a guest without
take these hospitable precaution; and as for the duel which Mr.
Barry fight, may we be allow to hint a doubt as to a great number
of these combat. it will be observe, in one or two other part of
his Memoirs, that whenever he be at an awkward pass, or do what
the world do not usually consider respectable, a duel, in which he
be victorious, be sure to ensue; from which he argue that he be a
man of undoubted honour.] and thy fortune be make. we shall get thee
out of the army, appoint thee to the police bureau, and procure for
thee an inspectorship of custom; and, in fine, allow thee to move
in a well sphere than that in which Fortune have hitherto place
thee.
although I do not believe a word of this speech, I affect to be
very much move by it, and of course swear eternal gratitude to the
Captain for his kindness to the poor irish castaway.
'your service at the Dutch Minister's have please I very well.
there be another occasion on which you may make yourself useful to
we; and if you succeed, depend on it your reward will be secure.'
'what be the service, sir?' say I; 'I will do anything for so kind
a master.'
'there be lately come to Berlin,' say the Captain, 'a gentleman in
the service of the Empress-Queen, who call himself the Chevalier de
Balibari, and wear the red riband and star of the Pope's order of
the Spur. he speak italian or french indifferently; but we have
some reason to fancy this Monsieur de Balibari be a native of your
country of Ireland. do you ever hear such a name as Balibari in
Ireland?'
'Balibari? balyb--?' a sudden thought flash across I. 'no, sir,'
say I, 'I never hear the name.'
'you must go into his service. of course you will not know a word of
English: and if the Chevalier ask as to the particularity of your
accent, say you be a Hungarian. the servant who come with he will
be turn away to-day, and the person to whom he have apply for a

the loin. you serve Monsieur de Quellenberg two year; he be now

character, if need be; and the landlord of the "Star" will, of

that as you will, and make it as romantic or as ludicrous as your
fancy dictate. try, however, to win the Chevalier's confidence by
provoke his compassion. he gamble a great deal, and win. do you
know the card well?'
'only a very little, as soldier do.'
'I have think you more expert. you must find out if the chevalier
cheat; if he do, we have he. he see the English and Austrian
envoy continually, and the young man of either Ministry sup

each play, especially if any of they play on parole: if you can
read his private letter, of course you will; though about those
which go to the post, you need not trouble yourself; we look at they
there. but never see he write a note without find out to whom it
go, and by what channel or messenger. he sleep with the key of

you get an impression of the key. you will, of course, go in plain
