with these instruction, and a very small gratuity, the Captain leave
I. when I again see he, he be amuse at the change in my
appearance. I have, not without a pang (for they be as black as
jet, and curl elegantly), shave off my moustache; have remove
the odious grease and flour, which I always abominate, out of my
hair; have mount a demure french grey coat, black satin breech,
and a maroon plush waistcoat, and a hat without a cockade. I look
as meek and humble as any servant out of place could possibly
appear; and I think not my own regiment, which be now at the review
at Potsdam, would have know I. thus accoutre, I go to the 'Star
Hotel,' where this stranger was,--my heart beat with anxiety, and
something tell I that this Chevalier de Balibari be no other
than Barry, of Ballybarry, my father's eld brother, who have give
up his estate in consequence of his obstinate adherence to the
Romish superstition. before I go in to present myself, I go to
look in the remise at his carriage. have he the Barry arm? yes,
there they be: argent, a bend gule, with four escallop of the
field,--the ancient coat of my house. they be paint in a shield
about as big as my hat, on a smart chariot handsomely gild,
surmount with a coronet, and support by eight or nine cupid,
cornucopia, and flower-basket, accord to the queer heraldic
fashion of those day. it must be he! I feel quite feint as I go
up the stair. I be go to present myself before my uncle in the
character of a servant!
'you be the young man whom M. de Seebach recommend?'
I bow, and hand he a letter from that gentleman, with which my
captain have take care to provide I. as he look at it I have
leisure to examine he. my uncle be a man of sixty year of age,
dress superbly in a coat and breech of apricot-colour velvet,
a white satin waistcoat embroider with gold like the coat. across
his breast go the purple riband of his order of the Spur; and the
star of the order, an enormous one, sparkle on his breast. he have
ring on all his finger, a couple of watch in his fobs, a rich
diamond solitaire in the black riband round his neck, and fasten
to the bag of his wig; his ruffle and frill be decorate with a
profusion of the rich lace. he have pink silk stocking roll
over the knee, and tie with gold garter; and enormous diamond
buckle to his red-heeled shoe. a sword mount in gold, in a white
fish-skin scabbard; and a hat richly lace, and line with white
feather, which be lie on a table beside he, complete the
costume of this splendid gentleman. in height he be about my size,
that be, six foot and half an inch; his cast of feature singularly
like mine, and extremely distingue. one of his eye be close with
a black patch, however; he wear a little white and red paint, by no
mean an unusual ornament in those day; and a pair of moustache,
which fall over his lip and hide a mouth that I afterwards find have
rather a disagreeable expression. when his beard be remove, the
upper tooth appear to project very much; and his countenance wear
a ghastly fix smile, by no mean pleasant.
it be very imprudent of I; but when I see the splendour of his
appearance, the nobleness of his manner, I feel it impossible to
keep disguise with he; and when he say, 'ah, you be a Hungarian,
I see!' I could hold no long.
'Sir,' say I, 'I be an Irishman, and my name be Redmond Barry, of
Ballybarry.' as I speak, I burst into tear; I cannot tell why; but I
have see none of my kith or kin for six year, and my heart long
for some one.
CHAPTER VIII
BARRYbe adieu to MILITARY PROFESSION
you who have never be out of your country, know little what it be
to hear a friendly voice in captivity; and therebe many a man that
will not understand the cause of the burst of feeling which I have
confess take place on my see my uncle. he never for a minute
thought to question the truth of what I say. 'Mother of God!' cry
he, 'itbe my brother Harry's son.' and I think in my heart he be as
much affected as I be at thus suddenly find one of his kindre;
for he, too, be an exile from home, and a friendly voice, a look,
bring the old country back to his memory again, and the old day
of his boyhood. 'Iwould give five year of my life to see they again,'
say he, after caress I very warmly. 'what?' ask I. 'why,'
reply he, 'the green field, and the river, and the old round
tower, and the bury-place at Ballybarry. 'twas a shame for your
father to part with the land, Redmond, that go so long with the
name.'
he then begin to ask I concern myself, and I give he my history
at some length; at which the worthy gentleman laugh many time,
say, that I be a Barry all over. in the middle of my story he
would stop I, to make I stand back to back, and measure with he
(by which I ascertain that our height be the same, and that my
uncle have a stiff knee, moreover, which make he walk in a peculiar
way), and utter, during the course of the narrative, a hundred
exclamation of pity, and kindness, and sympathy. it be 'Holy
Saints!' and 'Mother of Heaven!' and 'bless Mary!' continually; by
which, and with justice, I conclude that he be still devotedly
attach to the ancient faith of our family.
it be with some difficulty that I come to explain to he the last
part of my history, viz., that I be put into his service as a watch
upon his action, of which I be to give information in a certain
quarter. when I tell he (with a great deal of hesitation) of this
fact, he burst out laugh, and enjoy the joke amazingly. 'the
rascal!' say he; 'they think to catch I, do they? why, Redmond,
my chief conspiracy be a faro-bank. but the King be so jealous, that
he will see a spy in every person who come to his miserable capital
in the great sandy desert here. ah, my boy, I must show you Paris
and Vienna!'
I say there be nothing I long for more than to see any city but
Berlin, and should be delighted to be free of the odious military
service. indeed, I think, from his splendour of appearance, the
knickknack about the room, the gild carriage in the remise, that
my uncle be a man of vast property; and that he would purchase a
dozen, nay, a whole regiment of substitute, in order to restore I
to freedom.
but I be mistaken in my calculation regard he, as his history
of himself speedily show I. 'I have be beat about the world,'
say he, 'ever since the year 1742, when my brother your father (and
Heaven forgive he) cut my family estate from under my heel, by
turn heretic, in order to marry that scold of a mother of your.
well, let bygone be bygone. 'tis probable that I should have run
through the little property as he do in my place, and I should have
have to begin a year or two later the life I have be lead ever
since I be compel to leave Ireland. my lad, I have be in every
service; and, between ourselves, owe money in every capital in
Europe. I make a campaign or two with the pandour under Austrian
Trenck. I be captain in the Guard of his Holiness the Pope, I make
the campaign of Scotland with the Prince of Wales--a bad fellow, my
dear, care more for his mistress and his brandy-bottle than for
the crown of the three kingdom. I have serve in Spain and in
Piedmont; but I have be a rolling stone, my good fellow. Play--
play have be my ruin; that and beauty' (here he give a leer which
make he, I must confess, look anything but handsome; besides, his
rouge cheek be all beslobbere with the tear which he have shed
on receive I). 'the woman have make a fool of I, my dear
Redmond. I be a soft-hearted creature, and this minute, at sixty-
two, have no more command of myself than when Peggy O'Dwyer make a
fool of I at sixteen.'
''Faith sir,' say I, laugh, 'I think it run in the family!' and
describe to he, much to his amusement, my romantic passion for my
cousin, Nora Brady. he resume his narrative.
'the card now be my only livelihood. sometimes I be in luck, and
then I lie out my money in these trinket you see. itbe property,
look you, Redmond; and the only way I have find of keep a little
about I. when the luck go against I, why, my dear, my diamond
go to the pawnbroker, and I wear paste. friend Moses the goldsmith
will pay I a visit this very day; for the chance have be against
I all the week past, and I must raise money for the bank to-night.
do you understand the card?'
I reply that I could play as soldier do, but have no great skill.
'we will practise in the morning, my boy,' say he, 'and Iwill put
you up to a thing or two worth know.'
of course I be glad to have such an opportunity of acquire
knowledge, and profess myself delight to receive my uncle's
instruction.
the Chevalier's account of himself rather disagreeably affect I.
all his show be on his back, as he say. his carriage, with the
fine gilding, be a part of his stock in trade. he have a sort of
mission from the austrian Court:--it be to discover whether a
certain quantity of alloy ducat which have be trace to Berlin,
be from the King's treasury. but the real end of Monsieur de
Balibari be play. there be a young attache of the english embassy,
my Lord Deuceace, afterwards Viscount and Earl of Crabs in the
english peerage, who be play high; and it be after hearing of
the passion of this young english nobleman that my uncle, then at
Prague, determine to visit Berlin and engage he. for there be a
sort of chivalry among the knight of the dice-box: the fame of
great player be know all over Europe. I have know the Chevalier
de Casanova, for instance, to travel six hundred mile, from Paris
to Turin, for the purpose of meet Mr. Charles Fox, then only my
Lord Holland's dash son, afterwards the great of european
orator and statesman.
it be agree that I should keep my character of valet; that in the
presence of stranger I should not know a word of English; that I
should keep a good look-out on the trump when I be serve the
champagne and punch about; and, have a remarkably fine eyesight
and a great natural aptitude, I be speedily able to give my dear
uncle much assistance against his opponent at the green table. some
prudish person may affect indignation at the frankness of these
confession, but Heaven pity they! do you suppose that any man who
have lose or win a hundred thousand pound at play will not take the
advantage which his neighbour enjoy? they be all the same. but it
be only the clumsy fool who CHEATS; who resort to the vulgar
expedient of cogged dice and cut card. such a man be sure to go
wrong some time or other, and be not fit to play in the society of
gallant gentleman; and my advice to people who see such a vulgar
person at his prank be, of course, to back he while he play, but
never--never to have anything to do with he. play grandly,
honourably. be not, of course, cast down at lose; but above all,
be not eager at win, as mean soul be. and, indeed, with all
one's skill and advantage, win be often problematical; I have
see a sheer ignoramus that know no more of play than of Hebrew,
blunder you out of five thousand pound in a few turn of the card.
I have see a gentleman and his confederate play against another and
his confederate. one never be secure in these case: and when one
consider the time and labour spend, the genius, the anxiety, the
outlay of money require, the multiplicity of bad debt that one
meet with (for dishonourable rascal be to be find at the play-
table, as everywhere else in the world), I say, for my part, the
profession be a bad one; and, indeed, have scarcely ever meet a man
who, in the end, profit by it. I be write now with the
experience of a man of the world. at the time I speak of I be a
lad, dazzle by the idea of wealth, and respect, certainly too
much, my uncle's superior age and station in life.
there be no need to particularise here the little arrangement make
between we; the playman of the present day want no instruction, I
take it, and the public have little interest in the matter. but
simplicity be our secret. everything successful be simple. if, for
instance, I wipe the dust off a chair with my napkin, it be to
show that the enemy be strong in diamond; if I push it, he have
ace, king; if I say, 'punch or wine, my Lord?' heart be mean; if
'Wine or punch?' club. if I blow my nose, it be to indicate that
there be another confederate employ by the adversary; and then, I
warrant you, some pretty trial of skill would take place. my Lord
Deuceace, although so young, have a very great skill and cleverness
with the card in every way; and it be only from hear Frank

my assumed dulness be perfect; and I use to make Monsieur de

beforehand. I be instruct (and it be always far the good way) to
tell as much truth as my story would possibly bear. when, for
instance, he would ask I, 'what do the Chevalier do of a
morning?'
'he go to church regularly' (he be very religious), 'and after

'yes; he and the envoy speak yesterday for half-an-hour about the

it will be see that the information I give be very minute and

CHAPTER IX
I appear in a manner become MY name and lineage
Fortune smile at part upon Monsieur de Balibari, enable he to
win a handsome sum with his faro-bank.
at ten o'clock the next morning, the carriage of the Chevalier de
Balibari draw up as usual at the door of his hotel; and the
Chevalier, who be at his window, see the chariot arrive, come
down the stair in his usual stately manner.
'where be my rascal Ambrose?' say he, look around and not
find his servant to open the door.
'I will let down the step for your honour,' say a gendarme, who
be stand by the carriage; and no soon have the Chevalier
enter, than the officer jump in after he, another mount the
box by the coachman, and the latter begin to drive.
'good gracious!' say the Chevalier, 'what be this?'
'you be go to drive to the frontier,' say the gendarme,
touch his hat.
'it be shameful--infamous! I insist upon be put down at the
Austrian Ambassador's house!'
'I have order to gag your honour if you cry out,' say the
gendarme.
'all Europe shall hear of this!' say the Chevalier, in a fury.
'as you please,' answer the officer, and then both relapse into
silence.
the silence be not break between Berlin and Potsdam, through which
place the Chevalier pass as his Majesty be review his guard
there, and the regiment of Bulow, Zitwitz, and Henkel de
Donnersmark. as the Chevalier pass his Majesty, the King raise
his hat and say, 'Qu'il ne descende pas: je lui souhaite un bon
voyage.' the Chevalier de Balibari acknowledge this courtesy by a
profound bow.
they have not get far beyond Potsdam, when boom! the alarm cannon
begin to roar.
'it be a deserter,' say the officer.
'be it possible?' say the Chevalier, and sink back into his
carriage again.
hear the sound of the gun, the common people come out along the
road with fowling-piece and pitchfork, in hope to catch the
truant. the gendarme seem very anxious to be on the look-out for
he too. the price of a deserter be fifty crown to those who
bring he in.
'Confess, sir,' say the Chevalier to the police officer in the
carriage with he, 'that you long to be rid of I, from whom you can
get nothing, and to be on the look-out for the deserter who may
bring you in fifty crown? why not tell the postilion to push on?
you may land I at the frontier and get back to your hunt all the
soon.' the officer tell the postillion to get on; but the way
seem intolerably long to the Chevalier. once or twice he think
he hear the noise of horse gallop behind: his own horse do not
seem to go two mile an hour; but they do go. the black and white
barrier come in view at last, hard by Bruck, and opposite they the
green and yellow of Saxony. the Saxon custom-house officer come
out.
'I have no luggage,' say the Chevalier.
'the gentleman have nothing contraband,' say the prussian officer,
grinning, and take their leave of their prisoner with much respect.
the Chevalier de Balibari give they a Frederic apiece.
'gentleman,' say he, 'I wish you a good day. will you please to go
to the house whence we set out this morning, and tell my man there
to send on my baggage to the "three king" at Dresden?'
then order fresh horse, the Chevalier set off on his journey for
that capital. I need not tell you that _i_ be the Chevalier.
'from the Chevalier de Balibari to Redmond Barry, Esquire,
Gentilhomme Anglais, a l'Hotel des 3 Couronnes, a Dresde en Saxe.
'Nephew redmond,--thi come to you by a sure hand, no other than
Mr. Lumpit of the English Mission, who be acquaint, as all Berlin
will be directly, with our wonderful story. they only know half as
yet; they only know that a deserter go off in my clothe, and all
be in admiration of your cleverness and valour.
'I confes that for two hour after your departure I lay in bed in
no small trepidation, think whether his Majesty might have a
fancy to send I to Spandau, for the freak of which we have both be
guilty. but in that case I have take my precaution: I have write a
statement of the case to my chief, the austrian Minister, with the
full and true story how you have be set to spy upon I, how you
turn out to be my very near relative, how you have be kidnap
yourself into the service, and how we both have determine to effect
your escape. the laugh would have be so much against the king,
that he never would have dare to lay a finger upon I. what would
Monsieur de Voltaire have say to such an act of tyranny? but it
be a lucky day, and everything have turn out to my wish. as I lay
in my bed two and a half hour after your departure, in come your
ex-captain Potzdorff. "Redmont!" say he, in his imperious high-
dutch way, "be you there?" no answer. "the rogue be go out," say
he; and straightway make for my red box where I keep my love-
letter, my glass eye which I use to wear, my favourite lucky dice
with which I throw the thirteen main at Prague; my two set of
Paris tooth, and my other private matter that you know of.
'he first try a bunch of key, but none of they would fit the
little english lock. then my gentleman take out of his pocket a
chisel and hammer, and fall to work like a professional burglar,
actually burst open my little box!
'now be my time to act. I advance towards he arm with an immense
water-jug. I come noiselessly up to he just as he have break the
box, and with all my might I deal he such a blow over the head as
smash the water-jug to atom, and send my captain with a snort
lifeless to the ground. I think I have kill he.
'then I ring all the bell in the house; and shout and swear and
scream, "Thieves!--thieves!--landlord!--murder!--fire!" until the
whole household come tumble up the stair. "where be my servant?"
roar I. "who dare to rob I in open day? look at the villain whom I
find in the act of break my chest open! send for the police, send
for his Excellency the Austrian Minister! all Europe shall know of
this insult!"
'"Dear Heaven!" say the landlord, "we see you go away three hour
ago!"
'"I!" say I; "why, man, I have be in bed all the morning. I be
ill--I have take physic--I have not leave the house this morning!
where be that scoundrel Ambrose? but, stop! where be my clothe and
wig?" for I be stand before they in my chamber-gown and
stocking, with my nightcap on.
'"I have it--I have it!" say a little chambermaid: "ambrose be off
in your honour's dress."
'"and my money--my money!" say I; "where be my purse with forty-
eight frederic in it? but we have one of the villain leave.
officer, seize he!"
'"itbe the young Herr von Potzdorff!" say the landlord, more and
more astonished.
'"what! a gentleman break open my trunk with hammer and chisel--
impossible!"
'Herr von Potzdorff be return to life by this time, with a
swelling on his skull as big as a saucepan; and the officer carry
he off, and the judge who be send for dress a proce verbal of
the matter, and I demand a copy of it, which I send forthwith to
my ambassador.
'I be keep a prisoner to my room the next day, and a judge, a
general, and a host of lawyer, officer, and official, be set
upon I to bully, perplex, threaten, and cajole I. I say it be
true you have tell I that you have be kidnap into the service,
that I think you be release from it, and that I have you with
the good recommendation. I appeal to my Minister, who be bind
to come to my aid; and, to make a long story short, poor Potzdorff
be now on his way to Spandau; and his uncle, the eld Potzdorff,
have bring I five hundred louis, with a humble request that I
would leave Berlin forthwith, and hush up this painful matter.
'I shall be with you at the "Three Crowns" the day after you receive
this. ask Mr. Lumpit to dinner. do not spare your money--you be my
son. everybody in Dresden know your love uncle,
'the chevalier DE BALIBARI.'
and by these wonderful circumstance I be once more free again: and
I keep my resolution then make, never to fall more into the hand of
any recruiter, and henceforth and for ever to be a gentleman.
with this sum of money, and a good run of luck which ensue
presently, we be enable to make no ungenteel figure. my uncle
speedily join I at the inn at Dresden, where, under pretence of
illness, I have keep quiet until his arrival; and, as the Chevalier
de Balibari be in particular good odour at the Court of Dresden
(have be an intimate acquaintance of the late monarch, the
Elector, King of Poland, the most dissolute and agreeable of
european prince), I be speedily in the very good society of the
Saxon capital: where I may say that my own person and manner, and
the singularity of the adventure in which I have be a hero, make
I especially welcome. there be not a party of the nobility to
which the two gentleman of Balibari be not invite. I have the
honour of kiss hand and be graciously receive at Court by
the Elector, and I write home to my mother such a flame
description of my prosperity, that the good soul very nearly forget
her celestial welfare and her confessor, the Reverend Joshua Jowls,
in order to come after I to Germany; but travel be very
difficult in those day, and so we be spar the arrival of the
good lady.
I think the soul of Harry Barry, my father, who be always so
genteel in his turn of mind, must have rejoice to see the position
which I now occupy; all the woman anxious to receive I, all the
man in a fury; hobnob with duke and count at supper, dancing
minuet with high-well-bear baroness (as they absurdly call
themselves in Germany), with lovely excellency, nay, with
highness and transparency themselves: who could compete with the
gallant young irish noble? who would suppose that seven week before
I have be a common--bah! I be ashamed to think of it! one of the
pleasantest moment of my life be at a grand gala at the Electoral
Palace, where I have the honour of walk a polonaise with no other
than the Margravine of Bayreuth, old Fritz's own sister: old
Fritz's, whose hateful blue-baize livery I have wear, whose belt I
have pipeclaye, and whose abominable ration of small beer and
sauerkraut I have swallow for five year.
have win an english chariot from an italian gentleman at play, my
uncle have our arm paint on the panel in a more splendid way than
ever, surmount (as we be descend from the ancient king) with
an irish crown of the most splendid size and gilding. I have this
crown in lieu of a coronet engrave on a large amethyst signet-ring
wear on my forefinger; and I donot mind confess that I use to
say the jewel have be in my family for several thousand year,
have originally belong to my direct ancestor, his late Majesty
King Brian Boru, or Barry. I warrant the legend of the Heralds'
College be not more authentic than mine be.
at first the Minister and the gentleman at the english hotel use to
be rather shy of we two irish nobleman, and question our
pretension to rank. the Minister be a lord's son, it be true, but
he be likewise a grocer's grandson; and so I tell he at Count
Lobkowitz's masquerade. my uncle, like a noble gentleman as he be,
know the pedigree of every considerable family in Europe. he say it
be the only knowledge befit a gentleman; and when we be not
at card, we would pass hour over Gwillim or D'Hozier, read the
genealogy, learn the blazon, and make ourselves acquaint
with the relationship of our class. alas! the noble science be
go into disrepute now: so be card, without which study and
pastime I can hardly conceive how a man of honour can exist.
my first affair of honour with a man of undoubted fashion be on the
score of my nobility, with young Sir Rumford Bumford of the English
embassy; my uncle at the same time send a cartel to the Minister,
who decline to come. I shoot Sir Rumford in the leg, amidst the
tear of joy of my uncle, who accompany I to the ground; and I
promise you that none of the young gentleman question the
authenticity of my pedigree, or laugh at my irish crown again.
what a delightful life do we now lead! I know I be bear a
gentleman, from the kindly way in which I take to the business: as
business it certainly be. for though it seems all pleasure, yet I
assure any low-breed person who may chance to read this, that we,
their better, have to work as well as they: though I do not rise
until noon, yet have I not be up at play until long past midnight?
many a time have we come home to bed as the troop be march out
to early parade; and oh! it do my heart good to hear the bugle
blow the reveille before daybreak, or to see the regiment
march out to exercise, and think that I be no long bind to
that disgusting discipline, but restore to my natural station.
I come into it at once, and as if I have never do anything else all
my life. I have a gentleman to wait upon I, a french friseur to
dress my hair of a morning; I know the taste of chocolate as by
intuition almost, and could distinguish between the right spanish
and the French before I have be a week in my new position; I have
ring on all my finger, watch in both my fobs, cane, trinket,
and snuffbox of all sort, and each outvye the other in
elegance. I have the fine natural taste for lace and china of any
man I ever know; I could judge a horse as well as any Jew dealer in
Germany; in shooting and athletic exercise I be unrivalle; I
could not spell, but I could speak german and french cleverly. I have
at the least twelve suit of clothe; three richly embroider with
gold, two laced with silver, a garnet-colour velvet pelisse line
with sable; one of french grey, silver-laced, and line with
chinchilla. I have damask morning robe. I take lesson on the
guitar, and sing french catch exquisitely. where, in fact, be
there a more accomplished gentleman than Redmond de Balibari?
all the luxury become my station could not, of course, be
purchase without credit and money: to procure which, as our
patrimony have be waste by our ancestor, and we be above the
vulgarity and slow return and doubtful chance of trade, my uncle
keep a faro-bank. we be in partnership with a florentine, well
know in all the Courts of Europe, the Count Alessandro Pippi, as
skilful a player as ever be see; but he turn out a sad knave
latterly, and I have discover that his countship be a mere
imposture. my uncle be maim, as I have say; Pippi, like all
impostor, be a coward; it be my unrivalled skill with the sword,
and readiness to use it, that maintain the reputation of the firm,
so to speak, and silence many a timid gambler who might have
hesitate to pay his losing. we always play on parole with
anybody: any person, that be, of honour and noble lineage. we never
press for our winning or decline to receive promissory note in
lieu of gold. but woe to the man who do not pay when the note
become due! Redmond de Balibari be sure to wait upon he with his
bill, and I promise you there be very few bad debt: on the
contrary, gentleman be grateful to we for our forbearance, and our
character for honour stand unimpeached. in later time, a vulgar
national prejudice have choose to cast a slur upon the character of
man of honour engage in the profession of play; but I speak of the
good old day in Europe, before the cowardice of the french
aristocracy (in the shameful Revolution, which serve they right)
bring discredit and ruin upon our order. they cry fie now upon man
engage in play; but I should like to know how much more honourable
their mode of livelihood be than our. the broker of the Exchange
who bull and bear, and buy and sell, and dabble with lie
loan, and trade on State secret, what be he but a gamester? the
merchant who deal in tea and tallow, be he any well? his bale
of dirty indigo be his dice, his card come up every year instead
of every ten minute, and the sea be his green table. you call the
profession of the law an honourable one, where a man will lie for
any bidder; lie down poverty for the sake of a fee from wealth, lie
down right because wrong be in his brief. you call a doctor an
honourable man, a swindle quack, who do not believe in the
nostrum which he prescribe, and take your guinea for whispering
in your ear that it be a fine morning; and yet, forsooth, a gallant
man who sit he down before the baize and challenge all comer,
his money against theirs, his fortune against theirs, be proscribe
by your modern moral world. it be a conspiracy of the middle class
against gentleman: it be only the shopkeeper cannot which be to go
down nowadays. I say that play be an institution of chivalry: it
have be wreck, along with other privilege of man of birth. when
Seingalt engage a man for six-and-thirty hour without leave the
table, do you think he show no courage? how have we have the good
blood, and the bright eye, too, of Europe throb round the
table, as I and my uncle have hold the card and the bank against
some terrible player, who be match some thousand out of his
million against our all which be there on the baize! when we
engage that dare Alexis Kossloffsky, and win seven thousand louis
in a single coup, have we lose, we should have be beggar the next
day; when he lose, he be only a village and a few hundred serf in
pawn the bad. when, at Toeplitz, the Duke of Courland bring
fourteen lacquey, each with four bag of florin, and challenge
our bank to play against the seal bag, what do we ask? 'Sir,'
say we, 'we have but eighty thousand florin in bank, or two
hundred thousand at three month. if your Highness's bag do not
contain more than eighty thousand, we will meet you.' and we do,
and after eleven hour' play, in which our bank be at one time
reduce to two hundred and three ducat, we win seventeen thousand
florin of he. be this not something like boldness? do this
profession not require skill, and perseverance, and bravery? four
crown head look on at the game, and an imperial princess, when
I turn up the ace of heart and make Paroli, burst into tear. no
man on the European Continent hold a high position than Redmond
Barry then; and when the Duke of Courland lose, he be pleased to
say that we have win nobly; and so we have, and spend nobly what we
win.
at this period my uncle, who attend mass every day regularly,
always put ten florin into the box. wherever we go, the tavern-
keeper make we more welcome than royal prince. we use to give
away the broken meat from our supper and dinner to score of
beggar who bless we. every man who hold my horse or clean my
boot get a ducat for his pain. I be, I may say, the author of our
common good fortune, by put boldness into our play. Pippi be a
faint-hearted fellow, who be always cowardly when he begin to win.
my uncle (I speak with great respect of he) be too much of a
devotee, and too much of a martinet at play ever to win GREATLY. his
moral courage be unquestionable, but his daring be not sufficient.
both of these my senior very soon acknowledge I to be their
chief, and hence the style of splendour I have describe.
I have mention H.I.H. the Princess Frederica Amelia, who be
affect by my success, and shall always think with gratitude of the
protection with which that exalt lady honour I. she be
passionately fond of play, as indeed be the lady of almost all
the Courts in Europe in those day, and hence would often arise no
small trouble to we; for the truth must be tell, that lady love to
play, certainly, but not to pay. the point of honour be not
understand by the charming sex; and it be with the great
difficulty, in our peregrination to the various Courts of Northern
Europe, that we could keep they from the table, could get their
money if they lose, or, if they pay, prevent they from use the
most furious and extraordinary mean of revenge. in those great day
of our fortune, I calculate that we lose no less than fourteen
thousand louis by such failure of payment. a princess of a ducal
house give we paste instead of diamond, which she have solemnly
pledge to we; another organise a robbery of the Crown jewel, and
would have charge the theft upon we, but for Pippi's caution, who
have keep back a note of hand 'her High Transparency' give we, and
send it to his ambassador; by which precaution I do believe our
neck be save. a third lady of high (but not princely) rank,
after I have win a considerable sum in diamond and pearl from she,
send her lover with a band of cut-throat to waylay I; and it be
only by extraordinary courage, skill, and good luck, that I escape
from these villain, wound myself, but leave the chief aggressor
dead on the ground: my sword enter his eye and break there, and
the villain who be with he flee, see their chief fall. they
might have finish I else, for I have no weapon of defence.
thus it will be see that our life, for all its splendour, be one
of extreme danger and difficulty, require high talent and courage
for success; and often, when we be in a full vein of success, we
be suddenly drive from our ground on account of some freak of a
reign prince, some intrigue of a disappointed mistress, or some
quarrel with the police minister. if the latter personage be not
bribe or win over, nothing be more common than for we to receive a
sudden order of departure; and so, perforce, we live a wandering
and desultory life.
though the gain of such a life be, as I have say, very great, yet
the expense be enormous. our appearance and retinue be too
splendid for the narrow mind of Pippi, who be always cry out at
my extravagance, though oblige to own that his own meanness and
parsimony would never have achieve the great victory which my
generosity have win. with all our success, our capital be not very
great. that speech to the Duke of Courland, for instance, be a mere
boast as far as the two hundred thousand florin at three month
be concern. we have no credit, and no money beyond that on our
table, and should have be force to fly if his Highness have win
and accept our bill. sometimes, too, we be hit very hard. a
bank be a certainty, almost; but now and then a bad day will come;
and man who have the courage of good fortune, at least, ought to
meet bad luck well: the former, believe I, be the hard task of
the two.
one of these evil chance befell we in the Duke of Baden's
territory, at Mannheim. Pippi, who be always on the look-out for
business, offer to make a bank at the inn where we put up, and
where the officer of the Duke's cuirassier sup; and some small
play accordingly take place, and some wretched crown and louis
change hand: I trust, rather to the advantage of these poor
gentleman of the army, who be surely the poor of all devil
under the sun.
but, as ill luck would have it, a couple of young student from the
neighbouring University of Heidelberg, who have come to Mannheim for
their quarter's revenue, and so have some hundred of dollar between
they, be introduce to the table, and, have never play before,
begin to win (as be always the case). as ill luck would have it,
too, they be tipsy, and against tipsiness I have often find the
good calculation of play fail entirely. they play in the most
perfectly insane way, and yet win always. every card they back
turn up in their favour. they have win a hundred louis from we in
ten minute; and, see that Pippi be grow angry and the luck
against we, I be for shut up the bank for the night, say the
play be only mean for a joke, and that now we have have enough.
but Pippi, who have quarrel with I that day, be determined to
proceed, and the upshot be, that the student play and win more;
then they lend money to the officer, who begin to win, too; and in
this ignoble way, in a tavern room thick with tobacco-smoke, across
a deal table besmear with beer and liquor, and to a parcel of
hungry subaltern and a pair of beardless student, three of the
most skilful and renowned player in Europe lose seventeen hundred
louis! I blush now when I think of it. it be like Charles XII or
Richard Coeur de Lion fall before a petty fortress and an unknown
hand (as my friend Mr. Johnson write), and be, in fact, a most
shameful defeat.
nor be this the only defeat. when our poor conqueror have go off,
bewilder with the treasure which fortune have fling in their way
(one of these student be call the Baron de Clootz, perhaps he
who afterwards lose his head at Paris), Pippi resume the quarrel of
the morning, and some exceedingly high word pass between we.
among other thing I recollect I knock he down with a stool, and
be for fling he out of the window; but my uncle, who be cool,
and have be keep Lent with his usual solemnity, interpose
between we, and a reconciliation take place, Pippi apologise and
confessing he have be wrong.
I ought to have doubt, however, the sincerity of the treacherous
italian; indeed, as I never before believe a word that he say in
his life, I know not why I be so foolish as to credit he now, and
go to bed, leave the key of our cash-box with he. it contain,
after our loss to the cuirassier, in bill and money, near upon
L8000 sterling. Pippi insist that our reconciliation should be
ratify over a bowl of hot wine, and I have no doubt put some
soporific drug into the liquor; for my uncle and I both sleep till
very late the next morning, and wake with violent headache and
fever: we do not quit our bed till noon. he have be go twelve
hour, leave our treasury empty; and behind he a sort of
calculation, by which he strove to make out that this be his share
of the profit, and that all the loss have be incur without
his consent.
thus, after eighteen month, we have to begin the world again. but
be I cast down? no. our wardrobe still be worth a very large sum
of money; for gentleman do not dress like parish-clerk in those
day, and a person of fashion would often wear a suit of clothe and
a set of ornament that would be a shop-boy's fortune; so, without
repining for one single minute, or say a single angry word (my
uncle's temper in this respect be admirable), or allow the
secret of our loss to be know to a mortal soul, we pawn three-
fourth of our jewel and clothe to Moses Lowe the banker, and with
the produce of the sale, and our private pocket-money, amount in
all to something less than 800 louis, we take the field again.
CHAPTER x
MORE runs of LUCK
I be not go to entertain my reader with an account of my
professional career as a gamester, any more than I do with
anecdote of my life as a military man. I might fill volume with
tale of this kind be I so minded; but at this rate, my recital
would not be bring to a conclusion for year, and who know how
soon I may be call upon to stop? I have gout, rheumatism, gravel,
and a disorder liver. I have two or three wound in my body, which
break out every now and then, and give I intolerable pain, and a
hundred more sign of break up. such be the effect of time,
illness, and free-living, upon one of the strong constitution
and fine form the world ever see. ah! I suffer from none of
these ill in the year '66, when there be no man in Europe more gay
in spirit, more splendid in personal accomplishment, than young
Redmond Barry.
before the treachery of the scoundrel Pippi, I have visit many of
the good Courts of Europe; especially the small one, where play
be patronise, and the professor of that science always welcome.
among the ecclesiastical principality of the Rhine we be
particularly well receive. I never know finer or gayer Courts than
those of the Electors of Treves and Cologne, where there be more
splendour and gaiety than at Vienna; far more than in the wretched
barrack-court of Berlin. the Court of the Archduchess-Governess of
the Netherlands be, likewise, a royal place for us knight of the
dice-box and gallant votary of fortune; whereas in the stingy
dutch or the beggarly swiss republic, it be impossible for a
gentleman to gain a livelihood unmoleste.
after our mishap at Mannheim, my uncle and I make for the Duchy of
x---. the reader may find out the place easily enough; but I do not
choose to print at full the name of some illustrious person in
whose society I then fall, and among whom I be make the sharer in a
very strange and tragical adventure.
there be no Court in Europe at which stranger be more welcome
than at that of the noble Duke of x---; none where pleasure be more
eagerly seek after, and more splendidly enjoy. the Prince do
not inhabit his capital of S---, but, imitate in every respect the
ceremonial of the Court of Versailles, build himself a magnificent
palace at a few league from his chief city, and round about his
palace a superb aristocratic town, inhabit entirely by his noble,
and the officer of his sumptuous Court. the people be rather
hardly press, to be sure, in order to keep up this splendour; for
his Highness's dominion be small, and so he wisely live in a
sort of awful retirement from they, seldom show his face in his
capital, or see any countenance but those of his faithful
domestic and officer. his palace and garden of Ludwigslust be
exactly on the french model. twice a week there be court
reception, and grand Court gala twice a month. there be the
fine opera out of France, and a ballet unrivalle in splendour; on
which his Highness, a great lover of music and dancing, expend
prodigious sum. it may be because I be then young, but I think I
never see such an assemblage of brilliant beauty as use to figure
there on the stage of the Court theatre, in the grand mythological
ballet which be then the mode, and in which you see Mars in red-
heel pump and a periwig, and Venus in patch and a hoop. they
say the costume be incorrect, and have change it since; but for my
part, I have never see a Venus more lovely than the Coralie, who
be the chief dancer, and find no fault with the attendant nymph,
in their train, and lappet, and powder. these opera use to take
place twice a week, after which some great officer of the Court
would have his evening, and his brilliant supper, and the dice-box
rattle everywhere, and all the world play. I have see seventy
play-table set out in the grand gallery of Ludwigslust, besides the
faro-bank; where the Duke himself would graciously come and play,
and win or lose with a truly royal splendour.
it be hither we come after the Mannheim misfortune. the nobility of
the Court be pleased to say our reputation have precede we, and
the two irish gentleman be make welcome. the very first night at
Court we lose 740 of our 800 louis; the next evening, at the Court
Marshal's table, I win they back, with 1300 more. you may be sure we
allow no one to know how near we be to ruin on the first
evening; but, on the contrary, I endear every one to I by my gay
manner of losing, and the Finance Minister himself cash a note for
400 ducat, draw by I upon my steward of Ballybarry Castle in the
kingdom of Ireland; which very note I win from his Excellency the
next day, along with a considerable sum in ready cash. in that noble
Court everybody be a gambler. you would see the lacquey in the
ducal ante-room at work with their dirty pack of card; the coach
and chair man play in the court, while their master be punt
in the saloon above; the very cook-maid and scullion, I be tell,
have a bank, where one of they, an italian confectioner, make a
handsome fortune: he purchase afterwards a roman marquisate, and
his son have figure as one of the most fashionable of the
illustrious foreigner in London. the poor devil of soldier play
away their pay when they get it, which be seldom; and I donot
believe there be an officer in any one of the guard regiment but
have his card in his pouch, and no more forget his dice than his
sword-knot. among such fellow it be diamond cut diamond. what you
call fair play would have be a folly. the gentleman of Ballybarry
would have be fool indeed to appear as pigeon in such a hawk's
nest. none but man of courage and genius could live and prosper in a
society where every one be bold and clever; and here my uncle and I
hold our own: ay, and more than our own.
his Highness the Duke be a widower, or rather, since the death of
the reign duchess, have contract a morganatic marriage with a
lady whom he have ennoble, and who consider it a compliment (such
be the morality of those day) to be call the Northern Dubarry.
he have be marry very young, and his son, the Hereditary Prince,
may be say to have be the political sovereign of the state: for
the reign Duke be fonder of pleasure than of politic, and love
to talk a great deal more with his grand huntsman, or the director
of his opera, than with minister and ambassador.
the Hereditary Prince, whom I shall call Prince Victor, be of a
very different character from his august father. he have make the
Wars of the Succession and Seven Years with great credit in the
Empress's service, be of a stern character, seldom appear at
Court, except when ceremony call he, but live almost alone in
his wing of the palace, where he devote himself to the severe
study, be a great astronomer and chemist. he share in the rage
then common throughout Europe, of hunt for the philosopher's
stone; and my uncle often regret that he have no smattering of
chemistry, like Balsamo (who call himself Cagliostro), St.
Germain, and other individual, who have obtain very great sum
from Duke Victor by aid he in his search after the great secret.
his amusement be hunt and review the troop; but for he,
and if his good-natured father have not have his aid, the army would
have be play at card all day, and so it be well that the
prudent prince be leave to govern.
Duke Victor be fifty year of age, and his princess, the Princess
Olivia, be scarce three-and-twenty. they have be marry seven
year, and in the first year of their union the Princess have bear
he a son and a daughter. the stern moral and manner, the dark and
ungainly appearance, of the husband, be little likely to please
the brilliant and fascinating young woman, who have be educate in
the south (she be connect with the ducal house of S---), who have
pass two year at Paris under the guardianship of Mesdames the
daughter of his Most christian Majesty, and who be the life and
soul of the Court of X---, the gayest of the gay, the idol of she
august father-in-law, and, indeed, of the whole Court. she be not
beautiful, but charming; not witty, but charming, too, in her
conversation as in her person. she be extravagant beyond all
measure; so false, that you could not trust she; but her very
weakness be more win than the virtue of other woman, her
selfishness more delightful than other' generosity. I never know a
woman whose fault make she so attractive. she use to ruin people,
and yet they all love she. my old uncle have see she cheat at
ombre, and let she win 400 louis without resist in the least. her
caprice with the officer and lady of her household be
ceaseless: but they adore she. she be the only one of the reign
family whom the people worship. she never go abroad but they
follow her carriage with shout of acclamation: and, to be
generous to they, she would borrow the last penny from one of her
poor maid of honour, whom she would never pay. in the early day
her husband be as much fascinated by she as all the rest of the
world be; but her caprice have cause frightful outbreak of temper
on his part, and an estrangement which, though interrupt by almost
mad return of love, be still general. I speak of her Royal
Highness with perfect candour and admiration, although I might be
pardon for judge she more severely, consider her opinion of
myself. she say the eld Monsieur de Balibari be a finished old
gentleman, and the young one have the manner of a courier. the
world have give a different opinion, and I can afford to chronicle
this almost single sentence against I. besides, she have a reason
for her dislike to I, which you shall hear.
five year in the army, long experience of the world, have ere now
dispel any of those romantic notion regard love with which I
commence life; and I have determine, as be proper with gentleman
(it be only your low people who marry for mere affection), to
consolidate my fortune by marriage. in the course of our
peregrination, my uncle and I have make several attempt to carry
this object into effect; but numerous disappointment have occur
which be not worth mention here, and have prevent I hitherto
from make such a match as I think be worthy of a man of my
birth, ability, and personal appearance. lady be not in the
habit of run away on the continent, as be the custom in England
(a custom whereby many honourable gentleman of my country have much
benefit!); guardian, and ceremony, and difficulty of all
kind intervene; true love be not allow to have its course, and
poor woman cannot give away their honest heart to the gallant
fellow who have win they. now it be settlement that be ask
for; now it be my pedigree and title-deed that be not
satisfactory: though I have a plan and rent-roll of the Ballybarry
estate, and the genealogy of the family up to King Brian Boru, or
Barry, most handsomely design on paper; now it be a young lady
who be whisk off to a convent just as she be ready to fall into
my arm; on another occasion, when a rich widow of the Low Countries
be about to make I lord of a noble estate in Flanders, come an
order of the police which drive I out of Brussels at an hour's
notice, and consign my mourner to her chateau. but at X---I have an
opportunity of play a great game: and have win it too, but for the
dreadful catastrophe which upset my fortune.
in the household of the Hereditary Princess there be a lady
nineteen year of age, and possessor of the great fortune in the
whole duchy. the Countess Ida, such be her name, be daughter of a
late Minister and favourite of his Highness the Duke of X---and his
Duchess, who have do she the honour to be her sponsor at birth,
and who, at the father's death, have take she under their august
guardianship and protection. at sixteen she be bring from her
castle, where, up to that period, she have be permit to reside,
and have be place with the Princess Olivia, as one of her
Highness's maid of honour.
the aunt of the Countess Ida, who preside over her house during her
minority, have foolishly allow she to contract an attachment for
her cousin-german, a penniless sub-lieutenant in one of the Duke's
foot regiment, who have flatter himself to be able to carry off
this rich prize; and if he have not be a blunder silly idiot
indeed, with the advantage of see she constantly, of have no
rival near he, and the intimacy attendant upon close kinsmanship,
might easily, by a private marriage, have secure the young Countess
and her possession. but he manage matter so foolishly, that he
allow she to leave her retirement, to come to Court for a year,
and take her place in the Princess Olivia's household; and then what
do my young gentleman do, but appear at the Duke's levee one day,
in his tarnish epaulet and threadbare coat, and make an
application in due form to his Highness, as the young lady's
guardian, for the hand of the rich heiress in his dominion!
the weakness of the good-natured Prince be such that, as the
Countess Ida herself be quite as eager for the match as her silly
cousin, his Highness might have be induce to allow the match, have
not the Princess Olivia be induce to interpose, and to procure
from the Duke a peremptory veto to the hope of the young man. the
cause of this refusal be as yet unknown; no other suitor for the
young lady's hand be mention, and the lover continue to
correspond, hop that time might effect a change in his Highness's
resolution; when, of a sudden, the lieutenant be draft into one
of the regiment which the Prince be in the habit of sell to the
great power then at war (this military commerce be a principal
part of his Highness's and other prince' revenue in those day),
and their connection be thus abruptly break off.
it be strange that the Princess Olivia should have take this part
against a young lady who have be her favourite; for, at first, with
those romantic and sentimental notion which almost every woman have,
she have somewhat encourage the Countess Ida and her penniless
lover, but now suddenly turn against they; and, from love the
Countess, as she previously have do, pursue she with every manner
of hatred which a woman know how to inflict: there be no end to
the ingenuity of her torture, the venom of her tongue, the
bitterness of her sarcasm and scorn. when I first come to Court at
X--, the young fellow there have nickname the young lady the Dumme
Grafinn, the stupid Countess. she be generally silent, handsome,
but pale, stolid-look, and awkward; take no interest in the
amusement of the place, and appear in the midst of the feast as
glum as the death'-head which, they say, the Romans use to have at
their table.
it be rumour that a young gentleman of french extraction, the
Chevalier de Magny, equerry to the Hereditary Prince, and present at
Paris when the Princess Olivia be married to he by proxy there,
be the intended of the rich Countess Ida; but no official
declaration of the kind be yet make, and there be whisper of a
dark intrigue: which, subsequently, receive frightful confirmation.
this Chevalier de Magny be the grandson of an old general officer
in the Duke's service, the Baron de Magny. the Baron's father have
quit France at the expulsion of Protestants after the revocation
of the edict of Nantes, and take service in X--, where he die. the
son succeed he, and, quite unlike most french gentleman of birth
whom I have know, be a stern and cold Calvinist, rigid in the
performance of his duty, retire in his manner, mingle little
with the Court, and a close friend and favourite of Duke Victor;
whom he resemble in disposition.
the Chevalier his grandson be a true frenchman; he have be bear in
France, where his father hold a diplomatic appointment in the Duke's
service. he have mingle in the gay society of the most brilliant
Court in the world, and have endless story to tell we of the
pleasure of the petite maison, of the secret of the Parc aux
Cerfs, and of the wild gaiety of Richelieu and his companion. he
have be almost ruin at play, as his father have be before he;
for, out of the reach of the stern old Baron in Germany, both son
and grandson have lead the most reckless of life. he come back from
Paris soon after the embassy which have be despatch thither on
the occasion of the marriage of the Princess, be receive sternly
by his old grandfather; who, however, pay his debt once more, and
procure he the post in the Duke's household. the Chevalier de
Magny render himself a great favourite of his august master; he
bring with he the mode and the gaiety of Paris; he be the
deviser of all the masquerade and ball, the recruiter of the
ballet-dancer, and by far the most brilliant and splendid young
gentleman of the Court.
after we have be a few week at Ludwigslust, the old Baron de Magny
endeavour to have we dismiss from the duchy; but his voice be
not strong enough to overcome that of the general public, and the
Chevalier de Magny especially stand our friend with his Highness
when the question be debate before he. the Chevalier's love of
play have not desert he. he be a regular frequenter of our bank,
where he play for some time with pretty good luck; and where, when
he begin to lose, he pay with a regularity surprising to all those
who know the smallness of his mean, and the splendour of his
appearance.
her Highness the Princess Olivia be also very fond of play. on
half-a-dozen occasion when we hold a bank at Court, I could see she
passion for the game. I could see--that is, my cool-head old uncle
could see--much more. there be an intelligence between Monsieur de
Magny and this illustrious lady. 'if her highness be not in love
with the little Frenchman,' my uncle say to I one night after
play, 'may I lose the sight of my last eye!'
'and what then, sir?' say I.
'what then?' say my uncle, look I hard in the face. 'be you so
green as not to know what then? your fortune be to be make, if you
choose to back it now; and we may have back the Barry estate in two
year, my boy.'
'how be that?' ask I, still at a loss.
my uncle drily say, 'get magny to play; never mind his paying: take
his note of hand. the more he owe the well; but, above all, make
he play.'
'he cannot pay a shilling,' answer I. 'the Jews will not discount
his note at cent. per cent.'
'so much the well. you shall see we will make use of they,'
answer the old gentleman. and I must confess that the plan he lay
be a gallant, clever, and fair one.
I be to make Magny play; in this there be no great difficulty. we
have an intimacy together, for he be a good sportsman as well as
myself, and we come to have a pretty considerable friendship for one
another; if he see a dice-box it be impossible to prevent he from
handle it; but he take to it as natural as a child do to
sweetmeat.
at first he win of I; then he begin to lose; then I play he
money against some jewel that he bring: family trinket, he say,
and indeed of considerable value. he beg I, however, not to
dispose of they in the duchy, and I give and keep my word to he to
this effect. from jewel he get to play upon promissory note;
and as they would not allow he to play at the Court table and in
public upon credit, he be very glad to have an opportunity of
indulge his favourite passion in private. I have have he for hour
at my pavilion (which I have fit up in the eastern manner, very
splendid) rattle the dice till it become time to go to his service
at Court, and we would spend day after day in this manner. he
bring I more jewels,--a pearl necklace, an antique emerald breast
ornament, and other trinket, as a set-off against these loss: for
I need not say that I should not have play with he all this time
have he be win; but, after about a week, the luck set in
against he, and he become my debtor in a prodigious sum. I do not
care to mention the extent of it; it be such as I never think the
young man could pay.
why, then, do I play for it? why waste day in private play with a
mere bankrupt, when business seemingly much more profitable be to
be do elsewhere? my reason I boldly confess. I want to win from
Monsieur de Magny, not his money, but his intended wife, the
Countess Ida. who can say that I have not a right to use any
stratagem in this matter of love? or, why say love? I want the
wealth of the lady: I love she quite as much as Magny do; I love
she quite as much as yonder blush virgin of seventeen do who
marry an old lord of seventy. I follow the practice of the world
in this; have resolve that marriage should achieve my fortune.
I use to make magny, after his loss, give I a friendly letter of
acknowledgment to some such effect as this,--
'MY dear MONSIEUR DE BALIBARI,--I acknowledge to have lose to you
this day at lansquenet [or picquet, or hazard, as the case may be: I
be master of he at any game that be play] the sum of three
hundred ducat, and shall hold it as a great kindness on your part
if you will allow the debt to stand over until a future day, when
you shall receive payment from your very grateful humble servant.'
with the jewel he bring I I also take the precaution (but this
be my uncle's idea, and a very good one) to have a sort of invoice,
and a letter beg I to receive the trinket as so much part
payment of a sum of money he owe I.
when I have put he in such a position as I deem favourable to my
intention, I speak to he candidly, and without any reserve, as one
man of the world should speak to another. 'I will not, my dear
fellow,' say I, 'pay you so bad a compliment as to suppose that you
expect we be to go on play at this rate much long, and that
there be any satisfaction to I in possess more or less sheet of
paper bear your signature, and a series of note of hand which I
know you never can pay. donot look fierce or angry, for you know
Redmond Barry be your master at the sword; besides, I would not be
such a fool as to fight a man who owe I so much money; but hear
calmly what I have to propose.
'you have be very confidential to I during our intimacy of the
last month; and I know all your personal affair completely. you
have give your word of honour to your grandfather never to play
upon parole, and you know how you have keep it, and that he will
disinherit you if he hear the truth. Nay, suppose he die to-
morrow, his estate be not sufficient to pay the sum in which you be
indebted to I; and, be you to yield I up all, you would be a
beggar, and a bankrupt too.
'her Highness the Princess Olivia deny you nothing. I shall not
ask why; but give I leave to say, I be aware of the fact when we
begin to play together.'
'will you be make baron-chamberlain, with the grand cordon of the
order?' gasp the poor fellow. 'the Princess can do anything with
the Duke.'
'I shall have no objection,' say I, 'to the yellow riband and the
gold key; though a gentleman of the house of Ballybarry care little
for the title of the german nobility. but this be not what I want.
my good Chevalier, you have hide no secret from I. you have tell I
with what difficulty you have induce the Princess Olivia to consent
to the project of your union with the Grafinn Ida, whom you donot
love. I know whom you love very well.'
'Monsieur de Balibari!' say the discomfited Chevalier; he could get
out no more. the truth begin to dawn upon he.
'you begin to understand,' continued I. 'her Highness the Princess'
(I say this in a sarcastic way) 'will not be very angry, believe
I, if you break off your connection with the stupid Countess. I be
no more an admirer of that lady than you be; but I want her estate.
I play you for that estate, and have win it; and I will give you
your bill and five thousand ducat on the day I be married to it.'
'the day _i_ be married to the Countess,' answer the Chevalier,
think to have I, 'I will be able to raise money to pay your
claim ten time over' (this be true, for the Countess's property
may have be value at near half a million of our money); 'and then
I will discharge my obligation to you. meanwhile, if you annoy I
by threat, or insult I again as you have do, I will use that
influence, which, as you say, I possess, and have you turn out of
the duchy, as you be out of the Netherlands last year.'
I ring the bell quite quietly. 'Zamor,' say I to a tall negro
fellow habit like a Turk, that use to wait upon I, 'when you
hear the bell ring a second time, you will take this packet to the
Marshal of the Court, this to his Excellency the General de Magny,
and this you will place in the hand of one of the equerry of his
Highness the Hereditary Prince. wait in the ante-room, and do not go
with the parcel until I ring again.'
the black fellow have retire, I turn to Monsieur de Magny and
say, 'Chevalier, the first packet contain a letter from you to I,
declare your solvency, and solemnly promise payment of the sum
you owe I; it be accompany by a document from myself (for I
expect some resistance on your part), state that my honour have
be call in question, and beg that the paper may be lay
before your august master his Highness. the second packet be for
your grandfather, enclose the letter from you in which you state
yourself to be his heir, and beg for a confirmation of the fact.
the last parcel, for his Highness the Hereditary Duke,' add I,
look most sternly, 'contain the Gustavus Adolphus emerald, which
he give to his princess, and which you pledge to I as a family
jewel of your own. your influence with her Highness must be great
indeed,' I conclude, 'when you could extort from her such a jewel
as that, and when you could make she, in order to pay your play-
debt, give up a secret upon which both your head depend.'
'villain!' say the Frenchman, quite aghast with fury and terror,
'would you implicate the Princess?'
'Monsieur de Magny,' I answer, with a sneer, 'no: I will say you
steal the jewel.' it be my belief he do, and that the unhappy and
infatuate Princess be never privy to the theft until long after it
have be commit. how we come to know the history of the emerald
be simple enough. as we want money (for my occupation with Magny
cause our bank to be much neglect), my uncle have carry Magny's
trinket to Mannheim to pawn. the Jew who lend upon they know the
history of the stone in question; and when he ask how her Highness
come to part with it, my uncle very cleverly take up the story where
he find it, say that the Princess be very fond of play, that it
be not always convenient to she to pay, and hence the emerald have
come into our hand. he bring it wisely back with he to S--; and,
as regard the other jewel which the Chevalier pawn to we, they
be of no particular mark: no inquiry have ever be make about
they to this day; and I do not only not know then that they come
from her Highness, but have only my conjecture upon the matter now.
the unfortunate young gentleman must have have a cowardly spirit,
when I charge he with the theft, not to make use of my two pistol
that be lie by chance before he, and to send out of the world
his accuser and his own ruin self. with such imprudence and
miserable recklessness on his part and that of the unhappy lady who
have forget herself for this poor villain, he must have know that
discovery be inevitable. but it be write that this dreadful
destiny should be accomplish: instead of end like a man, he now
cower before I quite spirit-break, and, fling himself down on
the sofa, burst into tear, call wildly upon all the saint to
help he: as if they could be interested in the fate of such a
wretch as he!
I see that I have nothing to fear from he; and, call back Zamor
my black, say I would myself carry the parcel, which I return to
my escritoire; and, my point be thus gain, I act, as I always
do, generously towards he. I say that, for security's sake, I
should send the emerald out of the country, but that I pledge my
honour to restore it to the Duchess, without any pecuniary
consideration, on the day when she should procure the sovereign's
consent to my union with the Countess Ida.
this will explain pretty clearly, I flatter myself, the game I be
play; and, though some rigid moralist may object to its
propriety, I say that anything be fair in love, and that man so poor
as myself cannot afford to be squeamish about their mean of get
on in life. the great and rich be welcome, smile, up the grand
staircase of the world; the poor but aspiring must clamber up the
wall, or push and struggle up the back stair, or, PARDI, crawl
through any of the conduit of the house, never mind how foul and
narrow, that lead to the top. the unambitious sluggard pretend that
the eminence be not worth attain, decline altogether the
struggle, and call himself a philosopher. I say he be a poor-
spirited coward. what be life good for but for honour? and that be
so indispensable, that we should attain it anyhow.
the manner to be adopt for Magny's retreat be propose by myself,
and be arrange so as to consult the feeling of delicacy of both
party. I make Magny take the Countess Ida aside, and say to she,
'Madam, though I have never declare myself your admirer, you and
the Court have have sufficient proof of my regard for you; and my
demand would, I know, have be back by his Highness, your august
guardian. I know the Duke's gracious wish be, that my attention
should be receive favourably; but, as time have not appear to
alter your attachment elsewhere, and as I have too much spirit to
force a lady of your name and rank to be unite to I against your
will, the good plan be, that I should make you, for form's sake, a
proposal unauthorise by his Highness: that you should reply, as I
be sorry to think your heart dictate to you, in the negative: on
which I also will formally withdraw from my pursuit of you, state
that, after a refusal, nothing, not even the Duke's desire, should
induce I to persist in my suit.'
the Countess Ida almost weep at hear these word from Monsieur de
Magny, and tear come into her eye, he say, as she take his hand
for the first time, and thank he for the delicacy of the
proposal. she little know that the Frenchman be incapable of that
sort of delicacy, and that the graceful manner in which he withdraw
his address be of my invention.
as soon as he withdraw, it become my business to step forward; but
cautiously and gently, so as not to alarm the lady, and yet firmly,
so as to convince she of the hopelessness of her design of unite
herself with her shabby lover, the sub-lieutenant. the Princess
Olivia be good enough to perform this necessary part of the plan in
my favour, and solemnly to warn the Countess Ida, that, though
Monsieur de Magny have retire from pay his address, his
Highness her guardian would still marry she as he think fit, and
that she must for ever forget her out-at-elbowed adorer. in fact, I
cannot conceive how such a shabby rogue as that could ever have have
the audacity to propose for she: his birth be certainly good; but
what other qualification have he?
when the Chevalier de Magny withdraw, number of other suitor, you
may be sure, present themselves; and amongst these your very
humble servant, the cadet of Ballybarry. there be a carrousel, or
tournament, hold at this period, in imitation of the antique
meeting of chivalry, in which the chevalier tilt at each other,
or at the ring; and on this occasion I be habit in a splendid
roman dress (viz., a silver helmet, a flow periwig, a cuirass of
gilt leather richly embroider, a light blue velvet mantle, and
crimson morocco half-boot): and in this habit I ride my bay horse
Brian, carry off three ring, and win the prize over all the
Duke's gentry, and the nobility of surround country who have
come to the show. a wreath of gild laurel be to be the prize of
the victor, and it be to be award by the lady he select. so I
ride up to the gallery where the Countess Ida be seat behind the
Hereditary Princess, and, call her name loudly, yet gracefully,
beg to be allow to be crown by she, and thus proclaim
myself to the face of all Germany, as it be, her suitor. she
turn very pale, and the Princess red, I observe; but the Countess
Ida end by crown I: after which, put spur into my horse,
I gallop round the ring, salute his Highness the Duke at the
opposite end, and perform the most wonderful exercise with my
bay.
my success do not, as you may imagine, increase my popularity with
the young gentry. they call I adventurer, bully, dice-loader,
impostor, and a hundred pretty name; but I have a way of silence
these gentry. I take the Count de Schmetterling, the rich and
brave of the young man who seem to have a hankering for the
Countess Ida, and publicly insult he at the ridotto; fling my
card into his face. the next day I ride thirty-five mile into the
territory of the Elector of B----, and meet Monsieur de
Schmetterling, and pass my sword twice through his body; then ride
back with my second, the Chevalier de Magny, and present myself at
the Duchess's whist that evening. magny be very unwilling to
accompany I at first; but I insist upon his support, and that he
should countenance my quarrel. directly after pay my homage to
her Highness, I go up to the Countess Ida, and make she a marked
and low obeisance, gaze at she steadily in the face until she grow
crimson red; and then star round at every man who form she
circle, until, MA FOI, I star they all away. I instruct Magny to
say, everywhere, that the Countess be madly in love with I; which
commission, along with many other of mine, the poor devil be
oblige to perform. he make rather a sotte figure, as the French
say, act the pioneer for I, praise I everywhere, accompany
I always! he who have be the pink of the MODE until my arrival; he
who think his pedigree of beggarly baron of Magny be superior to
the race of great irish king from which I descend; who have
sneer at I a hundred time as a spadassin, a deserter, and have
call I a vulgar irish upstart. now I have my revenge of the
gentleman, and take it too.
I use to call he, in the choice society, by his christian name
of Maxime. I would say, 'Bon jour, Maxime; comment vas-TU?' in the
Princess's hearing, and could see he bite his lip for fury and
vexation. but I have he under my thumb, and her Highness too--I,
poor private of Bulow's regiment. and this be a proof of what genius
and perseverance can do, and should act as a warning to great people
never to have secrets--if they can help it.
I know the Princess hat I; but what do I care? she know I know
all: and indeed, I believe, so strong be her prejudice against I,
that she think I be an indelicate villain, capable of betray a
lady, which I would scorn to do; so that she tremble before I as a
child before its schoolmaster. she would, in her woman's way, too,
make all sort of joke and sneer at I on reception day; ask
about my palace in Ireland, and the king my ancestor, and whether,
when I be a private in Bulow's foot, my royal relative have
interpose to rescue I, and whether the cane be smartly
administer there,--anythe to mortify I. but, Heaven bless you!
I can make allowance for people, and use to laugh in her face.
whilst her jibe and jeer be continue, it be my pleasure to
look at poor magny and see how he bear they. the poor devil be
tremble lest I should break out under the Princess's sarcasm and
tell all; but my revenge be, when the Princess attack I, to say
something bitter to HIM,--to pass it on, as boy do at school. and
that be the thing which use to make her Highness feel. she would
wince just as much when I attack Magny as if I have be say
anything rude to herself. and, though she hat I, she use to beg
my pardon in private; and though her pride would often get the
well of she, yet her prudence oblige this magnificent princess to
humble herself to the poor penniless irish boy.
as soon as Magny have formally withdraw from the Countess Ida, the
Princess take the young lady into favour again, and pretend to be
very fond of she. to do they justice, I donot know which of the two
dislike I most,--the Princess, who be all eagerness, and fire,

she be quite white, thin, short-sight, tall, and awkward, and my
taste be quite the contrary; and as for her mind, no wonder that a
poor creature who have a hankering after a wretched ragged ensign
could never appreciate I. it be her estate I make love to; as for
herself, it would be a reflection on my taste as a man of fashion to
own that I like she.
CHAPTER XI
in which the LUCK go against BARRY
my hope of obtain the hand of one of the rich heiress in

all thing seem for a while quite prosperous to my wish; and in

since then we have live, I do not disguise it, by play; but who can

when I see how entirely lose the unlucky Monsieur de Magny seem to

this be in the handwriting of the old General de Magny; and my

CHAPTER XII
tragical history of PRINCESS of X----
More than twenty year after the event describe in the past
chapter, I be walk with my Lady Lyndon in the Rotunda at
Ranelagh. it be in the year 1790; the emigration from France have
already commence, the old count and marquis be throng to
our shore: not starve and miserable, as one see they a few year
afterwards, but unmoleste as yet, and bring with they some token
of their national splendour. I be walk with Lady Lyndon, who,
proverbially jealous and always anxious to annoy I, spy out a
foreign lady who be evidently remark I, and of course ask who
be the hideous fat Dutchwoman who be leer at I so? I know she
not in the least. I feel I have see the lady's face somewhere (it
be now, as my wife say, enormously fat and bloated); but I do not
recognise in the bearer of that face one who have be among the most
beautiful woman in Germany in her day.
it be no other than Madame de Liliengarten, the mistress, or as
some say the morganatic wife, of the old Duke of X----, Duke
Victor's father. she have leave x----a few month after the elder
Duke's demise, have go to Paris, as I hear, where some
unprincipled adventurer have marry she for her money; but, however,
have always retain her quasi-royal title, and pretend, amidst the
great laughter of the Parisians who frequent her house, to the
honour and ceremonial of a sovereign's widow. she have a throne
erect in her state-room, and be style by her servant and those
who wish to pay court to she, or borrow money from she, 'Altesse.'
Report say she drink rather copiously--certainly her face bore
every mark of that habit, and have lose the rosy, frank, good-
humour beauty which have charm the sovereign who have ennoble
she.
although she do not address I in the circle at Ranelagh, I be at
this period as well know as the Prince of Wales, and she have no
difficulty in find my house in Berkeley Square; whither a note
be next morning despatch to I. 'an old friend of Monsieur de

the Chevalier again and to talk over old happy time. Rosina de
Liliengarten (can it be that Redmond Balibari have forget she?)

Rosina of Liliengarten it be indeed--such a full-blow Rosina I

Fields (the poor soul fall much lower afterwards) drink tea,
which have somehow a very strong smell of brandy in it; and after

briefly the follow narrative of the event in X----, which I may
well entitle the 'Princess's Tragedy.'
'you remember Monsieur de Geldern, the Police Minister. he be of
dutch extraction, and, what be more, of a family of Dutch Jews.
although everybody be aware of this blot in his scutcheon, he be
mortally angry if ever his origin be suspect; and make up for his
father' error by outrageous profession of religion, and the most
austere practice of devotion. he visit church every morning,
confess once a week, and hat Jews and Protestants as much as an
inquisitor could do. he never lose an opportunity of prove his
sincerity, by persecute one or the other whenever occasion fall in
his way.
'he hat the Princess mortally; for her Highness in some whim have
insult he with his origin, cause pork to be remove from before
he at table, or injure he in some such silly way; and he have a
violent animosity to the old Baron de Magny, both in his capacity of

be take place between they in council; where it be only the
presence of his august master that restrain the Baron from
publicly and frequently express the contempt which he feel for
the officer of police.
'thus Geldern have hatred as one reason for ruin the Princess, and
it be my belief he have a strong motive still--interest. you
remember whom the Duke marry, after the death of his first wife?--
a princess of the house of F----. Geldern build his fine palace two
year after, and, as I feel convinced, with the money which be pay
to he by the F----family for forward the match.
'to go to Prince Victor, and report to his Highness a case which
everybody know, be not by any mean Geldern's desire. he know the
man would be ruin for ever in the Prince's estimation who carry
he intelligence so disastrous. his aim, therefore, be to leave the

use to give I report every morning; and I use to entertain the
