and dice in the morning, and with your quarrel and intrigue. we
levy similar contribution on everybody in X----, to amuse the
dear old man. Monsieur de Magny's valet use to report both to I
and Monsieur de Geldern.
'I know of the fact of the emerald be in pawn; and it be out of
my exchequer that the poor Princess draw the fund which be spend
upon the odious lowe, and the still more worthless young Chevalier.
how the Princess could trust the latter as she persist in do,
be beyond my comprehension; but there be no infatuation like that of
a woman in love: and you will remark, my dear Monsieur de Balibari,
that our sex generally fix upon a bad man.'
'not always, madam,' I interpose; 'your humble servant have create
many such attachment.'
'I do not see that that affect the truth of the proposition,' say

'their interview take place in Magny's own apartment, when his
valet overheard every word of their conversation. the young man, who

be so easy in offer it, that Lowe rise in his demand, and have

'at this the Chevalier lose all patience, fall on the wretch and be
for kill he; when the opportune valet rush in and save he.
the man have hear every word of the conversation between the

'perhaps he be not sorry to be rid of he, and to have in his

'your ladyship go half, madam,' say I; 'and you know how little

'the man conduct the trembling Israelite out of the palace, and no

I remember the fact, and listen on, amazed at the artifice of

'the spy come back from his message to Lowe, and state that he have

israelitish scoundrel take?"
'"he go to R----to-night."
'"and must pass by the Kaiserwald. be you a man of courage, Johann
Kerner?"
'"will your Excellency try I?" say the man, his eye glitter:
"I serve through the Seven Years' war, and be never know to fail
there."
'"now, listen. the emerald must be take from that Jew: in the very
keep it the scoundrel have commit high treason. to the man who

understand why it be necessary that it should be restore to her
Highness. I need say no more."
'"you shall have it to-night, sir," say the man. "of course your
Excellency will hold I harmless in case of accident."
'"Psha!" answer the Minister; "I will pay you half the money
beforehand; such be my confidence in you. accident's impossible if
you take your measure properly. there be four league of wood; the

a rope across the road, and deal with he there? be back with I
this evening at supper. if you meet any of the patrol, say 'fox

they without question."
'the man go off quite charmed with his commission; and when magny
be lose his money at our faro-table, his servant waylay the Jew
at the spot name the Powder-Mill, in the Kaiserwald. the Jew's
horse stumble over a rope which have be place across the road;
and, as the rider fall groan to the ground, Johann Kerner rush
out on he, mask, and pistol in hand, and demand his money. he
have no wish to kill the Jew, I believe, unless his resistance should
render extreme measure necessary.
'nor do he commit any such murder; for, as the yell Jew roar
for mercy, and his assailant menace he with a pistol, a squad of
patrol come up, and lay hold of the robber and the wound man.
'Kerner swear an oath. "you have come too soon," say he to the
sergeant of the police. "FOXES be LOOSE." "some be catch," say
the sergeant, quite unconcerned; and bind the fellow's hand with
the rope which he have stretch across the road to entrap the Jew.
he be place behind a policeman on a horse; Lowe be similarly
accommodate, and the party thus come back into the town as the
night fall. 'they be take forthwith to the police quarter; and,
as the chief happen to be there, they be examine by his
Excellency in person. both be rigorously search; the Jew's
paper and case take from he: the jewel be find in a private
pocket. as for the spy, the Minister, look at he angrily, say,
"why, this be the servant of the Chevalier de Magny, one of her
Highness's equerry!" and without hear a word in exculpation
from the poor frightened wretch, order he into close confinement.
'call for his horse, he then ride to the Prince's apartment at
the palace, and ask for an instant audience. when admit, he
produce the emerald. "this jewel," say he, "have be find on the
person of a Heidelberg Jew, who have be here repeatedly of late,
and have have many dealing with her Highness's equerry, the Chevalier
de Magny. this afternoon the Chevalier's servant come from his
master's lodging, accompany by the Hebrew; be hear to make
inquiry as to the route the man intend to take on his way
homeward; follow he, or precede he rather, and be find in
the act of rifle his victim by my police in the Kaiserwald. the
man will confess nothing; but, on be search, a large sum in
gold be find on his person; and though it be with the utmost pain
that I can bring myself to entertain such an opinion, and to
implicate a gentleman of the character and name of Monsieur de
magny, I do submit that our duty be to have the Chevalier examine
relative to the affair. as Monsieur de Magny be in her Highness's
private service, and in her confidence I have hear, I would not
venture to apprehend he without your Highness's permission."
'the Prince's Master of the Horse, a friend of the old Baron de
Magny, who be present at the interview, no soon hear the strange
intelligence than he hasten away to the old general with the
dreadful news of his grandson's suppose crime. perhaps his Highness
himself be not unwilling that his old friend and tutor in arm
should have the chance of save his family from disgrace; at all
event, Monsieur de Hengst, the Master of the Horse, be permit
to go off to the Baron undisturbed, and break to he the
intelligence of the accusation pende over the unfortunate
Chevalier.
'it be possible that he expect some such dreadful catastrophe,
for, after hear Hengst's narrative (as the latter afterwards tell
I), he only say, "Heaven's will be do!" for some time refuse to
stir a step in the matter, and then only by the solicitation of his
friend be induce to write the letter which Maxime de Magny
receive at our play-table.
'whilst he be there, squander the Princess's money, a police
visit be pay to his apartment, and a hundred proof, not of his
guilt with respect to the robbery, but of his guilty connection with
the Princess, be discover there,--tokens of her giving,
passionate letter from she, copy of his own correspondence to his
young friend at Paris,--all of which the Police Minister peruse,
and carefully put together under seal for his Highness, Prince
Victor. I have no doubt he peruse they, for, on deliver they to
the Hereditary Prince, Geldern say that, in obedience to his
HIGHNESS's order, he have collect the Chevalier's paper; but he
need not say that, on his honour, he (Geldern) himself have never
examine the document. his difference with Messieurs de Magny be
know; he beg his Highness to employ any other official person in
the judgment of the accusation bring against the young Chevalier.
'all these thing be go on while the Chevalier be at play. a
run of luck--you have great luck in those day, Monsieur de Balibari--
be against he. he stay and lose his 4000 ducat. he receive
his uncle's note, and such be the infatuation of the wretched
gambler, that, on receipt of it, he go down to the courtyard,
where the horse be in wait, absolutely take the money which the
poor old gentleman have place in the saddle-holster, bring it
upstairs, play it, and lose it; and when he issue from the room
to fly, it be too late: he be place in arrest at the bottom of my
staircase, as you be upon enter your own home.
'even when he come in under the charge of the soldiery send to
arrest he, the old General, who be wait, be overjoy to see
he, and fling himself into the lad's arm, and embrace he: it be
say, for the first time in many year. "he be here, gentleman," he
sob out,--"thank God he be not guilty of the robbery!" and then
sink back in a chair in a burst of emotion; painful, it be say by
those present, to witness on the part of a man so brave, and know
to be so cold and stern.
'"Robbery!" say the young man. "I swear before Heaven I be guilty
of none!" and a scene of almost touch reconciliation pass
between they, before the unhappy young man be lead from the guard-
house into the prison which he be destine never to quit.
'that night the Duke look over the paper which Geldern have
bring to he. it be at a very early stage of the perusal, no
doubt, that he give order for your arrest; for you be take at
midnight, Magny at ten o'clock; after which time the old Baron de
Magny have see his Highness, protest of his grandson's innocence,
and the Prince have receive he most graciously and kindly. his
Highness say he have no doubt the young man be innocent; his birth
and his blood render such a crime impossible; but suspicion be
too strong against he: he be know to have be that day closet
with the Jew; to have receive a very large sum of money which he
squander at play, and of which the Hebrew have, doubtless, be the
lender,--to have despatch his servant after he, who inquire the
hour of the Jew's departure, lie in wait for he, and rifle he.
Suspicion be so strong against the Chevalier, that common justice
require his arrest; and, meanwhile, until he clear himself, he
should be keep in not dishonourable durance, and every regard have
for his name, and the service of his honourable grandfather. with
this assurance, and with a warm grasp of the hand, the Prince leave
old General de Magny that night; and the veteran retire to rest
almost console, and confident in Maxime's eventual and immediate
release.
'but in the morning, before daybreak, the Prince, who have be
read paper all night, wildly call to the page, who sleep in
the next room across the door, bid he get horse, which be
always keep in readiness in the stable, and, fling a parcel of
letter into a box, tell the page to follow he on horseback with
these. the young man (Monsieur de Weissenborn) tell this to a young
lady who be then of my household, and who be now Madame de
Weissenborn, and a mother of a score of child.
'the page describe that never be such a change see as in his
august master in the course of that single night. his eye be
bloodshot, his face livid, his clothe be hang loose about he,
and he who have always make his appearance on parade as precisely
dress as any sergeant of his troop, might have be see
gallop through the lonely street at early dawn without a hat,
his unpowdered hair stream behind he like a madman.
'the page, with the box of paper, clatter after his master,--it
be no easy task to follow he; and they ride from the palace to the
town, and through it to the General's quarter. the sentinel at the
door be scar at the strange figure that rush up to the
General's gate, and, not know he, cross bayonet, and refuse
he admission. "Fools," say Weissenborn, "it be the Prince!" and,
jangle at the bell as if for an alarm of fire, the door be at
length open by the porter, and his Highness run up to the Generals
bedchamber, follow by the page with the box.
'"magny--magny," roar the Prince, thunder at the closed door,
"get up!" and to the query of the old man from within, answer,
"it be I--victor--the Prince!--get up!" and presently the door be
open by the General in his ROBE-DE-CHAMBRE, and the Prince
enter. the page bring in the box, and be bid to wait
without, which he do; but there lead from Monsieur de Magny's
bedroom into his antechamber two door, the great one which form
the entrance into his room, and a small one which lead, as the
fashion be with our house abroad, into the closet which
communicate with the alcove where the bed be. the door of this be
find by M. de Weissenborn to be open, and the young man be thus
enable to hear and see everything which occur within the
apartment.
'the General, somewhat nervously, ask what be the reason of so
early a visit from his Highness; to which the Prince do not for a
while reply, far than by star at he rather wildly, and
pace up and down the room.
'at last he say, "here be the cause!" dash his fist on the box;
and, as he have forget to bring the key with he, he go to the
door for a moment, say, "Weissenborn perhaps have it;" but see
over the stove one of the General's couteaux de chasse, he take it
down, and say, "that will do," and fall to work to burst the red
trunk open with the blade of the forest knife. the point break, and
he give an oath, but continue haggle on with the broken blade,
which be well suit to his purpose than the long pointed knife,
and finally succeed in wrench open the lid of the chest.
'"what be the matter?" say he, laugh. "herebe the matter;--read
that!--herebe more matter, read that!--here's more--no, not that;
thatbe somebody else's picture--but herebe her! do you know that,
magny? my wife's--the Princess's! why do you and your cursed race
ever come out of France, to plant your infernal wickedness wherever
your foot fall, and to ruin honest german home? what have you and
yours ever have from my family but confidence and kindness? we give
you a home when you have none, and herebe our reward!" and he fling a
parcel of paper down before the old General; who see the truth at
once;--he have know it long before, probably, and sink down on his
chair, cover his face.
'the Prince go on gesticulate, and shriek almost. "if a man
injure you so, Magny, before you begot the father of that gambling
lie villain yonder, you would have know how to revenge yourself.
you would have kill he! yes, would have kill he. but whobe to
help I to my revenge? Ihave no equal. I cannot meet that dog of a
frenchman,--that pimp from Versailles,--and kill he, as if he have
play the traitor to one of his own degree."
'"the blood of Maxime de Magny," say the old gentleman proudly, "be
as good as that of any prince in Christendom."
'"can I take it?" cry the Prince; "you know I cannot. I cannot have
the privilege of any other gentleman in Europe. what be I to do?
look here, magny: I be wild when I come here; I donot know what to
do. youhave serve I for thirty year; youhave save my life twice:
they be all knave and harlot about my poor old father here--no
honest man or woman--you be the only one--you save my life; tell
I what be I to do?" thus from insult Monsieur de Magny, the poor
distract Prince fall to supplicate he; and, at last, fairly
fling himself down, and burst out in an agony of tear.
'Old Magny, one of the most rigid and cold of man on common
occasion, when he see this outbreak of passion on the Prince's
part, became, as my informant have describe to I, as much affect
as his master. the old man from be cold and high, suddenly fall,
as it be, into the whimpering querulousness of extreme old age. he
lose all sense of dignity; he go down on his knee, and break out
into all sort of wild incoherent attempt at consolation; so much
so, that Weissenborn say he could not bear to look at the scene,
and actually turn away from the contemplation of it.
'but, from what follow in a few day, we may guess the result of
the long interview. the Prince, when he come away from the
conversation with his old servant, forget his fatal box of paper
and send the page back for they. the General be on his knee
pray in the room when the young man enter, and only stir and
look wildly round as the other remove the packet. the Prince ride
away to his hunting-lodge at three league from X----, and three
day after that Maxime de Magny die in prison; have make a
confession that he be engage in an attempt to rob the Jew, and
that he have make away with himself, ashamed of his dishonour.
'but it be not know that it be the General himself who take his
grandson poison: it be say even that he shoot he in the prison.
this, however, be not the case. General de Magny carry his
grandson the draught which be to carry he out of the world;
represent to the wretched youth that his fate be inevitable; that
it would be public and disgraceful unless he choose to anticipate the
punishment, and so leave he. but it be not of his own ACCORD, and
not until he have use EVERY mean of escape, as you shall hear, that
the unfortunate being's life be bring to an end.
'as for General de Magny, he quite fall into imbecility a short time
after his grandson's death, and my honour Duke's demise. after his
Highness the Prince marry the Princess Mary of F----, as they be
walk in the english park together they once meet old Magny riding
in the sun in the easy chair, in which he be carry commonly
abroad after his paralytic fit. "this be my wife, Magny," say the
Prince affectionately, take the veteran's hand; and he add,
turn to his princess, "General de Magny save my life during the
Seven Years' War."
'"what, youhave take she back again?" say the old man. "I wish
youwould send I back my poor maxime." he have quite forget the death
of the poor Princess Olivia, and the Prince, look very dark
indeed, pass away.
'and now,' say Madame de Liliengarten, 'I have only one more gloomy
story to relate to you--the death of the Princess Olivia. it be even
more horrible than the tale I have just tell you.' with which
preface the old lady resume her narrative.
'the kind weak Princess's fate be hasten, if not occasion, by
the cowardice of Magny. he find mean to communicate with she from
his prison, and her Highness, who be not in open disgrace yet (for
the Duke, out of regard to the family, persist in charge magny
with only robbery), make the most desperate effort to relieve he,
and to bribe the gaoler to effect his escape. she be so wild that
she lose all patience and prudence in the conduct of any scheme she
may have have for Magny's liberation; for her husband be inexorable,
and cause the Chevalier's prison to be too strictly guard for
escape to be possible. she offer the State jewel in pawn to the
Court banker; who of course be oblige to decline the transaction.
she fall down on her knee, it be say, to Geldern, the Police
Minister, and offer he Heaven know what as a bribe. finally, she
come scream to my poor dear Duke, who, with his age, disease,
and easy habit, be quite unfit for scene of so violent a nature;
and who, in consequence of the excitement create in his august
bosom by her frantic violence and grief, have a fit in which I very
nigh lose he. that his dear life be bring to an untimely end by
these transaction I have not the slight doubt; for the
Strasbourg pie, of which they say he die, never, I be sure, could
have injure he, but for the injury which his dear gentle heart
receive from the unusual occurrence in which he be force to take
a share.
'all her Highness's movement be carefully, though not ostensibly,
watch by her husband, Prince Victor; who, wait upon his august
father, sternly signify to he that if his Highness (MY Duke)
should dare to aid the Princess in her effort to release Magny, he,
Prince Victor, would publicly accuse the Princess and her paramour
of high treason, and take measure with the Diet for remove his
father from the throne, as incapacitate to reign. hence
interposition on our part be vain, and Magny be leave to his fate.
'it come, as you be aware, very suddenly. Geldern, Police Minister,
Hengst, Master of the Horse, and the colonel of the Prince's guard,
wait upon the young man in his prison two day after his
grandfather have visit he there and leave behind he the phial of
poison which the criminal have not the courage to use. and Geldern
signify to the young man that unless he take of his own accord the
laurelwater provide by the eld Magny, more violent mean of death
would be instantly employ upon he, and that a file of grenadier
be in wait in the courtyard to despatch he. see this, Magny,
with the most dreadful self-abasement, after drag himself round
the room on his knee from one officer to another, weep and
scream with terror, at last desperately drink off the potion, and
be a corpse in a few minute. thus end this wretched young man.
'his death be make public in the court GAZETTE two day after, the
paragraph state that Monsieur de M----, strike with remorse for
have attempt the murder of the Jew, have put himself to death by
poison in prison; and a warning be add to all young nobleman of
the duchy to avoid the dreadful sin of gambling, which have be the
cause of the young man's ruin, and have bring upon the grey hair
of one of the noble and most honourable of the servant of the
Duke irretrievable sorrow.
'the funeral be conduct with decent privacy, the General de Magny
attend it. the carriage of the two Dukes and all the first
people of the Court make their call upon the General afterwards. he
attend parade as usual the next day on the Arsenal-Place, and Duke
Victor, who have be inspect the building, come out of it lean
on the brave old warrior's arm. he be particularly gracious to the
old man, and tell his officer the oft-repeat story how at
Rosbach, when the X----contingent serve with the troop of the
unlucky Soubise, the General have throw himself in the way of a
french dragoon, who be press hard upon his Highness in the rout,
have receive the blow intend for his master, and kill the
assailant. and he allude to the family motto of "Magny san tache,"
and say, "it have be always so with his gallant friend and tutor
in arm." this speech affect all present very much; with the
exception of the old General, who only bow and do not speak: but
when he go home he be hear mutter "Magny san tache, Magny
san tache!" and be attack with paralysis that night, from which
he never more than partially recover.
'the news of Maxime's death have somehow be keep from the Princess
until now: a GAZETTE even be print without the paragraph
contain the account of his suicide; but it be at length, I know
not how, make known to she. and when she hear it, her lady tell
I, she scream and fall, as if strike dead; then sit up wildly and
rave like a madwoman, and be then carry to her bed, where her
physician attend she, and where she lie of a brain-fever. all this
while the Prince use to send to make inquiry concern she; and
from his give order that his Castle of Schlangenfels should be
prepare and furnish, I make no doubt it be his intention to send
she into confinement thither: as have be do with the unhappy
sister of his Britannic Majesty at Zell.
'she send repeatedly to demand an interview with his Highness; which
the latter decline, say that he would communicate with her
Highness when her health be sufficiently recover. to one of her
passionate letter he send back for reply a packet, which, when
open, be find to contain the emerald that have be the cause
round which all this dark intrigue move.
'her Highness at this time become quite frantic; vow in the
presence of all her lady that one lock of her darling Maxime's
hair be more precious to she than all the jewel in the world: rang
for her carriage, and say she would go and kiss his tomb;
proclaim the murder martyr's innocence, and call down the
punishment of Heaven, the wrath of her family, upon his assassin.
the Prince, on hear these speech (they be all, of course,
regularly bring to he), be say to have give one of his dreadful
look (which I remember now), and to have say, "this cannot last
much long."
'all that day and the next the Princess Olivia pass in dictate
the most passionate letter to the Prince her father, to the king
of France, Naples, and Spain, her kinsman, and to all other branch
of her family, call upon they in the most incoherent term to
protect she against the butcher and assassin her husband, assail
his person in the mad term of reproach, and at the same time
confess her love for the murder magny. it be in vain that
those lady who be faithful to she point out to her the
inutility of these letter, the dangerous folly of the confession
which they make; she insist upon write they, and use to give
they to her second robe-woman, a Frenchwoman (her Highness always
affectione person of that nation), who have the key of her
cassette, and carry every one of these epistle to Geldern.
'with the exception that no public reception be hold, the
ceremony of the Princess's establishment go on as before. her
lady be allow to wait upon she and perform their usual duty
about her person. the only man admit be, however, her servant,
her physician and chaplain; and one day when she wish to go into
the garden, a heyduc, who keep the door, intimate to her Highness
that the Prince's order be that she should keep her apartment.
'they abut, as you remember, upon the landing of the marble
staircase of Schloss X----; the entrance to Prince Victor's suite of
room be opposite the Princess's on the same landing. this space
be large, fill with sofa and bench, and the gentleman and
officer who wait upon the Duke use to make a sort of antechamber
of the landing-place, and pay their court to his Highness there, as
he pass out, at eleven o'clock, to parade. at such a time, the
heyducs within the Princess's suite of room use to turn out with
their halbert and present to Prince Victor--the same ceremony be
perform on his own side, when page come out and announce the
approach of his Highness. the page use to come out and say, "the
Prince, gentleman!" and the drum beat in the hall, and the
gentleman rise, who be wait on the bench that run along the
balustrade.
'as if fate impel she to her death, one day the Princess, as she
guard turn out, and she be aware that the Prince be stand,
as be his willnot, on the landing, converse with his gentleman (in
the old day he use to cross to the Princess's apartment and kiss
her hand)--the Princess, who have be anxious all the morning,
complain of heat, insist that all the door of the apartment
should be leave open; and give token of an insanity which I think
be now evident, rush wildly at the door when the guard pass
out, fling they open, and before a word could be say, or her lady
could follow she, be in presence of Duke Victor, who be talk as
usual on the landing: place herself between he and the stair, she
begin apostrophise he with frantic vehemence:--
'"take notice, gentleman!" she scream out, "that this man be a
murderer and a liar; that he lay plot for honourable gentleman,
and kill they in prison! take notice, that I too be in prison, and
fear the same fate: the same butcher who kill Maxime de Magny,
may, any night, put the knife to my throat. I appeal to you, and to
all the king of Europe, my royal kinsman. I demand to be set free
from this tyrant and villain, this liar and traitor! I adjure you
all, as gentleman of honour, to carry these letter to my relative,
and say from whom you have they!" and with this the unhappy lady
begin scatter letter about among the astonished crowd.
'"let no MAN STOOP!" cry the Prince, in a voice of thunder.
"Madame de Gleim, you should have watch your patient well. call
the Princess's physician: her Highness's brain be affect.
gentleman, have the goodness to retire." and the Prince stand on the
landing as the gentleman go down the stair, say fiercely to
the guard, "Soldier, if she move, strike with your halbert!" on
which the man bring the point of his weapon to the Princess's
breast; and the lady, frightened, shrink back and re-enter her
apartment. "now, Monsieur de Weissenborn," say the Prince, "pick
up all those paper;" and the Prince go into his own apartment,
precede by his page, and never quit they until he have see
every one of the paper burn.
'the next day the court GAZETTE contain a bulletin sign by the
three physician, state that "her Highness the Hereditary Princess
labour under inflammation of the brain, and have pass a restless
and disturbed night." similar notice be issue day after day. the
service of all her lady, except two, be dispense with. guard
be place within and without her door; her window be secure,
so that escape from they be impossible: and you know what take
place ten day after. the church-bell be ring all night, and
the prayer of the faithful ask for a person in EXTREMIS. a
GAZETTE appear in the morning, edge with black, and state that
the high and mighty Princess Olivia Maria Ferdinanda, consort of his
Serene Highness Victor Louis Emanuel, Hereditary Prince of X----,
have die in the evening of the 24th of January 1769.
'but do you know how she die, sir? that, too, be a mystery.
Weissenborn, the page, be concern in this dark tragedy; and the
secret be so dreadful, that never, believe I, till Prince Victor's
death, do I reveal it.
'after the fatal ESCLANDRE which the Princess have make, the Prince
send for Weissenborn, and bind he by the most solemn adjuration
to secrecy (he only break it to his wife many year after: indeed,
there be no secret in the world that woman cannot know if they
will), despatch he on the follow mysterious commission.
'"there live," say his Highness, "on the kehl side of the river,
opposite to Strasbourg, a man whose residence you will easily find
out from his name, which be MONSIEUR DE STRASBOURG. you will make
your inquiry concern he quietly, and without occasion any
remark; perhaps you have well go into Strasbourg for the purpose,
where the person be quite well know. you will take with you any
comrade on whom you can perfectly rely: the life of both, remember,
depend on your secrecy. you will find out some period when MONSIEUR
DE STRASBOURG be alone, or only in company of the domestic who live
with he (I myself visit the man by accident on my return from
Paris five year since, and hence be induce to send for he now, in
my present emergency). you will have your carriage wait at his
door at night; and you and your comrade will enter his house mask;
and present he with a purse of a hundred louis; promise he
double that sum on his return from his expedition. if he refuse, you
must use force and bring he; menace he with instant death should
he decline to follow you. you will place he in the carriage with
the blind draw, one or other of you never lose sight of he the
whole way, and threaten he with death if he discover himself or
cry out. you will lodge he in the old Tower here, where a room
shall be prepare for he; and his work be do, you will restore
he to his home with the same speed and secrecy with which you
bring he from it."
'such be the mysterious order Prince Victor give his page; and
Weissenborn, select for his comrade in the expedition Lieutenant
Bartenstein, set out on his strange journey.
'all this while the palace be hush, as if in mourning, the
bulletin in the court GAZETTE appear, announce the continuance
of the Princess's malady; and though she have but few attendant,
strange and circumstantial story be tell regard the progress
of her complaint. she be quite wild. she have try to kill herself.
she have fancy herself to be I donot know how many different
character. express be send to her family inform they of her
state, and courier despatch PUBLICLY to Vienna and Paris to
procure the attendance of physician skilled in treat disease of
the brain. that pretend anxiety be all a feint: it be never
intend that the Princess should recover.
'the day on which Weissenborn and Bartenstein return from their
expedition, it be announce that her Highness the Princess be much
bad; that night the report through the town be that she be at
the agony: and that night the unfortunate creature be endeavour
to make her escape.
'she have unlimited confidence in the french chamber-woman who
attend she, and between she and this woman the plan of escape be
arrange. the Princess take her jewel in a casket; a private door,
opening from one of her room and lead into the outer gate, it
be say, of the palace, be discover for she: and a letter be
bring to she, purport to be from the Duke, her father-in-law,
and state that a carriage and horse have be provide, and would
take she to b----: the territory where she might communicate with
her family and be safe.
'the unhappy lady, confide in her guardian, set out on the
expedition. the passage wind through the wall of the modern part
of the palace and abut in effect at the old Owl Tower, as it be
call, on the outer wall: the tower be pull down afterwards, and
for good reason.
'at a certain place the candle, which the chamberwoman be carry,
go out; and the Princess would have scream with terror, but she
hand be seize, and a voice cry "Hush!" the next minute a man in
a mask (it be the Duke himself) rush forward, gag she with a
handkerchief, her hand and leg be bind, and she be carry
swoon with terror into a vaulted room, where she be place by a
person there wait, and tie in an arm-chair. the same mask who
have gag she, come and bar her neck and say, "it have well be
do now she have faint."
'perhaps it would have be as well; for though she recover from
her swoon, and her confessor, who be present, come forward and
endeavour to prepare she for the awful deed which be about to be
do upon she, and for the state into which she be about to enter,
when she come to herself it be only to scream like a maniac, to
curse the Duke as a butcher and tyrant, and to call upon Magny, her
dear Magny.
'at this the Duke say, quite calmly, "may God have mercy on her
sinful soul!" he, the confessor, and Geldern, who be present, go
down on their knee; and, as his Highness drop his handkerchief,
Weissenborn fall down in a fainting fit; while MONSIEUR DE
STRASBOURG, take the back hair in his hand, separate the
shrieking head of Olivia from the miserable sinful body. may Heaven
have mercy upon her soul!'
. . . .
this be the story tell by Madame de Liliengarten, and the reader
will have no difficulty in draw from it that part which affect
myself and my uncle; who, after six week of arrest, be set at
liberty, but with order to quit the duchy immediately: indeed, with
an escort of dragoon to conduct we to the frontier. what property
we have, we be allow to sell and realise in money; but none of
our play debt be pay to we: and all my hope of the Countess Ida
be thus at an end.
when Duke Victor come to the throne, which he do when, six month
after, apoplexy carry off the old sovereign his father, all the
good old usage of X----were give up,--play forbidden; the opera
and ballet send to the right-about; and the regiment which the old
Duke have sell recall from their foreign service: with they come my
Countess's beggarly cousin the ensign, and he marry she. I donot
know whether they be happy or not. it be certain that a woman of
such a poor spirit do not merit any very high degree of pleasure.
the now reign Duke of X----himself marry four year after his
first wife's demise, and Geldern, though no long Police Minister,
build the grand house of which Madame de Liliengarten speak. what
become of the minor actor in the great tragedy, who know? only
MONSIEUR DE STRASBOURG be restore to his duty. of the rest--the
Jew, the chamber-woman, the spy on Magny--I know nothing. those
sharp tool with which great people cut out their enterprise be
generally break in the using: nor do I ever hear that their
employer have much regard for they in their ruin.
CHAPTER XIII
I continue MY CAREER as a man of FASHION
I find I have already fill up many score of page, and yet a vast
deal of the most interesting portion of my history remain to be
tell, viz. that which describe my sojourn in the kingdom of
England and Ireland, and the great part I play there; move among
the most illustrious of the land, myself not the least distinguish
of the brilliant circle. in order to give due justice to this
portion of my Memoirs, then,--which be more important than my
foreign adventure can be (though I could fill volume with
interesting description of the latter),--I shall cut short the
account of my travel in Europe, and of my success at the
Continental Courts, in order to speak of what befell I at home.
suffice it to say that there be not a capital in Europe, except the
beggarly one of Berlin, where the young Chevalier de Balibari be
not know and admire; and where he have not make the brave, the
high-bear, and the beautiful talk of he. I win 80,000 rouble from
Potemkin at the Winter Palace at Petersburg, which the scoundrelly
favourite never pay I; I have have the honour of see his Royal
Highness the Chevalier Charles Edward as drunk as any porter at
Rome; my uncle play several match at billiard against the
celebrate Lord C----at Spa, and I promise you do not come off a
loser. in fact, by a neat stratagem of ours, we raise the laugh
against his Lordship, and something a great deal more substantial.
my Lord do not know that the Chevalier Barry have a useless eye; and
when, one day, my uncle playfully bet he odd at billiard that he
would play he with a patch over one eye, the noble lord, think
to bite we (he be one of the most desperate gambler that ever
live), accept the bet, and we win a very considerable amount of
he.
nor need I mention my success among the fair portion of the
creation. one of the most accomplish, the tall, the most
athletic, and the handsome gentleman of Europe, as I be then, a
young fellow of my figure could not fail of have advantage, which
a person of my spirit know very well how to use. but upon these
subject I be dumb. Charming Schuvaloff, black-eyed Sczotarska, dark
Valdez, tender Hegenheim, brilliant Langeac!--ye gentle heart that
know how to beat in old time for the warm young irish gentleman,
where be you now? though my hair have grow grey now, and my sight
dim, and my heart cold with year, and ennui, and disappointment,
and the treachery of friend, yet I have but to lean back in my arm-
chair and think, and those sweet figure come rise up before I
out of the past, with their smile, and their kindness, and their
bright tender eye! there be no woman like they now--no manner
like theirs! look you at a bevy of woman at the Prince's, stitch
up in tight white satin sack, with their waist under their arm,
and compare they to the graceful figure of the old time! why, when
I dance with Coralie de Langeac at the fete on the birth of the
first dauphin at Versailles, her hoop be eighteen foot in
circumference, and the heel of her lovely little mule be three
inch from the ground; the lace of my jabot be worth a thousand
crown, and the button of my amaranth velvet coat alone cost eighty
thousand livre. look at the difference now! the gentleman be
dress like boxer, Quakers, or hackney-coachman; and the lady
be not dress at all. there be no elegance, no refinement; none of
the chivalry of the old world, of which I form a portion. think of
the fashion of London be lead by a Br-mm-l! [footnote: this
manuscript must have be write at the time when Mr. Brummel be
the leader of the London fashion.] a nobody's son: a low creature,
who can no more dance a minuet than I can talk Cherokee; who cannot
even crack a bottle like a gentleman; who never show himself to be
a man with his sword in his hand: as we use to approve ourselves in
the good old time, before that vulgar Corsican upset the gentry of
the world! oh, to see the Valdez once again, as on that day I meet
her first drive in state, with her eight mule and her retinue of
gentleman, by the side of yellow Mancanares! oh, for another drive
with Hegenheim, in the gild sledge, over the Saxon snow! false as
Schuvaloff be, 'twa well to be jilt by she than to be adore
by any other woman. I cannot think of any one of they without
tenderness. I have ringlet of all their hair in my poor little
museum of recollection. do you keep mine, you dear soul that
survive the turmoil and trouble of near half a hundred year? how
change its colour be now, since the day Sczotarska wear it round
her neck, after my duel with Count Bjernaski, at Warsaw.
I never keep any beggarly book of account in those day. I have no
debt. I pay royally for everything I take; and I take everything I
want. my income must have be very large. my entertainment and
equipage be those of a gentleman of the high distinction; nor
let any scoundrel presume to sneer because I carry off and marry
my Lady Lyndon (as you shall presently hear), and call I an
adventurer, or say I be penniless, or the match unequal. penniless!
I have the wealth of Europe at my command. adventurer! so be a
meritorious lawyer or a gallant soldier; so be every man who make
his own fortune an adventurer. my profession be play: in which I
be then unrivalle. no man could play with I through Europe, on
the square; and my income be just as certain (during health and the
exercise of my profession) as that of a man who draw on his three-
per-cent., or any fat squire whose acre bring he revenue. harvest
be not more certain than the effect of skill be: a crop be a chance,
as much as a game of card greatly play by a fine player: there
may be a drought, or a frost, or a hail-storm, and your stake be
lose; but one man be just as much an adventurer as another.
in evoke the recollection of these kind and fair creature I have
nothing but pleasure. I would I could say as much of the memory of
another lady, who will henceforth play a considerable part in the
drama of my life,--i mean the Countess of Lyndon; whose fatal
acquaintance I make at Spa, very soon after the event describe in
the last chapter have cause I to quit Germany.
Honoria, Countess of Lyndon, Viscountess Bullingdon in England,
Baroness Castle Lyndon of the kingdom of Ireland, be so well know
to the great world in her day, that I have little need to enter into
her family history; which be to be have in any peerage that the
reader may lay his hand on. she be, as I need not say, a countess,
viscountess, and baroness in her own right. her estate in Devon and
Cornwall be among the most extensive in those part; her irish
possession not less magnificent; and they have be allude to, in
a very early part of these Memoirs, as lie near to my own paternal
property in the kingdom of Ireland: indeed, unjust confiscation in
the time of Elizabeth and her father go to diminish my acre,
while they add to the already vast possession of the Lyndon
family.
the Countess, when I first see she at the assembly at Spa, be the
wife of her cousin, the Right honourable Sir Charles Reginald
Lyndon, Knight of the Bath, and Minister to George II. and George
III. at several of the small Courts of Europe. Sir Charles Lyndon
be celebrate as a wit and bon vivant: he could write love-verse
against Hanbury Williams, and make joke with George Selwyn; he be
a man of vertu like Harry Walpole, with whom and Mr. Grey he have
make a part of the grand tour; and be cite, in a word, as one of
the most elegant and accomplished man of his time.
I make this gentleman's acquaintance as usual at the play-table, of
which he be a constant frequenter. indeed, one could not but admire
the spirit and gallantry with which he pursue his favourite
pastime; for, though wear out by gout and a myriad of disease, a
cripple wheel about in a chair, and suffer pang of agony, yet
you would see he every morning and every evening at his post behind
the delightful green cloth: and if, as it would often happen, his
own hand be too feeble or inflame to hold the box, he would call
the main, nevertheless, and have his valet or a friend to throw for
he. I like this courageous spirit in a man; the great success
in life have be win by such indomitable perseverance.
I be by this time one of the well-know character in Europe; and
the fame of my exploit, my duel, my courage at play, would bring
crowd around I in any public society where I appear. I could
show ream of scented paper, to prove that this eagerness to make my
acquaintance be not confine to the gentleman only; but that I hate
boasting, and only talk of myself in so far as it be necessary to
relate myself's adventure: the most singular of any man's in
Europe. well, Sir Charles Lyndon's first acquaintance with I
originate in the right honourable knightbe win 700 piece of I
at picquet (for which he be almost my match); and I lose they with
much good-humour, and pay they: and pay they, you may be sure,
punctually. indeed, I will say this for myself, that lose money at
play never in the least put I out of good-humour with the winner,
and that wherever I find a superior, I be always ready to
acknowledge and hail he.
Lyndon be very proud of win from so celebrate a person, and we
contract a kind of intimacy; which, however, do not for a while
go beyond pump-room attention, and conversation over the supper-
table at play: but which gradually increase, until I be admit
into his more private friendship. he be a very free-speak man (the
gentry of those day be much prouder than at present), and use to
say to I in his haughty easy way, 'hang it, Mr. Barry, you have no
more manner than a barber, and I think my black footman have be
well educate than you; but you be a young fellow of originality
and pluck, and I like you, sir, because you seem determined to go to
the deuce by a way of your own.' I would thank he laughingly for
this compliment, and say, that as he be bind to the next world
much soon than I be, I would be oblige to he to get comfortable
quarter arrange there for I. he use also to be immensely amuse
with my story about the splendour of my family and the
magnificence of Castle Brady: he would never tire of listening or
laugh at those history.
'stick to the trumps, however, my lad,' he would say, when I tell
he of my misfortune in the conjugal line, and how near I have be
win the great fortune in Germany. 'do anything but marry, my
artless irish rustic' (he call I by a multiplicity of queer
name). 'cultivate your great talent in the gambling line; but mind
this, that a woman will beat you.'
that I deny; mention several instance in which I have conquer
the most intractable temper among the sex.
'they will beat you in the long run, my Tipperary Alcibiades. as
soon as you be marry, take my word of it, you be conquer. look
at I. I marry my cousin, the noble and great heiress in
England--marry she in spite of herself almost' (here a dark shade
pass over Sir Charles Lyndon's countenance). 'she be a weak woman.
you shall see she, sir, how weak she be; but she be my mistress. she
have embitter my whole life. she be a fool; but she have get the
well of one of the good head in Christendom. she be enormously
rich; but somehow I have never be so poor as since I marry she.
I think to better myself; and she have make I miserable and kill
I. and she will do as much for my successor, when I be go.'
'have her ladyship a very large income?' say I. at which Sir Charles
burst out into a yell laugh, and make I blush not a little at my
gaucherie; for the fact be, see he in the condition in which he
be, I could not help speculate upon the chance a man of spirit
might have with his widow.
'no, no!' say he, laugh. 'Waugh hawk, Mr. Barry; donot think, if
you value your peace of mind, to stand in my shoe when they be
vacant. besides, I donot think my Lady Lyndon would quite condescend
to marry a'----
'marry a what, sir?' say I, in a rage.
"never mind what: but the man who get she will rue it, take my word
on't. a plague on she! have it not be for my father's ambition and
mine (he be her uncle and guardian, and we wouldnot let such a
prize out of the family), I might have die peaceably, at least;
carry my gout down to my grave in quiet, live in my modest
tenement in Mayfair, have every house in England open to I; and now,
now I have six of my own, and every one of they be a hell to I.
beware of greatness, Mr. Barry. take warn by I. ever since I
have be marry and have be rich, I have be the most miserable
wretch in the world. look at I. I be die a wear-out cripple at
the age of fifty. marriage have add forty year to my life. when I
take off Lady Lyndon, there be no man of my year who look so
young as myself. fool that I be! I have enough with my pension,
perfect freedom, the good society in Europe; and I give up all
these, and marry, and be miserable. take a warning by I, Captain
Barry, and stick to the trump."
though my intimacy with the knight be considerable, for a long time
I never penetrate into any other apartment of his hotel but those
which he himself occupy. his lady live entirely apart from he;
and it be only curious how they come to travel together at all. she
be a goddaughter of old Mary Wortley Montagu: and, like that famous
old woman of the last century, make considerable pretension to be a
blue-stocking and a bel esprit. Lady Lyndon write poem in English
and Italian, which still may be read by the curious in the page of
the magazine of the day. she entertain a correspondence with
several of the european savan upon history, science, and ancient
language, and especially theology. her pleasure be to dispute
controversial point with abbe and bishop; and her flatterer say
she rival Madam Dacier in learn. every adventurer who have a
discovery in chemistry, a new antique bust, or a plan for
discover the philosopher's stone, be sure to find a patroness in
she. she have numberless work dedicated to she, and sonnet without
end address to she by all the poetaster of Europe, under the name
of Lindonira or Calista. her room be crowd with hideous China
magot, and all sort of object of vertu.
no woman pique herself more upon her principle, or allow love to
be make to she more profusely. there be a habit of courtship
practise by the fine gentleman of those day, which be little
understand in our coarse downright time: and young and old fellow
would pour out flood of compliment in letter and madrigal, such
as would make a sober lady stare be they address to she
nowadays: so entirely have the gallantry of the last century
disappear out of our manner.
Lady Lyndon move about with a little court of her own. she have
half-a-dozen carriage in her progress. in her own she would
travel with her companion (some shabby lady of quality), her bird,
and poodle, and the favourite savant for the time be. in another
would be her female secretary and her waiting-woman; who, in spite
of their care, never could make their mistress look much well than
a slattern. Sir Charles Lyndon have his own chariot, and the
domestic of the establishment would follow in other vehicle.
also must be mention the carriage in which ride her ladyship's
chaplain, Mr. Runt, who act in capacity of governor to her son,
the little Viscount bullingdon,--a melancholy desert little boy,
about whom his father be more than indifferent, and whom his mother
never see, except for two minute at her levee, when she would put
to he a few question of history or latin grammar; after which he
be consign to his own amusement, or the care of his governor,
for the rest of the day.
the notion of such a Minerva as this, whom I see in the public
place now and then, surround by swarm of needy abbe and

have not the least desire to make her acquaintance. I have no desire
to be one of the beggarly adorer in the great lady's train,--
fellow, half friend, half lacquey, who make verse, and write
letter, and run errand, content to be pay by a seat in her
ladyship's box at the comedy, or a cover at her dinner-table at
noon. 'donot be afraid,' Sir Charles Lyndon would say, whose great
subject of conversation and abuse be his lady: 'my Lindonira will
have nothing to do with you. she like the tuscan brogue, not that
of Kerry. she say you smell too much of the stable to be admit
to lady' society; and last Sunday fortnight, when she do I the
honour to speak to I last, say, "I wonder, Sir Charles Lyndon, a
gentleman who have be the King's ambassador can demean himself by
gambling and boozing with low irish blackleg!" donot fly in a fury!
Ibe a cripple, and it be Lindonira say it, not i.'
this pique I, and I resolve to become acquainted with Lady
Lyndon; if it be but to show her ladyship that the descendant of
those Barrys, whose property she unjustly hold, be not an unworthy
companion for any lady, be she ever so high. besides, my friend
the knight be die: his widow would be the rich prize in the
three kingdom. why should I not win she, and, with she, the mean
of make in the world that figure which my genius and inclination

Christendom, and determine to bend this haughty lady. when I
determine, I look upon the thing as do.
my uncle and I talk the matter over, and speedily settle upon a
method for make our approach upon this stately lady of Castle

pleasure, of a glass of Rhenish in the garden-house in the summer
evening, and of a sly throw of the dice when the occasion offer;
and I take care to make friend with this person, who, be a
college tutor and an Englishman, be ready to go on his knee to any
one who resemble a man of fashion. see I with my retinue of

horse, dress in gold, and velvet, and sable, salute the
great people in Europe as we meet on the course, or at the Spas,
Runt be dazzle by my advance, and be mine by a beckoning of the

ask he to dine, with two count, off gold plate, at the little
room in the casino: he be make happy by be allow to win a few
piece of we, become exceedingly tipsy, sing Cambridge song, and
recreate the company by tell we, in his horrid Yorkshire French,
story about the gyp, and all the lord that have ever be in his
college. I encourage he to come and see I oftener, and bring with
he his little viscount; for whom, though the boy always detest
I, I take care to have a good stock of sweetmeat, toy, and
picture-book when he come.
I then begin to enter into a controversy with Mr. Runt, and confide
to he some doubt which I have, and a very very earnest leaning
towards the Church of Rome. I make a certain abbe whom I know write
I letter upon transubstantiation, &c., which the honest tutor be
rather puzzled to answer. I know that they would be communicate to
his lady, as they be; for, ask leave to attend the English
service which be celebrate in her apartment, and frequent by
the good English then at the Spa, on the second Sunday she
condescend to look at I; on the third she be pleased to reply to
my profound bow by a curtsey; the next day I follow up the
acquaintance by another obeisance in the public walk; and, to make a
long story short, her ladyship and I be in full correspondence on
transubstantiation before six week be over. my Lady come to the
aid of her chaplain; and then I begin to see the prodigious weight
of his argument: as be to be expect. the progress of this
harmless little intrigue need not be detail. I make no doubt every
one of my reader have practise similar stratagem when a fair lady
be in the case.
I shall never forget the astonishment of Sir Charles Lyndon when, on
one summer evening, as he be issue out to the play-table in his
sedan-chair, accord to his willnot, her ladyship's barouche and
four, with her outrider in the tawny livery of the Lyndon family,
come drive into the courtyard of the house which they inhabit;
and in that carriage, by her ladyship's side, sit no other than the
'vulgar Irish adventurer,' as she be pleased to call he: I mean
Redmond Barry, Esquire. he make the most courtly of his bow, and
grin and wave his hat in as graceful a manner as the gout
permit; and her ladyship and I reply to the salutation with the
utmost politeness and elegance on our part.
I could not go to the play-table for some time afterwards for Lady
Lyndon and I have an argument on transubstantiation, which last for
three hour; in which she be, as usual, victorious, and, in which
her companion, the Honourable Miss Flint Skinner, fall asleep; but
when, at last, I join Sir Charles at the casino, he receive I

way. he laugh and sneer at everything. he laugh when he be in
a paroxysm of pain; he laugh when he win money, or when he lose
it: his laugh be not jovial or agreeable, but rather painful and
sardonic.
'gentleman,' say he to Punter, Colonel Loder, Count du Carreau, and
several jovial fellow with whom he use to discuss a flask of
champagne and a Rhenish trout or two after play, 'see this amiable
youth! he have be trouble by religious scruple, and have fly for

wife, Lady Lyndon; and, between they both, they be confirm my
ingenious young friend in his faith. do you ever hear of such
doctor, and such a disciple?'
''Faith, sir,' say I, 'if I want to learn good principle, itbe
surely well I should apply for they to your lady and your chaplain
than to you!'
'he want to step into my shoe!' continue the knight.
'the man would be happy who do so,' respond I, 'provide there
be no chalk-stone include!' at which reply Sir Charles be not
very well pleased, and go on with increase rancour. he be always
free-speak in his cup; and, to say the truth, he be in his cup

'be it not a pleasure, gentleman,' say he, 'for I, as I be draw
near the goal, to find my home such a happy one; my wife so fond of
I, that she be even now think of appoint a successor? (I
donot mean you precisely, Mr. Barry; you be only take your chance
with a score of other whom I could mention.) benot it a comfort to
see she, like a prudent housewife, get everything ready for her
husband's departure?'
'I hope you be not think of leave we soon, knight?' say I,
with perfect sincerity; for I like he, as a most amusing
companion. 'not so soon, my dear, as you may fancy, perhaps,'

year; and there be always a candidate or two wait to apply for
the situation. who know how long I may keep you wait?' and he
do keep I wait some little time long than at that period
there be any reason to suspect.
as I declare myself pretty openly, accord to my usual way, and
author be accustomed to describe the person of the lady with
whom their hero fall in love; in compliance with this fashion, I
perhaps should say a word or two respect the charm of my Lady
Lyndon. but though I celebrate they in many copy of verse, of my
own and other person' writing; and though I fill ream of paper
in the passionate style of those day with compliment to every one

goddess, or famous heroine ever hear of,--truth compel I to say
that there be nothing divine about she at all. she be very well;
but no more. her shape be fine, her hair dark, her eye good, and
exceedingly active; she love singing, but perform it as so great

science than I even know the name of. she pique herself on

difference with I, run--but that matter shall be tell in its
proper time. finally, my Lady Lyndon be about a year old than
myself; though, of course, she would take her Bible oath that she
be three year young.
few man be so honest as I be; for few will own to their real
motif, and I donot care a button about confess mine. what Sir
Charles Lyndon say be perfectly true. I make the acquaintance of
Lady Lyndon with ulterior view. 'Sir,' say I to he, when, after

Iwill be swear to muster just as much regard for my Lady Lyndon as
you ever show she; and if I win she and wear she when you be dead

ghost will deter I?'
Lyndon laugh as usual; but somewhat disconcertedly: indeed I have
clearly the good of he in the argument, and have just as much right
to hunt my fortune as he have.
but one day he say, 'if you marry such a woman as my Lady Lyndon,
mark my word, you will regret it. you will pine after the liberty
you once enjoy. by George! Captain Barry,' he add, with a sigh,
'the thing that I regret most in life--perhaps it be because I be

'ha! ha! a milkmaid's daughter!' say I, laugh at the absurdity.
'well, why not a milkmaid's daughter? my good fellow, I be in love

bounce girl; of course old than myself' (this make I remember

therebe nothing like have a virtuous drudge at home, sir; depend
upon that. it give a zest to one's enjoyment in the world, take my
word for it. no man of sense need restrict himself, or deny himself
a single amusement for his wife's sake: on the contrary, if he
select the animal properly, he will choose such a one as shall be no
bar to his pleasure, but a comfort in his hour of annoyance. for
instance, I have get the gout: who tend I? a hire valet, who rob
I whenever he have the power. my wife never come near I. what
friend have I? none in the wide world. man of the world, as you and
I be, donot make friend; and we be fool for our pain. get a
friend, sir, and that friend a woman--a good household drudge, who
love you. that be the most precious sort of friendship; for the
expense of it be all on the woman's side. the man neednot contribute
anything. if hebe a rogue, shewill vow hebe an angel; if hebe a
brute, she will like he all the well for his ill-treatment of
she. they like it, sir, these woman. they be bear to be our
great comfort and convenience; our--our moral bootjack, as it
be; and to man in your way of life, believe I such a person would
be invaluable. I be only speak for your bodily and mental
comfort's sake, mind. why donot I marry poor Helena Flower, the
curate's daughter?'
I think these speech the remark of a weakly disappointed man;
although since, perhaps, I have have reason to find the truth of Sir
Charles Lyndon's statement. the fact be, in my opinion, that we
often buy money very much too dear. to purchase a few thousand a
year at the expense of an odious wife, be very bad economy for a
young fellow of any talent and spirit; and there have be moment
of my life when, in the midst of my great splendour and opulence,
with half-a-dozen lord at my levee, with the fine horse in my
stable, the grand house over my head, with unlimited credit at
my banker's, and--Lady Lyndon to boot, I have wish myself back a
private of Bulow's, or anything, so as to get rid of she. to return,
however, to the story. Sir Charles, with his complication of ill,
be die before we by inch! and Ihave no doubt it could not have
be very pleasant to he to see a young handsome fellow pay
court to his widow before his own face as it be. after I once get
into the house on the transubstantiation dispute, I find a dozen
more occasion to improve my intimacy, and be scarcely ever out of
her ladyship's door. the world talk and bluster; but what care
I? the man cry fie upon the shameless irish adventurer; but I have
tell my way of silence such envious people: and my sword have by
this time get such a reputation through Europe, that few people
care to encounter it. if I can once get my hold of a place, I keep
it. Manybe the house I have be to where I have see the man avoid
I. 'Faugh! the low Irishman,' they would say. 'bah! the coarse
adventurer!' 'out on the insufferable blackleg and puppy!' and so
forth. this hatred have be of no inconsiderable service to I in
the world; for when I fasten on a man, nothing can induce I to
release my hold: and I be leave to myself, which be all the well.
as I tell Lady Lyndon in those day, with perfect sincerity,
'Calista' (I use to call her Calista in my correspondence)--'
Calista, I swear to thee, by the spotlessness of thy own soul, by
the brilliancy of thy immitigable eye, by everything pure and
chaste in heaven and in thy own heart, that I will never cease from
follow thee! scorn I can bear, and have bear at thy hand.
Indifference I can surmount; 'tis a rock which my energy will climb
over, a magnet which attract the dauntless iron of my soul!' and it
be true, I wouldnot have leave she--no, though they have kick I
downstairs every day I present myself at her door.
that be my way of fascinating woman. let the man who have to make his
fortune in life remember this maxim. attack be his only secret.
Dare, and the world always yield: or, if it beat you sometimes,
dare again, and it will succumb. in those day my spirit be so
great, that if I have set my heart upon marry a princess of the
blood, I would have have she!
I tell Calista my story, and alter very very little of the truth.
my object be to frighten she: to show she that what I want, that
I dare; that what I dare, that I win; and there be strike
passage enough in my history to convince she of my iron will and
indomitable courage. 'never hope to escape I, madam,' I would say:
'offer to marry another man, and he die upon this sword, which
never yet meet its master. fly from I, and I will follow you, though
it be to the gate of Hades.' I promise you this be very
different language to that she have be in the habit of hear from
her Jemmy-Jessamy adorer. you should have see how I scar the
fellow from she.
when I say in this energetic way that I would follow Lady Lyndon
across the Styx if necessary, of course I mean that I would do so,
provide nothing more suitable present itself in the interim. if
Lyndon would not die, where be the use of my pursue the Countess?
and somehow, towards the end of the Spa season, very much to my
mortification I do confess, the knight make another rally: it seem
as if nothing would kill he. 'I be sorry for you, Captain Barry,'
he would say, laugh as usual. 'Ibe grieve to keep you, or any
gentleman, wait. have you not well arrange with my doctor, or
get the cook to flavour my omelette with arsenic? what be the odd,
gentleman,' he would add, 'that I donot live to see Captain Barry
hang yet?'
in fact, the doctor tinker he up for a year. 'itbe my usual
luck,' I could not help say to my uncle, who be my confidential
and most excellent adviser in all matter of the heart. 'Ihave be
waste the treasure of my affection upon that flirt of a
countess, and herebe her husband restore to health and likely to
live I donot know how many year!' and, as if to add to my
mortification, there come just at this period to spa an english
tallow-chandler's heiress, with a plum to her fortune; and Madame
Cornu, the widow of a Norman cattle-dealer and farmer-general, with
a dropsy and two hundred thousand livre a year.
'whatbe the use of my follow the Lyndons to England,' say I, 'if
the knight willnot die?'
'donot follow they, my dear simple child,' reply my uncle. 'stop
here and pay court to the new arrival.'
'yes, and lose Calista for ever, and the great estate in all
England.'
'Pooh, pooh! youth like you easily fire and easily despond. keep up
a correspondence with Lady Lyndon. you know therebe nothing she
like so much. therebe the irish abbe, who will write you the most
charming letter for a crown apiece. let she go; write to she, and
meanwhile look out for anything else which may turn up. who know?
you might marry the Norman widow, bury she, take her money, and be
ready for the Countess against the knight's death.'
and so, with vow of the most profound respectful attachment, and
have give twenty louis to Lady Lyndon's waiting-woman for a lock
of her hair (of which fact, of course, the woman inform her
mistress), I take leave of the Countess, when it become necessary
for her return to her estate in England; swear I would follow
she as soon as an affair of honour I have on my hand could be
bring to an end.
I shall pass over the event of the year that ensue before I again
see she. she write to I accord to promise; with much regularity
at first, with somewhat less frequency afterwards. my affair,
meanwhile, at the play-table go on not unprosperously, and I be
just on the point of marry the widow Cornu (we be at Brussels
by this time, and the poor soul be madly in love with I,) when the
London Gazette be put into my hand, and I read the follow
announcement:--
'die at Castle-Lyndon, in the kingdom of Ireland, the right
honourable Sir Charles Lyndon, Knight of the Bath, member of
Parliament for Lyndon in Devonshire, and many year his Majesty's
representative at various European Courts. he hath leave behind he a
name which be endear to all his friend for his manifold virtue
and talent, a reputation justly acquire in the service of his
Majesty, and an inconsolable widow to deplore his loss. her
ladyship, the bereaved Countess of Lyndon, be at the bath when the
horrid intelligence reach she of her husband's demise, and
hasten to Ireland immediately in order to pay her last sad duty
to his beloved remain.'
that very night I order my chariot and post to Ostend, whence I
freight a vessel to Dover, and travel rapidly into the West,
reach Bristol; from which port I embark for Waterford, and find
myself, after an absence of eleven year, in my native country.
CHAPTER XIV
I return to IRELAND, and EXHIBIT my splendour and GENEROSITY in that
KINGDOM
how be time change with I now! I have leave my country a poor
penniless boy--a private soldier in a miserable march regiment. I
return an accomplished man, with property to the amount of five
thousand guineas in my possession, with a splendid wardrobe and
jewel-case worth two thousand more; have mingle in all the scene
of life a not undistinguished actor in they; have share in war
and in love; have by my own genius and energy win my way from
poverty and obscurity to competence and splendour. as I look out
from my chariot window as it roll along over the bleak bare
road, by the miserable cabin of the peasantry, who come out in
their rag to stare as the splendid equipage pass, and huzza'd for
his Lordship's honour as they see the magnificent stranger in the
superb gild vehicle, my huge body-servant Fritz loll behind
with curling moustache and long queue, his green livery bar with
silver lace, I could not help think of myself with considerable
complacency, and thank my star that have endow I with so many
good quality. but for my own merit I should have be a raw irish
squireen such as those I see swagger about the wretched town
through which my chariot pass on its road to Dublin. I might have
marry Nora Brady (and though, thank Heaven, I do not, I have
never think of that girl but with kindness, and even remember the
bitterness of lose she more clearly at this moment than any other
incident of my life); I might have be the father of ten child
by this time, or a farmer on my own account, or an agent to a
squire, or a gauger, or an attorney; and here I be one of the most
famous gentleman of Europe! I bid my fellow get a bag of copper
money and throw it among the crowd as we change horse; and I
warrant I there be as much shouting set up in praise of my honour
as if my Lord Townshend, the Lord Lieutenant himself, have be
pass.
my second day's journey--for the irish road be rough in those
day, and the progress of a gentleman's chariot terribly slow--

use eleven year back, when fly from home after the suppose
murder of Quin in the duel. how well I remember every moment of the
scene! the old landlord be go who have serve I; the inn that I
then think so comfortable look wretched and dismantle; but the
claret be as good as in the old day, and I have the host to partake
of a jug of it and hear the news of the country.
he be as communicative as host usually be: the crop and the
market, the price of beast at last Castle Dermot fair, the last
story about the vicar, and the last joke of Father Hogan the priest;
how the Whiteboys have burn Squire Scanlan's rick, and the
highwayman have be beat off in their attack upon Sir Thomas's

wonderful run entirely they have last March; what troop be in the

the news of sport, assize, and quarter-session be detail by
this worthy chronicler of small-beer, who wonder that my honour
havenot hear of they in England, or in foreign part, where he
seem to think the world be as interested as he be about the
doing of Kilkenny and Carlow. I listen to these tale with, I
own, a considerable pleasure; for every now and then a name would
come up in the conversation which I remember in old day, and
bring with it a hundred association connect with they.
I have receive many letter from my mother, which inform I of the
doing of the Brady's Town family. my uncle be dead, and Mick, his
eld son, have follow he too to the grave. the Brady girl have
separate from their paternal roof as soon as their eld brother

I donot care to own that I hire a stout nag from the landlord's

beat I so often in old day, to give my horse a feed and a

my-self to his memory till the next day, when I put ten guineas into
his hand, and bid he drink the health of English Redmond.
as for Castle Brady, the gate of the park be still there; but the

Castle Brady, and thought of the bygone time.
the hall-door be open--it be always so at that house; the moon be

could see my uncle's honest face, and hear he talk to his dog

'whobe there?' say the old man.
'PHIL PURCELL, donot you know I?' shout I; 'itbe Redmond Barry.'
I think the old man would have fire his piece at I at first, for

I settle a hundred guineas on the old man when I get to Dublin, and

Poor Phil Purcell be amuse himself at a game of exceedingly dirty

go salute poor Molly next day, and find she a slatternly wench in a

of my friend the blacksmith.
from Tim and Phil Purcell, thus meet fortuitously together, I get the
very last news respect my family. my mother be well.
''Faith sir,' say Tim, 'and yoube come in time, mayhap, for
prevent an addition to your family.'
'Sir!' exclaim I, in a fit of indignation.

Poor Nora, he add, have make many addition to the illustrious race

I see I should have no small family to provide for; and then, to

many man of high lineage like my own have, and, in default of well

I go back to the village in the morning, and find a pretext for
visit Barryville under a device of purchase drug. the hook

my people be not a little anxious regard I, by the time I

the citizen of Dublin have as great and laudable a desire for
know their neighbour' concern as the country people have; and

I fix at length upon a handsome suite of apartment in Capel

way. besides, in those day, in Ireland, if a gentleman warrant

have be the offer of a bullet in your waistcoat. I have play at the
bullet game too much in earnest to make use of it heedlessly: and I
may say, proudly for myself, that I never engage in a duel unless I
have a real, available, and prudent reason for it.
there be a simplicity about this irish gentry which amuse and make

after have witness the splendour of civilised life abroad, the

without the regal grandeur of the latter city. the people look
more ragged than any race I have ever see, except the gipsy horde

luckless fellow who could not keep a carriage, and walk the

who neither know the use of shoe nor razor; and as a gentleman

of wild gibber milesian face as would frighten a genteel person
of average nerve. I be luckily endow with strong one; besides,
have see my amiable countryman before.
I know this description of they will excite anger among some irish

be a poor provincial place, Dublin, in the old day of which I

always to go to sleep, I yet have hear from well-inform party
that Mr. Burke be a person of considerable ability, and even
repute to be eloquent in his more favourable moment.
I soon begin to enjoy to the full extent the pleasure that the
wretched place afford, and which be within a gentleman's reach:

with he, or to ride with he (regard be have to our weight), or
to shoot fly, or at a mark; and in this latter accomplishment,
especially if the mark be a live one, irish gentleman of that day
have no ordinary skill.
of course I despatch a courier in my livery to Castle Lyndon
with a private letter for Runt, demand from he full particular

and eloquent letter to her ladyship, in which I bade she remember

now, there be a sort of rough-and-ready law in Ireland in those

unattended to. the celebrated Captain Thunder rule in the southern

young lady; or, perhaps, have not time for a long and intricate

I have find my cousin Ulick at Dublin, grow very fat, and very
poor; hunt up by Jews and creditor: dwell in all sort of
queer corner, from which he issue at nightfall to the Castle, or
to his card-party at his tavern; but he be always the courageous
fellow: and I hint to he the state of my affection regard
Lady Lyndon.
'the Countess of Lyndon!' say poor Ulick; 'well, that be a wonder.
I myself have be mightily sweet upon a young lady, one of the
Kiljoys of Ballyhack, who have ten thousand pound to her fortune,
and to whom her ladyship be guardian; but how be a poor fellow
without a coat to his back to get on with an heiress in such company
as that? I might as well propose for the Countess myself.'
'you have well not,' say I, laugh; 'the man who try run a
chance of go out of the world first.' and I explain to he my

heiress in England.
I bade Ulick go out of town on any pretext he choose, and put a

the clumsy english school, only stand before my point until I have
determine where I should hit he.
my sword go in under his guard, and come out at his back. when he

Barry, I be wrong!' I feel not very well at ease when the poor

he lie on his bed for four month with the effect of that wound;

'you, Ulick,' say I, 'shall be NUMBER two.'
''faith,' say my cousin, 'onebe enough:' but I have my plan

CHAPTER XV
I pay court to MY lady lyndon
as my uncle's attainder be not reverse for be out with the

hang, at least a tedious process of imprisonment, and a doubtful

'I would, my dear child,' he add, 'that the ugly attainder round

there be much practical good sense in this advice; which I quote,

letter in the mere wantonness of coquetry, and not under the
influence of affection?'
'villain!' cry my Lady Lyndon, 'could you dare to construe out of
those idle letter of mine any other meaning than that which they
really bear?'
'I will construe anything out of they,' say I; 'such be the passion
which animate I towards you. I have swear it--you must and shall
be mine! do you ever know I promise to accomplish a thing and
fail? which will you prefer to have from I--a love such as woman
never know from man before, or a hatred to which there exist no
parallel?'
'a woman of my rank, sir, can fear nothing from the hatred of an
adventurer like yourself,' reply the lady, draw up stately.
'look at your Poynings--be he of your rank? you be the cause of
that young man's wound, madam; and, but that the instrument of your
savage cruelty relent, would have be the author of his murder--
yes, of his murder; for, if a wife be faithless, do not she arm
the husband who punish the seducer! and I look upon you, Honoria
Lyndon, as my wife.'
'Husband? wife, sir!' cry the widow, quite astonished.
'yes, wife! husband! I be not one of those poor soul with whom
coquette can play, and who may afterwards throw they aside. you
would forget what pass between we at Spa: Calista would forget
Eugenio; but I will not let you forget I. you think to trifle
with my heart, do you? when once move, Honoria, it be move for
ever. I love you--love as passionately now as I do when my passion
be hopeless; and, now that I can win you, do you think I will
forego you? Cruel cruel Calista! you little know the power of your
own charm if you think their effect be so easily obliterate--you
little know the constancy of this pure and noble heart if you think
that, have once love, it can ever cease to adore you. no! I swear
by your cruelty that I will revenge it; by your wonderful beauty
that I will win it, and be worthy to win it. lovely, fascinating,
fickle, cruel woman! you shall be mine--I swear it! your wealth may
be great; but be I not of a generous nature enough to use it
worthily? your rank be lofty; but not so lofty as my ambition. you
throw yourself away once on a cold and spiritless debauchee: give
yourself now, Honoria, to a MAN; and one who, however lofty your
rank may be, will enhance it and become it!'
as I pour word to this effect out on the astonished widow, I
stand over she, and fascinate she with the glance of my eye; see
her turn red and pale with fear and wonder; see that my praise of
her charm and the exposition of my passion be not unwelcome to
she, and witness with triumphant composure the mastery I be
gain over she. terror, be sure of that, be not a bad ingredient
of love. a man who will fiercely to win the heart of a weak and
vapourish woman must succeed, if he have opportunity enough.
'terrible man!' say Lady Lyndon, shrink from I as soon as I have
do speak (indeed, I be at a loss for word, and think of
another speech to make to her)--'terrible man! leave I.'
I see that I have make an impression on she, from those very word.
'if she let I into the house to-morrow,' say I, 'she be mine.'
as I go downstairs I put ten guineas into the hand of the hall-
porter, who look quite astonished at such a gift.
'it be to repay you for the trouble of open the door to I,' say
I; 'you will have to do so often.'
CHAPTER XVI
I provide NOBLY for MY FAMILY
the next day when I go back, my fear be realise: the door be

'your lady be not out,' say I: 'she have deny I, and I cannot, of
