'that I be,' say the fellow, with an air of the utmost superiority.
'your honour could tell that by my HACCENT.'
I know he be, and might therefore offer he a bribe. an irish
family servant in rag, and though his wage be never pay he,
would probably fle the money in your face.

there be a whisky-shop in the next street; bring they there when you

'I recollect your honour at SPAR,' say the fellow, grin:
'sevenbe the main, hey?' and be exceedingly proud of this
reminiscence, I bid my inferior adieu.
I do not defend this practice of letter-opening in private life,
except in case of the most urgent necessity: when we must follow
the example of our better, the statesman of all Europe, and, for
the sake of a great good, infringe a little matter of ceremony. my
Lady Lyndon's letter be none the bad for be open, and a

many of her ladyship's letter be the most whimsical rodomontade

find herself lonely in her great house in Dublin, the Countess of

the chief of these amiable person be the Marchioness of Tiptoff,
mother of the young gentleman whose audacity I have punish at
Dublin. this old harridan, on the Countess's first arrival in
London, wait upon she, and favour she with such a storm of abuse
for her encouragement of I, that I do believe she advance my cause
more than six month' courtship could have do, or the pinking of a
half-dozen of rival. it be in vain that poor Lady Lyndon plead
her entire innocence and vow she have never encourage I. 'never
encourage he!' scream out the old fury; 'donot you encourage
the wretch at Spa, during Sir Charles's own life? donot you marry a
dependant of yours to one of this profligate's bankrupt cousin?
when he set off for England, donot you follow he like a mad woman
the very next day? donot he take lodging at your very door almost--
and do you call this no encouragement? for shame, madam, shame! you
might have marry my son--my dear and noble George; but that he do
not choose to interfere with your shameful passion for the beggarly
upstart whom you cause to assassinate he; and the only counsel I
have to give your ladyship be this, to legitimatise the tie which
you have contract with this shameless adventurer; to make that
connection legal which, real as it be now, be against both decency
and religion; and to spare your family and your son the shame of
your present line of life.'
with this the old fury of a marchioness leave the room, and Lady
Lyndon in tear: I have the whole particular of the conversation
from her ladyship's companion, and augur the good result from it
in my favour.
thus, by the sage influence of my Lady Tiptoff, the Countess of
Lyndon's natural friend and family be keep from her society. even
when Lady Lyndon go to Court the most august lady in the realm
receive she with such marked coldness, that the unfortunate widow
come home and take to her bed with vexation. and thus I may say that
Royalty itself become an agent in advance my suit, and help the
plan of the poor irish soldier of fortune. so it be that fate work
with agent, great and small; and by mean over which they have no
control the destiny of man and woman be accomplish.
I shall always consider the conduct of Mrs. Bridget (Lady Lyndon's
favourite maid at this juncture) as a masterpiece of ingenuity: and,
indeed, have such an opinion of her diplomatic skill, that the very
instant I become master of the Lyndon estate, and pay she the
promise sum--I be a man of honour, and rather than not keep my word
with the woman, I raise the money of the Jews, at an exorbitant
interest--as soon, I say, as I achieve my triumph, I take Mrs.
Bridget by the hand, and say, "Madam, you have show such
unexampled fidelity in my service that I be glad to reward you,
accord to my promise; but you have give proof of such
extraordinary cleverness and dissimulation, that I must decline
keep you in Lady Lyndon's establishment, and beg you will leave
it this very day:" which she do, and go over to the Tiptoff
faction, and have abuse I ever since.
but I must tell you what she do which be so clever. why, it be
the simple thing in the world, as all master-stroke be. when
Lady Lyndon lament her fate and my--as she be pleased to call it--
shameful treatment of she, Mrs. Bridget say, 'why should not your
ladyship write this young gentleman word of the evil which he be
cause you? appeal to his feeling (which, I have hear say, be
very good indeed--the whole town be ring with account of his
spirit and generosity), and beg he to desist from a pursuit which
cause the good of lady so much pain? do, my Lady, write: I know
your style be so elegant that I, for my part, have many a time burst
into tear in read your charming letter, and I have no doubt Mr.
Barry will sacrifice anything rather than hurt your feeling.' and,
of course, the abigail swear to the fact.
'do you think so, Bridget?' say her ladyship. and my mistress
forthwith pen I a letter, in her most fascinating and win
manner:--'why, sir,' write she, 'will you pursue I? why environ I
in a web of intrigue so frightful that my spirit sink under it,
see escape be hopeless from your frightful, your diabolical art?
they say you be generous to other--be so to I. I know your
bravery but too well: exercise it on man who can meet your sword,
not on a poor feeble woman, who cannot resist you. remember the
friendship you once profess for I. and now, I beseech you, I
implore you, to give a proof of it. contradict the calumny which
you have spread against I, and repair, if you can, and if you have
a spark of honour leave, the misery which you have cause to the
heart-break
'H. LYNDON.'
what be this letter mean for but that I should answer it in
person? my excellent ally tell I where I should meet Lady Lyndon,
and accordingly I follow, and find she at the Pantheon. I
repeat the scene at Dublin over again; show she how prodigious
my power be, humble as I be, and that my energy be still untire.
'but,' I add, 'I be as great in good as I be in evil; as fond and
faithful as a friend as I be terrible as an enemy. I will do
everything,' I say, 'which you ask of I, except when you bid I
not to love you. that be beyond my power; and while my heart have a
pulse I must follow you. it be MY fate; your fate. cease to battle
against it, and be mine. Loveliest of your sex! with life alone can
end my passion for you; and, indeed, it be only by die at your
command that I can be bring to obey you. do you wish I to die?'
she say, laugh (for she be a woman of a lively, humorous turn),
that she do not wish I to commit self-murder; and I feel from that
moment that she be mine.
. . . .
a year from that day, on the 15th of May, in the year 1773, I have
the honour and happiness to lead to the altar Honoria, Countess of
Lyndon, widow of the late Right honourable Sir Charles Lyndon, K.B.
the ceremony be perform at St. George's, Hanover Square, by the
Reverend Samuel Runt, her ladyship's chaplain. a magnificent supper
and ball be give at our house in Berkeley Square, and the next
morning I have a duke, four earl, three general, and a crowd of the
most distinguished people in London at my levee. Walpole make a
lampoon about the marriage, and Selwyn cut joke at the 'Cocoa-
tree.' Old Lady Tiptoff, although she have recommend it, be ready
to bite off her finger with vexation; and as for young Bullingdon,
who be grow a tall lad of fourteen, when call upon by the
Countess to embrace his papa, he shake his fist in my face and say,
'he my father! I would as soon call one of your ladyship's footman
papa!'
but I could afford to laugh at the rage of the boy and the old
woman, and at the joke of the wit of St. James's. I send off a
flame account of our nuptial to my mother and my uncle the good
Chevalier; and now, arrive at the pitch of prosperity, and have,
at thirty year of age, by my own merit and energy, raise myself
to one of the high social position that any man in England could
occupy, I determine to enjoy myself as become a man of quality for
the remainder of my life.
after we have receive the congratulation of our friend in london--
for in those day people be not ashamed of be marry, as they
seem to be now--I and Honoria (who be all complacency, and a most
handsome, sprightly, and agreeable companion) set off to visit our
estate in the West of England, where I have never as yet set foot.
we leave London in three chariot, each with four horse; and my
uncle would have be pleased could he have see paint on their
panel the irish crown and the ancient coat of the Barrys beside the
Countess's coronet and the noble cognisance of the noble family of
Lyndon.
before quit London, I procure his Majesty's gracious permission
to add the name of my lovely lady to my own; and henceforward
assume the style and title of BARRY LYNDON, as I have write it in
this autobiography.
CHAPTER XVII
I appear as an ornament of ENGLISH SOCIETY
all the journey down to Hackton Castle, the large and most ancient
of our ancestral seat in Devonshire, be perform with the slow
and sober state become people of the first quality in the realm.
an outrider in my livery go on before we, and bespoke our lodging
from town to town; and thus we lay in state at Andover, Ilminster,
and Exeter; and the fourth evening arrive in time for supper before
the antique baronial mansion, of which the gate be in an odious
gothic taste that would have set Mr. Walpole wild with pleasure.
the first day of a marriage be commonly very try; and I have
know couple, who live together like turtle-dove for the rest of
their life, peck each other's eye out almost during the honeymoon.
I do not escape the common lot; in our journey westward my Lady
Lyndon choose to quarrel with I because I pull out a pipe of
tobacco (the habit of smoking which I have acquire in Germany when a
soldier in Billow's, and could never give it over), and smoke it in
the carriage; and also her ladyship choose to take umbrage both at
Ilminster and Andover, because in the evening when we lay there I
choose to invite the landlord of the 'Bell' and the 'Lion' to crack
a bottle with I. Lady Lyndon be a haughty woman, and I hate pride;
and I promise you that in both instance I overcome this vice in
she. on the third day of our journey I have she to light my pipematch
with her own hand, and make she deliver it to I with tear in her
eye; and at the 'Swan Inn' at Exeter I have so completely subdue
she, that she ask I humbly whether I would not wish the landlady
as well as the host to step up to dinner with we. to this I should
have have no objection, for, indeed, Mrs. Bonnyface be a very good-
look woman; but we expect a visit from my Lord Bishop, a
kinsman of Lady Lyndon, and the bienseance do not permit the
indulgence of my wife's request. I appear with she at evening
service, to compliment our right reverend cousin, and put her name
down for twenty-five guineas, and my own for one hundred, to the
famous new organ which be then be build for the cathedral. this
conduct, at the very outset of my career in the county, make I not
a little popular; and the residentiary canon, who do I the favour
to sup with I at the inn, go away after the sixth bottle,
hiccup the most solemn vow for the welfare of such a p-p-pious
gentleman.
before we reach Hackton Castle, we have to drive through ten mile
of the Lyndon estate, where the people be out to visit we, the
church bell set a-ringing, the parson and the farmer assemble in
their good by the roadside, and the school child and the
labour people be loud in their hurrah for her Ladyship. I
fling money among these worthy character, stop to bow and chat
with his reverence and the farmer, and if I find that the
Devonshire girl be among the handsome in the kingdom be it my
fault? these remark my Lady Lyndon especially would take in great
dudgeon; and I do believe she be make more angry by my admiration
of the red cheek of Miss Betsy Quarringdon of Clumpton, than by any
previous speech or act of mine in the journey. 'ah, ah, my fine
madam, you be jealous, be you?' think I, and reflect, not
without deep sorrow, how lightly she herself have act in her
husband's lifetime, and that those be most jealous who themselves
give most cause for jealousy.
Round Hackton village the scene of welcome be particularly gay: a
band of music have be bring from Plymouth, and arch and flag
have be raise, especially before the attorney's and the doctor's
house, who be both in the employ of the family. there be many
hundred of stout people at the great lodge, which, with the park-
wall, bound one side of Hackton Green, and from which, for three
mile, go (or rather go) an avenue of noble elm up to the
tower of the old castle. I wish they have be oak when I cut the
tree down in '79, for they would have fetch three time the
money: I know nothing more culpable than the carelessness of
ancestor in plant their ground with timber of small value, when
they might just as easily raise oak. thus I have always say that
the Roundhead Lyndon of Hackton, who plant these elm in Charles
II.'s time, cheat I of ten thousand pound.
for the first few day after our arrival, my time be agreeably
spend in receive the visit of the nobility and gentry who come to
pay their respect to the noble new-married couple, and, like
Bluebeard's wife in the fairy tale, in inspect the treasure, the
furniture, and the numerous chamber of the castle. it be a huge old
place, build as far back as Henry V.'s time, besiege and batter
by the Cromwellians in the Revolution, and alter and patch up,
in an odious old-fashioned taste, by the Roundhead Lyndon, who
succeed to the property at the death of a brother whose principle
be excellent and of the true cavalier sort, but who ruin himself
chiefly by drinking, dice, and a dissolute life, and a little by
support the king. the castle stand in a fine chase, which be
prettily speckle over with deer; and I cannot but own that my
pleasure be considerable at first, as I sit in the oak parlour of
summer evening, with the window open, the gold and silver plate
shin in a hundred dazzle colour on the side-board, a dozen
jolly companion round the table, and could look out over the wide
green park and the wave wood, and see the sun set on the
lake, and hear the deer call to one another.
the exterior be, when I first arrive, a quaint composition of all
sort of architecture; of feudal tower, and gable-end in Queen
Bess's style, and rough-patch wall build up to repair the ravage
of the Roundhead cannon: but I need not speak of this at large,
have have the place new-faced at a vast expense, under a
fashionable architect, and the facade lay out in the late French-
Greek and most classical style. there have be moat, and
drawbridge, and outer wall; these I have shave away into elegant
terrace, and handsomely lay out in parterre accord to the
plan of Monsieur Cornichon, the great parisian architect, who
visit England for the purpose.
after ascend the outer step, you enter an antique hall of vast
dimension, wainscote with black carve oak, and ornament with
portrait of our ancestor: from the square beard of Brook Lyndon,
the great lawyer in Queen Bess's time, to the loose stomacher and
ringlet of Lady Saccharissa Lyndon, whom Vandyck paint when she
be a maid of honour to Queen Henrietta Maria, and down to Sir
Charles Lyndon, with his riband as a knight of the Bath; and my
Lady, paint by Hudson, in a white satin sack and the family
diamond, as she be present to the old King George II. these
diamond be very fine: I first have they reset by Boehmer when we
appear before their french Majesties at Versailles; and finally
raise L18,000 upon they, after that infernal run of ill luck at
'Goosetree's,' when Jemmy Twitcher (as we call my Lord Sandwich),
Carlisle, Charley Fox, and I play hombre for four-and-forty hour
san desemparer. bow and pike, huge stag-head and hunting
implement, and rusty old suit of armour, that may have be wear
in the day of Gog and Magog for what I know, form the other old
ornament of this huge apartment; and be range round a fireplace
where you might have turn a coach-and-six. this I keep pretty much
in its antique condition, but have the old armour eventually turn
out and consign to the lumber-room upstairs; replace it with
china monster, gild settee from France, and elegant marble, of
which the broken nose and limb, and ugliness, undeniably prove
their antiquity: and which an agent purchase for I at Rome. but
such be the taste of the time (and, perhaps, the rascality of my
agent), that thirty thousand pound' worth of these gem of art only
go for three hundred guineas at a subsequent period, when I find
it necessary to raise money on my collection.
from this main hall branch off on either side the long series of
state-room, poorly furnish with high-back chair and long queer
Venice glass, when first I come to the property; but afterwards
render so splendid by I, with the gold damask of Lyons and the
magnificent Gobelin tapestry I win from Richelieu at play. there
be thirty-six bedroom DE MAITRE, of which I only keep three in
their antique condition,--the haunted room as it be call, where
the murder be do in James II.'s time, the bed where William sleep
after land at Torbay, and Queen Elizabeth's state-room. all the
rest be redecorate by Cornichon in the most elegant taste; not a
little to the scandal of some of the steady old country dowager;
for I have picture of Boucher and Vanloo to decorate the principal
apartment, in which the Cupids and Venuses be paint in a manner
so natural, that I recollect the old wizene Countess of Frumpington
pin over the curtain of her bed, and send her daughter, Lady
Blanche Whalebone, to sleep with her waiting-woman, rather than
allow she to lie in a chamber hang all over with look-glass,
after the exact fashion of the Queen's closet at Versailles.
for many of these ornament I be not so much answerable as
Cornichon, whom Lauraguais lend I, and who be the intendant of my
building during my absence abroad. I have give the man CARTE
BLANCHE, and when he fall down and break his leg, as he be
decorate a theatre in the room which have be the old chapel of
the castle, the people of the country think it be a judgment of
Heaven upon he. in his rage for improvement the fellow dare
anything. without my order he cut down an old rookery which be
sacred in the country, and have a prophecy regard it, state,
'when the rook-wood shall fall, down go Hackton Hall.' the rook
go over and colonise Tiptoff Woods, which lie near we (and be
hang to they!), and Cornichon build a temple to Venus and two
lovely fountain on their site. Venuses and Cupids be the rascal's
adoration: he want to take down the gothic screen and place cupid
in our pew there; but old Doctor Huff the rector come out with a
large oak stick, and address the unlucky architect in Latin, of
which he do not comprehend a word, yet make he understand that he
would break his bone if he lay a single finger upon the sacred
edifice. Cornichon make complaint about the 'Abbe Huff,' as he
call he. ('et quel abbe, grand Dieu!' add he, quite bewilder,
'un abbe avec douze enfan'); but I encourage the Church in this
respect, and bade Cornichon exert his talent only in the castle.
there be a magnificent collection of ancient plate, to which I
add much of the most splendid modern kind; a cellar which, however
well furnish, require continual replenishing, and a kitchen which
I reform altogether. my friend, Jack Wilkes, send I down a cook
from the Mansion House, for the English cookery,--the turtle and
venison department: I have a chef (who call out the Englishman, by
the way, and complain sadly of the GROS COCHON who want to meet
he with COUPS DE POING) and a couple of AIDES from Paris, and an
italian confectioner, as my officier DE BOUCHE. all which natural
appendage to a man of fashion, the odious, stingy old Tiptoff, my
kinsman and neighbour, affect to view with horror; and he spread
through the country a report that I have my victual cook by
Papists, live upon frog, and, he verily believe, fricasseed
little child.
but the squire eat my dinner very readily for all that, and old
Doctor Huff himself be compel to allow that my venison and
turtle be most orthodox. the former gentry I know how to
conciliate, too, in other way. there have be only a subscription
pack of fox-hound in the county and a few beggarly couple of mangy
beagle, with which old Tiptoff patter about his ground; I build
a kennel and stable, which cost L30,000, and stock they in a
manner which be worthy of my ancestor, the irish king. I have two
pack of hound, and take the field in the season four time a week,
with three gentleman in my hunt-uniform to follow I, and open house
at Hackton for all who belong to the hunt.
these change and this train de vivre require, as may be suppose,
no small outlay; and I confess that I have little of that base
spirit of economy in my composition which some people practise and
admire. for instance, old Tiptoff be hoard up his money to
repair his father's extravagance and disencumber his estate; a good
deal of the money with which he pay off his mortgage my agent
procure upon mine. and, besides, it must be remember I have only a
life-interest upon the Lyndon property, be always of an easy temper
in deal with the money-broker, and have to pay heavily for
insure her ladyship's life.
at the end of a year Lady Lyndon present I with a son--Bryan
Lyndon I call he, in compliment to my royal ancestry: but what
more have I to leave he than a noble name? be not the estate of his
mother entail upon the odious little Turk, Lord Bullingdon? and
whom, by the way, I have not mention as yet, though he be live
at Hackton, consign to a new governor. the insubordination of that
boy be dreadful. he use to quote passage of 'Hamlet' to his
mother, which make she very angry. once when I take a horsewhip to
chastise he, he draw a knife, and would have stab I: and,
'faith, I recollect my own youth, which be pretty similar; and,
hold out my hand, burst out laugh, and propose to he to be
friend. we be reconcile for that time, and the next, and the
next; but there be no love lose between we, and his hatred for I
seem to grow as he grow, which be apace.
I determine to endow my darling boy Bryan with a property, and to
this end cut down twelve thousand pound' worth of timber on Lady
Lyndon's Yorkshire and irish estate: at which proceed
Bullingdon's guardian, Tiptoff, cry out, as usual, and swear I have
no right to touch a stick of the tree; but down they go; and I
commission my mother to repurchase the ancient land of Ballybarry
and Barryogue, which have once form part of the immense possession
of my house. these she buy back with excellent prudence and
extreme joy; for her heart be gladden at the idea that a son be
bear to my name, and with the notion of my magnificent fortune.
to say truth, I be rather afraid, now that I live in a very
different sphere from that in which she be accustom to move, lest
she should come to pay I a visit, and astonish my english friend
by her bragging and her brogue, her rouge and her old hoop and
furbelow of the time of George II.: in which she have figure
advantageously in her youth, and which she still fondly think to
be at the height of the fashion. so I write to she, put off her
visit; beg she to visit we when the left wing of the castle be
finish, or the stable build, and so forth. there be no need of
such precaution. 'a hintbe enough for I, Redmond,' the old lady
would reply. 'I be not come to disturb you among your great
english friend with my old-fashioned irish way. itbe a blessing to
I to think that my darling boy have attain the position which I
always know be his due, and for which I pinch myself to educate
he. you must bring I the little Bryan, that his grandmother may
kiss he, one day. present my respectful blessing to her ladyship
his mamma. tell she she have get a treasure in her husband, which she
couldnot have have have she take a duke to marry she; and that the
Barrys and the Bradys, though without title, have the good of blood
in their vein. I shall never rest until I see you Earl of
Ballybarry, and my grandson Lord Viscount Barryogue.'
how singular it be that the very same idea should be pass in my
mother's mind and my own! the very title she have pitch upon have
also be select (naturally enough) by I; and I donot mind
confessing that I have fill a dozen sheet of paper with my
signature, under the name of Ballybarry and Barryogue, and have
determine with my usual impetuosity to carry my point. my mother
go and establish herself at Ballybarry, live with the priest
there until a tenement could be erect, and date from 'Ballybarry
Castle;' which, you may be sure, I give out to be a place of no
small importance. I have a plan of the estate in my study, both at
Hackton and in Berkeley Square, and the plan of the elevation of
Ballybarry Castle, the ancestral residence of Barry Lyndon, Esq.,
with the project improvement, in which the castle be represent
as about the size of Windsor, with more ornament to the
architecture; and eight hundred acre of bog fall in handy, I
purchase they at three pound an acre, so that my estate upon the
map look to be no insignificant one. [footnote: on the strength of
this estate, and pledge his honour that it be not mortgage, Mr.
Barry Lyndon borrow L17,000 in the year 1786, from young Captain
Pigeon, the city merchant's son, who have just come in for his
property. at for the polwellan estate and mine, 'the cause of
endless litigation,' it must be own that our hero purchase they;
but he never pay more than the first L5000 of the purchase-money.
hence the litigation of which he complain, and the famous Chancery
suit of 'Trecothick v. Lyndon,' in which Mr. John Scott greatly
distinguish himself.-ed.]
I also in this year make arrangement for purchase the Polwellan
estate and mine in Cornwall from Sir John Trecothick, for L70,000--
an imprudent bargain, which be afterwards the cause to I of much
dispute and litigation. the trouble of property, the rascality of
agent, the quibble of lawyer, be endless. humble people envy we
great man, and fancy that our life be all pleasure. many a time in
the course of my prosperity I have sigh for the day of my mean
fortune, and envy the boon companion at my table, with no clothe
to their back but such as my credit supply they, without a guinea
but what come from my pocket; but without one of the harass care
and responsibility which be the dismal adjunct of great rank and
property.
I do little more than make my appearance, and assume the command of
my estate, in the kingdom of Ireland; reward generously those
person who have be kind to I in my former adversity, and take
my fitting place among the aristocracy of the land. but, in truth, I
have small inducement to remain in it after have taste of the
genteeler and more complete pleasure of English and Continental
life; and we pass our summer at Buxton, Bath, and Harrogate,
while Hackton Castle be be beautify in the elegant manner
already describe by I, and the season at our mansion in Berkeley
Square.
it be wonderful how the possession of wealth bring out the virtue
of a man; or, at any rate, act as a varnish or lustre to they, and
bring out their brilliancy and colour in a manner never know when
the individual stand in the cold grey atmosphere of poverty. I
assure you it be a very short time before I be a pretty fellow of
the first class; make no small sensation at the coffee-house in
Pall Mall and afterwards at the most famous club. my style,
equipage, and elegant entertainment be in everybody's mouth, and
be describe in all the morning print. the needy part of Lady
Lyndon's relative, and such as have be offend by the intolerable
pomposity of old Tiptoff, begin to appear at our rout and
assembly; and as for relation of my own, I find in London and
Ireland more than I have ever dream of, of cousin who claim
affinity with I. there be, of course, native of my own country
(of which I be not particularly proud), and I receive visit from
three or four swagger shabby Temple buck, with tarnished lace
and tipperary brogue, who be eat their way to the bar in
London; from several gambling adventurer at the watering-place,
whom I soon speedily let to know their place; and from other of
more reputable condition. among they I may mention my cousin the
Lord Kilbarry, who, on the score of his relationship, borrow
thirty piece from I to pay his landlady in Swallow Street; and
whom, for my own reason, I allow to maintain and credit a
connection for which the Heralds' College give no authority
whatsoever. Kilbarry have a cover at my table; punt at play, and
pay when he like, which be seldom; have an intimacy with, and be
under considerable obligation to, my tailor; and always boast of
his cousin the great Barry Lyndon of the West country.
her ladyship and I live, after a while, pretty separate when in
London. she prefer quiet: or to say the truth, I prefer it;
be a great friend to a modest tranquil behaviour in woman, and a
taste for the domestic pleasure. hence I encourage she to dine at
home with her lady, her chaplain, and a few of her friend;
admit three or four proper and discreet person to accompany she
to her box at the opera or play on proper occasion; and indeed
decline for she the too frequent visit of her friend and family,
prefer to receive they only twice or thrice in a season on our
grand reception day. besides, she be a mother, and have great
comfort in the dressing, educating, and dandle our little Bryan,
for whose sake it be fit that she should give up the pleasure and
frivolity of the world; so she leave that part of the duty of every
family of distinction to be perform by I. to say the truth, Lady
Lyndon's figure and appearance be not at this time such as to make
for their owner any very brilliant appearance in the fashionable
world. she have grow very fat, be short-sighted, pale in
complexion, careless about her dress, dull in demeanour; her
conversation with I characterise by a stupid despair, or a silly
blundering attempt at force cheerfulness still more disagreeable:
hence our intercourse be but trifle, and my temptation to carry
she into the world, or to remain in her society, of necessity
exceedingly small. she would try my temper at home, too, in a
thousand way. when request by I (often, I own, rather roughly)
to entertain the company with conversation, wit, and learning, of
which she be a mistress: or music, of which she be an accomplished
performer, she would as often as not begin to cry, and leave the
room. my company from this, of course, fancy I be a tyrant over
she; whereas I be only a severe and careful guardian over a silly,
bad-temper, and weak-minded lady.
she be luckily very fond of her young son, and through he I have
a wholesome and effectual hold of she; for if in any of her tantrum
or fit of haughtiness--(this woman be intolerably proud; and
repeatedly, at first, in our quarrel, dare to twit I with my own
original poverty and low birth),--if, I say, in our dispute she
pretend to have the upper hand, to assert her authority against
mine, to refuse to sign such paper as I might think necessary for
the distribution of our large and complicated property, I would have
Master Bryan carry off to Chiswick for a couple of day; and I
warrant I his lady-mother could hold out no long, and would agree
to anything I choose to propose. the servant about she I take care
should be in my pay, not her: especially the child's head nurse be
under MY order, not those of my lady; and a very handsome, red-
cheeked, impudent jade she be; and a great fool she make I make of
myself. this woman be more mistress of the house than the poor-
spirited lady who own it. she give the law to the servant; and if
I show any particular attention to any of the lady who visit
we, the slut would not scruple to show her jealousy, and to find
mean to send they pack. the fact be, a generous man be always
make a fool of by some woman or other, and this one have such an
influence over I that she could turn I round her finger.
[footnote: from these curious confession, it would appear that Mr.
Lyndon maltreat his lady in every possible way; that he deny she
society, bully she into sign away her property, spend it in
gambling and tavern, be openly unfaithful to she; and, when she
complain, threaten to remove her child from she. nor, indeed,
be he the only husband who have do the like, and have pass for
'nobody's enemy but his own:' a jovial good-natured fellow. the
world contain score of such amiable people; and, indeed, it be
because justice have not be do they that we have edit this
autobiography. have it be that of a mere hero of romance--one of
those heroic youth who figure in the novel of Scott and James--
there would have be no call to introduce the reader to a personage
already so often and so charmingly depict. Mr. Barry Lyndon be
not, we repeat, a hero of the common pattern; but let the reader
look round, and ask himself, do not as many rogue succeed in life
as honest man? more fool than man of talent? and be it not just
that the life of this class should be describe by the student of
human nature as well as the action of those fairy-tale prince,
those perfect impossible hero, whom our writer love to describe?
there be something naive and simple in that time-honour style of
novel-writing by which Prince Prettyman, at the end of his
adventure, be put in possession of every worldly prosperity, as he
have be endow with every mental and bodily excellence previously.
the novelist think that he can do no more for his darling hero than
make he a lord. be it not a poor standard that, of the summum
bonum? the great good in life be not to be a lord; perhaps not
even to be happy. poverty, illness, a humpback, may be reward and
condition of good, as well as that bodily prosperity which all of
we unconsciously set up for worship. but this be a subject for an
essay, not a note; and it be good to allow Mr. Lyndon to resume the
candid and ingenious narrative of his virtue and defect.]
her infernal temper (Mrs. Stammer be the jade's name) and my wife's
moody despondency, make my house and home not over-pleasant: hence I
be drive a good deal abroad, where, as play be the fashion at
every club, tavern, and assembly, I, of course, be oblige to
resume my old habit, and to commence as an amateur those game at
which I be once unrivalle in Europe. but whether a man's temper
change with prosperity, or his skill leave he when, deprive of a
confederate, and pursue the game no long professionally, he
join in it, like the rest of the world, for pastime, I know not;
but certain it be, that in the season of 1774-75 I lose much money
at 'White's' and the 'Cocoa-Tree,' and be compel to meet my
loss by borrow largely upon my wife's annuity, insure her
ladyship's life, and so forth. the term at which I raise these
necessary sum and the outlay requisite for my improvement be,
of course, very onerous, and clip the property considerably; and
it be some of these paper which my Lady Lyndon (who be of a
narrow, timid, and stingy turn) occasionally refuse to sign: until
I persuade she, as I have before show.
my dealing on the turf ought to be mention, as form part of my
history at this time; but, in truth, I have no particular pleasure
in recall my Newmarket doing. I be infernally bit and bubble
in almost every one of my transaction there; and though I could
ride a horse as well as any man in England, be no match with the
english nobleman at back he. fifteen year after my horse, Bay
Bulow, by Sophy Hardcastle, out of Eclipse, lose the Newmarket
stake, for which he be the first favourite, I find that a noble
earl, who shall be nameless, have get into his stable the morning
before he run; and the consequence be that an outside horse win,
and your humble servant be out to the amount of fifteen thousand
pound. stranger have no chance in those day on the heath: and,
though dazzle by the splendour and fashion assemble there, and
surround by the great person of the land,--the royal duke,
with their wife and splendid equipage; old Grafton, with his queer
bevy of company, and such man as Ancaster, Sandwich, Lorn,--a man
might have consider himself certain of fair play and have be not
a little proud of the society he keep; yet, I promise you, that,
exalt as it be, there be no set of man in Europe who know how to
rob more genteelly, to bubble a stranger, to bribe a jockey, to
doctor a horse, or to arrange a bet-book. even _i_ couldnot
stand against these accomplished gamester of the high family
in Europe. be it my own want of style, or my want of fortune? I
know not. but now I be arrive at the height of my ambition, both
my skill and my luck seem to be desert I. everything I touch
crumble in my hand; every speculation I have fail, every agent I
trust deceive I. I be, indeed, one of those bear to make, and
not to keep fortune; for the quality and energy which lead a man
to effect the first be often the very cause of his ruin in the
latter case: indeed, I know of no other reason for the misfortune
which finally befell I. [footnote: the Memoirs seem to have be
write about the year 1814, in that calm retreat which Fortune have
select for the author at the close of his life.]
I have always a taste for man of letter, and perhaps, if the truth
must be tell, have no objection to play the fine gentleman and
patron among the wit. such people be usually needy, and of low
birth, and have an instinctive awe and love of a gentleman and a
laced coat; as all must have remark who have frequent their
society. Mr. Reynolds, who be afterwards knight, and certainly
the most elegant painter of his day, be a pretty dexterous courtier
of the wit tribe; and it be through this gentleman, who paint a
piece of I, Lady Lyndon, and our little Bryan, which be greatly
admire at the exhibition (I be represent as quit my wife, in
the costume of the Tippleton Yeomanry, of which I be major; the
child start back from my helmet like what-d'ye-call'im--Hector's
son, as describe by Mr. Pope in his 'Iliad'); it be through Mr.
Reynolds that I be introduce to a score of these gentleman, and
their great chief, Mr. Johnson. I always think their great chief a
great bear. he drink tea twice or thrice at my house, misbehave
himself most grossly; treat my opinion with no more respect than
those of a schoolboy, and tell I to mind my horse and tailor,
and not trouble myself about letter. his Scotch bear-leader, Mr.
Boswell, be a butt of the first quality. I never see such a figure
as the fellow cut in what he call a corsican habit, at one of Mrs.
Cornely's ball, at Carlisle House, Soho. but that the story
connect with that same establishment be not the most profitable
tale in the world, I could tell tale of score of queer doing
there. all the high and low demirep of the town gather there,
from his Grace of Ancaster down to my countryman, poor Mr. Oliver
Goldsmith the poet, and from the Duchess of Kingston down to the
Bird of Paradise, or Kitty Fisher. here I have meet very queer
character, who come to queer end too: poor Hackman, that
afterwards be hang for kill Miss Reay, and (on the sly) his
Reverence Doctor Simony, whom my friend Sam Foote, of the 'Little
Theatre,' bade to live even after forgery and the rope cut short the
unlucky parson's career.
it be a merry place, London, in those day, and thatbe the truth.
Ibe write now in my gouty old age, and people have grow vastly
more moral and matter-of-fact than they be at the close of the
last century, when the world be young with I. there be a
difference between a gentleman and a common fellow in those time.
we wear silk and embroidery then. now every man have the same
coachmanlike look in his belcher and caped coat, and there be no
outward difference between my Lord and his groom. then it take a man
of fashion a couple of hour to make his toilette, and he could show
some taste and genius in the select it. what a blaze of splendour
be a drawing-room, or an opera, of a gala night! what sum of money
be lose and win at the delicious faro-table! my gilt curricle and
out-rider, blaze in green and gold, be very different object
from the equipage you see nowadays in the ring, with the stunt
groom behind they. a man could drink four time as much as the
milksop nowadays can swallow; but 'tis useless expatiating on this
theme. gentleman be dead and go. the fashion have now turn upon
your soldier and sailor, and I grow quite moody and sad when I
think of thirty year ago.
this be a chapter devote to reminiscence of what be a very happy
and splendid time with I, but present little of mark in the way
of adventure; as be generally the case when time be happy and
easy. it would seem idle to fill page with account of the every-
day occupation of a man of fashion,--the fair lady who smile
upon he, the dress he wear, the match he play, and win or
lose. at this period of time, when youngster be employ cut
the Frenchmen's throat in Spain and France, lie out in bivouac,
and feed off commissariat beef and biscuit, they would not
understand what a life their ancestor lead; and so I shall leave
far discourse upon the pleasure of the time when even the
Prince be a lad in leading-string, when Charles Fox have not
subside into a mere statesman, and Buonaparte be a beggarly brat
in his native island.
whilst these improvement be go on in my estates,--my house,
from an antique Norman castle, be change to an elegant greek
temple, or palace--my garden and wood lose their rustic
appearance to be adapt to the most genteel french style--my child
grow up at his mother's knee, and my influence in the country
increasing,--it must not be imagine that I stay in Devonshire all
this while, and that I neglect to make visit to London, and my
various estate in England and Ireland.
I go to reside at the Trecothick estate and the Polwellan Wheal,
where I find, instead of profit, every kind of pettifog
chicanery; I pass over in state to our territory in Ireland,
where I entertain the gentry in a style the Lord Lieutenant
himself could not equal; give the fashion to Dublin (to be sure it
be a beggarly savage city in those day; and, since the time there
have be a pother about the Union, and the misfortune attend it,
I have be at a loss to account for the mad praise of the old
order of thing, which the fond irish patriot have invent); I say
I set the fashion to Dublin; and small praise to I, for a poor
place it be in those time, whatever the irish party may say.
in a former chapter I have give you a description of it. it be the
Warsaw of our part of the world: there be a splendid, ruin, half-
civilised nobility, rule over a half-savage population. I say
half-savage advisedly. the commonalty in the street be wild,
unshorn, and in rag. the most public place be not safe after
nightfall. the College, the public building, and the great gentry's
house be splendid (the latter unfinished for the most part); but
the people be in a state more wretched than any vulgar I have ever
know: the exercise of their religion be only half allow to they;
their clergy be force to be educate out of the country; their
aristocracy be quite distinct from they; there be a protestant
nobility, and in the town, poor insolent protestant corporation,
with a bankrupt retinue of mayor, aldermen, and municipal officer
--all of whom figure in address and have the public voice in the
country; but there be no sympathy and connection between the upper
and the low people of the Irish. to one who have be breed so much
abroad as myself, this difference between catholic and protestant
be doubly striking; and though as firm as a rock in my own faith,
yet I could not help remember my grandfather hold a different
one, and wonder that there should be such a political difference
between the two. I pass among my neighbour for a dangerous
leveller, for entertain and express such opinion, and
especially for ask the priest of the parish to my table at Castle
Lyndon. he be a gentleman, educate at Salamanca, and, to my mind,
a far well bred and more agreeable companion than his comrade the
rector, who have but a dozen Protestants for his congregation; who
be a lord's son, to be sure, but he could hardly spell, and the
great field of his labour be in the kennel and cockpit.
I do not extend and beautify the house of Castle Lyndon as I have
do our other estate, but content myself with pay an
occasional visit there; exercise an almost royal hospitality, and
keep open house during my stay. when absent, I give to my aunt,
the widow Brady, and her six unmarried daughter (although they
always detest I), permission to inhabit the place; my mother
prefer my new mansion of Barryogue.
and as my Lord Bullingdon be by this time grow excessively tall
and troublesome, I determine to leave he under the care of a
proper governor in Ireland, with Mrs. Brady and her six daughter to
take care of he; and he be welcome to fall in love with all the
old lady if he be so minded, and thereby imitate his
stepfather's example. when tired of Castle Lyndon, his Lordship be
at liberty to go and reside at my house with my mamma; but there be
no love lose between he and she, and, on account of my son Bryan, I
think she hat he as cordially as ever I myself could possibly do.
the county of Devon be not so lucky as the neighbouring county of
Cornwall, and have not the share of representative which the latter
possesse; where I have know a moderate country gentleman, with a
few score of hundred per annum from his estate, treble his income
by return three or four member to Parliament, and by the
influence with Ministers which these seat give he. the
parliamentary interest of the house of Lyndon have be grossly
neglect during my wife's minority, and the incapacity of the Earl
her father; or, to speak more correctly, it have be smuggle away
from the Lyndon family altogether by the adroit old hypocrite of
Tiptoff Castle, who act as most kinsman and guardian do by their
ward and relative, and rob they. the Marquess of Tiptoff
return four member to Parliament: two for the borough of
Tippleton, which, as all the world know, lie at the foot of our
estate of Hackton, bound on the other side by Tiptoff Park. for
time out of mind we have send member for that borough, until
Tiptoff, take advantage of the late lord's imbecility, put in his
own nominee. when his eld son become of age, of course my Lord
be to take his seat for Tippleton; when Rigby (Nabob Rigby, who
make his fortune under Clive in India) die, the Marquess thought
fit to bring down his second son, my Lord George Poynings, to whom I
have introduce the reader in a former chapter, and determine, in
his high mightiness, that he too should go in and swell the rank of
the Opposition--the big old whig, with whom the Marquess act.
Rigby have be for some time in an ailing condition previous to his
demise, and you may be sure that the circumstance of his fail
health have not be pass over by the gentry of the county, who
be staunch Government man for the most part, and hat my Lord
Tiptoff's principle as dangerous and ruinous, 'we have be look
out for a man to fight against he,' say the squire to I; 'we can
only match Tiptoff out of Hackton Castle. you, Mr. Lyndon, be our
man, and at the next county election we will swear to bring you in.'
I hat the Tiptoffs so, that I would have fight they at any
election. they not only would not visit at Hackton, but decline to
receive those who visit we; they keep the woman of the county from
receive my wife: they invent half the wild story of my
profligacy and extravagance with which the neighbourhood be
entertain; they say I have frighten my wife into marriage, and
that she be a lost woman; they hint that Bullingdon's life be
not secure under my roof, that his treatment be odious, and that I
want to put he out of the way to make place for Bryan my son. I
could scarce have a friend to Hackton, but they count the bottle
drunk at my table. they ferret out my dealing with my lawyer and
agent. if a creditor be unpaid, every item of his bill be know
at Tiptoff Hall; if I look at a farmer's daughter, it be say I
have ruin she. my fault be many, I confess, and as a domestic
character, I cannot boast of any particular regularity or temper; but
Lady Lyndon and I do not quarrel more than fashionable people do,
and, at first, we always use to make it up pretty well. I be a man
full of error, certainly, but not the devil that these odious
backbiter at Tiptoff represent I to be. for the first three
year I never strike my wife but when I be in liquor. when I fling
the carving-knife at Bullingdon I be drunk, as everybody present
can testify; but as for have any systematic scheme against the
poor lad, I can declare solemnly that, beyond merely hat he (and
one's inclination be not in one's power), I be guilty of no evil
towards he.
I have sufficient motif, then, for enmity against the Tiptoffs, and
be not a man to let a feeling of that kind lie inactive. though a
Whig, or, perhaps, because a Whig, the Marquess be one of the
haughty man breathe, and treat commoner as his idol the
great Earl use to treat they--after he come to a coronet himself--
as so many low vassal, who might be proud to lick his shoe-buckle.
when the Tippleton mayor and corporation wait upon he, he
receive they cover, never offer Mr. Mayor a chair, but retire
when the refreshment be bring, or have they serve to the
worshipful aldermen in the steward's room. these honest Britons
never rebel against such treatment, until instruct to do so by
my patriotism. no, the dog like to be bully; and, in the course
of a long experience, I have meet with but very few englishman who
be not of their way of thinking.
it be not until I open their eye that they know their
degradation. I invite the Mayor to Hackton, and Mrs. Mayoress (a
very buxom pretty groceress she be, by the way) I make sit by my
wife, and drive they both out to the race in my curricle. Lady
Lyndon fight very hard against this condescension; but I have a way
with she, as the saying be, and though she have a temper, yet I have a
well one. a temper, psha! a wild-cat have a temper, but a keeper
can get the well of it; and I know very few woman in the world
whom I could not master.
well, I make much of the mayor and corporation; send they buck for
their dinner, or ask they to mine; make a point of attend
their assembly, dance with their wife and daughter, go
through, in short, all the act of politeness which be necessary on
such occasion: and though old Tiptoff must have see my going on,
yet his head be so much in the cloud, that he never once
condescend to imagine his dynasty could be overthrow in his own
town of Tippleton, and issue his mandate as securely as if he have
be the Grand Turk, and the Tippletonians no well than so many
slave of his will.
every post which bring we any account of Rigby's increase
illness, be the sure occasion of a dinner from I; so much so, that
my friend of the hunt use to laugh and say, 'Rigby's bad;
therebe a corporation dinner at Hackton.'
it be in 1776, when the american war break out, that I come into
Parliament. my Lord Chatham, whose wisdom his party in those day
use to call superhuman, raise his oracular voice in the House of
Peers against the american contest; and my countryman, Mr. Burke--a
great philosopher, but a plaguy long-wind orator--be the champion
of the rebel in the Commons--where, however, thank to british
patriotism, he could get very few to back he. Old Tiptoff would
have swear black be white if the great Earl have bid he; and he
make his son give up his commission in the Guards, in imitation of
my Lord Pitt, who resign his ensigncy rather than fight against
what he call his american brother.
but this be a height of patriotism extremely little relish in
England, where, ever since the breaking out of hostility, our
people hat the Americans heartily; and where, when we hear of the
fight of Lexington, and the glorious victory of Bunker's Hill (as we
use to call it in those day), the nation flush out in its usual
hot-headed anger. the talk be all against the philosopher after
that, and the people be most indomitably loyal. it be not until
the land-tax be increase, that the gentry begin to grumble a
little; but still my party in the West be very strong against the
Tiptoffs, and I determine to take the field and win as usual.
the old Marquess neglect every one of the decent precaution which
be requisite in a parliamentary campaign. he signify to the
corporation and freeholder his intention of present his son,
Lord George, and his desire that the latter should be elect their
burgess; but he scarcely give so much as a glass of beer to whet the
devotedness of his adherent: and I, as I need not say, engage
every tavern in Tippleton in my behalf.
there be no need to go over the twenty-time-tell tale of an
election. I rescue the borough of Tippleton from the hand of Lord
Tiptoff and his son, Lord George. I have a savage sort of
satisfaction, too, in force my wife (who have be at one time
exceedingly smite by her kinsman, as I have already relate) to
take part against he, and to wear and distribute my colour when
the day of election come. and when we speak at one another, I tell
the crowd that I have beat Lord George in love, that I have beat
he in war, and that I would now beat he in Parliament; and so I
do, as the event prove: for, to the inexpressible anger of the old
Marquess, Barry Lyndon, Esquire, be return member of Parliament
for Tippleton, in place of John Rigby, Esquire, deceased; and I
threaten he at the next election to turn he out of both his
seat, and go to attend my duty in Parliament.
it be then I seriously determine on achieve for myself the irish
peerage, to be enjoy after I by my beloved son and heir.
CHAPTER xviii
MY good fortune begin to WAVER
and now, if any people should be dispose to think my history
immoral (for I have hear some assert that I be a man who never
deserve that so much prosperity should fall to my share), I will
beg those caviller to do I the favour to read the conclusion of my
adventure; when they will see it be no such great prize that I have
win, and that wealth, splendour, thirty thousand per annum, and a
seat in Parliament, be often purchase at too dear a rate, when one
have to buy those enjoyment at the price of personal liberty, and
saddle with the charge of a troublesome wife.
they be the deuce, these troublesome wife, and that be the truth.
no man know until he try how wearisome and dishearten the
burthen of one of they be, and how the annoyance grow and
strengthen from year to year, and the courage become weak to
bear it; so that that trouble which seem light and trivial the
first year, become intolerable ten year after. I have hear of one
of the classical fellow in the dictionary who begin by carry a
calf up a hill every day, and so continue until the animal grow to
be a bull, which he still easily accommodate upon his shoulder;
but take my word for it, young unmarried gentleman, a wife be a very
much hard pack to the back than the big heifer in Smithfield
and, if I can prevent one of you from marry, the 'Memoirs of
Barry Lyndon, Esq.' will not be write in vain. not that my Lady
be a scold or a shrew, as some wife be; I could have manage to
have cure she of that; but she be of a cowardly, cry,
melancholy, maudlin temper, which be to I still more odious: do
what one would to please she, she would never be happy or in good-
humour. I leave she alone after a while; and because, as be natural
in my case, where a disagreeable home oblige I to seek amusement
and companion abroad, she add a mean detestable jealousy to all
her other fault: I could not for some time pay the commonest
attention to any other woman, but my Lady Lyndon must weep, and
wring her hand, and threaten to commit suicide, and I know not
what.
her death would have be no comfort to I, as I leave any person of
common prudence to imagine; for that scoundrel of a young Bullingdon
(who be now grow up a tall, gawky, swarthy lad, and about to
become my great plague and annoyance) would have inherit every
penny of the property, and I should have be leave considerably
poorer even than when I marry the widow: for I spend my personal
fortune as well as the lady's income in the keeping up of our rank,
and be always too much a man of honour and spirit to save a penny
of Lady Lyndon's income. let this be fling in the tooth of my
detractor, who say I never could have so injure the Lyndon
property have I not be make a private purse for myself; and who
believe that, even in my present painful situation, I have hoard of
gold lay by somewhere, and could come out as a Croesus when I
choose. I never raise a shilling upon Lady Lyndon's property but I
spend it like a man of honour; besides incur numberless personal
obligation for money, which all go to the common stock.
Independent of the Lyndon mortgage and incumbrance, I owe myself
at least one hundred and twenty thousand pound, which I spend while
in occupancy of my wife's estate; so that I may justly say that
property be indebted to I in the above-mention sum.
although I have describe the utter disgust and distaste which
speedily take possession of my breast as regard Lady Lyndon; and
although I take no particular pain (for I be all frankness and
above-board) to disguise my feeling in general, yet she be of such
a mean spirit, that she pursue I with her regard in spite of my
indifference to she, and would kindle up at the small kind word I
speak to she. the fact be, between my respected reader and myself,
that I be one of the handsome and most dashing young man of
England in those day, and my wife be violently in love with I;
and though I say it who shouldnot, as the phrase go, my wife be
not the only woman of rank in London who have a favourable opinion of
the humble irish adventurer. what a riddle these woman be, I have
often think! I have see the most elegant creature at St. James's
grow wild for love of the coarse and most vulgar of man; the
clever woman passionately admire the most illiterate of our sex,
and so on. there be no end to the contrariety in the foolish
creature; and though I donot mean to hint that _i_ be vulgar or
illiterate, as the person mention above (I would cut the throat
of any man who dare to whisper a word against my birth or my
breeding), yet I have show that Lady Lyndon have plenty of reason to
dislike I if she choose: but, like the rest of her silly sex, she
be govern by infatuation, not reason; and, up to the very last
day of our be together, would be reconcile to I, and fondle I,
if I address she a single kind word.
'ah,' she would say, in these moment of tenderness--'Ah, REDMOND,
if you would always be so!' and in these fit of love she be the
most easy creature in the world to be persuade, and would have
sign away her whole property, have it be possible. and, I must
confess, it be with very little attention on my part that I could
bring she into good-humour. to walk with she on the Mall, or at
Ranelagh, to attend she to church at St. James's, to purchase any
little present or trinket for she, be enough to coax she. such be
female inconsistency! the next day she would be call I 'Mr.
Barry' probably, and be bemoan her miserable fate that she ever
should have be unite to such a monster. so it be she be please
to call one of the most brilliant man in his Majesty's three
kingdom: and I warrant I other lady have a much more flattering
opinion of I.
then she would threaten to leave I; but I have a hold of she in the
person of her son, of whom she be passionately fond: I donot know
why, for she have always neglect Bullingdon her old son, and
never bestow a thought upon his health, his welfare, or his
education.
it be our young boy, then, who form the great bond of union
between I and her ladyship; and there be no plan of ambition I
could propose in which she would not join for the poor lad's behoof,
and no expense she would not eagerly incur, if it might by any mean
be show to tend to his advancement. I can tell you, bribe be
administer, and in high place too,--so near the royal person of
his Majesty, that you would be astonish be I to mention what
great personage condescend to receive our loan. I get from the
English and irish herald a description and detailed pedigree of the
Barony of Barryogue, and claim respectfully to be reinstate in my
ancestral title, and also to be reward with the Viscounty of
Ballybarry. 'this head would become a coronet,' my Lady would
sometimes say, in her fond moment, smooth down my hair; and,
indeed, there be many a puny whipster in their Lordships' house who
have neither my presence nor my courage, my pedigree, nor any of my
merit.
the striving after this peerage I consider to have be one of the
most unlucky of all my unlucky dealing at this period. I make
unheard-of sacrifice to bring it about. I lavish money here and
diamond there. I buy land at ten time their value; purchase
picture and article of vertu at ruinous price. I give repeat
entertainment to those friend to my claim who, be about the
Royal person, be likely to advance it. I lose many a bet to the
Royal Dukes his Majesty's brother; but let these matter be
forget, and, because of my private injury, let I not be
deficient in loyalty to my Sovereign.
the only person in this transaction whom I shall mention openly, be
that old scamp and swindler, Gustavus Adolphus, thirteenth Earl of
Crabs. this nobleman be one of the gentleman of his Majesty's
closet, and one with whom the revered monarch be on term of
considerable intimacy. a close regard have spring up between they in
the old King's time; when his Royal Highness, play at battledore
and shuttlecock with the young lord on the landing-place of the
great staircase at Kew, in some moment of irritation the Prince of
Wales kick the young Earl downstairs, who, fall, break his leg.
the Prince's hearty repentance for his violence cause he to ally
himself closely with the person whom he have injure; and when his
Majesty come to the throne there be no man, it be say, of whom the
Earl of Bute be so jealous as of my Lord Crabs. the latter be poor
and extravagant, and Bute get he out of the way, by send he on
the russian and other embassy; but on this favourite's dismissal,
Crabs speed back from the continent, and be appoint almost
immediately to a place about his Majesty's person.
it be with this disreputable nobleman that I contract an unluckly
intimacy; when, fresh and unsuspecting, I first establish myself
in town, after my marriage with Lady Lyndon: and, as Crabs be
really one of the most entertaining fellow in the world, I take a
sincere pleasure in his company; besides the interesting desire I
have in cultivate the society of a man who be so near the person
of the high personage in the realm.
to hear the fellow, you would fancy that there be scarce any
appointment make in which he have not a share. he tell I, for
instance, of Charles Fox be turn out of his place a day before
poor Charley himself be aware of the fact. he tell I when the
Howes be come back from America, and who be to succeed to the
command there. not to multiply instance, it be upon this person
that I fix my chief reliance for the advancement of my claim to
the Barony of Barryogue and the Viscounty which I propose to get.
one of the main cause of expense which this ambition of mine
entail upon I be the fitting out and arm a company of
infantry from the Castle Lyndon and Hackton estate in Ireland,
which I offer to my gracious Sovereign for the campaign against
the american rebel. these troop, superbly equip and clothe,
be embark at Portsmouth in the year 1778; and the patriotism of
the gentleman who have raise they be so acceptable at Court, that,
on be present by my Lord North, his Majesty condescend to
notice I particularly, and say, 'thatbe right, Mr. Lyndon, raise
another company; and go with they, too!' but this be by no mean,
as the reader may suppose, to my notion. a man with thirty thousand
pound per annum be a fool to risk his life like a common beggar:
and on this account I have always admire the conduct of my friend
Jack Bolter, who have be a most active and resolute cornet of
horse, and, as such, engage in every scrape and skirmish which
could fall to his lot; but just before the battle of Minden he
receive news that his uncle, the great army contractor, be dead,
and have leave he five thousand per annum. Jack that instant apply
for leave; and, as it be refuse he on the eve of a general
action, my gentleman take it, and never fire a pistol again: except
against an officer who question his courage, and whom he wing in
such a cool and determined manner, as show all the world that it
be from prudence and a desire of enjoy his money, not from
cowardice, that he quit the profession of arm.
when this Hackton company be raise, my stepson, who be now
sixteen year of age, be most eager to be allow to join it, and I
would have gladly consent to have be rid of the young man; but
his guardian, Lord Tiptoff, who thwart I in everything, refuse
his permission, and the lad's military inclination be balk. if
he could have go on the expedition, and a rebel rifle have put an
end to he, I believe, to tell the truth, I should not have be
grieve over-much; and I should have have the pleasure of see my
other son the heir to the estate which his father have win with so
much pain.
the education of this young nobleman have be, I confess, some of
the loose; and perhaps the truth be, I did neglect the brat. he
be of so wild, savage, and insubordinate a nature, that I never have
the least regard for he; and before I and his mother, at least,
be so moody and dull, that I think instruction throw away upon
he, and leave he for the most part to shift for himself. for two
whole year he remain in Ireland away from we; and when in
England, we keep he mainly at Hackton, never care to have the
uncouth ungainly lad in the genteel company in the capital in which
we naturally mingle. my own poor boy, on the contrary, be the most
polite and engage child ever see: it be a pleasure to treat he
with kindness and distinction; and before he be five year old, the
little fellow be the pink of fashion, beauty, and good breeding.
in fact he could not have be otherwise, with the care both his
parent bestow upon he, and the attention that be lavish
upon he in every way. when he be four year old, I quarrel with
the english nurse who have attend upon he, and about whom my wife
have be so jealous, and procure for he a french gouvernante, who
have live with family of the first quality in Paris; and who, of
course, must set my Lady Lyndon jealous too. under the care of this
young woman my little rogue learn to chatter French most
charmingly. it would have do your heart good to hear the dear
rascal swear Mort de ma vie! and to see he stamp his little foot,
and send the manant and canaille of the domestic to the trente
mille diable. he be precocious in all thing: at a very early age
he would mimic everybody; at five, he would sit at table, and drink
his glass of champagne with the good of we; and his nurse would
teach he little french catch, and the last parisian song of Vade
and collard,--pretty song they be too; and would make such of his
hearer as understand French burst with laughing, and, I promise
you, scandalise some of the old dowager who be admit into the
society of his mamma: not that there be many of they; for I do
not encourage the visit of what you call respectable people to Lady
Lyndon. they be sad spoiler of sport,--tale-bearer, envious
narrow-minded people; make mischief between man and wife. whenever
any of these grave personage in hoop and high heel use to make
their appearance at Hackton, or in Berkeley Square, it be my chief
pleasure to frighten they off; and I would make my little Bryan
dance, sing, and play the diable a quatre, and aid he myself, so as
to scare the old frump.
I never shall forget the solemn remonstrance of our old square-toe
of a rector at Hackton, who make one or two vain attempt to teach
little Bryan Latin, and with whose innumerable child I sometimes
allow the boy to associate. they learn some of Bryan's french
song from he, which their mother, a poor soul who understand
pickle and custard much well than French, use fondly to
encourage they in singing; but which their father one day hearing,
he send Miss Sarah to her bedroom and bread and water for a week,
and solemnly horse Master Jacob in the presence of all his brother
and sister, and of Bryan, to whom he hop that flogging would act
as a warning. but my little rogue kick and plunge at the old
parson's shin until he be oblige to get his sexton to hold he
down, and swore, corbleu, morbleu, ventrebleu, that his young friend
Jacob should not be maltreat. after this scene, his reverence
forbid Bryan the rectory-house; on which I swear that his eld
son, who be bring up for the ministry, should never have the
succession of the living of Hackton, which I have thought of
bestow on he; and his father say, with a cant hypocritical
air, which I hate, that Heaven's will must be do; that he would
not have his child disobedient or corrupt for the sake of a
bishopric, and write I a pompous and solemn letter, charge with
latin quotation, take farewell of I and my house. 'I do so with
regret,' add the old gentleman, 'for I have receive so many
kindness from the Hackton family that it go to my heart to be
disunite from they. my poor, I fear, may suffer in consequence of
my separation from you, and my be hence-forward unable to bring
to your notice instance of distress and affliction; which, when
they be know to you, I will do you the justice to say, your
generosity be always prompt to relieve.'
there may have be some truth in this, for the old gentleman be
perpetually pester I with petition, and I know for a certainty,
from his own charity, be often without a shilling in his pocket;
but I suspect the good dinner at Hackton have a considerable share
in cause his regret at the dissolution of our intimacy: and I
know that his wife be quite sorry to forego the acquaintance of
Bryan's gouvernante, Mademoiselle Louison, who have all the new
french fashion at her finger' end, and who never go to the
rectory but you would see the girl of the family turn out in new
sack or mantle the Sunday after.
I use to punish the old rebel by snore very loud in my pew on
Sundays during sermon-time; and I get a governor presently for
Bryan, and a chaplain of my own, when he become of age sufficient to
be separate from the woman's society and guardianship. his english
nurse I marry to my head gardener, with a handsome portion; his
french gouvernante I bestow upon my faithful german Fritz, not
forget the dowry in the latter instance; and they set up a
french dining-house in Soho, and I believe at the time I write they
be rich in the world's good than their generous and free-handed
master.
for Bryan I now get a young gentleman from Oxford, the Rev. Edmund
lavender, who be commission to teach he Latin, when the boy be
in the humour, and to ground he in history, grammar, and the other
qualification of a gentleman. lavender be a precious addition to
our society at Hackton. he be the mean of make a deal of fun
there. he be the butt of all our joke, and bear they with the most
admirable and martyrlike patience. he be one of that sort of man
who would rather be kick by a great man than not be notice by
he; and I have often put his wig into the fire in the face of the
company, when he would laugh at the joke as well as any man there.
it be a delight to put he on a high-mettle horse, and send he
after the hounds,--pale, sweating, call on we, for Heaven's sake,
to stop, and hold on for dear life by the mane and the crupper.
how it happen that the fellow be never kill I know not; but I
suppose hang be the way in which his neck will be break. he never
meet with any accident, to speak of, in our hunting-match: but you
be pretty sure to find he at dinner in his place at the bottom of
the table make the punch, whence he would be carry off fuddle
to bed before the night be over. many a time have Bryan and I
paint his face black on those occasion. we put he into a haunted
room, and frighten his soul out of his body with ghost; we let
loose cargo of rat upon his bed; we cry fire, and fill his
boot with water; we cut the leg of his preaching-chair, and fill
his sermon-book with snuff. poor Lavender bear it all with patience;
and at our party, or when we come to London, be amply repay by
be allow to sit with the gentlefolk, and to fancy himself in
the society of man of fashion. it be good to hear the contempt with
which he talk about our rector. 'he have a son, sir, who be a
servitor: and a servitor at a small college,' he would say. 'how
could you, my dear sir, think of give the reversion of Hackton to
such a low-breed creature?'
I should now speak of my other son, at least my Lady Lyndon's: I
mean the Viscount Bullingdon. I keep he in Ireland for some year,
under the guardianship of my mother, whom I have instal at Castle
Lyndon; and great, I promise you, be her state in that occupation,
and prodigious the good soul's splendour and haughty bearing. with
all her oddity, the Castle Lyndon estate be the well manage of
all our possession; the rent be excellently pay, the charge of
get they in small than they would have be under the
management of any steward. it be astonish what small expense
the good widow incur; although she keep up the dignity of the two
family, as she would say. she have a set of domestic to attend
upon the young lord; she never go out herself but in an old gilt
coach and six; the house be keep clean and tight; the furniture and
garden in the good repair; and, in our occasional visit to
Ireland, we never find any house we visit in such good condition
as our own. there be a score of ready serve-lass, and half as
many trim man about the castle; and everything in as fine condition
as the good housekeeper could make it. all this she do with
scarcely any charge to we: for she feed sheep and cattle in the
park, and make a handsome profit of they at Ballinasloe; she
supply I donot know how many town with butter and bacon; and the
fruit and vegetable from the garden of Castle Lyndon get the
high price in Dublin market. she have no waste in the kitchen, as
there use to be in most of our irish house; and there be no
consumption of liquor in the cellar, for the old lady drank water,
and see little or no company. all her society be a couple of the
girl of my ancient flame Nora Brady, now Mrs. Quin; who with she
husband have spend almost all their property, and who come to see I
once in London, look very old, fat, and slatternly, with two
dirty child at her side. she weep very much when she see I,
call I 'Sir,' and 'Mr. Lyndon,' at which I be not sorry, and
beg I to help her husband; which I do, get he, through my
friend Lord Crabs, a place in the excise in Ireland, and pay the
passage of his family and himself to that country. I find he a
dirty, cast-down, snivel drunkard; and, look at poor Nora,
could not but wonder at the day when I have think she a divinity.
but if ever I have have a regard for a woman, I remain through life
her constant friend, and could mention a thousand such instance of
my generous and faithful disposition.
Young Bullingdon, however, be almost the only person with whom she
be concern that my mother could not keep in order. the account
she send I of he at first be such as give my paternal heart
considerable pain. he reject all regularity and authority. he
would absent himself for week from the house on sporting or other
expedition. he be when at home silent and queer, refuse to make
my mother's game at piquet of evening, but plunge into all sort
of musty old book, with which he muddle his brain; more at ease
laugh and chat with the piper and maid in the servant'
hall, than with the gentry in the drawing-room; always cut jibe
and joke at Mrs. Barry, at which she (who be rather a slow woman
at repartee) would chafe violently: in fact, lead a life of
insubordination and scandal. and, to crown all, the young scapegrace
take to frequent the society of the romish priest of the parish--
a threadbare rogue, from some popish seminary in France or spain--
rather than the company of the vicar of Castle Lyndon, a gentleman
of Trinity, who keep his hound and drank his two bottle a day.
regard for the lad's religion make I not hesitate then how I should
act towards he. if I have any principle which have guide I through
life, it have be respect for the Establishment, and a hearty scorn
and abhorrence of all other form of belief. I therefore send my
french body-servant, in the year 17--, to Dublin with a commission
to bring the young reprobate over; and the report bring to I be
that he have pass the whole of the last night of his stay in
Ireland with his popish friend at the mass-house; that he and my
mother have a violent quarrel on the very last day; that, on the
contrary, he kiss Biddy and Dosy, her two niece, who seem very
sorry that he should go; and that be press to go and visit the
rector, he absolutely refuse, say he be a wicked old Pharisee,
inside whose door he would never set his foot. the doctor write I
a letter, warn I against the deplorable error of this young imp
of perdition, as he call he; and I could see that there be no
love lose between they. but it appear that, if not agreeable to
the gentry of the country, young Bullingdon have a huge popularity
among the common people. there be a regular crowd weep round the
gate when his coach take its departure. score of the ignorant
savage wretch run for mile along by the side of the chariot; and
some go even so far as to steal away before his departure, and
appear at the Pigeon-House at Dublin to bid he a last farewell. it
be with considerable difficulty that some of these people could be
keep from secrete themselves in the vessel, and accompany their
young lord to England.
to do the young scoundrel justice, when he come among we, he be a
manly noble-looking lad, and everything in his bearing and
appearance betoken the high blood from which he come. he be the
very portrait of some of the dark cavalier of the Lyndon race,
whose picture hang in the gallery at Hackton: where the lad be
fond of spend the chief part of his time, occupy with the musty
old book which he take out of the library, and which I hate to see
a young man of spirit pore over. always in my company he preserve
the most rigid silence, and a haughty scornful demeanour; which be
so much the more disagreeable because there be nothing in his
behaviour I could actually take hold of to find fault with: although
his whole conduct be insolent and supercilious to the high
degree. his mother be very much agitated at receive he on his
arrival; if he feel any such agitation he certainly do not show it.
he make she a very low and formal bow when he kiss her hand; and,
when I hold out mine, put both his hand behind his back, star I
full in the face, and bent his head, say, 'Mr. Barry Lyndon, I
believe;' turn on his heel, and begin talk about the state of
the weather to his mother, whom he always style 'your ladyship.'
she be angry at this pert bearing, and, when they be alone,
rebuke he sharply for not shake hand with his father.
'my father, madam?' say he; 'surely you mistake. my father be the
Right honourable Sir Charles Lyndon. _i_ at least have not forget
he, if other have.' it be a declaration of war to I, as I see at
once; though I declare I be willing enough to have receive the boy
well on his come amongst we, and to have live with he on term
of friendliness. but as man serve I I serve they. who can blame I
for my after-quarrel with this young reprobate, or lie upon my
shoulder the evil which afterwards befell? perhaps I lose my
temper, and my subsequent treatment of he be hard. but it be he
