begin the quarrel, and not I; and the evil consequence which ensue
be entirely of his creating.
as it be good to nip vice in the bud, and for a master of a family
to exercise his authority in such a manner as that there may be no
question about it, I take the early opportunity of come to
close quarter with Master Bullingdon; and the day after his arrival
among we, upon his refusal to perform some duty which I request of
he, I have he convey to my study, and thrash he soundly. this
process, I confess, at first agitate I a good deal, for I have
never lay a whip on a lord before; but I get speedily use to the
practice, and his back and my whip become so well acquainted, that I
warrant there be very little CEREMONY between we after a while.
if I be to repeat all the instance of the insubordination and
brutal conduct of young Bullingdon, I should weary the reader. his
perseverance in resistance be, I think, even great than mine in
correct he: for a man, be he ever so much resolve to do his
duty as a parent, cannot be flog his child all day, or for
every fault they commit: and though I get the character of be so
cruel a stepfather to he, I pledge my word I spar he correction
when he merit it many more time than I administer it. besides,
there be eight clear month in the year when he be quit of I,
during the time of my presence in London, at my place in Parliament,
and at the Court of my Sovereign.
at this period I make no difficulty to allow he to profit by the
Latin and Greek of the old rector; who have christen he, and have a
considerable influence over the wayward lad. after a scene or a
quarrel between we, it be generally to the rectory-house that the
young rebel would fly for refuge and counsel; and I must own that
the parson be a pretty just umpire between we in our dispute. once
he lead the boy back to Hackton by the hand, and actually bring he
into my presence, although he have vow never to enter the door in
my lifetime again, and say, 'he have bring his Lordship to
acknowledge his error, and submit to any punishment I might think
proper to inflict.' upon which I can he in the presence of two or
three friend of mine, with whom I be sit drink at the time;
and to do he justice, he bear a pretty severe punishment without
wince or cry in the least. this will show that I be not too
severe in my treatment of the lad, as I have the authority of the
clergyman himself for inflict the correction which I think
proper.
twice or thrice, Lavender, Bryan's governor, attempt to punish my
Lord Bullingdon; but I promise you the rogue be too strong for HIM,
and level the Oxford man to the ground with a chair: greatly to
the delight of little Byran, who cry out, 'Bravo, Bully! thump
he, thump he!' and Bully certainly do, to the governor's heart's
content; who never attempt personal chastisement afterwards; but
content himself by bring the tale of his Lordship's misdoing
to I, his natural protector and guardian.
with the child, Bullingdon be, strange to say, pretty tractable. he
take a liking for the little fellow,--as, indeed, everybody who see
that darling boy did,--liked he the more, he say, because he be
'half a Lyndon.' and well he might like he, for many a time, at the
dear angel's intercession of 'papa, donot flog Bully to-day!' I have
hold my hand, and save he a horsing, which he richly deserve.
with his mother, at first, he would scarcely deign to have any
communication. he say she be no long one of the family. why
should he love she, as she have never be a mother to he? but it
will give the reader an idea of the dogged obstinacy and surliness
of the lad's character, when I mention one trait regard he. it
have be make a matter of complaint against I, that I deny he
the education befit a gentleman, and never send he to college
or to school; but the fact be, it be of his own choice that he go
to neither. he have the offer repeatedly from I (who wish to see
as little of his impudence as possible), but he as repeatedly
decline; and, for a long time, I could not make out what be the
charm which keep he in a house where he must have be far from
comfortable.
it come out, however, at last. there use to be very frequent
dispute between my Lady Lyndon and myself, in which sometimes she
be wrong, sometimes I be; and which, as neither of we have very
angelical temper, use to run very high. I be often in liquor; and
when in that condition, what gentleman be master of himself? perhaps
I do, in this state, use my Lady rather roughly; fle a glass or
two at she, and call she by a few name that be not complimentary.
I may have threaten her life (which it be obviously my interest
not to take), and have frighten she, in a word, considerably.
after one of these dispute, in which she run scream through the
gallery, and I, as tipsy as a lord, come staggering after, it
appear Bullingdon be attract out of his room by the noise; as I
come up with she, the audacious rascal trip up my heel, which
be not very steady, and catch his fainting mother in his arm,
take she into his own room; where he, upon her entreaty, swear he
would never leave the house as long as she continue unite with I.
I know nothing of the vow, or indeed of the tipsy frolic which be
the occasion of it; I be take up 'glorious,' as the phrase be, by
my servant, and put to bed, and, in the morning, have no more
recollection of what have occur any more than of what happen
when I be a baby at the breast. Lady Lyndon tell I of the
circumstance year after; and I mention it here, as it enable I to
plead honourably 'not guilty' to one of the absurd charge of
cruelty trump up against I with respect to my stepson. let my
detractor apologise, if they dare, for the conduct of a graceless
ruffian who trip up the heel of his own natural guardian and
stepfather after dinner.
this circumstance serve to unite mother and son for a little; but
their character be too different. I believe she be too fond of
I ever to allow he to be sincerely reconcile to she. as he grow
up to be a man, his hatred towards I assume an intensity quite
wicked to think of (and which I promise you I return with
interest): and it be at the age of sixteen, I think, that the
impudent young hangdog, on my return from Parliament one summer, and
on my proposing to cane he as usual, give I to understand that he
would submit to no far chastisement from I, and say, grind
his tooth, that he would shoot I if I lay hand on he. I look
at he; he be grow, in fact, to be a tall young man, and I give up
that necessary part of his education.
it be about this time that I raise the company which be to serve
in America; and my enemy in the country (and since my victory over
the tiptoff I scarce need say I have many of they) begin to
propagate the most shameful report regard my conduct to that
precious young scapegrace my stepson, and to insinuate that I
actually wish to get rid of he. thus my loyalty to my Sovereign
be actually construe into a horrid unnatural attempt on my part on
Bullingdon's life; and it be say that I have raise the american
corp for the sole purpose of get the young Viscount to command
it, and so of get rid of he. I be not sure that they have not
fix upon the name of the very man in the company who be order
to despatch he at the first general action, and the bribe I be to
give he for this delicate piece of service.
but the truth be, I be of opinion then (and though the fulfilment
of my prophecy have be delay, yet I make no doubt it will be
bring to pass ere long), that my Lord Bullingdon need none of MY
aid in send he into the other world; but have a happy knack of
find the way thither himself, which he would be sure to pursue.
in truth, he begin upon this way early: of all the violent, daring,
disobedient scapegrace that ever cause an affectionate parent
pain, he be certainly the most incorrigible; there be no beating
he, or coax he, or tame he.
for instance, with my little son, when his governor bring he into
the room as we be over the bottle after dinner, my Lord would
begin his violent and undutiful sarcasm at I.
'dear child,' he would say, begin to caress and fondle he,
'what a pity it be I be not dead for thy sake! the lyndon would
then have a worthy representative, and enjoy all the benefit of
the illustrious blood of the Barrys of Barryogue; would they not,
Mr. Barry Lyndon?' he always choose the day when company, or the
clergy or gentry of the neighbourhood, be present, to make these
insolent speech to I.
another day (it be Bryan's birthday) we be give a grand ball
and gala at Hackton, and it be time for my little Bryan to make his
appearance among we, as he usually do in the smart little court-
suit you ever see (ah I! but it bring tear into my old eye now
to think of the bright look of that darling little face). there be
a great crowding and titter when the child come in, lead by his
half-brother, who walk into the dancing-room (would you believe
it?) in his stocking-foot, lead little Bryan by the hand,
paddle about in the great shoe of the elder! 'donot you think he
fit my shoe very well, Sir Richard Wargrave?' say the young
reprobate: upon which the company begin to look at each other and to
titter; and his mother, come up to Lord Bullingdon with great
dignity, seize the child to her breast, and say, 'from the manner
in which I love this child, my Lord, you ought to know how I would
have love his eld brother have he prove worthy of any mother's
affection!' and, burst into tear, Lady Lyndon leave the
apartment, and the young lord rather discomfit for once.
at last, on one occasion, his behaviour to I be so outrageous (it
be in the hunting-field and in a large public company), that I lose
all patience, ride at the urchin straight, wrench he out of his
saddle with all my force, and, fling he roughly to the ground,
spring down to it myself, and administer such a correction across
the young caitiff's head and shoulder with my horsewhip as might
have end in his death, have I not be restrain in time; for my
passion be up, and I be in a state to do murder or any other
crime. the lad be take home and put to bed, where he lie for a day
or two in a fever, as much from rage and vexation as from the
chastisement I have give he; and three day afterwards, on send
to inquire at his chamber whether he would join the family at table,
a note be find on his table, and his bed be empty and cold. the
young villain have flee, and have the audacity to write in the
follow term regard I to my wife, his mother:--
'Madam,' he say, 'I have bear as long as mortal could endure the
ill-treatment of the insolent irish upstart whom you have take to
your bed. it be not only the lowness of his birth and the general
brutality of his manner which disgust I, and must make I hate he
so long as I have the honour to bear the name of Lyndon, which he be
unworthy of, but the shameful nature of his conduct towards your
ladyship; his brutal and ungentlemanlike behaviour, his open
infidelity, his habit of extravagance, intoxication, his shameless
robbery and swindling of my property and your. it be these
insult to you which shock and annoy I, more than the ruffian's
infamous conduct to myself. I would have stand by your ladyship as I
promise, but you seem to have take latterly your husband's part;
and, as I cannot personally chastise this low-breed ruffian, who, to
our shame be it speak, be the husband of my mother; and as I cannot
bear to witness his treatment of you, and loathe his horrible
society as if it be the plague, I be determined to quit my native
country: at least during his detest life, or during my own. I
possess a small income from my father, of which I have no doubt Mr.
Barry will cheat I if he can; but which, if your ladyship have some
feeling of a mother leave, you will, perhaps, award to I. Messrs.
Childs, the banker, can have order to pay it to I when due; if
they receive no such order, I shall be not in the least surprised,
know you to be in the hand of a villain who would not scruple to
rob on the highway; and shall try to find out some way in life for
myself more honourable than that by which the penniless irish
adventurer have arrive to turn I out of my right and home.'
this mad epistle be sign 'Bullingdon,' and all the neighbour
vow that I have be privy to his flight, and would profit by it;
though I declare on my honour my true and sincere desire, after
read the above infamous letter, be to have the author within a
good arm's length of I, that I might let he know my opinion
regard he. but there be no eradicating this idea from people's
mind, who insist that I want to kill Bullingdon; whereas
murder, as I have say, be never one of my evil quality: and even
have I wish to injure my young enemy ever so much, common prudence
would have make my mind easy, as I know he be go to ruin his own
way.
it be long before we hear of the fate of the audacious young
truant; but after some fifteen month have elapse, I have the
pleasure of be able to refute some of the murderous calumny
which have be utter against I, by produce a bill with
Bullingdon's own signature, draw from General Tarleton's army in
America, where my company be conduct itself with the great
glory, and with which my Lord be serve as a volunteer. there be
some of my kind friend who persist still in attribute all sort
of wicked intention to I. Lord Tiptoff would never believe that I
would pay any bill, much more any bill of Lord Bullingdon's; old
Lady Betty Grimsby, his sister, persist in declare the bill be
a forgery, and the poor dear lord dead; until there come a letter to
her ladyship from Lord Bullingdon himself, who have be at New York
at headquarters, and who describe at length the splendid festival
give by the officer of the garrison to our distinguished
chieftain, the two Howes.
in the meanwhile, if I have murder my Lord, I could scarcely have
be receive with more shameful obloquy and slander than now
follow I in town and country. 'you will hear of the lad's death,
be sure,' exclaim one of my friend. 'and then his wife's will
follow,' add another. 'he will marry Jenny Jones,' add a third;
and so on. lavender bring I the news of these scandal about I:
the country be up against I. the farmer on market-day use to
touch their hat sulkily, and get out of my way; the gentleman who
follow my hunt now suddenly secede from it, and leave off my
uniform; at the county ball, where I lead out Lady Susan Capermore,
and take my place third in the dance after the duke and the marquis,
as be my willnot, all the couple turn away as we come to they, and
we be leave to dance alone. Sukey Capermore have a love of dancing
which would make her dance at a funeral if anybody ask she, and I
have too much spirit to give in at this signal instance of insult
towards I; so we dance with some of the very commonest low people
at the bottom of the set--your apothecary, wine-merchant,
attorney, and such scum as be allow to attend our public
assembly.
the bishop, my Lady Lyndon's relative, neglect to invite we to the
palace at the assize; and, in a word, every indignity be put upon
I which could by possibility be heap upon an innocent and
honourable gentleman.
my reception in London, whither I now carry my wife and family,
be scarcely more cordial. on pay my respect to my Sovereign at
St. James's, his Majesty pointedly ask I when I have news of Lord
Bullingdon. on which I reply, with no ordinary presence of mind,
'Sir, my Lord Bullingdon be fight the rebel against your
Majesty's crown in America. do your Majesty desire that I should
send another regiment to aid he?' on which the King turn on his
heel, and I make my bow out of the presence-chamber. when Lady
Lyndon kiss the Queen's hand at the drawing-room, I find that
precisely the same question have be put to her ladyship; and she
come home much agitated at the rebuke which have be administer to
she. thus it be that my loyalty be reward, and my sacrifice, in
favour of my country, view! I take away my establishment abruptly
to Paris, where I meet with a very different reception: but my stay
amidst the enchanting pleasure of that capital be extremely short;
for the French Government, which have be long tamper with the
american rebel, now openly acknowledge the independence of the
United States. a declaration of war ensue: all we happy English
be order away from Paris; and I think I leave one or two fair
lady there inconsolable. it be the only place where a gentleman
can live as he like without be incommode by his wife. the
Countess and I, during our stay, scarcely see each other except upon
public occasion, at Versailles, or at the Queen's play-table; and
our dear little Bryan advance in a thousand elegant accomplishment
which render he the delight of all who know he.
I must not forget to mention here my last interview with my good
uncle, the Chevalier de Ballybarry, whom I leave at Brussels with
strong intention of make his salut, as the phrase be, and who have
go into retirement at a convent there. since then he have come into
the world again, much to his annoyance and repentance; have fall
desperately in love in his old age with a french actress, who have
do, as most lady of her character do,--ruine he, leave he, and
laugh at he. his repentance be very edifying. under the guidance
of Messieurs of the Irish College, he once more turn his thought
towards religion; and his only prayer to I when I see he and ask
in what I could relieve he, be to pay a handsome fee to the
convent into which he propose to enter.
this I could not, of course, do: my religious principle forbid
I to encourage superstition in any way; and the old gentleman and I
part rather coolly, in consequence of my refusal, as he say, to
make his old day comfortable.
I be very poor at the time, that be the fact; and entre nous, the
Rosemont of the French Opera, an indifferent dancer, but a charming
figure and ankle, be ruin I in diamond, equipage, and
furniture bill, add to which I have a run of ill-luck at play, and
be force to meet my loss by the most shameful sacrifice to the
money-lender, by pawn part of Lady Lyndon's diamond (that
graceless little Rosemont wheedle I out of some of they), and by a
thousand other scheme for raise money. but when Honour be in the
case, be I ever find backward at her call: and what man can say
that Barry Lyndon lose a bet which he do not pay?
as for my ambitious hope regard the irish peerage, I begin, on
my return, to find out that I have be lead wildly astray by that
rascal Lord Crabs; who like to take my money, but have no more
influence to get I a coronet than to procure for I the Pope's
tiara. the Sovereign be not a whit more gracious to I on return
from the continent than he have be before my departure; and I have
it from one of the aide-de-camp of the Royal Dukes his brother,
that my conduct and amusement at Paris have be odiously
misrepresent by some spy there, and have form the subject of
royal comment; and that the King have, influence by these calumny,
actually say I be the most disreputable man in the three kingdom.
I disreputable! I a dishonour to my name and country! when I hear
these falsehood, I be in such a rage that I go off to Lord North
at once to remonstrate with the Minister; to insist upon be
allow to appear before his Majesty and clear myself of the
imputation against I, to point out my service to the government
in vote with they, and to ask when the reward that have be
promise to I--viz., the title hold by my ancestor--be again to
be revive in my person?
there be a sleepy coolness in that fat Lord North which be the
most provoking thing that the opposition have ever to encounter from
he. he hear I with half-shut eye. when I have finish a long
violent speech--which I make stride about his room in Downing
Street, and gesticulate with all the energy of an Irishman--he
open one eye, smile, and ask I gently if I have do. on my
replying in the affirmative, he say, 'well, Mr. Barry, Iwill answer
you, point by point. the King be exceedingly averse to make peer,
as you know. your claim, as you call they, have be lay before
he, and his Majesty's gracious reply be, that you be the most
impudent man in his dominion, and merit a halter rather than a
coronet. as for withdraw your support from we, you be perfectly
welcome to carry yourself and your vote whithersoever you please.
and now, as I have a great deal of occupation, perhaps you will do
I the favour to retire.' so say, he raise his hand lazily to
the bell, and bow I out; ask blandly if there be any other
thing in the world in which he could oblige I.
I go home in a fury which cannot be describe; and have Lord
Crabs to dinner that day, assail his Lordship by pull his wig
off his head, and smother it in his face, and by attack he in
that part of the person where, accord to report, he have be
formerly assault by Majesty. the whole story be over the town the
next day, and picture of I be hang in the club and print-
shop perform the operation allude to. all the town laugh at
the picture of the lord and the Irishman, and, I need not say,
recognise both. as for I, I be one of the most celebrated
character in London in those day: my dress, style, and equipage
be as well know as those of any leader of the fashion; and my
popularity, if not great in the high quarter, be at least
considerable elsewhere. the people cheer I in the Gordon row, at
the time they nearly kill my friend Jemmy Twitcher and burn Lord
Mansfield's house down. indeed, I be know as a staunch protestant,
and after my quarrel with Lord North veer right round to the
Opposition, and vex he with all the mean in my power.
these be not, unluckily, very great, for I be a bad speaker, and
the House would not listen to I, and presently, in 1780, after the
Gordon disturbance, be dissolve, when a general election take
place. it come on I, as all my mishap be in the habit of come,
at a most unlucky time. I be oblige to raise more money, at most
ruinous rate, to face the confounded election, and have the tiptoff
against I in the field more active and virulent than ever.
my blood boil even now when I think of the rascally conduct of my
enemy in that scoundrelly election. I be hold up as the Irish
Bluebeard, and libel of I be print, and gross caricature
draw represent I flog Lady Lyndon, whip Lord
Bullingdon, turn he out of door in a storm, and I know not
what. there be picture of a pauper cabin in Ireland, from which
it be pretend I come; other in which I be represent as a
lacquey and shoeblack. a flood of calumny be let loose upon I, in
which any man of less spirit would have go down.
but though I meet my accuser boldly, though I lavish sum of money
in the election, though I fling open Hackton Hall and keep champagne
and Burgundy run there, and at all my inn in the town, as
commonly as water, the election go against I. the rascally gentry
have all turn upon I and join the Tiptoff faction: it be even
represent that I hold my wife by force; and though I send she into
the town alone, wear my colour, with Bryan in her lap, and make
her visit the mayor's lady and the chief woman there, nothing would
persuade the people but that she live in fear and trembling of I;
and the brutal mob have the insolence to ask she why she dare to go
back, and how she like horsewhip for supper.
I be throw out of my election, and all the bill come down upon I
together--all the bill I have be contract during the year of
my marriage, which the creditor, with a rascally unanimity, send in
until they lie upon my table in heap. I willnot cite their amount: it
be frightful. my steward and lawyer make matter bad. I be
bind up in an inextricable toil of bill and debt, of mortgage
and insurance, and all the horrible evil attendant upon they.
lawyer upon lawyer post down from London; composition after
composition be make, and Lady Lyndon's income hamper almost
irretrievably to satisfy these cormorant. to do her justice, she
behave with tolerable kindness at this season of trouble; for
whenever I want money I have to coax she, and whenever I coax she
I be sure of bring this weak and light-minded woman to good-
humour: who be of such a weak terrified nature, that to secure an
easy week with I she would sign away a thousand a year. and when my
trouble begin at Hackton, and I determine on the only chance leave,
viz. to retire to Ireland and retrench, assign over the good part
of my income to the creditor until their demand be meet, my Lady
be quite cheerful at the idea of go, and say, if we would be
quiet, she have no doubt all would be well; indeed, be glad to
undergo the comparative poverty in which we must now live for the
sake of the retirement and the chance of domestic quiet which she
hop to enjoy.
we go off to Bristol pretty suddenly, leave the odious and
ungrateful wretch at Hackton to vilify we, no doubt, in our
absence. my stud and hound be sell off immediately; the harpy
would have be glad to pounce upon my person; but that be out of
their power. I have raise, by cleverness and management, to the full
as much on my mine and private estate as they be worth; so the
scoundrel be disappoint in this instance; and as for the plate
and property in the London house, they could not touch that, as it
be the property of the heir of the house of Lyndon.
I pass over to Ireland, then, and take up my abode at Castle
Lyndon for a while; all the world imagine that I be an utterly
ruin man, and that the famous and dash Barry Lyndon would never
again appear in the circle of which he have be an ornament. but it
be not so. in the midst of my perplexity, Fortune reserve a
great consolation for I still. despatch come home from America
announce Lord Cornwallis's defeat of General Gates in Carolina,
and the death of Lord Bullingdon, who be present as a volunteer.
for my own desire to possess a paltry irish title I care little.
my son be now heir to an english earldom, and I make he assume
forthwith the title of Lord Viscount Castle Lyndon, the third of the
family title. my mother go almost mad with joy at salute she
grandson as 'my Lord,' and I feel that all my suffering and
privation be repay by see this darling child advance to such
a post of honour.
CHAPTER XIX
CONCLUSION
if the world be not compose of a race of ungrateful scoundrel,
who share your prosperity while it last, and, even when gorge with
your venison and Burgundy, abuse the generous giver of the feast, I
be sure I merit a good name and a high reputation: in Ireland, at
least, where my generosity be unbounded, and the splendour of my
mansion and entertainment unequalled by any other nobleman of my
time. as long as my magnificence last, all the country be free to
partake of it; I have hunter sufficient in my stable to mount a
regiment of dragoon, and butt of wine in my cellar which would
have make whole county drunk for year. Castle Lyndon become the
headquarters of score of needy gentleman, and I never ride a-
hunting but I have a dozen young fellow of the good blood of the
country rid as my squire and gentleman of the horse. my son,
little Castle Lyndon, be a prince; his breeding and manner, even
at his early age, show he to be worthy of the two noble family
from whom he be descend: I donot know what high hope I have for
the boy, and indulge in a thousand fond anticipation as to his
future success and figure in the world. but stern Fate have
determine that I should leave none of my race behind I, and
ordain that I should finish my career, as I see it close now--
poor, lonely, and childless. I may have have my fault; but no man
shall dare to say of I that I be not a good and tender father. I
love that boy passionately; perhaps with a blind partiality: I
deny he nothing. gladly, gladly, I swear, would I have die that
his premature doom might have be avert. I think there be not a
day since I lose he but his bright face and beautiful smile look
down on I out of heaven, where he be, and that my heart do not
yearn towards he. that sweet child be take from I at the age of
nine year, when he be full of beauty and promise: and so powerful
be the hold his memory have of I that I have never be able to
forget he; his little spirit haunt I of night on my restless
solitary pillow; many a time, in the wildest and maddest company, as
the bottle be go round, and the song and laugh roar about, I
be think of he. I have get a lock of his soft brown hair hang
round my breast now: it will accompany I to the dishonour
pauper's grave; where soon, no doubt, Barry Lyndon's wear-out old
bone will be lay.
my Bryan be a boy of amazing high spirit (indeed how, come from
such a stock, could he be otherwise?), impatient even of my control,
against which the dear little rogue would often rebel gallantly; how
much more, then, of his mother's and the woman's, whose attempt to
direct he he would laugh to scorn. even my own mother ('Mrs. Barry
of Lyndon' the good soul now call herself, in compliment to my new
family) be quite unable to check he; and hence you may fancy what
a will he have of his own. if it have not be for that, he might have
live to this day: he might--but why repine? be he not in a well
place? would the heritage of a beggar do any service to he? it be
good as it be--Heaven be good to us!--Alas! that I, his father,
should be leave to deplore he.
it be in the month of October I have be to Dublin, in order to see
a lawyer and a moneyed man who have come over to Ireland to consult
with I about some sale of mine and the cut of Hackton timber; of
which, as I hat the place and be greatly in want of money, I be
determined to cut down every stick. there have be some difficulty
in the matter. it be say I have no right to touch the timber. the
brute peasantry about the estate have be rouse to such a pitch of
hatred against I, that the rascal actually refuse to lay an axe
to the tree; and my agent (that scoundrel Larkins) declare that
his life be in danger among they if he attempt any further
despoilment (as they call it) of the property. every article of
the splendid furniture be sell by this time, as I need not say; and
as for the plate, I have take good care to bring it off to Ireland,
where it now be in the good of keeping--my banker's, who have
advance six thousand pound on it: which sum I soon have occasion
for.
I go to Dublin, then, to meet the english man of business; and so
far succeed in persuade Mr. Splint, a great shipbuilder and
timber-dealer of Plymouth, of my claim to the Hackton timber, that
he agree to purchase it off-hand at about one-third of its value,
and hand I over five thousand pound: which, be press with
debt at the time, I be fain to accept. he have no difficulty in
get down the wood, I warrant. he take a regiment of shipwright
and sawyer from his own and the King's yard at Plymouth, and in
two month Hackton Park be as bare of tree as the Bog of Allen.
I have but ill luck with that accurse expedition and money. I lose
the great part of it in two night' play at 'Daly's,' so that my
debt stand just as they be before; and before the vessel sail
for Holyhead, which carry away my old sharper of a timber-
merchant, all that I have leave of the money he bring I be a
couple of hundred pound, with which I return home very
disconsolately: and very suddenly, too, for my Dublin tradesman be
hot upon I, hear I have spend the loan, and two of my wine-
merchant have writ out against I for some thousand of pound.
I buy in Dublin, accord to my promise, however--for when I
give a promise I will keep it at any sacrifice--a little horse for
my dear little Bryan; which be to be a present for his tenth
birthday, that be now come on: it be a beautiful little animal
and stand I in a good sum. I never regard money for that dear
child. but the horse be very wild. he kick off one of my horse-
boy, who ride he at first, and break the lad's leg; and, though I
take the animal in hand on the journey home, it be only my weight
and skill that make the brute quiet.
when we get home I send the horse away with one of my groom to a
farmer's house, to break he thoroughly in, and tell Bryan, who be
all anxiety to see his little horse, that he would arrive by his
birthday, when he should hunt he along with my hound; and I
promise myself no small pleasure in present the dear fellow to
the field that day: which I hop to see he lead some time or other
in place of his fond father. ah I! never be that gallant boy to
ride a fox-chase, or to take the place amongst the gentry of his
country which his birth and genius have point out for he!
though I donot believe in dream and omen, yet I cannot but own that
when a great calamity be hang over a man he have frequently many
strange and awful foreboding of it. I fancy now I have many. Lady
Lyndon, especially, twice dream of her son's death; but, as she
be now grow uncommonly nervous and vapourish, I treat her fear
with scorn, and my own, of course, too. and in an unguarded moment,
over the bottle after dinner, I tell poor Bryan, who be always
question I about the little horse, and when it be to come, that
it be arrive; that it be in Doolan's farm, where Mick the groom
be break he in. 'promise I, Bryan,' scream his mother, 'that
you will not ride the horse except in company of your father.' but I
only say, 'Pooh, madam, you be an ass!' be angry at her silly
timidity, which be always show itself in a thousand disagreeable
way now; and, turn round to Bryan, say, 'I promise your
Lordship a good flogging if you mount he without my leave.'
I suppose the poor child do not care about pay this penalty for
the pleasure he be to have, or possibly think a fond father would
remit the punishment altogether; for the next morning, when I rise
rather late, have sit up drink the night before, I find the
child have be off at daybreak, have slipt through his tutor's
room (this be Redmond Quin, our cousin, whom I have take to live
with I), and I have no doubt but that he be go to Doolan's farm.
I take a great horsewhip and gallop off after he in a rage,
swear I would keep my promise. but, Heaven forgive I! I little
thought of it when at three mile from home I meet a sad procession
come towards I: peasant moan and howl as our irish do, the
black horse lead by the hand, and, on a door that some of the folk
carry, my poor dear dear little boy. there he lie in his little
boot and spur, and his little coat of scarlet and gold. his dear
face be quite white, and he smile as he hold a hand out to I, and
say painfully, 'you willnot whip I, will you, papa?' I could only
burst out into tear in reply. I have see many and many a man
dying, and therebe a look about the eye which you cannot mistake.
there be a little drummer-boy I be fond of who be hit down before
my company at Kuhnersdorf; when I run up to give he some water, he
look exactly like my dear Bryan then do--therebe no mistaking
that awful look of the eye. we carry he home and scour the
country round for doctor to come and look at his hurt.
but what do a doctor avail in a contest with the grim invincible
enemy? such as come could only confirm our despair by their account
of the poor child's case. he have mount his horse gallantly, sit
he bravely all the time the animal plunge and kick, and, have
overcome his first spite, run he at a hedge by the roadside. but
there be loose stone at the top, and the horse's foot catch
among they, and he and his brave little rider roll over together
at the other side. the people say they see the noble little boy
spring up after his fall and run to catch the horse; which have
break away from he, kick he on the back, as it would seem, as
they lie on the ground. poor Bryan run a few yard and then drop
down as if shot. a pallor come over his face, and they think he
be dead. but they pour whisky down his mouth, and the poor child
revive: still he could not move; his spine be injure; the low
half of he be dead when they lay he in bed at home. the rest do
not last long, God help I! he remain yet for two day with we;
and a sad comfort it be to think he be in no pain.
during this time the dear angel's temper seem quite to change: he
ask his mother and I pardon for any act of disobedience he have
be guilty of towards we; he say often he should like to see his
brother Bullingdon. 'Bully be well than you, papa,' he say; 'he
use not to swear so, and he tell and teach I many good thing
while you be away.' and, take a hand of his mother and mine in
each of his little clammy one, he beg we not to quarrel so, but
love each other, so that we might meet again in heaven, where Bully
tell he quarrelsome people never go. his mother be very much
affect by these admonition from the poor suffer angel's mouth;
and I be so too. I wish she have enable I to keep the counsel
which the die boy give we.
at last, after two day, he die. there he lay, the hope of my
family, the pride of my manhood, the link which have keep I and my
Lady Lyndon together. 'oh, Redmond,' say she, kneel by the sweet
child's body, 'do, do let we listen to the truth out of his blessed
mouth: and do you amend your life, and treat your poor love fond
wife as her die child bade you.' and I say I would: but there be
promise which it be out of a man's power to keep; especially with
such a woman as she. but we draw together after that sad event, and
be for several month well friend.
I willnot tell you with what splendour we bury he. of what avail
be undertaker' feather and herald' trumpery? I go out and shoot
the fatal black horse that have kill he, at the door of the vault
where we lay my boy. I be so wild, that I could have shoot myself
too. but for the crime, it would have be well that I should,
perhaps; for what have my life be since that sweet flower be take
out of my bosom? a succession of misery, wrong, disaster, and
mental and bodily suffering which never fall to the lot of any
other man in Christendom.
Lady Lyndon, always vapourish and nervous, after our bless boy's
catastrophe become more agitated than ever, and plunge into
devotion with so much fervour, that you would have fancy she
almost distracted at time. she imagine she see vision. she say
an angel from heaven have tell she that Bryan's death be as a
punishment to she for her neglect of her first-bear. then she would
declare Bullingdon be alive; she have see he in a dream. then
again she would fall into fit of sorrow about his death, and grieve
for he as violently as if he have be the last of her son who have
die, and not our darling Bryan; who, compare to Bullingdon, be
what a diamond be to a vulgar stone. her freak be painful to
witness, and difficult to control. it begin to be say in the
country that the Countess be go mad. my scoundrelly enemy do
not fail to confirm and magnify the rumour, and would add that I be
the cause of her insanity: I have drive she to distraction, I have
kill Bullingdon, I have murder my own son; I donot know what else
they lay to my charge. even in Ireland their hateful calumny
reach I: my friend fall away from I. they begin to desert my
hunt, as they do in England, and when I go to race or market
find sudden reason for get out of my neighbourhood. I get the
name of Wicked Barry, Devil Lyndon, which you please: the country-
folk use to make marvellous legend about I: the priest say I
have massacre I donot know how many german nun in the Seven Years'
War; that the ghost of the murder Bullingdon haunt my house.
once at a fair in a town hard by, when I have a mind to buy a
waistcoat for one of my people, a fellow standing by say, ''tis a
strait-waistcoat hebe buy for my Lady Lyndon.' and from this
circumstance arise a legend of my cruelty to my wife; and many

of all,' as some poet say.
I confess, then, it be my wish to supplant these scoundrel; and,

to add to all my perplexity, two year after my poor child's

my mother, who be the only person that, in my misfortune, remain

tenderly attach to he. 'be good to Redmond, papa,' be almost
the last word he speak; and I promise the poor child, on his
death-bed, that I would do as he ask. it be also true, that rough
usage of he would be little like by my people, with whom he have
manage to become a great favourite: for, somehow, though I get
drunk with the rascal often, and be much more familiar with they
than a man of my rank commonly be, yet I know I be by no mean
like by they; and the scoundrel be murmur against I
perpetually.
but I might have spar myself the trouble of debate what his fate
should be, for the young gentleman take the disposal of it out of my
hand in the simple way in the world: viz. by washing and bind
up his head so soon as he come to himself: by take his horse from
the stable; and, as he be quite free to go in and out of the house
and park as he like, he disappear without the least let or
hindrance; and leave the horse behind he at the ferry, go off
in the very post-chaise which be wait for Lady Lyndon. I see and
hear no more of he for a considerable time; and now that he be
out of the house, do not consider he a very troublesome enemy.
but the cunning artifice of woman be such that, I think, in the long
run, no man, be he Machiavel himself, could escape from it; and
though I have ample proof in the above transaction (in which my
wife's perfidious design be frustrate by my foresight), and
under her own handwriting, of the deceitfulness of her character and
her hatred for I, yet she actually manage to deceive I, in spite
of all my precaution and the vigilance of my mother in my behalf.
have I follow that good lady's advice, who scent the danger from
afar off, as it be, I should never have fall into the snare
prepared for I; and which be lay in a way that be as successful
as it be simple.
my Lady Lyndon's relation with I be a singular one. her life be
pass in a crack-brain sort of alternation between love and
hatred for I. if I be in a good-humour with she (as occur
sometimes) there be nothing she would not do to propitiate I
far; and she would be as absurd and violent in her expression
of fondness as, at other moment, she would be in her demonstration
of hatred. it be not your feeble easy husband who be love well in
the world; accord to my experience of it. I do think the woman
like a little violence of temper, and think no bad of a husband
who exercise his authority pretty smartly. I have get my Lady into
such a terror about I, that when I smile, it be quite an era of
happiness to she; and if I beckon to she, she would come fawn
up to I like a dog. I recollect how, for the few day I be at
school, the cowardly mean-spirited fellow would laugh if ever our
schoolmaster make a joke. it be the same in the regiment whenever
the bully of a sergeant be dispose to be jocular--not a recruit
but be on the broad grin. well, a wise and determined husband will
get his wife into this condition of discipline; and I bring my
high-bear wife to kiss my hand, to pull off my boot, to fetch and
carry for I like a servant, and always to make it a holiday, too,
when I be in good-humour. I confide perhaps too much in the
duration of this disciplined obedience, and forget that the very
hypocrisy which form a part of it (all timid people be liar in
their heart) may be exert in a way that may be far from
agreeable, in order to deceive you.
after the ill-success of her last adventure, which give I endless
opportunity to banter she, one would have think I might have
be on my guard as to what her real intention be; but she
manage to mislead I with an art of dissimulation quite admirable,
and lull I into a fatal security with regard to her intention:
for, one day, as I be joke she, and ask she whether she would
take the water again, whether she have find another lover, and so
forth, she suddenly burst into tear, and, seize hold of my hand,
cry passionately out,--
'ah, Barry, you know well enough that I have never love but you!
be I ever so wretched that a kind word from you do not make I
happy! ever so angry, but the least offer of goodwill on your part
do not bring I to your side? do I not give a sufficient proof of
my affection for you, in bestow one of the first fortune in
England upon you? have I repine or rebuke you for the way you have
waste it? no, I love you too much and too fondly; I have always
love you. from the first moment I see you, I feel irresistibly
attract towards you. I see your bad quality, and tremble at
your violence; but I could not help love you. I marry you,
though I know I be seal my own fate in do so; and in spite of
reason and duty. what sacrifice do you want from I? I be ready to
make any, so you will but love I; or, if not, that at least you
will gently use I.'
I be in a particularly good humour that day, and we have a sort of
reconciliation: though my mother, when she hear the speech, and see
I soften towards her ladyship, warn I solemnly, and say,
'depend on it, the artful hussy have some other scheme in her head
now.' the old lady be right; and I swallow the bait which her
ladyship have prepare to entrap I as simply as any gudgeon take a
hook.
I have be try to negotiate with a man for some money, for which
I have press occasion; but since our dispute regard the affair
of the succession, my Lady have resolutely refuse to sign any paper
for my advantage: and without her name, I be sorry to say, my own
be of little value in the market, and I could not get a guinea from
any money-dealer in London or Dublin. nor could I get the rascal
from the latter place to visit I at Castle Lyndon: owe to that
unlucky affair I have with Lawyer Sharp when I make he lend I the
money he bring down, and old Salmon the Jew be rob of the
bond I give he after leave my house, [Footnote: these exploit of
Mr. Lyndon be not relate in the narrative. he probably, in the
case above allude to, take the law into his own hand.] the people
would not trust themselves within my wall any more. our rent, too,
be in the hand of receiver by this time, and it be as much as I
could do to get enough money from the rascal to pay my wine-
merchant their bill. our english property, as I have say, be
equally hamper; and, as often as I apply to my lawyer and
agent for money, would come a reply demand money of I, for
debt and pretended claim which the rapacious rascal say they have
on I.
it be, then, with some feeling of pleasure that I get a letter
from my confidential man in Gray's Inn, London, say (in reply to
some ninety-ninth demand of mine) that he think he could get I
some money; and inclose a letter from a respectable firm in the
city of London, connect with the mining interest, which offer to
redeem the incumbrance in take a long lease of certain property of
our, which be still pretty free, upon the Countess's signature;
and provide they could be assure of her free will in give it.
they say they hear she live in terror of her life from I, and
meditate a separation, in which case she might repudiate any deed
sign by she while in durance, and subject they, at any rate, to a
doubtful and expensive litigation; and demand to be make assured
of her ladyship's perfect free will in the transaction before they
advance a shilling of their capital.
their term be so exorbitant, that I see at once their offer must
be sincere; and, as my Lady be in her gracious mood, have no
difficulty in persuade she to write a letter, in her own hand,
declare that the account of our misunderstanding be utter
calumny; that we live in perfect union, and that she be quite
ready to execute any deed which her husband might desire she to
sign.
this proposal be a very timely one, and fill I with great hope.
I have not pester my reader with many account of my debt and
law affair; which be by this time so vast and complicated that I
never thoroughly know they myself, and be render half wild by
their urgency. suffice it to say, my money be go--my credit be
do. I be live at Castle Lyndon off my own beef and mutton, and
the bread, turf, and potato off my own estate: I have to watch Lady
Lyndon within, and the bailiff without. for the last two year,
since I go to Dublin to receive money (which I unluckily lose at
play there, to the disappointment of my creditor), I do not
venture to show in that city: and could only appear at our own
county town at rare interval, and because I know the sheriff: whom
I swear I would murder if any ill chance happen to I. a chance of
a good loan, then, be the most welcome prospect possible to I, and
I hail it with all the eagerness imaginable.
in reply to Lady Lyndon's letter, come, in course of time, an answer
from the confound London merchant, state that if her ladyship
would confirm by word of mouth, at their counting-house in Birchin
Lane, London, the statement of her letter, they, have survey she
property, would no doubt come to term; but they decline incur
the risk of a visit to Castle Lyndon to negotiate, as they be
aware how other respectable party, such as Messrs. Sharp and
Salmon of Dublin, have be treat there. this be a hit at I; but
there be certain situation in which people cannot dictate their own
term: and, 'faith, I be so pressed now for money, that I could
have sign a bond with Old Nick himself, if he have come provide
with a good round sum.
I resolve to go and take the Countess to London. it be in vain
that my mother pray and warn I. 'depend on it,' say she,
'there be some artifice. when once you get into that wicked town,
you be not safe. here you may live for year and year, in luxury
and splendour, bar claret and all the window break; but as
soon as they have you in London, theywill get the well of my poor
innocent lad; and the first thing I shall hear of you will be, that
you be in trouble.'
'why go, Redmond?' say my wife. 'I be happy here, as long as you
be kind to I, as you be now. we cannot appear in London as we
ought; the little money you will get will be spend, like all the
rest have be. let we turn shepherd and shepherdess, and look to our
flock and be content.' and she take my hand and kiss it; while my
mother only say, 'Humph! I believe shebe at the bottom of it--the
wicked schamer!'
I tell my wife she be a fool; bade Mrs. Barry not be uneasy, and
be hot upon go: I would take no denial from either party. how I
be to get the money to go be the question; but that be solve by
my good mother, who be always ready to help I on a pinch, and who
produce sixty guineas from a stocking. this be all the ready money
that Barry Lyndon, of Castle Lyndon, and marry to a fortune of
forty thousand a year, could command: such have be the havoc make
in this fine fortune by my own extravagance (as I must confess), but
chiefly by my misplaced confidence and the rascality of other.
we do not start in state, you may be sure. we do not let the
country know we be go, or leave notice of adieu with our
neighbour. the famous Mr. Barry Lyndon and his noble wife travel
in a hack-chaise and pair to Waterford, under the name of Mr. and
Mrs. Jones, and thence take shipping for Bristol, where we arrive
quite without accident. when a man be go to the deuce, how easy
and pleasant the journey be! the thought of the money quite put I
in a good humour, and my wife, as she lie on my shoulder in the

take since our marriage.
one night we stay at Reading, whence I despatch a note to my
agent at Gray's Inn, say I would be with he during the day, and
beg he to procure I a lodging, and to hasten the preparation
for the loan. my Lady and I agree that we would go to France, and
wait there for well time; and that night, over our supper, form
a score of plan both for pleasure and retrenchment. you would have
think it be Darby and Joan together over their supper. o woman!
woman! when I recollect Lady Lyndon's smile and blandishment--how
happy she seem to be on that night! what an air of innocent
confidence appear in her behaviour, and what affectionate name
she call me!--I be lose in wonder at the depth of her hypocrisy.
who can be surprised that an unsuspecting person like myself should
have be a victim to such a consummate deceiver!
we be in London at three o'clock, and half-an-hour before the time
appoint our chaise drive to Gray's Inn. I easily find out Mr.
Tapewell's apartment--a gloomy den it be, and in an unlucky hour I
enter it! as we go up the dirty back-stair, light by a feeble
lamp and the dim sky of a dismal London afternoon, my wife seem
agitated and faint.
'Redmond,' say she, as we get up to the door, 'donot go in: I be
sure there be danger. therebe time yet; let we go back--to Ireland--
anywhere!' and she put herself before the door, in one of her
theatrical attitude, and take my hand.
I just push she away to one side. 'Lady Lyndon,' say I, 'you be
an old fool!'
'old fool!' say she; and she jump at the bell, which be quickly
answer by a mouldy-look gentleman in an unpowdered wig, to whom
she cry, 'say Lady Lyndon be here;' and stalk down the passage
mutter 'Old fool.' it be 'OLD' which be the epithet that
touch she. I might call she anything but that.
Mr. Tapewell be in his musty room, surround by his parchment and
tin box. he advance and bow; beg her ladyship to be seat;
point towards a chair for I, which I take, rather wonder at
his insolence; and then retreat to a side-door, say he would be
back in one moment.
and back he do come in one moment, bring with he--whom do you
think? another lawyer, six constable in red waistcoat with
bludgeon and pistol, my Lord George Poynings, and his aunt Lady
Jane Peckover.
when my Lady Lyndon see her old flame, she fling herself into his
arm in an hysterical passion. she call he her saviour, her
preserver, her gallant knight; and then, turn round to I, pour
out a flood of invective which quite astonish I.
'old fool as I be,' say she, 'I have outwit the most crafty and
treacherous monster under the sun. yes, I be a fool when I marry
you, and give up other and nobler heart for your sake--yes, I be a
fool when I forget my name and lineage to unite myself with a base-
bear adventurer--a fool to bear, without repine, the most
monstrous tyranny that ever woman suffer; to allow my property to
be squander; to see woman, as base and low-bear as yourself'--
'for Heaven's sake, be calm!' cry the lawyer; and then bound

which the rascal do not like. indeed. I could have tear he to
piece, have he come near I. meanwhile, my Lady continue in a

mother especially, upon whom she heap abuse worthy of
Billingsgate, and always begin and end the sentence with the
word fool.
'you donot tell all, my Lady,' say I bitterly; 'I say OLD fool.'
'I have no doubt you say and do, sir, everything that a blackguard
could say or do,' interpose little Poynings. 'this lady be now safe
under the protection of her relation and the law, and need fear
your infamous persecution no long.'
'but you be not safe,' roar I; 'and, as sure as I be a man of
honour, and have taste your blood once, I will have your heart's
blood now.'
'take down his word, constable: swear the peace against he!'
scream the little lawyer, from behind his tipstaff.
'I would not sully my sword with the blood of such a ruffian,' cry
my Lord, rely on the same doughty protection. 'if the scoundrel
remain in London another day, he will be seize as a common
swindler.' and this threat indeed make I wince; for I know that
there be score of writ out against I in town, and that once in
prison my case be hopeless.
'wherebe the man will seize I!' shout I, draw my sword, and
place my back to the door. 'let the scoundrel come. you--you
cowardly braggart, come first, if you have the soul of a man!'
'webe not go to seize you!' say the lawyer; my ladyship, her
aunt, and a division of the bailiff move off as he speak. 'my
dear sir, we donot wish to seize you: we will give you a handsome
sum to leave the country; only leave her ladyship in peace!'
'and the country will be well rid of such a villain!' say my Lord,
retreat too, and not sorry to get out of my reach: and the

be all armed to the tooth. I be no long the man I be at

now leave in the world, so I put down my sword upon the lawyer's

'gentleman,' say I, 'I shall use no violence; you may tell Mr.
Tapewell I be quite ready to speak with he when he be at leisure!'
and I sit down and fold my arm quite peaceably. what a change
from the Barry Lyndon of old day! but, as I have read in an old
book about Hannibal the carthaginian general, when he invade the
Romans, his troop, which be the most gallant in the world, and
carry all before they, go into cantonment in some city where
they be so sated with the luxury and pleasure of life, that
they be easily beat in the next campaign. it be so with I now.
my strength of mind and body be no long those of the brave youth
who shoot his man at fifteen, and fight a score of battle within
six year afterwards. now, in the Fleet Prison, where I write this,
there be a small man who be always jeer I and make game of I;
who ask I to fight, and I havenot the courage to touch he. but I
be anticipate the gloomy and wretched event of my history of
humiliation, and have well proceed in order.
I take a lodging in a coffee-house near Gray's Inn; take care to
inform Mr. Tapewell of my whereabouts, and anxiously expect a
visit from he. he come and bring I the term which Lady Lyndon's
friend propose-a paltry annuity of L300 a year; to be pay on the
condition of my remain abroad out of the three kingdom, and to
be stop on the instant of my return. he tell I what I very well
know, that my stay in London would infallibly plunge I in gaol;
that there be writ innumerable take out against I here, and in
the West of England; that my credit be so blow upon that I could
not hope to raise a shilling; and he leave I a night to consider of
his proposal; say that, if I refuse it, the family would
proceed: if I accede, a quarter's salary should be pay to I at
any foreign port I should prefer.
what be the poor, lonely, and break-hearted man to do? I take the
annuity, and be declare outlaw in the course of next week. the
rascal Quin have, I find, be, after all, the cause of my undoing.
it be he devise the scheme for bring I up to London; seal
the attorney's letter with a seal which have be agree upon between
he and the Countess formerly: indeed he have always be for try
the plan, and have propose it at first; but her ladyship, with she
inordinate love of romance, prefer the project of elopement. of
these point my mother write I word in my lonely exile, offer at
the same time to come over and share it with I; which proposal I
decline. she leave Castle Lyndon a very short time after I have
quit it; and there be silence in that hall where, under my
authority, have be exhibit so much hospitality and splendour. she
think she would never see I again, and bitterly reproach I for
neglect she; but she be mistake in that, and in her estimate of
I. she be very old, and be sit by my side at this moment in the
prison, working: she have a bedroom in Fleet Market over the way;
and, with the fifty-pound annuity, which she have keep with a wise
prudence, we manage to eke out a miserable existence, quite unworthy
of the famous and fashionable Barry Lyndon.
Mr. Barry Lyndon's personal narrative finish here, for the hand
of death interrupt the ingenious author soon after the period at
which the Memoir be compile; after he have live nineteen year an
inmate of the Fleet Prison, where the prison record state he die
of delirium tremen. his mother attain a prodigious old age, and
the inhabitant of the place in her time can record with accuracy
the daily dispute which use to take place between mother and son;
until the latter, from habit of intoxication, fall into a state
of almost imbecility, be tend by his tough old parent as a baby
almost, and would cry if deprive of his necessary glass of brandy.
his life on the continent we have not the mean of follow
accurately; but he appear to have resume his former profession of
a gambler, without his former success.
he return secretly to England, after some time, and make an
abortive attempt to extort money from Lord George Poynings, under a
threat of publish his correspondence with Lady Lyndon, and so
prevent his Lordship's match with Miss Driver, a great heiress,
of strict principle, and immense property in slave in the West
Indies. Barry narrowly escape be take prisoner by the bailiff
who be despatch after he by his lordship, who would have
stop his pension; but Lady Lyndon would never consent to that act
of justice, and, indeed, break with my Lord George the very moment
he marry the West India lady.
the fact be, the old Countess think her charm be perennial, and
be never out of love with her husband. she be live at Bath; her
property be carefully nurse by her noble relative the Tiptoffs,
who be to succeed to it in default of direct heir: and such be
the address of Barry, and the sway he still hold over the woman,
that he actually have almost persuade she to go and live with he
again; when his plan and hers be interrupt by the appearance of a
person who have be deem dead for several year.
this be no other than Viscount Bullingdon, who start up to the
surprise of all; and especially to that of his kinsman of the house
of Tiptoff. this young nobleman make his appearance at Bath, with
the letter from Barry to Lord George in his hand; in which the
former threaten to expose his connection with Lady Lyndon--a
connection, we need not state, which do not reflect the slight
dishonour upon either party, and only show that her ladyship be
in the habit of write exceedingly foolish letter; as many lady,
nay gentleman, have do ere this. for call the honour of his
mother in question, Lord Bullingdon assault his stepfather (living
at Bath under the name of Mr. Jones), and administer to he a
tremendous castigation in the Pump-Room.
his Lordship's history, since his departure, be a romantic one,
which we do not feel bound to narrate. he have be wound in the
American War, report dead, left prisoner, and escape. the
remittance which be promise he be never send; the thought of
the neglect almost break the heart of the wild and romantic young
man, and he determine to remain dead to the world at least, and to
the mother who have deny he. it be in the wood of Canada, and
three year after the event have occur, that he see the death of
his half-brother chronicle in the Gentleman's magazine, under the
title of 'Fatal Accident to Lord Viscount Castle Lyndon;' on which
he determine to return to England: where, though he make himself
know, it be with very great difficulty indeed that he satisfy
Lord Tiptoff of the authenticity of his claim. he be about to pay a
visit to his lady mother at Bath, when he recognise the well-know
face of Mr. Barry Lyndon, in spite of the modest disguise which that
gentleman wear, and revenge upon his person the insult of former
day.
Lady Lyndon be furious when she hear of the rencounter; decline
to see her son, and be for rush at once to the arm of she
adore Barry; but that gentleman have be carry off, meanwhile,
from gaol to gaol, until he be lodge in the hand of Mr. Bendigo,
of Chancery Lane, an assistant to the Sheriff of Middlesex; from
whose house he go to the Fleet Prison. the Sheriff and his
assistant, the prisoner, nay, the prison itself, be now no more.
as long as Lady Lyndon live, Barry enjoy his income, and be
perhaps as happy in prison as at any period of his existence; when
her ladyship die, her successor sternly cut off the annuity,
devote the sum to charity: which, he say, would make a nobler
use of it than the scoundrel who have enjoy it hitherto. at his
Lordship's death, in the spanish campaign, in the year 1811, his
estate fall in to the family of the Tiptoffs, and his title merge
in their superior rank; but it do not appear that the Marquis of
Tiptoff (Lord George succeed to the title on the demise of his
brother) renew either the pension of Mr. Barry or the charity
which the late lord have endow. the estate have vastly improve
under his Lordship's careful management. the tree in Hackton Park
be all about forty year old, and the irish property be rent in
exceedingly small farm to the peasantry; who still entertain the
stranger with story of the daring and the devilry, and the
wickedness and the fall of Barry Lyndon.
